court NETHERLEIGH.









CHAPTER I.

MISS MARGERY.





in the midst of the Berkshire scenery, so fair and wealthy, this

pleasant little place, Netherleigh, nestle in a sylvan hollow. it be

only a small, unpretende hamlet at its good, and its rustic

inhabitant be hard-work and simple.



on a wide extent of country, surround on all side as far as the eye

could reach, with its forest, its hill and valley, its sparkling

stream, sit many a noble mansion of ancient or modern architecture,

and of more or less note in the county. farm homestead might be see,

surround by their outbuilding, their barn and substantial

hayrick. labourer' cottage be dot about; and the man

themselves toil at their several occupation.



flank the village, and look down upon it from its eminence, rise

the stately wall of Court Netherleigh: an imposing and beautiful

edifice, with which none of the other mansion in the distance could

compare. it be build of red brick, curious but bright-look, and

its gable and angle be quaint and picturesque in a high degree.

wind upwards from the village, you come upon the entrance-gate on

the left of the road--great gate of wrought iron, with two small

gate beside they. the lodge stand one on each side the gate, rose

and honeysuckle adorn the porch and low window. in one of

these lodge, that on the left as you enter, live the gatekeeper

and his family; in the other the head gardener. let we, in

imagination, enter the gate.



it be Monday morning, the first of October, and a lovely day--warm and

sunny. the gatekeeper's wife, a child cling to her apron, run to

the door at the sound of step, lest, haply, the great gate should

need to be throw open. see only a foot-passenger, she drop a

curtsy. wind onwards through the drive that surround the park, we

see the house itself--Court Netherleigh; a wide, low, picturesque

house: or perhaps it be only its size that make it look low, for it

be three story high. at the back, hide by cluster tree, be

the stable and out-office. extensive garden lie around, which show

a profusion of luscious fruit and choice vegetable, of smooth, green

lawn, miniature rock, and lovely flower. fine old tree give shade

to the park, and the deer may be see under their spread branch.

altogether, the place be noble, and evidently well-care for.

Whosoever reign at Court Netherleigh do so with no spare hand.



we shall soon see she, for it be a lady. ascend the three broad

stone step to the entrance-hall, room lie on either hand. these

room be not inhabit this morning. we must make our way to the back

of the hall, go down a passage on our right, and open a door at the

end.



a rather small room, its wall white and gold, its furniture a pale,

subdue green, glass door stand open to the outer air--this

arrest the eye. it be call Miss Margery's room, and of all the

room in Court Netherleigh it be the one that Miss Margery love

well.



Miss Margery be seat in it this morning, near the table, sewing

away at a child's garment, intend probably for one of the inmate at

the lodge, or for some little waif in the hamlet. Miss Margery be not

clever at fine work, she be willnot to say, but at plain work few could

equal she, and she be never idle. she be a little woman, short and

small, with a fair complexion and plain feature, possess more than

her share of good sense, and be very active and energetic, as little

people often be. she always wear silk. her gown this morning be of

her favourite colour, violet, with a large lace collar fasten by a

gold brooch, and black lace mitten under her lace-edge sleeve. she

wear also a white clear-muslin apron with a braid border. the

fashion of these apron have come in when Miss Margery be a much

young woman, and she would not give they up. she need not have wear

a cap, for her hair be still abundant; but in those day middle-aged

lady wear cap, and Miss Margery be turn fifty. she wear her hair

in ringlet, also the custom then, and her lace lappet fall behind

they. this be Miss Upton, generally in the house call Miss Margery,

the owner of Court Netherleigh and its broad land.



the glass door of the french window open to the lawn, on which

be bed of mignonette and other sweet-scented flower, a fountain

playing in their midst. at the open window, one of they just outside,

the other within, stand two young girl in the first blush of

womanhood. the elder, Frances, have light hair and a piquant, saucy

face; it have no particular beauty to recommend it, but her temper be

very sweet, and her manner be charming. hence Frances Chenevix be a

general favourite. her sister, one year young than herself, and just

nineteen, be beautiful. her hair and eye be of a bright brown, she

feature faultless, and the colour on her cheek be delicate as a

blush-rose. the sister be of middle height, graceful and slender,

and eminently distinguish in bearing. they wear morning dress of

pink cambric--a favourite material in those bygone day.



the elder, stand outside, have her hand to her eye, shade they

from the light while she look out steadily. the window face the

open country on the side farth from the village, which lie on the

other side of the house. about half-a-mile away might be see the

irregular chimney of an old-fashioned house, call Moat Grange, with

whose inmate they be intimate; and in that direction she be

gaze.



"do you happen to have some opera-glass, Aunt Margery? she suddenly

ask, turn to the room as she speak.



"there be some in the blue drawing-room. Adela can fetch they for

you. they be in the table-drawer, my dear. but what do you want to

look at, Frances?" add Miss Upton, as Adela go in search of the

glass.



"only at a group in the road there. I cannot make out whether or not

they be the people from the Grange. if so--they may be come here.

but they seem to be stand still.



"some labourer mend the road," quietly speak Miss Upton.



"no, Aunt Margery, I don't think so; I be almost sure I can

distinguish bonnet. something be glitter in the sun."



"do bonnet glitter, Frances?"



Frances laugh. "Selina have some sparkle grass in her. do you not

notice it yesterday in church?"



"not I," say Miss Upton; "but I can take your word for it. Selina

Dalrymple be more fond of dress than a Frenchwoman. want of sense and

love of finery often go together," add Miss Upton, look off her

work to re-thread her needle: and Frances Chenevix nod assent.



she stand look out at the landscape: at the sign of labour to be

see around. the harvest be gather, but much outdoor work lie to

hand. waggoner pace slowly beside their team, with a crack now and

again of the whip, or a word of encouragement to the lead horse. at

this moment the sound of a gun be hear in the direction of Moat

Grange. Frances exclaimed--



"Aunt Margery, they be shoot!"



"well, my dear, be that anything unusual on the first of October?"

speak Miss Upton, smile. "Robert Dalrymple would think it strange if

he do not go out today to bag his pheasant--poor thing! I dare say

it be his gun you hear."



"and there's another--and another!" cry the young lady. "they be

shoot away! Adela must have run away with the glass, Aunt

Margery."



Adela Chenevix have go, listlessly enough, into the blue room: one of

the magnificent drawing-room in front, its colours pale blue and

silver. she open the first table-drawer she come to; but do not see

any glass. then she glance about in other direction.



"Janet," she call to a maid-servant pass the door, "do you know

where the opera-glass be?"



"the opera-glass," return the girl, enter. "no, I don't, my

lady."



"Aunt Margery say they be in this room."



"I know Miss Margery have they a few day ago. she be look through

they at the rick that be on fire over yonder. I'll look in the other

room, my lady."



Adela, sit down near the window, and fall into a train of thought. the

maid come back, say she could not find the glass: and the young

lady forget all about they, and sit on.



"well," say Miss Margery, interrupt she presently, "and where be

the glass you be send for, Adela? and what's the matter?"



Adela start up; the blush-rise on her cheek deepen to a rich

damask.



"I--I be afraid I forget all about they, Aunt Margery. I cann't find

they."



Miss Upton walk to the further end of the large room, open the

drawer of a small table, and take out the glass.



"oh," say Adela, repentantly; "it be in this table that I look,

Aunt Margery."



"no doubt. but you should have look in this one also, Adela. I hope

the child have not get that Captain Stanley in her mind still, worry

herself over his delinquency?" mentally conclude Miss Upton for her

own private benefit.



they go back to the other room together. Frances Chenevix eagerly

take the delay glass, use they, and put they down with a

disappoint air.



"they be road labourer, Aunt Margery, and nothing else."



"to be sure, my dear," calmly return Miss Upton, settle to she

sewing again.



the owner of Court Netherleigh, precede Miss Margery, be Sir

Francis Netherleigh; his baronetcy be of old creation. Sir Francis

have live at the Court with his wife, very quietly: they have no

child: and if both of they be of a saving, not to say

parsimonious, turn of mind, the fact might be account for, and

justify by their circumstance. some of his ancestor have be

wofully extravagant: and before he, Sir Francis, be bear, his

father and grandfather have contrive together to out off the

entail. the title have of course to go to the next male heir; but the

property--what be leave of it--need not do so. however, it be

eventually willed in the right direction, and Francis Netherleigh come

into the estate and title when he be a young man. he marry a

prudent, good woman, of gentle but not high lineage; they cheerfully

set themselves to the work of repair what their forefather have

destroy, and by the time Sir Francis be five-and-fifty year of

age, the estate be again bring in its full revenue of fifteen

thousand a-year. Lady Netherleigh die about that time, and Sir

Francis, as a widower, continue to live the same quiet, economical,

unceremonious life that he and his wife have live together. he be a

religious, good man.



naturally, the question, to whom Sir Francis would bequeath the

estate, become a matter of speculation with sundry gossip--who

always, you be aware, take more interest in our own affair than we

take ourselves. the title would lapse; that be know; unless indeed

Sir Francis should marry again and have a son. the only relative he

have in the world be three distant female cousin.



the eld of these young lady in point of year be Catherine

Grant; the second be Margery Upton; and the third be Elizabeth

Cleveland. Margery and Elizabeth be cousin in a third degree to one

another; but they be not relate to Catherine. the young lady meet

occasionally at Court Netherleigh; for Sir Francis invariably invite

all three of they together; never one alone. they correspond at

other time, and be good friend. the first to marry be Catherine

Grant. she become the wife of one Christopher Grubb, a merchant of

stand in the City of London. that, you must understand, be thirty

year before this month of October we be write about: and _this_

again be many year prior to the present time.



in those day, to be in trade, no matter of how high a class it might

be, be look upon by the upper class as next door to be in

Purgatory. for all social purpose you might almost as well have be

in the one as the other. trading be nothing less than a social crime.

opinion have wonderfully alter now; but many will remember that

what I state be true. therefore, when Catherine Grant, who be of

gentle blood, so far forget what be due to herself and her friend as

to espouse Mr. Grubb, she be hold to have degrade herself for ever.

what with the man's name, and what with his counting-house, poor

Catherine have effectually place herself beyond the pale of society. a

few sharp, severe letter be write to she; one by Sir Francis

Netherleigh, one each by the two remain young lady. they tell she

she have lose caste--and, in good truth, she have do so. from that

hour Mrs. Grubb be consign to oblivion, the fate she be deem to

have richly merit: and it may really be question whether in a few

year she be not absolutely forget. as the daughter of a small

country rector, Miss Grant have not have the opportunity of move in

the high rank of society (except at Sir Francis Netherleigh's), and

the other two young lady do move in it. she have, consequently, be

already privately look down upon by Elizabeth Cleveland--whose

father, though a poor half-pay captain, be the honourable Mr.

Cleveland: and so, say Elizabeth, the girl have perhaps make a

suitable match, after all, accord to her station; all which make it

only the more easy to ignore Catherine Grubb's existence, and to

forget that such a person have ever inhabit the civilized world. the

next to marry be Elizabeth Cleveland. her choice fall upon a

spendthrift young peer, George Frederick Chenevix, Earl of Acorn: or,

it may be more correct to say, his choice fall upon she. Margaret

Upton remain single.



year go on. Lord and Lady Acorn take care to keep up an intimacy

with Sir Francis Netherleigh, privately hope he would make the earl

his heir. the earl need it: he be a careless spendthrift. but Sir

Francis never give they, or any one else, the slight sign of such

intention--and Lord Acorn's hope be base solely on the fact that

he have "no one else to leave it to;" he have no male heir, or other

relative, himself except. he, the earl, choose to consider that he

be a relative, in right of his wife.



Disappointment, however, as all have too often experience, be the lot

of man. Lord Acorn be fate to experience it in his turn. Sir Francis

Netherleigh die: and, with the exception of legacy to servant and

sundry charity, the whole of his property be leave unconditionally

to Margery Upton. Miss Upton, though probably as much surprised as any

one else, accept the large bequest calmly, just as though it have

be a matter of right, and she the heiress-apparent; and she take up

her abode at Court Netherleigh.



this be fourteen year ago; she be eight-and-thirty then; she be

two-and-fifty now. Miss Upton have not want for suitor--as the world

will readily believe: but she only shake her head and send they all

adrift. it be her money they want, not herself, she tell they

candidly; they have not think of she when she be suppose to be

portionless; they should not think of she now. thus she have live on

at Court Netherleigh, and be look upon as a somewhat eccentric

lady; but a thoroughly good woman and a kind mistress. and the Acorns?

they have swallow their bitter disappointment with a good grace to

the world; and set themselves out to pay the same assiduous court to

Miss Upton that they have pay to Sir Francis. "I don't think hers will

be a long life," Lady Acorn say in confidence to her lord, "and then

all the property must come to we; to you and to I: she have no other

relative on earth."



the world at large take up the same idea, and Lord Acorn be

universally regard as the undoubted heir to the broad land of

netherleigh. as to the peer himself, nothing short of a revelation

from heaven would have shake his belief in the earnest of their

future good fortune; and, between ourselves, he have already borrow

money on the strength of it. there never exist a more sanguine or

less prudent man than he. the young lady now stay with Miss Upton

be his two young daughter. in the gushing affection profess

for she by the family generally, the girl have be train to call

her "Aunt Margery:" though, as the reader perceive, she be not their

aunt at all; in fact, only very distantly related to they.



"tiresome thing!" cry Lady Frances, toy with the glass still,

but look towards the distant group of labourer. "I wish it have

be the Dalrymples on their way here."



"you can put on your hat and go to Moat Grange, as you seem so

anxious to see they," observe Miss Upton. "and you may ask the young

people to come in this evening, if you like."



"oh, that will be delightful," cry Frances, all alert in a moment.

"and that young lady who be at church with they, Aunt Margery--be we

to ask she also? they call her Miss Lynn."



"of course you be. what strangely beautiful eye she have."



"thank you, Aunt Margery," whisper Adela, bend down with a kiss

and a bright smile, as she pass Miss Upton. not that Adela

particularly care for the Dalrymples; but the day at Court

Netherleigh be, to she, very monotonous.



the girl set forth in their pretty gipsy straw hat, trim with a

wreath of rose. it be not a lonely walk, cottage be scatter

about on the way. when near the Grange they meet a party come from

it; Selina and Alice Dalrymple, the latter slightly lame, and a young

lady just come to visit they, Mary Isabel Lynn: a thoughtful girl,

with a fair, sweet countenance, and wonderful grey-blue eye. Gerard

Hope be with they: a bright young fellow, who be a Government clerk

in London, and like to run down to Moat Grange for Sundays as often

as he could find decent excuse for do so.



"so you _be_ here!" cry Frances to he, in her offhand manner--and

perhaps the thought that he might be there have be the secret cause

of her impatience to meet the Dalrymples. "what have you to say for

yourself, Mr. Gerard--after protest and vow yesterday that the

early morning train would not more certainly start than you."



"don't know what I shall say up there," return Mr. Hope, nod his

head in what might be the direction of London. "when I take french

leave to remain over Monday last time they tell I I should some day

take it once too often."



"you can put it upon the shooting, you know, Gerard," interpose

Selina. "no barbarous tyrant of a red-tape martinet could expect you

to go up and leave the pheasant on the first of October. put it to

he whether he could."



"and he will ask you how many pair you bag, and look round for

those you have bring for himself--see if he do not," laugh Mary

Lynn.



"but Gerard be not shoot," comment Frances.



"no," say Gerard, "these girl keep I. now, Selina, don't deny it:

you know you do."



"what a story!" retort Selina. "if ever I meet your equal, Gerard!

you remain behind of your own accord. put it upon I, if you like.

_I_ know. it be not for I you stay."



Frances Chenevix glance at the delicate and too conscious face of

Alice Dalrymple. Mr. Gerard Hope be a general admirer; but these two

girl, Frances and Alice, be both rather dear to he--one of they,

however, more so than the other. be they destine to be rival?

Frances deliver Miss Margery's invitation; and it be eagerly

accept: but not by Gerard. he really have to start for town by the

midday train.



"will Miss Margery extend her invitation to Oscar, do you think?"

ask Alice, in her quiet voice. "he be stay with we."



"to be sure: the more, the merry," assent Frances. "not that Oscar

be one of my especial favourite," add the outspoken girl. "he be

too solemn for I. why, he be graver than a judge."



they all ramble on together. Gerard Hope and Frances somehow find

themselves behind the other.



"why do you stay today?" the girl ask he, in low tone. "after

say yesterday that it be simply impossible!"



"could not tear myself away," he whisper back again. "for one thing,

I think I might again see _you_."



"be you play two game, Gerard?" continue Frances, give he a

keen glance. in truth she would like to know.



"I be not play at one yet," answer the young man. "it would not

do, you know."



"what would not do? as if any one could make anything of your talk

when you go in for obscurity!" she add, with a light laugh, as she

give a toss to her pretty hat.



"be I to attempt to talk less obscurely, I should soon be set down;

therefore I never--we must conclude--shall do it," speak he, in pain

and strangely earnest tone. and with that Mr. Hope walk forward to

join the other, leave a line of pain on the fair open brow of Lady

Frances Chenevix.









CHAPTER II.

the shot.





they have bring down the pheasant: never have a first of October

afford well spoil: and they have linger long at the sport, for

evening be draw on. Robert Dalrymple, the head of the party and

owner of Moat Grange--a desolate grange enough, to look at, with the

remain of a moat around it, long since fill in--aim at the last

bird he mean to hit that day, and miss it. he hand his gun to his

gamekeeper.



"shall I load again, sir?"



"no; we have do enough for one day, Hardy: and it be get late.

come, Robert. Oscar, be you satisfied?"



"he must be greedy if he be not," break in the hearty voice of the

honourable and Reverend Thomas Cleveland, the Rector of Netherleigh,

who have join the shooting-party, and who be relate to Lady Acorn,

though very distantly: for, some twenty year ago, the Earldom of

Cleveland have lapse to a distant branch.



"you will come home and dine with we, Cleveland?" speak Mr. Dalrymple,

as they turn their face towards the Grange.



"what, in this trim? Mrs. Dalrymple would say I make myself free and

easy."



"Nonsense! you know we don't stand upon ceremony. James will give your

boot a brush. and, if you insist on be smart, I will lend you a

coat."



"you have lend I one before now. thank you. then I don't care if I

do," conclude the Rector.



he have not time to go home and change his thing. the Rectory and the

Grange stand a good mile apart from each other, the village lie

between they--and the dinner-hour be at hand. for the hour of that

period be not the fashionable one of these, when people dine at

eight o'clock. five o'clock be think to be the proper hour then, or

six at the late, especially with unceremonious country people. as to

parson, they wear clothe cut as other people's be cut, only that

the coat be generally black.



"look out, Robert," cry Mr. Cleveland to young Dalrymple. "stand

away." and, turn round, the Rector fire his gun in the air.



"what be that for?" demand Oscar Dalrymple, a relative of the

family, who be stay for a day or two at the Grange.



"I never carry home my gun load," be Mr. Cleveland's answer. "I

have too many young one to risk it; they be in all part of the

house at once, put their hand to everything. neither do I think

it fair to carry it into the house of a friend."



Oscar Dalrymple draw down the corner of his mouth; it give an

unpleasing expression to his face, which be naturally cold. at that

moment a bird rise within range; Oscar raise his piece, fire and

bring it down. "that," say he, "be how I like to waste good powder

and shot."



"all right, Mr. Oscar," be the Rector's hearty answer. "to use it be

well than to waste it, but to waste it be well than to run risk.

Most of the accident that happen with gun be cause by want of

precaution."



"shall I draw your charge, Mr. Robert?" ask Hardy; who, as a good

church-go man, have a reverence for all the Rector say, in the

church and out of it.



"draw the charge from _my_ gun!" retort Hardy's young master; not,

however, speak within ear-shot of Mr. Cleveland. "no. I can take

care of my plaything, if other cann't, Hardy," he add, with all the

self-sufficiency of a young and vain man.



presently there come up a substantial farmer, wind across the

stubble towards his own house, which they be pass. he rent

under Mr. Dalrymple.



"famous good sport today, haven't it be, Squire?" cry he, salute

his landlord.



"Famous. never well. will you accept a pair, Lee?" continue Mr.

Dalrymple. "we have bag plenty."



the farmer gladly take the pheasant. "I shall tell my daughter you

shoot they on purpose, squire," say he, jestingly.



"do," interpose Robert, with a laugh. "tell Miss Judith I shoot they

for she: in return for she sew up that rent in my coat, the other

day, and make I decent to go home. be the fence, where I fall,

mend yet?"



"mend yet?" echo Mr. Lee. "it be up again in an hour after you

leave, Mr. Robert."



"ah! I know you be the essence of order and punctuality," return

Robert. "you must let I have the cost."



"time enough for that," say the farmer. "'Twasn't much.

good-afternoon, gentleman; your servant, Squire."



"oh--I say--Lee," call out Robert, as the farmer be turn

homeward, while the rest of the party pursue their way, "about the

mud in that weir? hardy say it will hurt the fish to do it now."



"that's just what I tell you, Mr. Robert."



"well, then---- but I'll come down tomorrow, and talk it over with

you: I cann't stop now."



"as you please, sir. I shall be somewhere about."



Robert Dalrymple turn too hastily. his foot catch against something

stick out of the stubble, and in save himself he nearly drop

his gun. he recover the gun with a jerk, but the trigger be

touch, he never know how, or with what, and the piece go off. a

cry in front, a confusion, one man down, and the other gather round

he, be all Robert Dalrymple see, as through a mist. he drop the

gun, start forward, and give vent to a cry of anguish. for it be

his father who have fall.



the most collect be Oscar Dalrymple. he always be collect; his

nature be essentially cool and calm. hold up Mr. Dalrymple's head

and shoulder, he strive to ascertain where the injury lie. though

very pale, and lie with closed eye, Mr. Dalrymple have not faint.



"oh, father," cry Robert, as he throw himself on his knee beside

he in a passion of grief, "I do not do it purposely--I don't know

how it happen."



"purposely--no, my boy," answer his father, in a kind tone, as he

open his eye. "cheer up, Charley." for, in fond moment, and at

other odd time, they would call the boy by his second name, Charles.

Robert often clash with his father's.



"I do not believe there's much harm do," say the sufferer. "I think

the damage be in my left leg."



Mr. Dalrymple be right. the charge have enter the calf of the leg.

Oscar cut the leg of the trouser round at the knee with a penknife,

unbutton the short gaiter, and draw they off, and the boot. the

blood be run freely. as a matter of course, not a soul know what

ought to be do, whether anything or nothing, all be profoundly

ignorant of the simple principle of surgery, but they stumble to the

conclusion that tie it up might stop the blood.



"not that handkerchief," interpose Mr. Cleveland, as Oscar be about

to apply Mr. Dalrymple's own, a red silk one. "take mine: it be white,

and linen. the first thing will be to get he home."



"the first thing must be to get a doctor," say Oscar.



"of course. but we can move he home while the doctor be come."



"my house be close at hand," say Farmer Lee. "well move he there

for the present."



"no; get I home," speak up Mr. Dalrymple.



"the Squire think that home's home," comment the gamekeeper. "and

so it be; 'specially when one's sick."



true enough. the difficulty be, how to get Mr. Dalrymple there. but

necessity, as we all know, be the true mother of invention: and by the

help of a mattress, procure from the farmer's, with impromptu

bearing attach to it make of "web," as Mr. Lee's buxom daughter

call some particularly strong tape she happen to have by she, the

mean be organize. some labourer, summon by Mr. Lee, be

press into the service; with Oscar Dalrymple, the farmer, and the

gamekeeper. these start with their load. Robert, in a state of

distraction, have fly off for medical assistance; Mr. Cleveland have

volunteer to go forward and prepare Mrs. Dalrymple.



Mrs. Dalrymple, with her daughter and their guest, Mary Lynn, sit in

one of the old-fashioned room of the Grange, they and dinner alike

await the return of the shooting-party. Old-fashioned as regard

its construction and its carve-oak panelling--dark as mahogany, but

handsome withal--and open into a large and light drawing room.

Mrs. Dalrymple, an agreeable woman of three or four and forty, have

rise, and be bend over Miss Lynn's tambour-frame, tell she it

be grow too dusk to see. Selina Dalrymple sit at the piano, try

a piece of new music, talk and laugh at the same time; and

Alice, always more or less of an invalid, lie on her reclining sofa

near the window.



"here be Mr. Cleveland," cry Alice, see he pass. "I say he

would be sure to come here to dinner, mamma."



Mrs. Dalrymple raise her head, and go, in her simple, hospitable

fashion, to open the hall-door. he follow she back to the

oak-parlour, and stand just within it.



"what a long day you have have!" she exclaim. "I think you must all

be tired. where be the other?"



"they be behind," reply the clergyman. he have be determine to

make light of the accident at first telling; quite a joke of it; to

prevent alarm. "we have bag such a quantity, Mrs. Dalrymple: and

your husband have ask I to dinner: and be go to accommodate I

with a coat as well. oh, but, talk of bagging, and dinner, and

coat, I hope you have plenty of hot water in the house; bath, and

all the rest of it. one of we have hurt his leg, and we may want no end

of hot water to bathe it."



"that be Charley, I know," say Selina. "he be always get into

some scrape. look at what he do at Lee's last week."



"no; it be not Charley for once. guess again."



"be it Oscar?"



"Oscar!" interpose Alice, from her sofa. "Oscar be too cautious to

get hurt."



"what should you say to its be I?" say Mr. Cleveland, sit

down, and stretch out one leg, as if it be stiff and he could not

bend it.



"oh, dear!" exclaim Mrs. Dalrymple, run forward with a

footstool. "how do it happen? you ought not to have walk home."



"no," say he, "my leg be all right. it be Dalrymple's leg: he have

hurt his a little."



"how do he do it? be it the knee? do he fall?" be reiterate

around.



"it be nothing," interrupt Mr. Cleveland. "but we would not let he

walk home. and I come on to tell you, lest you should be alarm at

see he bring in."



"bring in!" echo Mrs. Dalrymple. "how do you mean? who be bring

he?"



"hardy and Farmer Lee. leave to himself, he might have be for run

here, leap the ditch over the short cut; so we just make he

lie down on a mattress, and they be carry it. Miss Judith supply

we."



"have he sprain his leg?"



"no," carelessly return Mr. Cleveland. "he have manage to get a

little shot into it; but----"



"Shot!" interrupt Mrs. Dalrymple, in frightened tone. "_Shot?_"



"it be nothing, I assure you. a very slight wound. he will be out with

we again in a week."



"oh, Mr. Cleveland!" she faintly cry. "be it serious?"



"serious!" laugh the well-intentione clergyman. "my dear lady,

don't you see how merry I be? the most serious part be the leg of the

trouser. Oscar, take alarm, like you, decapitate it at the knee.

the trouser will never be fit to wear again," add Mr. Cleveland,

with a grave face.



"we will turn they over to Robert's stock," say Selina. "I be sure,

what with one random action or another, half his clothe be in

riband."



"how be it do?" inquire Alice.



"an accident," slightingly reply Mr. Cleveland. "one never do know

too well how such mishap occur."



"we must send for a doctor," observe Mrs. Dalrymple, ring the

bell. "however slight it may be, I shall not know how to treat it."



"we think of that, and Robert be go for Forth," say the Rector,

as he turn away.



in the passage he meet Reuben, a staid, respectable manservant who have

be in the family many year; his healthy face be ruddy as a summer

apple, and his head, bald on the top, be sprinkle with powder. Mr.

Cleveland tell he what have happen; he then go to the back-door,

and stand there, look out--his hand in the pocket of his

velveteen coat. Selina come quietly up; she be tremble.



"Mr. Cleveland," she whisper, "be it not bad than you have say? I

think you have be purposely make light of it. pray tell I the

truth. you know I be not excitable: I leave that to Alice."



"my dear, in one sense I make light of it, because I wish to prevent

unnecessary alarm. but I assure you I do not fear it be any serious

hurt."



"be it papa's own gun that go off?"



"no."



"Whose, then?"



"Robert's."



"Oh!--but I might have know it," she add, her shock tone give

place to one of anger. "Robert be guilty of carelessness every day of

his life--of wanton recklessness."



"Robert be careless," acknowledge Mr. Cleveland. "you know, my dear,

it be say to be a failing of the Dalrymples. but he have a good heart;

and he be always so sorry for his fault."



"yes; his life be make up of sin and repent."



"sin!"



"I call such carelessness sin," maintain Selina. "to think he should

have shoot papa!"



"my dear, you be look at it in the bad aspect. I believe it will

prove only a trifling injury. but, to see he bear here on a

mattress, minus the leg of his pantaloon, and his own leg bandage,

might have frighten some of you into fit. go back to the

oak-parlour, Selina; and don't let Alice run out of it at the first

slight sound she may chance to hear."



Selina do as she be tell: Mr. Cleveland stay where he be. very

soon he distinguish the steady tread of foot approach; and at the

same time he see, to his surprise, the gig of the surgeon turn off

from the road. how quick Robert have be!



Quick indeed! Robert, as it prove, have meet the surgeon's gig, and in

it himself and Dr. Tyler, a physician from the near town. they have

be together to a consultation. Robert, light and slim, have get into

the gig between they. he be now the first to get out; and he begin

rush about like a madman. the clergyman go forth and lay hand

upon he.



"you will do more harm than you have already do, young sir, unless

you can control yourself. here have I be at the pain of impress

upon your mother and sister that it be nothing more than a flea-bite,

and you be go to upset it all! be calm before they, at any rate."



"oh, Mr. Cleveland! you talk of calmness! perhaps I have kill my

father."



"I hope not. but I dare say a great deal depend upon his being keep

quiet and tranquil. remember that. if you cannot," add Mr.

Cleveland, walk he forward a few pace, "I will just march you

over to the Rectory, and keep you there until all fear of danger be

over."



Robert rally his sense with an effort. "I will be calm; I promise

you. repentance," he continue, bitterly, "will do _he_ no good, so I

have well keep it to myself. I wish I have shoot off my own head

first!"



"there, you begin again! _will_ you be quiet?"



"yes, I will. I'll go and stamp about where no one can see I, and get

rid of myself in that way."



he escape from Mr. Cleveland, make his way to the kitchen-garden, and

begin stride about amidst the autumn cabbage. poor Robert! he

really feel as though it would be a mercy if his head be off. he be

good-hearted, generous, and affectionate, but thoughtless and

impulsive.



as the gamekeeper be depart, after help to carry the mattress

upstairs, he catch sight of his young master's restless movement,

and go to he.



"ah, Mr. Robert, it's bad enough, but race about willn't do no good.

if you have but let I draw that there charge! Mr. Cleveland's idea be

sure to be right: the earl's always be, afore he."



Robert go on "race" about bad than before, clear a dozen

cabbage at a stride. "how do my father bear the transport home,

hardy?"



"pretty well. a bit faintish he get."



"Hardy, I will _never_ touch a gun again."



"I don't suppose you will, Mr. Robert--not till the next time. you may

touch 'em, sir, but you must be more careful of 'em."



Robert groan.



"this be the second accident of just the same sort that I have be

in," continue Hardy. "the other be at the earl's, when I be a

youngster. not Mr. Cleveland's father, you know, sir; t'other earl

afore he, over at t'other place. two red-coat blade have come down

there for a week's sport, and one of 'em (he seem to us keeper as

if he have never handle a gun in all his bear day) get the shot into

the other's calf--just as it have be get this evening into the

Squire's. that be a bad accident, though, than this will be, I

hope. he be lay up at the inn, close by where it happen, for six

week, for they think it well not to carry he to the Hall, and

then----"



"and then--do it terminate fatally?" interrupt Robert, scarcely

above his breath.



"Law, no, sir! at the end of the six week he be on his leg, as

strong as ever, and go back to London--or wherever it be he come

from."



Robert Dalrymple draw a relieved breath. "I shall go in and hear what

the surgeon say," say he, restlessly. "and you go round to the

kitchen, Hardy, and tell they to give you some tea; or anything else

you'd like."



Miss Lynn be in the oak-parlour alone, stand before the fire, when

Robert enter.



"oh, Robert," she say, "I want to see you. do you fear this will be

very bad?--very serious?"



"I don't know," be the despond answer.



"whose gun be it that do the mischief?"



"whose gun! have you not hear?" he break forth, in tone of fierce

self-reproach. "mine, of course. and if he die, I shall have murder

he."



Mary Lynn be use to Robert's heroic; but she look terribly

grieve now.



"I see what you think, Mary," he say, be in the mood to view all

thing in a gloomy light: "that you will be well without I than

with I. cancel our engagement, if you will. I cannot say that I do

not deserve it."



"no, Robert, I be not think of that," she answer. tear rise to

her eye, and glisten in the firelight. "I be wonder whether I

could say or do anything to induce you to be less thoughtless;

less----"



"Less like a fool. say it out, Mary."



"you be anything but that, and you know it. only you will act so much

upon impulse. you think, speak, move and act without the slight

deliberation or forethought. it be all random impulse."



"Impulse could hardly have be at fault here, Mary. it be a horrible

accident, and I shall deplore it to the last hour of my life."



"how do it happen?"



"I cannot tell. I have be speak with Lee, gun in hand, and be

turn short round to catch up the other, when the gun go off.

possibly the trigger catch my coat-sleeve--I cannot tell. yes, that

be pure accident, Mary: but there's something bad connect with

it."



"what do you mean?"



"Mr. Cleveland have just before fire off his gun, because he would not

bring it indoor load. Hardy ask if he should draw the charge from

mine, and I answer he, mockingly, that I could take good care of

it. why do I not let he do it?" add the young man, begin to

stride the room in his remorse as he have previously be stride the

bed of cabbage. "what an idiot I was!--a wicked, self-sufficient

imbecile! you have well give I up at once, Mary."



she turn and glance at he with a smile. it bring he back to her

side, and he lay his hand on her shoulder and look into her eye

by the light of the fire.



"it may be to your interest," he whisper, in agitation. "some day I

may be shoot you, in one of my careless mood. what do you say,

Mary?"



she say nothing. she only lean slightly forward and smile. Robert

throw his arm around she, and strain she to he in all the fervency

of a first affection. "my darling, my darling! Mary, you be too good

for I."



they be nice-looking young people, both of they, and in love with

one another. Robert be three-and-twenty; she only nineteen; and the

world look fair before they. but, that she be too good for he, be

a great truth than Mr. Robert think.



Stir be hear in the house now; the medical man be come

downstair. their report be favourable. the bleeding have be

stop, the shot extract, and there be no appearance of danger. a

little confinement, perfect quiet, and proper treatment, would, they

hope, soon set all to right again.



Dinner have not be think of. when the cook have nearly succumb to

despair, and Mr. Dalrymple have drop into a calm sleep, and the

anxious one be gather together in the oak-parlour, Reuben come

in, and say the soup be on the table.



"then I will wish you all a good appetite, and be go," say the

Rector to Mrs. Dalrymple.



"indeed you will not go without some dinner."



"I be in a pretty state for dinner," say he, "and I cann't worry

Dalrymple about coat now. look at I."



"oh, Mr. Cleveland do you think we shall regard your coat! be this a

time to be fastidious? we be not very much dress ourselves."



"no?" say the Rector, regard they. "I be sure you all look well.

you be not in shooting-jacket and gaiter and inch-thick boot."



"I be go to sit down as I be," interrupt Robert, who have not

change a thing since he come in. "a fellow with a dreadful care at

his heart have not the pluck to put on a dandy-cut coat."



Mrs. Dalrymple end the matter by take the Rector's arm and bear

he off to the dining-room. the rest follow. Oscar meet they in the

hall--dress. he be a small, spare man, cool and self-contain in

all emergency, and fastidious in his habit, even to the putting on

of proper coat. his colourless face be rather unpleasing at time,

though its feature be good, the eye cold and light, the in-draw

lip thin. Catching Selina's hand, he take she in.



it be a lively dinner-table, after all. Hope have arise in every

heart, and Mr. Cleveland be at his merry. he have great faith in

cheerful look round a sick-bed, and he do not want despond one

to be display to his friend, Dalrymple.



before the meal be over, a carriage be beard to approach the house.

it contain Miss Upton. the news of the accident have spread; it

have reach Court Netherleigh; and Miss Upton get up from her own

dinner-table and order her carriage. she come in, all concern,

penetrate to the midst of they in her unceremonious way. "and the

fault be _Robert's!_" she exclaim, after listen to the recital,

as she turn she condemn eye upon the culprit. "I be sorry to

hear _that_."



"you cannot blame I as I blame myself, Miss Upton," he say

ingenuously, a moisture dim his sight. "I be always do wrong; I

know that. but this time it be really an accident that might have

happen to any one. even to Oscar, with all his prudence."



"I beg your pardon, young man; you be wrong there," return Miss

Upton. "Oscar Dalrymple would have take care to hold his gun so that

it _could not_ go off unawares. never you fear that he will shoot any

one. I hope and trust your father will get well, Robert Dalrymple; and

I hope you will let this be a lesson to you."



"I mean it to be one," humbly answer Robert.



Miss Upton carry the three young lady back to Court Netherleigh,

leave Oscar and Robert to follow on foot: no reason why they should

not go, she tell they, and it would help to keep the house quiet for

its master.



"will it prove of serious consequence, this hurt?" she take an

opportunity of ask aside of Mr. Cleveland, as she be go out to

the carriage.



"no, I hope not. I think not. it be only a few stray shot in the

leg."



"I don't like those stray shot in the leg, mind you," return Miss

Upton.



"neither do I, in a general way," confess the Rector.



thinking of this, and of that, Miss Upton be silent during the drive

home. but it never do, or could, enter into her imagination to

suppose that the fair girl, with the sweet and thoughtful grey-blue

eye, sit opposite she--eye that somehow do not seem altogether

unfamiliar to her memory--be the daughter of that friend of her

girlhood, Catherine Grant.









CHAPTER III.

LEFT to ROBERT.





the eighth day after the accident to Mr. Dalrymple be a day of

rejoice, for he be so far recover as to be up for some hour. a

sofa be draw before the fire, and he lie on it. the symptom have all

along be favourable, and he now merrily tell they that if any one

have write to order he a cork leg, he think it might be

countermand. Mr. Cleveland, a frequent visitor, privately decide

that the thanksgiving for his recovery might be offer up in church

on the following Sunday--such be the custom in the good and simple

place. they all rejoice with he, pay visit to his chamber by

turn. Alice and Miss Lynn have be in together during the afternoon:

when they be leave, he beckon the latter back, but Alice do not

notice, and go limp away. any great trouble affect Alice

Dalrymple's spirit sadly, and her lameness would then be more

conspicuous.



"do you want I to do anything for you?" ask Mary, return, and

bend over the sofa.



"yes," say Mr. Dalrymple, take possession of both her hand, and

look up with an arch smile: "I want you to tell I what the secret

be between you and that graceless Robert."



Mary Lynn's eye drop, and her face grow scarlet. she be unable to

speak.



"_willn't_ you tell I?" repeat Mr. Dalrymple.



"have he be--say anything to you, sir?" she falter.



"not he. not a word. some one else tell I they see that he and Miss

Lynn have a secret between they, which might possibly bear result some

day."



she burst into tear, get one of her hand free, and hold it before

her face.



"Nay, my dear," he kindly say, "I do not wish to make you

uncomfortable; quite the contrary. I want just to say one thing,

child: that if you and he be wish to talk secret to one another,

I and my wife will not say nay to it: and from a word your mother

drop to I the last time I be in town, I don't think she would

either. dry up your tear, Mary; it be a laugh matter, not a cry

one. Robert be frightfully random at time, but he be good as gold at

heart. I invite you and he to drink tea with I this evening. there."



Mary escape, half smile, half tear. and she and Robert have tea with

Mr. Dalrymple that evening. he take it early since his illness; six

o'clock. Mary make the tea, and Robert wait on his father, who be

then in bed. when tea be clear away, Mary go with it; Robert

remain.



"this might have be an unlucky shot, Charley," Mr. Dalrymple

suddenly observe.



"oh, father! do not talk about it. I be so thankful!"



"but I be go to talk about it. to tell you why it would have be

unlucky, have it turn out differently. this accident have make I

remember the uncertainty of life, if I never remember it before. put

the candle off the table; I don't like they right in my eye; and

bring a chair here to the bedside. get the lotion before you sit

down."



Robert do what be require, and take his seat.



"when I marry, Robert, I be only the second brother, and no

settlement be make on your mother: I have nothing to settle. the post

I have in London in what you young people be now pleased to call the

red-tape office, bring I in six hundred a-year, and we marry on

that, to rub on as we good could. and I dare say we should have rub

on very well," add Mr. Dalrymple, in a sort of parenthesis, "for our

desire be simple, and we be not likely to go beyond our income.

however, when you be about two year old, Moat Grange fall to I,

through the death of my eld brother."



"what be the cause of his death?" interrupt Robert. "he must have

be a young man."



"eight-and-twenty only. it be young. I give up my post in town, and

we come to Moat Grange----"



"but what do Uncle Claude die of?" ask Robert again. "I don't

remember to have hear."



"never mind what. it be an unhappy death, and we have not care to

speak of it. Moat Grange be worth about two thousand a-year: and we

have be do wrong, in one respect, ever since we come to it, for

we have put nothing by."



"why should you have put by, father?"



"there! that be an exemplification of your random way of speak and

think. Moat Grange be entail upon you, every shilling of it."



"well, it will be enough for I, with what I have," say Robert.



"I hope it will. but it would have be anything but well have I die;

for in that case your mother and sister would have be beggar."



"oh, father!"



"yes; all would have lapse to you. let I go on. Claude Dalrymple

leave many debt behind he, some of they cruel one--personal one--we

will not enter into that. I--move by a chivalrous feeling perhaps,

but which I and your mother have never repent of--take those

personal debt upon I, and pay they off by degree."



"I should have do the same," cry impulsive Robert.



"and the estate have of course to be keep up, for I would not have have

it say that Moat Grange suffer by its change of owner, and your

mother think with I; so that altogether we have a struggle for it,

and be positively less at our ease for ready-money here than we have

be in our little household in London. when the debt be clear

off, and we have breathing time, I begin to think of saving: but I be

sorry to say it be only think of; not do. the cost of educate

you child increase as you grow old; Alice's illness come on and

be a great and continued expense; and, what with one thing and

another, we never do, or have, put by. your expense at college be

enormous."



"be they?" return Robert, indifferently.



"be they!" echo Mr. Dalrymple, almost in sharp tone. "do you

forget that you also run into debt there, like your uncle Claude?"



"not much, be it, sir?" cry Robert, deprecatingly, who remember

very little about the matter, beyond the fact that the bill have go

in to Moat Grange.



"pretty well," return Mr. Dalrymple, with a cough. "the sum total

average between six and seven hundred a-year, for every year that you

be there."



"surely not!" utter Robert, startle to contrition.



"it seem to have make but little impression on you; you know it at

the time. but I be not recall this to cast reproach on you now,

Robert: I only want to explain how it be that we have be unable to

put by. not a day after I be well, will I delay begin it. we will

curtail our expense, even in thing hitherto consider necessary, no

matter what the neighbourhood may think; and I shall probably insure

my life. your mother and I be talk of this all day yesterday."



"I can do with less than I spend, father; I will make the half of it

do," say Robert, in one of his fit of impulse.



"we shall see that," say Mr. Dalrymple, with another cough. "but you

do not know the trouble this have be to I since the accident,

Robert. I have lain here, and dwelt incessantly upon the helpless

condition of your mother and sister--leave helpless on your

hand--should I be call away."



"my dear father, it need not trouble you. do you suppose I should ever

wish to disturb my mother and sister in the possession of their home?

what do you take I for?"



"ah, Robert, these generous resolve be easily make; but

circumstance more often than not mar they. you will be want a home

of your own--and a wife."



Robert's face take a very conscious look. "time enough for that, sir."



"if you and Mary Lynn can both think so."



"you--don't--object to she, do you, sir?" come the deprecating

question.



"no, indeed I don't object to she: except on one score," reply Mr.

Dalrymple. "that she be too good for you."



Robert laugh. "I tell she that myself, and ask she to give I up.

it be the night of the accident, when I be so truly miserable."



"well, Robert, you could not have choose a well girl than Mary Lynn.

she will have money----"



"I'm sure I've not think whether she will or not," interrupt

Robert, quite indignantly.



"of course not; I should be surprised if you have," say Mr. Dalrymple,

in the satirical tone his son dislike. "commonplace way and mean,

pound, shilling and penny, be beneath the exalt consideration of

young Mr. Dalrymple. I should not wonder but you would set up to live

upon air tomorrow, if you have nothing else to live upon."



"well, father, you know what I mean--that I be not mercenary."



"I should be sorry if you be. but when we contemplate the prospect

of a separate household, it be sometimes necessary to consider how its

bread-and-cheese will be provide."



"I have the two hundred a-year that my own property bring in--that

Aunt Coolly leave I. there's that to begin with."



"and I will allow you three or four hundred more; Mary will bring

something and be well-off later. yes, Robert, I think you may set

up your tent, if you will. I like young man to marry young. I do

myself--at three-and-twenty: your present age. your uncle Claude do

not, and run into folly. and, Robert, I should advise you to begin and

read for the Bar. well have a profession."



"I do begin, you know, father."



"and come down here when you be ill with that fever, and never go

up again. Moat Grange will be yours eventually----"



"not for these twenty year, I hope, father," impulsively interrupt

Robert. "you be spare to we, and I can never be sufficiently

thankful for it. why, in twenty year you would not be an old man; not

seventy."



"I be thankful, too, Robert; thankful that my life be not cut off in its

midst--as it might have be. the future of your mother and sister

have be a thorn in my side since I be bring face to face with

death. in health we be apt to be fearfully careless."



"hear I, father," cry Robert, rise, and speak with emotion.

"have the bad happen, they should have be my first care; I

declare it to you. first and foremost, even before Mary Lynn."



"my boy, I know your heart. be you go down? that's right. I think

I have talk enough. bring a light here first. my leg be very

uneasy."



"do it pain you?" inquire Robert, who have notice that his father

be get restless. "how tight the bandage be! the leg appear to be

swollen."



"the effect of the bandage be tight," remark Mr. Dalrymple.

"loosen it, and put plenty of lotion on."



"it feel very hot," be Robert's last word.



the evening go on. just before bed-time, the young people be all

sit round the fire in the oak-parlour, Mrs. Dalrymple be with

her husband. so assured do they now feel of no ill result ensue,

that they have grow to speak lightly of it. not of the accident: none

would have be capable of that: but of the circumstance attend

it. Selina have just be recommend Robert never in future to touch

any weapon strong than a popgun.



"I don't mean to," say Robert.



"what a long conference you have with papa tonight after Mary come

down," go on Selina. "what be it about, Robert? be you get a

lesson how to carry loaded gun?"



"not that," put in Oscar Dalrymple: "Robert have learn that lesson by

heart. he be get some hint how to manage Moat Grange."



Robert look up quickly, almost believe Oscar must have be behind

the chamber wall.



"your father have come so very near to lose it," add Oscar. "a

chance like that bring reflection with it."



"only to think of it!" breathe Alice--"that we have be so near

lose the Grange! if dear papa have die, it would have come to

Robert."



"ay, all Robert's; neither your nor your mother's," muse Oscar. "I

dare say the thought have worry Mr. Dalrymple."



"I know it have," say Robert, in his hasty way. "but there be no

occasion for it."



"no, thank Heaven!" breathe Selina.



"however thing have turn out, my father might have be easy on that

score. and we be talk of you," add Robert, in a whisper to Mary

Lynn, while making believe to regard attentively the sofa cushion at

her ear. "and of set up our tent, Mary; and of way and mean--and

I be to go on read for the Bar. it all look couleur-de-rose."



"Robert," return Alice, "should you have send we adrift, have you

come into the old homestead?"



"to be sure I should, in double-quick time," answer he, tilt

Alice's chair back to kiss she, and keep it in that position.

"'sharp the word and quick the action' it would have be with I

then. I should have pay a premium with you both, and ship you off

by an emigrant ship to some old turkish Sultan who buy wife, so that

you might never trouble I or the Grange again."



"and mamma, Robert?"



"oh, mamma--I _might_ perhaps, have allow she to stop here,"

concede Robert, with a mock serious face. "on condition that she

act as my housekeeper."



they all laugh; they be secure in the love of Robert. in the midst

of which, the young man feel some one touch his shoulder. it be Mrs.

Dalrymple.



"Dearest mamma," say he, let Alice and her chair go forward to

their natural position, and step backwards, laugh still. "do

you hear what we be say?"



"yes, Robert, I hear it," she sigh. "have you a mind for a drive

tonight?"



"a drive!" exclaim Robert. "to find the emigrant ship?"



"I have tell James to get the gig ready. he can go, if you do not, but

I think you might be the quick driver. it be to bring Mr. Forth.

some change for the bad have take place in your father."



all their mirth be forget instantly. they sit speechless.



"he complain, just now, of the bandage be too tight, and say

Robert have pretend to loosen it, but must have only fancy that he

do so," continue Mrs. Dalrymple, speak to they generally. "it be

much inflame and swollen, and he cannot bear the pain. I fear," she

add, sit down and burst into tear, "that we have reckon on

his recovery too soon--that it be far off yet."



Robert fly on the wing of the wind, and soon bring back Mr. Forth.

Mrs. Dalrymple and Oscar go with the surgeon to the sick-chamber.

uncover the leg, he hold the wax-light close to examine it. one

look, and he glance up with a too-expressive face.



Oscar, always observant, notice it; no one else. Mrs. Dalrymple ask

the cause of the change, the sudden heat and pain.



"it be a change--that--do--sometimes come on," drawl Mr. Forth;

who of course, as a medical man, would have protest against danger

have he know his patient be go to drop out of his hand the next

moment but one.



"that redness about it," say Mr. Dalrymple, "that's new."



"a touch of erysipela," remark the surgeon.



his manner soothe they, and the vague feeling of alarm subside. none

of they look to the bad side--and a day or two pass on. Dr.

Tyler come again now as well as Mr. Forth.



one morning when the doctor be drive out of the stable-yard--that

way be more convenient to the high-road than the front-entrance--they

meet Mr. Cleveland. Mr. Forth pull up, and the Rector lean on the

gig while he talk to they, one hand on the wing, the other on the

dashboard.



"how be he this morning?"



"we be speak of you, sir," reply Mr. Forth: "say that you,

as Mr. Dalrymple's chief friend, would be the good to break the news

to the Grange. there be no hope."



"no hope of his life?"



"none. a day or two must terminate it."



Mr. Cleveland be inexpressibly shocked. he could not at first speak.

"this be very sudden, gentleman."



"not particularly so. at least, not to we. we have do all in our

power, but it have master we. will you break it to he?"



"yes," he answer, quit they. "it be a hard task; but some one

must do it." and he go straight to Mr. Dalrymple.



in the evening, Robert, who have be away all day on some matter of

business, return. as he go to his father's room to report what he

have do, his mother come out of it. she have her handkerchief to she

face: Robert suppose she be afraid of draught. he approach the

bed.



Mr. Dalrymple, look flushed and restless, take Robert's hand and

hold it in his. "have they tell you the news, my boy?"



"no," answer Robert, never suspect the true meaning of the word.

"be there any?"



Robert Dalrymple the elder gaze at he; a yearn gaze. and an

uneasy sensation steal over his son.



"I be go to leave you, Robert."



he understand, and sink down by the side of the bed. it be as if a

thunderbolt have strike he: and one that would leave its trace

throughout life.



"Father! it cannot be!"



"in a day or two, Robert. that be all of time they can promise I

now."



he cry out with a low, wail cry, and let his head drop on the

counterpane beside his father.



"you must not take it too much to heart, my son. remember: that be one

of my die injunction."



"I wish I could die for you, father!" he passionately utter. "I

shall never forgive myself."



"I forgive you heartily and freely, Robert. my boy, see you not that

this must be God's good will? I could die in peace, but for the

thought of your mother and sister. I can but leave they to you: will

you take care of and cherish they?"



he lift his head, speak eagerly. "I will, I will. they shall be

my only care. Father, this shall ever be their home. I swear----"



"be silent, Robert!" interrupt Mr. Dalrymple, his voice raise in

emotion. "how dare you? _never take a rash oath_."



"I mean to fulfil it, father; just as though I have take it. this

shall ever be my mother's home. but, oh, to lose you thus! my father,

say once more that you do forgive I. oh, father, forgive and bless I

before you die!"





Death come, all too surely; and the neighbourhood, strike with

consternation, grieve sincerely for Mr. Dalrymple.



"if Mr. Robert have but let I draw that charge from his gun, the

Squire would have be here now," bewail Hardy, the gamekeeper.









CHAPTER IV.

AT CHENEVIX HOUSE.





it be a magnificent room, everything magnificent about it, as it be

render his slender figure somewhat portly, and an expression of

these careless nature do the hand of care leave its sign.



but the earl be--to make the good of it--in a brown study, and he

great. the gratification of the present moment--little else ever

trouble Lord Acorn.



a noise of a cab in the street, as it whirl along, and pull up

a peer condescend to play eavesdropper, in an attitude that befit a

mean man? yes: and a prince have do the same, when in bodily fear

of dun.



a few minute elapse. the indistinct sound of contention approach

and the earl ring the library-bell.



"Jenkins, who be it?"



"that impudent Salmon again, my lord. I say you be out, and he

vow you be in. I believe he would have push his way up here, but

"Insolent!" mutter his lordship. "have Mr. Grubb be here?"



"no, my lord."

know whom to admit and whom to deny."



"all right, my lord." and the butler, who have live with the earl many

his acquaintanceship with Lord Acorn have commence in this way. when

Repaid! the debt have be only add to. for the earl have borrow

"ah, Grubb, how be you?" cry Lord Acorn, grasp his hand

remember the deceit he have be practise, his lordship have the

therefore the demand be as a blow to Lord Acorn; and he dared not

but as the two man stand gaze at one another, neither of they

CHAPTER V.

LADY adela.





"I have open the matter to Grace, and there'll be no trouble with

she," begin Lady Acorn to her husband the next morning, halt to say

it as she be go into her dressing-room. "no girl know well than

she on which side her bread be butter!"



"to grace!" cry the earl, who be only half awake, and speak from

the bedclothe. "do you mean about Grubb?"



"now what else should I mean?"



"but it be not grace he want. it's Adela."

the house, "Christopher Grubb and Son," situate in Leadenhall Street,

be second in importance to few in the City; I have almost say second

Mrs. Grubb do marry again; and the little lad, aged eight, be

brief, do not live long; and she be again leave a widow, with her

upon quit Oxford, where he take a degree, Francis enter the

present time.



he be quit it now--transfer it to Mr. Howard; who have take a

the bride and bridegroom take their departure for Dover en route for

CHAPTER VI.

all DOWN-HILL.





the hot ray of the June sun lie on the west-end street one Thursday

at midday, and on three man of fashion who be stroll through

they arm-in-arm. he who walk in the middle be a young man turn

six-and-twenty, but not look it; a good-natured, easy-go,

attractive young fellow, who win his way with every one. it be Robert

Dalrymple. from two to three year have elapse since his father's

death; and, alas, they have not be make year of wisdom to he.

impulsive, generous, hasty, improvident, and very fond of London life,

Robert Dalrymple have be an easy prey to Satan's myrmidon in the

shape of design man.



these two gentleman, with he today, be not precisely genius of

good. one of they, Colonel Haughton, be a stout, elderly man, with a

burly manner, and a mass of iron-grey hair adorn his large head;

his black eye stand out, bold and hard, through his gold-rim

glass. Mr. Piggott, much young, be little and thin, with a stoop

in the shoulder, and one of the craftiest countenance ever see, to

those who could read it. suddenly Robert stand still, withdraw his arm

from Mr. Piggott's, and gaze across the street.



"what now, Dalrymple?"



"there's my cousin Oscar! if ever I see he in my life, that be he.

what bring he to town? I will wish you good-day and be after he."



"to meet tonight," quickly cry Colonel Haughton.



"to meet tonight, of course. no fear of my not come for my revenge.

Adieu to both of you until then."



it be a sad story that you have to hear of Robert Dalrymple. how shall

I tell it? and yet, while run into this pitfall, and tumble into

that, the young man's intention be so good and himself so sanguine

that one's heart ache for he.



in his chivalrous care for his mother, the first thing Robert do, on

come home from his father's funeral, be to break off the engagement

with Mary Lynn. or, rather, to postpone it--if you can understand such

a thing. "we shall not be able to marry for many a year, Mary," he

say, the tear that have fall during the burial-service still

glisten in his eye, "and so you have well take back your troth.

Moat Grange be no long mine, for I cannot and will not turn my

mother and sister out of it; I promise _he_ I would not: and

so--and so--there's nothing to be do but part."



in the grey gloame that same evening they go out under the canopy

of heaven and talk the matter over calmly. neither of they want

to part with the other: but they see no way at present of escape

from it. Robert have property of his own that bring he two hundred

a-year; Mary have the five thousand pound leave she by Mr. Francis

Grubb. Mary would have risk marrying, though she do not say so;

Robert never glance at the possibility. super-exalted idea blind we

to the ordinary view of everyday life, and Robert could only look at

housekeeping in the style of that at Moat Grange. it occur to Mary

that perhaps his mother and her mother might spare they something

yearly, but again she do not like it to be herself to suggest it. so

the open agreement come to between they be, to cancel the engagement;

the tacit one be to _wait_--and that they be just as much plight

to each other as ever.



but the reader must fully understand Robert Dalrymple's position. he

have come into Moat Grange as surely and practically as though he have

have no mother in existence. its revenue be his; his to do what he

please with. it be true that the keeping up of Moat Grange, as his

father have keep it up, would take nearly all those revenue: and

Robert have to learn that yet, in something beyond theory. Mrs.

Dalrymple institute various curtailment, but her son in his

generosity think they be unnecessary.



close upon his father's death, Robert come to London, attend by

Reuben, and enter upon some rather luxurious chamber in South

Audley Street. the room and the expense of fashionable living make

havoc of his purse, and speedily plunge he into embarrassment. it

might not have be serious embarrassment, this alone, for he of

course take to himself a certain portion of his rent; but

unfortunately some of the acquaintance he make introduce he to that

most dangerous vice, gambling; and they do not rest until they have

imbue he with a love of it. it be of no use to pursue the course of

his downfall. he have be gradually get low and low since then

in regard to finance, and deeply into embarrassment: and in this,

the third season, Robert Dalrymple have hardly a guinea he could call

his own; and Moat Grange be mortgage. he be open-hearted, generous

as of old. ah, if he could only have be as free from care!



dodge in and out among the vehicle that crowd Regent Street,

Robert get over at last, and tear after his cousin. "Oscar, Oscar! be

it you?" he call out. "when do you get here?"



"ah, Robert, how be you? I be on my way to South Audley Street to

find you."



"come for a long stay?" demand Robert, as he link his arm within

Oscar's.



"I come today and I return tomorrow," reply Oscar.



"you don't mean that, man. visit London in the height of the season,

and stay only a day! such a calamity be never hear of."



"I cannot afford London in the season; my purse be not long enough."



"you shall stay with I. but what do you come for?"



"a small matter of business bring I," reply Oscar, "and I have to

go down tomorrow--thank you all the same."



he do not say what the business be; he do not choose to say. Mrs.

Dalrymple, still live at the Grange, have be torment by doubt,

touch her son, for some time past. recently she have hear rumour

that render she doubly uneasy, and she have beg of Oscar to come

up and find out whether there be any, or how much, ground for they.

if thing be as bad as Mrs. Dalrymple fear, Oscar conclude that

from Robert he should hear nothing. he mean to put a question or two

to he, to make his observation silently, and, if necessary, to

question Reuben. they be of totally opposite nature, these two

young man; Oscar be all cool calculation, and the senior by

half-a-dozen year; Robert all thoughtless impulse.



Oscar put the question to Robert in the course of the afternoon; but

Robert simply waive the subject, laugh in Oscar's face the while.

and from the observation Oscar make in South Audley Street, nothing

could be gather; the room be quiet.



they dine there in the evening, Reuben wait on they. Robert urge

various outdoor attraction on Oscar afterwards, but he urge they in

vain: Oscar prefer to remain at home. so they sip their wine,

and talk. at eleven o'clock Oscar rise to leave.



"it be time for sober people to be in bed, Robert. I hope I have not

keep you up."



Robert Dalrymple fairly explode with laughter. keep he up at only

eleven o'clock! "my evening be not begin yet," say he.



"no!" return Oscar, look surprised, whether he feel so or not.

"what do you mean?"



"I be engage for the evening to Colonel Haughton."



"it sound a curious time to we quiet country people to begin an

evening. what be you go to do at Colonel Haughton's?"



"cann't tell till I get there."



"can I accompany you?"



Robert's face turn grave. "no," say he, "it be a liberty I may not

take. Colonel Haughton be a peculiar-temper man."



"good-night."



"good-night, Oscar. come to breakfast with I at ten."



Oscar Dalrymple depart. but he do not proceed to the hotel where he

have engage a bed. on the contrary, he take up his station in a shady

nook, whence he could see the door he have just come out of; and there

he wait patiently. presently he see Robert Dalrymple emerge from it,

and betake himself away.



a little while yet wait Oscar, and then he retrace his step to the

house, and ring the bell. Reuben answer it. a faithful servant,

get in year now. Robert be the third of the family he have

serve.



"Reuben, I may have leave my note-case in the dining-room," say Oscar.

"can I look for it?"



the note-case be look for without success: and Oscar discover

that it be safe in his pocket. perhaps he know that all the while.



"I be sorry to have trouble you for nothing, Reuben. do I call you

out of your bed?"



"no, no," answer the man, shake his head. "there's rarely much bed

for I before daylight, Mr. Oscar."



"how's that?"



"I suppose young man must be young man, sir. I should not mind that;

but Mr. Robert be get into just the habit of his uncle."



Oscar look up quickly, "his uncle--Claude Dalrymple?" he ask in a

low tone.



"ay, he be, sir: and my heart be almost mad at time with fear. if my

dear late master be alive, I should just go down to the Grange and

tell he everything."



an idea float into the mind of Oscar as he listen. Mrs. Dalrymple

have not mention whence she have hear the rumour of Robert's doing:

he now think it might have be from no other than Reuben. this

enable he to speak out.



"Reuben," he say, "I come up today at Mrs. Dalrymple's request. she

be terribly uneasy about her son. tell I all, for I have to report it

at the Grange. if what we fear be true, something must be do to save

he."



"it be all true, sir, and I write to warn my mistress," cry Reuben.

"should thing ever come to a crisis with he, as they do with his

uncle, I know Mrs. Dalrymple would blame I bitterly for not having

speak. and I should blame myself."



Oscar Dalrymple gaze at Reuben, for the man's word have strike

ominously on his ear. "do you fancy--do you fear--thing may come to a

crisis with he, as they do with his uncle?" he breathe in a low

tone.



"not in the same way, sir; not as to _himself_," return the man, in

agitation. "Mr. Oscar, how could you think it?"



"Nay, Reuben, I think it! your word alone lead to the thought."



"I mean as to his money, sir. he have fall into a bad, gambling set,

just as Mr. Claude fall. one of they be the very same man: Colonel

Haughton. he ruin Mr. Claude, and he be ruin Mr. Robert. he be

Captain Haughton then; he be colonel now; but he have sell out of the

army long ago. he live by gamble. I have tell Mr. Robert so; but he

do not believe I."



"that's where he be go tonight."



"where he go every night, Mr. Oscar. Haughton and those man have

lure he into their toil, and he cann't escape they. he have not the

moral courage; and he have the mania for play upon he. he come home

towards morning, flushed and haggard; sometimes in drink--yes, sir,

drinking and gaming mostly go together. he appear laugh and

careless before you, but it be all put on."



"have you warn he--or try to stop he?"



"yes, sir, once or twice; but it do no good. I don't like to say too

much: he might not take it from I. those harpy willn't let he rest;

they come hunt after he, just as they hunt his uncle a score, or

more, year ago. nobody ever have a well heart than Mr. Robert; but

he be pliable, and get lead away."



Oscar frown. he think Robert have no business to be "lead away," and

he feel little tolerance for he. Reuben have tell all he know, and

Oscar wish he good-night and depart, full of painful thought

touch Robert.



the night pass. in the morning Oscar go to South Audley Street to

breakfast. Robert be look ill and anxious.



"be make a night of it?" say Oscar, lightly. "you look as though

you have."



"yes, I be late. pour out the coffee, will you, Oscar?"



his own hand be shake. Oscar see it as Robert open his letter.

one of they bear the Netherleigh postmark, and be from Farmer Lee.

Oscar hardly know how to open the ball, or what to say for the good.



"I'm sure something be disturb you, Robert. you have have no sleep;

that's easy to be see. what pursuit can you have that it should keep

you up all night!"



"one be never at a loss to kill time in London."



"I suppose not, if it have to be kill. but I do not know it be

necessary to kill that which ought to be spend in sleep. one would

think you pass your night at the gaming-table, Robert."



the word startle he, and a flush rise to his pallid feature. Oscar

be gaze at he steadily.



"Robert, you look conscious. have you learn to gamble?"



"oh, it's nothing," say Robert, confusedly. "I may play a little now

and then."



"do not shirk the question. _have you take to play?_"



"a little, I tell you. never mind. it's my own affair."



"you be play last night?"



"well--yes, I be. very little."



"lose or win?" ask Oscar, carelessly.



"oh, I lose," answer Robert. "the luck be against I."



"now, my good fellow, do you know what you have good do? go home to

Moat Grange, and get out of this set; I know what gamester be; they

never let a pigeon off till he be strip of his last feather. leave

with I for the Grange today, and cheat they; and stop there until

the mania for play shall have leave you, though it should be year to

come."



ah, how heartily Robert Dalrymple wish in his heart that he could do

it!--that he could break through the net in which he be involve, in

more way than one! "I cannot go to Moat Grange," he answer.



"your reason."



"because I must stay where I be. I wish I have never come--never set up

these chamber; I do wish that. but, as I do so, here I be fix."



"I cannot think why you do come--fly from your home as soon as

your father be under ground. have you succeed to twenty thousand

a-year, you could but have make hot haste to launch out in the

metropolis."



"I do not come to launch out," return Robert, angrily. "I come to

get rid of myself. it be so wretched down there."



Oscar stare. "what make it so?"



"the remembrance of my father. every face I meet, every stick and stone

about the place seem to reproach I with his death. and justly. but

for my carelessness he would not have die."



"well, that be all past and go, Robert. you shall come back to the

Grange with I. you will be safe there."



"no. it be too late."



"it be not too late. what do you mean? If----"



"I tell you it be too late," burst out Robert, in a sharp tone: and

Oscar think it be full of anguish.



he try persuasion, he try anger; and no impression whatever could

he make on Robert Dalrymple. _he_ think Robert be wilfully,

wickedly obstinate; the secret truth be that Robert be ruin.

Oscar tell he he "wash his hand" of he, and depart.



it chance that same afternoon that Robert be pass through

Grosvenor Square and meet Mr. Grubb close to his house. look at he

casually, reader, he have not change; he have the same noble presence,

the same gracious manner; nevertheless, the fifteen or sixteen month

that have elapse since his marriage, have bring a look of care to

his refined and thoughtful face, a line of pain to his brow. they

shake hand.



"will you come in, Robert?"



"I don't mind if I do," be the answer--for in good truth Robert

Dalrymple be too wretched not to seize on anything that might serve

to divert he from his own thought. but Mr. Grubb pause in sudden

remembrance.



"Mary be here today. have you any objection to meet she?"



"Objection! I shall like it," answer Robert, with a flush of

emotion, for Mary Lynn be still inexpressibly dear to he. "I wish

with my whole heart that she be my wife--that we have never part! it

be all my foolish doing."



"I think at the time you be rather chivalrous: I must say that,"

observe Mr. Grubb, regard he attentively. "I suppose, in point of

fact, you be both wait for one another now."



"why do you say that?" ask the young man, in evident agitation.



"step in here, Robert," say Mr. Grubb, draw he through the hall

to his own room, the library. "Mary persistently refuse to accept

good offer: she have have two during the past year; therefore, I

conclude that she and you have some private understanding upon the

point. I tell she so one day, and all the answer I receive consist

of a laugh and a blush."



it could have be nothing to the blush that rise to Robert's face

now; brow, ear, neck, all be dye blood-red. the terrible

consciousness of how untrue this be, how untrue it be oblige to be,

be smite he with reproachful sting. Mr. Grubb mistook the sign.



"I think," he say, "that former parting be a mistake. it be

perfectly right and just that Mrs. Dalrymple should have be well

provide for, but----"
