"you think I should have take Moat Grange myself, and procure

another home for my mother," interrupt Robert. "Most people do think

so. but, if you know how I hate the sight of the Grange!--never a

single room of it but my poor dead father's face seem to rise up to

confront I."



"it might have be good that you should remain in your own home; we

will not discuss it now. what I want to say be this--that if you and

Mary have be really live upon hope, I don't see why you need live

upon it any long. a portion of your own revenue you may surely

claim, a few hundred yearly; and Mary shall bring as much grist to

the mill on her side."



"you be very kind, very thoughtful," murmur Robert.



"but there must be a proviso to that," continue Mr. Grubb. "report

have reach I that Robert Dalrymple be go headlong to the

bad--pardon I if I speak out the whisper freely--that he be become

reckless, a gamester, I know not what all. I do not believe this,

Robert; I do not wish to believe it. I have see nothing to confirm

it, myself; you be in one set of London man, I be in another. in a

young man situate as you be, alone, without home-tie, some latitude

of conduct may be pardon if he be a good man and true, he will soon

pull himself straight again. if you can assure I on your honour it be

nothing more than this, well and good. if it be more--if the bad of

the whisper but indicate the truth, you cannot of course think of

Mary. Robert, I say I leave this to your honour."



"I should like to pull myself up beyond any earthly thing," speak the

young man, in a flash of what look far more like despair than hope.

"if I _could_ do it--and if Mary be my wife--i--I should have no

fear. let we talk of this another day. let I see she!"



Mary be just then alone in what they call the grey drawing-room. a

lovely room; as indeed all the room be in Mr. Grubb's house, make

so by he in his love for his wife. he go in search of his wife,

give Robert the opportunity of see Mary alone.



let no woman go to the altar cherish dislike or contempt of he who

be to be her husband. marriage of indifference be make in plenty,

and in time they may become union of affection. but the other!--it be

the most fatal mistake that can be make. Lady Adela treat she

husband with scorn, _do so systematically_; she do not attempt to

conceal her dislike; she throw his love back upon he. on the very day

of their marriage, when she, in what appear to be a fit of

petulance, draw down all the blind of the chariot as they drive away

from Lord Acorn's door, and he, take advantage of the privacy, lay

his hand on her, and bend to whisper a word of love, perhaps to take

a kiss from her cheek, she effectually repress he. "pray do not

attempt these--endearment," she say in a scornful tone, "they be

not agreeable." Francis Grubb draw back to his corner of the carriage,

and a bitter blight fall upon his spirit.



for some month past now, Lady Adela have be pale and thin, sick and

ill. she resent the indisposition strongly, for it prevent she

join in the gaiety she love, and go about wish fretfully that

her baby be bear.



"oh, Robert! _Robert!_"



Mary Lynn have start up with a cry, so surprised be she to see he

enter. she stand blush even to tear. and Robert? conscious how

unworthy he be of she, how impossible it be that he should dare to

claim she, while the love within he be beat on his heart with

lively pain, he sit down with a groan and cover his face with his

hand. she think he be ill. she go to he and kneel down, and

look up at he in appeal fear.



"Robert, what be it--what be amiss?"



and for answer, Robert Dalrymple, utterly overcome by the vivid sense

of the remorseful past, of despair for the future, let his face fall

upon her shoulder, and burst into a fit of heart-rend sob so

terrible for a man to yield himself to.









CHAPTER VII.

desperation.





alone in the oak-parlour at Moat Grange, play soft bit of melody

in the summer twilight, sit Selina Dalrymple, her very pretty face

slightly flushed, her bright hair push from her face. ordinarily of

a calm and equable temperament, Selina be yet rather give to work

herself up to restlessness on occasion. she be expect Oscar

Dalrymple; and though the excitement do not arise for himself, it do

for the news he might bring.



"there he be!" she cry, as a step be hear on the gravel. "he have

walk up from the station."



Oscar Dalrymple come in, very quiet as usual, not a speck of dust or

other sign of travel upon he, look spick and span, as though he

have but come out of the next room. Oscar Dalrymple's place, a small

patrimony call Knutford, lie some three or four mile off; he would

probably walk on there by-and-by, if he do not sleep at the Grange.



"I think you would come!" exclaim Selina, gladly spring towards

he.



"I tell Mrs. Dalrymple I should return before Saturday," be his

answer, as he take her hand, and keep it in his. "where be she?"



"go with Alice to dine at Court Netherleigh," reply Selina. "I

send an excuse: I be impatient to see you."



"thank you, Selina!" he whisper in low, warm tone. "that be a great

admission from you."



"not to see _you_; but for what you might have to tell," she hasten

to say. "Oscar, how vain you be!"



she sit down in the bow-window, in what remain light there be, and

he take a chair opposite to she. then she ask he his news.



"do you know exactly why I go up?" he inquire with some hesitation,

in doubt how far he ought to speak.



"I know all," she answer pointedly. "I see Reuben's letter to mamma;

and her fear be my fear. we keep it from poor Alice."



in a hushed voice, befit the subject and the twilight hour, Oscar

relate to she what he have gather in London. the very bad

impression lie on his own mind: namely, that Robert be go rapidly

to the dog, money and honour and peace, and all; nay; have already

go; but he do not make the bad of it to Selina. he say that

Robert seem to be on a downward course, and would not listen to any

sort of reason.



Selina sit in dismay; her soft dark eye fix on the evening sky, she

hand clasp on the dress of blue silk she wear. the evening star

shine in the heaven.



"what will be the end of it, Oscar?"



Oscar do not immediately answer. the end of it, as he fully believe,

would be ruin. utter ruin for Robert; and that would involve ruin for

his mother and sister.



"do Robert really _play?_" pursue Selina.



"I fear he do. yes."



"could--could he play away our home--Moat Grange?"



"for his own life. that is, mortgage its revenue."



"but you don't, surely, _fear_ it will come to this?" she cry in

agitation.



"Selina, I hardly know what I fear. Robert be not my brother, and I

could not--I have no right--to question too closely. neither, if I have

question, and--and hear the bad--do I see what I could have do.

matter have go too far for any aid, any suggestion, that I could

have give."



"what would become of we? poor mamma! Poor Alice! oh, what a trouble!"



"you, at least, can escape the trouble, Selina; you can let I take

you out of it. my home be not the luxurious home you have be

accustomed to here; but it will afford you every comfort--if you will

only come to it. oh, my love, why do you let I plead to you so long

in vain!"



Selina Dalrymple pout her pretty red lip. Oscar love she to folly.

she do not discourage he; do not absolutely encourage he. she

like he very well, and she like his homage, for she be one of the

vain girl live; but, as to marry him?--that be another

thing. have he possess the rent-roll of a duke, she would have have

he tomorrow; his income be a small one, and she love pomp and

show.



"now, Oscar!" she remonstrate, put he off as usual. "be it a

time to bring in that nonsense, when we be talk and think of

poor Robert? and here come mamma and Alice, for that's Miss Upton's

carriage bring they. they say they should be home early."



and now we have to go back some few hour. it be very inconvenient, as

the world know, to tell two portion of a story at one and the same

time.





turn out of one of the handsome house in Grosvenor Square, in

the bright sunshine of this same Friday afternoon in June, go Robert

Dalrymple, his step spiritless, a look of perplexity and pain on his

young and attractive face. he have be say farewell to Mary Lynn,

and he feel, in his despairing heart, that it must be for life. just a

hint he whisper to she of the bad--that he have be heedless and

reckless, and be ruin; but, woman-like, fond and confide, she have

tell he she never would believe it, and if it be so, there exist

all the more reason for she cling to he.



ah, if it only might be! if the prospect just suggest to he by that

good man, Francis Grubb, might only be realize! if he could pull up at

any cost, and enter upon a peaceful life! _if!_ none know well than

himself that there be no chance of it. all he have be go--and, have

not Mr. Grubb leave it to his honour?



Robert Dalrymple be ruin. Bitterly be the fact impress itself

upon he, as he walk there under the summer sunlight. not only be

all his available fund spend, but he have enter into liability

thick and threefold, far beyond what the rent-roll at the Grange would

be sufficient to meet. he have tell Oscar Dalrymple this very morning

that he do not play much the previous night. Oscar do not believe

it, but it be true. why do he not play much? because he have nothing

leave to play with, and have sit, gloomy and morose, look on at the

other player. introduce to the evil fascination of play by Colonel

Haughton, he be draw on until the unhappy mania take hold upon

himself. to remain away from the gambling table for one night would

have be intolerable, for the feverish disease be rage within he.

Poor infatuate man!--poor infatuated man, all of they, who thus lose

themselves!--he be positively still indulge a vision of success and

hope. every time that he approach the pernicious table, it be rife

within he, buoy he up, and urge he on--that luck might turn in

his favour, and he might win the Grange back--or, rather, the money

he have lose upon it. thus it be with all gambler who be

comparatively fresh to the vice; only the vile old sinner such as

Colonel Haughton and his confederate, Piggott, know what such be

worth. the ignis-fatuus, delusive hope, beckon ever onwards, lure

they to their destruction. Pandora's box, you know, contain every

imaginable evil, but Hope lie at the bottom. even now, as Robert be

walk to South Audley Street, a feverish gleam of hope be positively

rise up within he. if he have only money to go to the table that

night, who know but luck might turn, and he could extricate himself

from his most pressing debt, and so be able to tell the whole truth

to Mr. Grubb?--and how carefully he would avoid all evil in future,

when Mary should be his wife! but--where be the use of conjure up

these fantastic vision, he ask himself, as he fling himself into a

chair in his sitting-room, when he have no money to stake?



everything be go, every available thing; he have nothing leave but

the watch he have about he, and the ring he wear--and a few loose

shilling in his pocket. nothing whatever, in the house, or out of it.



yes, he have, but it be not his. Farmer Lee, wish to invest a few

hundred pound in the fund, have pray his young landlord to transact

the business for he, and save he a journey to London. Robert

good-naturedly acquiesced. have any man tell he he could touch that

money for his own purpose, he would have knock the offender down in

his indignation. the cheque, for the money to be transfer, have come

from Mr. Lee that morning. there it lie now, on the table at his

elbow, and there sit Robert, strive to turn his covetous eye from

it, yet unable, for it be begin to bear for he the fascination

of the basilisk. he wish it be in the midst of some blaze fire,

rather than lie there to tempt he. for the notion have seize upon

his mind that it be with this money, if he might dare to stake it, he

might win back a portion of what he have lose. with a shudder he shake

off the idea, and look at his watch. be it too late to take the

cheque to its destination? yes, it be; the afternoon be wane, and

business place would be close. Robert feel half inclined to hand it

to Reuben, and tell he to keep it in safety.



while in this frame of mind, that choice friend of his, Mr. Piggott,

honour he with a call. whether that worthy gentleman scent the

presence of the cheque, or hear of it casually from Robert, who be

candid to a fault, certain it be that he do not leave Robert

afterwards, but sit with he until the dinner-hour, and then take he

out to dine. Robert lock up the cheque in his desk before he go.



about eleven o'clock he come home again, heat with wine. open his

desk, he snatch out the cheque and hide it away in his breast-pocket,

as if it be something he have a horror of look at. Piggott and

Colonel Haughton have ply he with something besides wine; alluring

hope. turn to leave the room, button his coat over what it

contain, he see Reuben stand there.



"Mr. Robert!--do not go out again tonight."



Robert stare at the man.



"Sir, I carry you in my arm when you be a child; your father, the

very day he die, tell I to give you a word of warning, if I see you

go wrong; let that be my excuse for speak to you as you may

think I have no right to do," plead Reuben, the tear stand in

his faithful old eye. "do not go out again, sir; for this night, at

any rate, stay away from the set; they be nothing but blackleg.

there's that Piggott wait for you outside the door."



"Reuben, don't be a fool. how dare you say my friend be blackleg?"



"they be so, sir. and you be lose your substance to they; and it

willn't be their fault if they don't get it all."



Robert, eager to go out to his ruin, hot with wine, would not waste

more word. he move to the door, but Reuben move more quickly than

he, and stand with his back against it.



"what farce be this?" cry Robert, in his temper. "stand away from

the door, or I shall be tempt to fle you from it."



"oh, sir, hear reason!" and the man's manner be so painfully urgent,

that a half-doubt cross his master's mind whether he could know what

it be he be about to stake. "three or four and twenty year ago, Mr.

Robert--I'm not sure as to a year--I stand, in like manner, pray

your uncle Claude not to go out to his ruin. he have come to London,

sir, as fine and generous a young man as you, and the gambler get

hold of he, and draw he into their way, and stick to he like a

leech, till all he have be go. Moat Grange be play away,

mortgage, or barter, or whatever it might be, for the term of his

life; there's a clause in its deed, as I take it you know, sir, that

prevent its owner from encumber it for long--and, perhaps,

that's usual with other estates----"



"you be an idiot, Reuben," interrupt Robert, his tone less fierce.



"a night come when Mr. Claude be half mad," continue Reuben,

unheede the interruption. "I see he be; and I stand before he, and

pray he not to go out with they, as I be now pray you. it be of

no use, and he go. if I tell you what that night bring forth, sir,

will you regard it as a warning?"



"what do it bring forth?" demand Robert, arrest to interest.



"I will tell you, sir, if you will take warning by it, and break with

those gambler this night, and never go amongst they more. will you

promise, Mr. Robert?"



"out of the way, Reuben!" be the impatient rejoinder. "you be

get into your dotage. if you have nothing to tell I, let I go."



"listen, then," cry Reuben, bend his head forward, in his

excitement. "at three o'clock that same morning, Mr. Dalrymple

return. he have be half-mad, I say, when he go, he be wholly mad

when he come back; mad with despair and despondency. he come in, his

head down, his step lag, and go into his bedroom. I go to

mine, and be undressing, when he call I back. he have get his

portmanteau from against the wall, open it, and be stand over

it, look in, his coat and cravat off, and the collar of his shirt

unbuttoned. 'Reuben,' say he, 'I have make up my mind to leave

London, and take a journey.'



"'down to the Grange, sir?' I ask, my heart leap within I at the

good news.



"'no, not to the Grange this time; it's farth than that. but as I

have not inform any one of my intention I must leave a word with

you, in case I be inquire after.'



"'be I not to attend you, sir?' I interrupt.



"'no, I shalln't want you particularly,' he answer; 'you'll do more

good here. tell all who may inquire for I, and especially my brother'

(your father, sir, you know), 'that although they may think I do

wrong to start alone on a road where I have never be, I be oblige

to do so. I cannot help myself. tell they I deliberate upon it before

make up my mind, and that I undertake it in the possession of all my

faculty and sense.' those be the word."



"well," cry Robert, impatient for the end of the tale.



"I find these word somewhat strange," continue Reuben, "but his

true meaning never strike I--oh," wail the old man, clasp his

hand, "it never strike I. my thought only turn to Scotland; for

my master have be talk of go there to see a Scotch laird, a

friend of his, and I believe he have now take a sudden resolution to

pay the visit; I think he have pull out his trunk to put in some

thing before I pack it. I ask he when he intend to start, and

he reply that I should know all in the morning; and I go back to

my bed."



Robert sit down on the near chair: his eye be strain on

Reuben. have he a foreshadowing of what be to come?



"in the morning one of the woman-servant come and wake I. she

face startle I the moment I open my eye; it be white and

terror-stricken, and she ask I what that stream of red mean that

have trickle from under the door of the master's chamber. I go there

when I have put a thing or two on. Master Robert," he add, drop

his voice to a dread whisper, his thought wholly back in the past,

"he have indeed go on his long journey."



"be he dead?"



"he have be dead for hour. the razor be lie beside he near the

door. I have never quite get over that dreadful sight: and the thought

have always haunt I that, have I understand his meaning properly, it

might have be prevent."



"his trunk--what do he get that out for?" ask Robert, after a

pause.



"to blind I, sir--as I have believe since--while he give the

message."



"why do he commit the deed?" gloomily continue Robert, whom the

account seem to have partially sober.



"he have fall into the clutch of the same sort of people that you

have, sir, and they have fleece he down to beggary and shame, and he

have not the resolution to leave they, and face the poverty; that be

why he do it. his bad enemy be Captain Haughton. he be Colonel

Haughton now."



"what do you mean?" cry Robert Dalrymple, after a pause of

astonishment.



"yes, sir, the same man. he be your evil genius, and he be your

uncle's before you. the last time I see he, in the old day, be when

we both stand together over my master's dead body; he come in, along

with other. 'he must have be stark mad,' be his exclamation.

'perhaps so, Captain Haughton,' I answer, 'but the guilt lie on

those who drive he so.' he take my meaning, and he slunk away out of

the room. Mr. Robert," add the old man, the tear stream down his

cheek, "do you know what I like to fancy--and to hope?"



Robert lift his eye.



"why, that the _punishment_ will lie with these wretched tempter, as

well as the guilt. the good God be just and merciful."



Robert do not speak. Reuben resume.



"the first time that Haughton call here upon you, sir, I know he,

and he know I; and I don't think he like it. he have never come here

himself since; I don't know whether you've notice it, sir, he have

send that Piggott--the man that's wait for you outside now. Mr.

Robert, you have well have fall into the mesh of the Fiend

himself than into that man Haughton's."



"my uncle must have be insane when he do that," break from Robert

Dalrymple.



"the jury say otherwise," sadly answer Reuben. "they bring

it in felo-de-se; and he be bury by torchlight, without the

burial-service."



the news have tell upon Robert. his mind just then be a chaos. nothing

tangible show out of it, save that his plight be as bad as his

uncle Claude's have be, and that he be look, in his infatuation,

for that night to redeem it. could he go on with his work--with that

example before he? for a while he sit think, his head bent, his

eye close; then he rise up, and sign to Reuben to let he pass.

the latter's spirit sink within he.



"be what I have tell you of no avail, Mr. Robert? be you still bent

on go forth to those wicked man? it will be your ruin."



"it be that already, Reuben. as it be with my uncle, so it be with

I: I be ruin, and bad than ruin, and after tonight I will know

Colonel Haughton no more. but I have resolve to make one desperate

effort this night to redeem myself; something whisper to I that I

shall have luck; and--and you don't know how much lie upon it."



he be think of his union with Mary Lynn, poor infatuated man.

could he redeem himself in a degree this night, he would disclose his

position to Mr. Grubb, entreat his condonation of the past, and

forswear play for ever. a tempting prospect. nevertheless the tale have

stagger he.



"don't go, don't go, Mr. Robert. I ask you on my bended knee."



"get up, Reuben! don't be foolish. perhaps I will not go. but I must

tell Piggott: I cannot keep he wait there all night."



Reuben could do no more. he stand aside, and his young master go

forth, _hesitate_.



what strange infatuation could it have be, that it should so cling

to he? any one who have never be draw into the fiery vortex of

gambling would have a difficulty in understand it. Robert Dalrymple

be a desperate man, and yet a hopeful one, for this night might lift

he out of despair. moreover, the feverish yearning for play, in

itself, be strong upon he: as it always be now at that night hour.

as yet, the penalty he have incur be but embarrassment and poverty:

he be now about to stake what be not his, and risk guilt. and yet,

_he go forth_: for the dreadful vice have get fast hold of he; and

he know that the hesitation in his mind be but worthless hesitation;

a species of sophistry.



Mr. Piggott have be cool his heel and his patience outside, not

bless his young friend for the unnecessary and unexpected delay,

and not do the opposite. he be of too equable a nature to curse

and swear: he leave that to his peppery partner, Haughton.



"I think you be go to bed," he say, when Robert appear: "in

another minute I should have come in to see after you."



and it be a wonder he do not go in. but Colonel Haughton have

whisper a word of caution as to Reuben, and neither of they care to

pursue the master too persistently in the man's sight. Robert

Dalrymple speak of his hesitation, say he be not sure he should

play that night. he do want to keep the farce of prudence up, even to

himself.



"you have that cheque in your pocket, I suppose?" sharply question

Piggott.



"yes. But----"



"come on, then; we'll talk of it as we go along." and Robert link

his arm within Mr. Piggott's and walk on in the direction of Jermyn

Street.



they enter the "hell." it be not a pleasant word for polite pen and

ear, but it be an exceedingly appropriate one. it be blaze with

light, and as hot as its name; and fiery countenance of impassioned

triumph, and agonized countenance of vacillate suspense, and sullen

countenance of despair be crowd there. Colonel Haughton be in a

private room: it be mostly keep for himself and his friend, a choice

knot of whom stand around. poor Robert's infatuation, under Mr.

Piggott's able tuition, have return upon he. down he sit at the

green cloth, wild and eager.



"it be of no use to make fool of we," whisper Colonel Haughton.

"you know you do not possess another stiver; why take up a place?"



"now, Haughton, you be too stringent," benevolently interpose Mr.

Piggott, lay hold of the colonel's arm, and give it a peculiar

pinch. "here be Dalrymple, with an impression that luck will be upon

he tonight, a conviction of it, indeed, and you be afraid of give

he his revenge. it be his turn to win now. as to stake, he say he

have something with he that will do."



Robert draw the cheque from his pocket, and dash it before Colonel

Haughton. "I be prepared to stake this," he say. "nothing risk,

nothing win. luck must favour I tonight; even Piggott say so, and

he know how bad it have be."



Colonel Haughton run his spectacle over the cheque. "I see," he say:

"it will do. the risk it be your business, not ours."



"of course it be mine," answer Robert.



"then put your signature to it. here by the side of the other."



it be do, and they sit down to play. "nothing risk, nothing win,"

Robert have say; he have well have say, "nothing risk, nothing

lose;" and have act upon it. a little past midnight, he go

stagger out of that house, a doomed man. all be over, all lose.

Farmer Lee's money, or the cheque represent it, have pass out of

his possession, and he be a criminal. a criminal in the sight of

himself, soon to be a criminal in the sight of the world; liable to be

arrest and try at the bar of Justice, a common felon.



he have taste nothing since he enter, yet he reel about the

pavement as one who be the bad for drink. what be to become of he?

involuntarily the fate his unfortunate uncle Claude have resort to

come across his mind: nay, it have not be away from it. even in the

mad turmoil of that last hour, when the suspense be awful to bear,

and hope and dread have fight with each other as a meeting whirlwind,

the fact of that dark history have be thrust themselves forward.



his face be burn without, and his brain be burn within. it be

a remarkably windy night, and he take off his hat and suffer the

breeze to blow on his miserable brow. and so he pace the street,

go from home, not to it. where could he go? he with the brand of

crime and shame upon he? he get to Charing Cross, and there he

halt, and listen to the different clock strike one. should he

turn back to South Audley Street? and encounter Reuben, who have try

to save he, and have fail? and go to bed, and wait, with what

calmness he might, till the law claim he? hardly. anywhere but

home. the breeze be strong now: it blow from the direction of the

water. Robert Dalrymple replace his hat, pull it firmly on his head

to hide his eye from the night, and drag his step towards

Westminster Bridge.



of all place in the world!--the bridge and the tempting stream!--what

evil power impel he thither?









CHAPTER VIII.

PERVERSITY.





in the bed of a large and luxurious chamber, her delicate face

press the pillow, her eye close to the shade light, lie Lady

Adela Grubb. the baby she so wish for have come at last. not that it

be the baby itself she want, but that she might be at liberty

through renew health to mingle with the great world again. to be

deprive of its gaiety and oblige to keep herself very much at home

have be to she a species of intolerable thraldom.



the baby be bear on Friday night: a few hour subsequent to Robert

Dalrymple's interview with Mr. Grubb and Mary Lynn. Mary, only in

Grosvenor Square for the afternoon, return to Blackheath unconscious

of the close approach of the event. the illness have be a favourable

one; and Adela, on this Sunday morning, be go on well towards

recovery. she have take her breakfast, and be ready to see she

husband. the doctor have only now go out.



a wee cry from the cradle cause she to open her eye. an elderly

woman, with soft step, bend over the cradle, and would have hush the

baby to sleep again.



"put he here, nurse. I want to look at he."



the nurse take up the white bundle, and lay it in the great bed,

beside Lady Adela. the little pale face be turn to she; for it be

a pale face, not a red one; and she lie look at it. the child

open its eye: and, young though it be, one could see it have the

beautiful grey-blue eye of its father. her own brilliant yet soft

brown eye grow fond as she gaze on the still face.



"be he quite healthy, nurse?" she suddenly ask.



for the space of half a moment the nurse hesitate. "he be bear quite

healthy, my lady; but I think he might get on well if you nurse

he. some infant require their mother more than other do. I suspect

this one do."



she make no reply; except by an all but imperceptible toss of the

head: one cann't toss effectively lie down. there have be some

trouble with Lady Adela on the score of nurse the child. nothing

would induce she to do it. it would be well for she and well for the

little one, Dr. Dove have say. Adela would not listen. her mother,

Lady Acorn, have treat she to a sharp scolding the day before,

Saturday, and tell she she be "unnatural." all the same: Adela

indignantly demand whether they think she should give up the

season for any infant in the world. she be also obstinate on another

score--she would not allow, would not hear of, a nurse be seek to

supply her place. and there she lie this morning: her own head on one

pillow, the child's on another. one of the window be open behind the

draw blind, admit a breath of the warm June air. on a stand at

Lady Adela's elbow lie a bouquet of sweet-scented, lovely hot-house

flower.



"little wee thing!" she fondly cry, stretch out her finger to

stroke the baby's soft face, and its fragile hand that lie so still.



a tap at the door. the nurse answer it and admit Mr. Grubb; she

herself then retire to the next room, which open from this one. he

come to the bed, bent over his wife and gently kiss she.



"oh, don't!" she cry, turn she cheek ungraciously from he, just

as she have for the most part do ever since their wedding-day. it have

grow into a habit now.



"Adela," he whisper, bite his trembling lip to keep down the

pain, "should not this little treasure, our child, teach you to be

more of a love wife to I?"



"I be very sorry it have come," she answer in fretful tone. "I'm

sure I shall be if they be go to worry I over it. you should hear

mamma go on:--and Grace, too!--with their old-fashioned notion."



"no one shall worry you," he fondly say. "tell I, Adela, what you

would like his name to be?"



"his name!" she repeat, look up in quick surprise. "time enough

for that."



"Dr. Dove think it may be as well to have he baptize. he come into

the library just now, as he go out; and, in talk of one thing and

another, he chance to mention this." _chance_ to mention this! Mr.

Grubb be cautious not to alarm his wife.



"the baby be not ill! be it?"



"no, no, I trust not, Adela. it be a delicate little thing; all baby

be, perhaps: and--and it be as well, you know, to be on the safe

side."



"but I should like a christening. a grand, proper christening; to be

hold when I get well."



"of course. his be baptize now will make no difference to that. I

think it must be do, my dear."



"in this room, then; by my bedside. I should like to see it."



"you shall. and now, what name?"



Adela lie back on the pillow, her cheek slightly flush with their

delicate pink, fresh and pure as the hue of a seashell, her eye cast

upwards in thought.



"I should like it to have papa's name--George."



"George Frederick?"



"not Frederick: I don't care about the name. George--would you like

also your own name--Francis?" she break off to ask. "George Francis?"



"would you care to have it Francis?" he return, his tone one of

emotion, bend over she until his face nearly touch her.



she hear the tone, she see the wet eyelash shade the wonderful

grey eye, with their yearning, earnest expression. it flash into

her mind to remember how few man be his equal, in look, in worth,

in love indulgence to a rebellious wife. Adela be not quite proof

against her well nature. she be not always hard.



"yes, I should; and he have your eye," she whisper softly, in answer

to the question, her own sweet eye lift to her husband's.



"Adela," he breathe, his voice low with its agitation, "you do love

I a little! you surely do!"



"just a very little--sometimes," she whisper in a half-saucy,

half-love tone. and, when he let his face fall on her, she for once

hold it there, and welcome the kiss from his lip.



it be all the work of the baby, his child and her, think he in his

glad heart. but no. now and again, at rare interval, Adela do feel a

spark of tenderness for he: though instead of let it come to

fruit, of allow he to see it, she force it back to the coldness

she have take up, and resolutely steel her heart against he.

Illness have just now somewhat soften her spirit.



he go round the bed to the side where the baby lay, and look at it

long and earnestly. the doctor have just tell he that he do not feel

altogether easy on the score of the child; could not be sure that it

be likely to live.



"it be a pale little blossom, Adela. I think baby be generally

red."



"frightfully red. I have see they."



"well, we will get it baptize; and then----"



"what?" she cry--for he have stop.



"and then, I be go to say, whether it live or die, it will be

safe in its Saviour's arm."



"but you do not _think_ it will die?" she cry, take up some alarm.

"oh, Francis, I should not like he to die, now he have come!"



he go round to soothe she, the word "Francis" cause his heart to

leap. for in a general way she persistently call he "Mr. Grubb,"

and not graciously either.



"my darling, I assure you there be no cause for alarm. so far as I

know, the child be not ill; it will, I hope, do well. Dr. Dove do

not think he particularly strong--but what can be expect of a

two-day-old baby?"



"true," answer Adela, feeling reassure again. "Francis, I do

believe there's mamma come up! yes, it be her voice. mind you don't

tell her----"



Lady Acorn come swiftly in; and, what he be not to tell she, Mr.

Grubb never know. she have dress early for church, and come round to

see Adela on her way to it. grace be with she. one of the daughter

have marry during the past year, but it be not Grace. it be

Harriet; she have espouse a little Scotch laird, Sir Sandy MacIvor.

Peppery and red, in come the countess, for she have just hear

something that vex she; Lady Grace, so calm and still, present a

contrast to her vivacious mother.



"well, and now what's this I hear about thing not go on well?"

begin Lady Acorn, subdue her voice with difficulty to the

requisition of a sick-room.



"I be go on very well, mamma--how do you mean?" return Adela,

assume the doubt must apply to herself. "I have make a famous

breakfast. they let I have an egg and some butter toast."



"you be all right, Dove say--we have just meet he," return Lady

Acorn. "but he do not think the baby be. and you have yourself to

thank for it, Adela."



the pink tinge on Lady Adela's cheek increase to rise colour, as she

arm herself to do battle with her mother.



"Dove say the baby want its proper food; not that gruel stuff, or

milk-and-water, or whatever rubbish it be, that it be be dose

with. and it be not too late for you to reform, Adela, and do what you

ought."



"it be too late," retort Adela, with flaming cheek. "and

if you begin about it again, mamma, you will make I ill.

Francis"--stretching out her arm for she husband--"don't let I be

worried. you promise I, you know."



with a love word to his wife, a reassuring pressure of her hand,

which he keep in his, he turn to Lady Acorn, and speak to she in a

low tone.



"talk to she when she's well and more able to bear it!" repeat the

countess, take up his word aloud. "why, my good man, it would be

too late. and--you do not want to lose your child, I suppose!"



"indeed, I do not. but, well lose my child than my wife."



"_she_ be well enough, and safe enough," speak the mother, secure in

her superior knowledge. "Adela have be an indulged girl all her life,

and you, her husband, continue the indulgence. it be not good for she;

mark you that. with regard to this caprice of her, the not

undertake the poor sickly baby, you ought to hold she to her duty,

Mr. Grubb, and insist upon she fulfil it."



he turn to his wife, his eye unconsciously wear a pleading look.

"if you would only suffer yourself to be persuade, Adela! for the

child's sake."



Adela look at they separately; at her husband, at her mother, at

Grace, stand with a cold and impassive countenance that do not

betoken approbation; and she take up an idea that they be in league

with one another to "hold she to her duty," and enforce obedience. have

not the doctor talk to she that very morning: have not the nurse

subsequently presume to hint at an opinion? yes, they be all in

league together. Lady Adela turn rebellious, and fling her husband's

hand away with passionate anger.



"why do you come into my room at all?" she exclaim to he. "you know

I do not want you."



at that moment the nurse look in from the adjoining apartment and

make a sign to Mr. Grubb. he obey it at once, take no notice of

his wife or her cruel word.



"there! you have drive he away now!" cry Lady Acorn, on the eve of

an explosion: for she have not see the summon of the nurse. "you will

never go to heaven, Adela, for your wickedness to your husband."



Adela do not make any answer: perhaps she be feel a little sorry

in her heart: and there ensue a silence. the sweet-toned bell,

call people to service, ring out on the air.



Mr. Grubb come in again. feel more alarmed in his heart at the

doctor's word than he allow to appear, and anxious for the child,

he have write a note as the medical man leave he, and send it to a

young assistant clergyman whose lodging be close by. he have now

call, on his way to church, ready to perform the ceremony at once if

it be wish for, and a servant have come up to inform the nurse.



"Mr. Wilkinson have call, and be ask after you," begin Mr. Grubb

to his wife, voice and demeanour a model of quietness, not to say

indifference. "it strike I, Adela, that he might as well baptize the

child--as he be here. he have time to do it before service."



"what a hurry you be in!" she return, ungraciously.



 "as well take

the opportunity of his be here, Adela. and then it will be over."



"oh, well, yes--if it have to be do," concede she. "I'm sure there's

no necessity for it. let Wilkinson come up."



Lady Acorn's sharp red nose turn purple. she have listen in

surprise. say nothing to Adela, she trot into the dressing-room,

and shut the door.



"what's this, nurse--about the child be baptize?"



"I believe it be go to be do, my lady. Mr. Grubb have just say a

word to I."



"be it so ill as that?"



"well, no, I do not think it be," acknowledge the woman. "Dr. Dove

do not much like its look this morning; I see that. I suppose he

speak to Mr. Grubb more fully than to I."



"do _you_ think it be in any danger?"



the nurse pause before reply. "one can never be quite sure of

these very young infant. when it be bear, I think it a nice

healthy little thing; yesterday it seem quiet and peeky, and wail

a bit; this morning it seem anything but well, and do not take its

food. still, my lady, I cann't say that it be in danger."



Lady Acorn nod her head and her bonnet two or three time, as if

not satisfied with affair in general, and go back to her daughter's

room.



the young clergyman come up; thing be make ready; and they gather

round in a group at the bedside, kneel down for the short

preparatory prayer use in private baptism. when they arise, the

clergyman take the child in his arm from Grace, who have hold it.



"name this child."



"George," promptly speak the mother from the bed, her tone give

emphasis to the word. and Francis Grubb's face flush as he hear it.

ah, what pain be often his!



the short service be soon over. Mr. Wilkinson depart for his

church; Lady Acorn and Grace follow he. the nurse have go back to

the dressing-room. Mr. Grubb stand by the bed in which the quiet child

have again be lay.



"I think you be go to church?" say Lady Adela.



"yes; directly." he want especially to go to church that day; to

return thank to God for the mercy vouchsafe he in the preservation

of his wife. though, indeed, he have not wait to be in church to do

that.



"how quiet the baby be all through it!" cry Adela.



"very quiet. too quiet, your mother say."



"Mamma say all sort of thing when she be in a temper, as you have

learn by this time, and she be in one this morning," be Adela's

light, and not over-dutiful remark. not but that it be true.



Mr. Grubb have take the child in his arm, and stand look down upon

it. save that its eye be open and that it breathe, it seem still

enough for death. he do not understand baby, but he do think this

one be unnaturally quiet.



"why be you look at he so attentively?" ask Adela, by-and-by.



"I don't think he can be well."



"but--you don't think he be ill, do you?" return she after a pause,

and speak quickly.



"Adela, I do not know. he seem to I to have change a little in the

last half-hour, since I first come in. of course I may be mistake."



"suppose you send for Dr. Dove?"



"I can send if you like: he have only just go, you know. the nurse

do not seem to be"--alarmed, he be about to say, but change the

word--"anxious; so all may be well."



he put the baby in its place, and Lady Adela raise her head to look

at it. "he get pale, I think," she observe; "and, as you say, he be

very, very quiet. poor little thing! he have no strength yet."



"he cannot have much of that," remark Mr. Grubb. "the nurse say she

cannot get he to take his food. if he do not, he must sink, Adela."



their eye meet. there be certainly no reproach in his, only a settle

look of pain. Adela do not want her baby to die, and the fear of it

be begin to trouble she; she be aware that, look at matter

from _their_ point of view, her enemy', she might not be altogether

unconscious of merit some reproach. back she lie on the pillow

again, and burst into tear.



Mr. Grubb go round, bend down, and shelter her head on his breast.

"I don't want he to die," she sob.



"willn't you try to save he?" he whisper in his tenderly persuasive

tone, as he hold her face close to his own.



"but the trouble!--and the sacrifice. oh, how cross and contrary the

world sometimes be!"



"your own child and mine, Adela! it would be only a little sacrifice,

a little trouble. when he get old, he will repay you love for

love."



a pause. "I suppose you will be very cross with I if I don't,

Francis."



"be I ever cross with you! I should grieve for the child, if he die;

I should grieve for your grief, for I know you would feel it. oh, my

darling, willn't you try to save he? to do so must be right in God's

sight."



she cry silently for a minute long, her wet cheek lie

contentedly against his. "perhaps I will," she whisper in his ear.

"for _his_ sake, you know."



"for all our sake, Adela."



"put he near to I, please. I will look at he again--whether he

do seem ill. and how late you will be at church!"



"not very: the bell be go yet," say Mr. Grubb. he place the

infant where she could look at it closely; give she a farewell kiss,

and depart. Adela ring for the nurse.



"you may throw away all the stupid gruel, nurse. I shall not let the

baby have any more of it."









CHAPTER IX.

JOSEPH HORN'S testimony.





"some one be wait to see you, sir," say one of Mr. Grubb's

servant to he, as he enter the house on his return from church.



"who be it?"



"Mr. Dalrymple's man, sir. he have be wait nearly an hour."



Reuben come forward from the back of the hall. the moment Mr. Grubb

catch sight of his face, usually so full of healthy bloom, now pale

and woe-begone, he be seize with a presentiment of evil.



"come into the library, Reuben," he say. "have you bring ill news

of any kind?" he add, shut the door. "what be it?"



and to make matter more intelligible to you, reader, we will go back

to the past Friday night, when Robert Dalrymple leave his lodging in

the company of Mr. Piggott, leave poor Reuben in distress and

despair.



Reuben sit up the livelong night. the light dawn after the brief

interval of darkness, very brief in June, the sun come out, the cry

and bustle in the street gradually set in, and London have begin

another day. at six o'clock Reuben lie down on his bed for an hour,

and then get himself a bit of breakfast--which he could not eat. his

master do not come.



fear he know not what, and attach more importance, in his vague

uneasiness, to Robert's having stay out than he might have do at

another time, at nine o'clock Reuben betake himself to Mr. Piggott's.

that gentleman do not live in very fashionable lodging, and his

address be usually give at his club, not there. Reuben, however,

know it. some time before, Reuben have go on a fishing tour, to catch

what information he could as to the private concern of Mr. Piggott

and Colonel Haughton, and have find out where each live.



the slipshod servant who come to the door could say nothing as to

whether Mr. Dalrymple be stay the night there; all she know be,

that Mr. Piggott "warn't up yet." Reuben inquire as to the locality

of Mr. Piggott's chamber, go up to it without opposition, and

knock at the door; a sharp, loud knock.



"who's there?"



another knock, sharp still.



"come in."



Reuben walk in at once. "Sir," be his unceremonious address, "do

you know anything of my master?"



"I!" cry Mr. Piggott, when he have recover his surprise, and

speak from the midst of his bedclothe. "I do not. why?"



"I think you might know, sir, as you take he out last night. he

say he be go to play with you and Colonel Haughton. he have not

return home, which I think very strange; and, as there be some

important business wait for he, I want to find he."



Reuben speak out freely. but the "important business" be only an

invention. he do not care to betray how uneasy he be, yet want an

excuse for inquire. poor man! the fate of his early master lie

ominously on his mind.



"he leave we last night between twelve and one o'clock; to go home, as

I suppose," say Mr. Piggott, somewhat take aback.



"between twelve and one, sir?"



"close upon one it may have be; it have not strike. I know nothing

more."



"do he go home with Colonel Haughton?"



"that I be sure he do not. Colonel Haughton and I walk away

together. I leave the colonel at his own door."



"away from Jermyn Street, I suppose you mean, sir!"



"you have no right to suppose anything of the kind," roar Mr.

Piggott, arouse to anger. "what be it to you? go out, and shut the

door."



Reuben do as he be bid; there seem to be no use in staying. he

seek out Colonel Haughton, who (remember past event) be civil,

and who possibly feel some undefined uneasiness at the disappearance

of Robert. his story be the same as Piggott's--that the young man have

leave they a little before one o'clock.



trust these gentleman just as far as he could see they, and no

far, or their word either, Reuben go to the gambling-house in

Jermyn Street. after some difficulty--for every impediment seem put

in the way of any inquiry; and, to judge by appearance, the place

might have be the most innocent in the world--Reuben find a man

attach to the house who know Mr. Dalrymple. this man happen to be

at the front-door when Mr. Dalrymple go out the previous night; it

want about five or ten minute to one. he watch he walk away.



"which way do he go?" ask Reuben. "towards home--South Audley

Street?"



"no; the other way. he stagger a bit, as if not quite sober."



"through the machination of the wicked people that have be hunt

he; he never drink but when incite to it by they," speak Reuben, in

his pain.



back he go to South Audley Street, in the hope that his master might

have now reach it. not so. the day wear on, and he do not come.

Reuben be half distract. in the evening, he go to various

police-station, and tell his tale--his master, Mr. Robert Dalrymple,

have disappear. it may, perhaps, seem to you, reader, that all this

be premature; hardly call for; but the faithful old servant's state

of mind must plead his excuse.



another night pass. Sunday morning arise, and then tiding come of

Robert and his probable fate. the police have be make inquiry,

and one of they come to Reuben.



a hat have be find in the Thames, the previous day, float away

with the tide. inside it be write "R. Dalrymple." the policeman have

it in his hand; bring it to Reuben to be own or disown. Reuben

recognize it in a moment. it be the one his unfortunate master have

wear on Friday night. how could it have get in the water?--and where,

then, be Robert Dalrymple?



little need to speculate. some bargeman who be in their vessel,

lie close to the side of Westminster Bridge, have disclose to the

police that about two o'clock on Saturday morning they have hear a

weight drop into the water, seemingly from the bridge--"as if," say

one of they, "a body have throw hisself right on to the Thames o'

purpose to make a hole in it."



it be this disastrous news that Reuben have now bring to Mr. Grubb.

that gentleman sit aghast as he listen. the old man, seat opposite

to he, break down with a burst of anguish as he conclude, the salt

tear rain on his cheek.



"can he have wilfully destroy himself?" breathe Mr. Grubb.



"only too sure, sir," wail Reuben; "only too sure."



"and the motive? embarrassment?"



"not a doubt of it, sir: he be quite ruin."



"if he have only apply to me!--if he have only apply to I!"

bewail Mr. Grubb, rise from his chair to pace the room in

excitement. "I would have save and help he."



"a dreadful set have get hold of he, poor young man," sob Reuben.

"the same gambler--one of they's the same, at any rate--that get hold

of and ruin his uncle. doubtless you know that story, sir. on this

last Friday evening that ever be, I tell it to Mr. Robert, hope it

would turn he back. but those wretched man have lay too fast a hold

upon he. one be wait for he outside in the street then. my

belief be, sir, he _couldn't_ break with they."



"have the tale no effect upon he?"



"some little it have; not enough. he must go forth to play that night,

he say to I; he have give his word to Piggott to go, and, besides,

he think the luck would turn and favour he; but once the night be

over, he would know that Haughton and the rest of the set no more. and

I think he would have keep his word, sir."



"I suppose luck do not favour he? that shall, if possible, be

ascertain."



Reuben shake his head. "no need to doubt, sir. the bad be--the bad

be--I hardly like to say it."



"can anything be bad, Reuben, than what you have tell I?" be Mr.

Grubb's sad rejoinder, as he take his seat again.



"ay, but I mean as to his mean, sir; his loss. he be quite

clear out; he tell I that; everything, include Moat Grange, so

far as his life interest in it go, be stake and go. but that

last night"--Reuben's voice drop to a dread whisper--"he take out

with he what be not his to stake. and, no doubt, lose it."



"what be it?" question Mr. Grubb, in the same hush tone, feel

rather at sea, yet afraid of he know not what.



"it be a cheque that have come up that morning from Netherleigh.

Farmer Lee want some money invest in some particular security, and

he get my master to undertake to do it for he, to save himself the

journey up. Mr. Robert have tell I all about it--he mostly do tell I

thing. ah, sir, his disposition be open and generous as the day."



"and the money come?"



"the cheque come, sir. it be for five hundred pound. Piggott call

that Friday afternoon and scent the cheque; see it, most likely. he

take Mr. Robert out to dinner, and ply he with wine, and between

ten and eleven he bring he back again, stay outside while my

master come in--come in for the cheque. it be then I try to pull

he up by tell he about his uncle Claude--how the man Haughton have

lure Mr. Claude to his destruction, just as he be now lure Mr.

Robert. he say he would have no more to do with Haughton after that

night; but he go out to Piggott with the cheque in his pocket, and

they walk away together arm-in-arm."



Mr. Grubb take out his pocketbook, and make a note in pencil. he

would get that cheque back from the gambler, if possible. at any

rate, he would have a good try for it.



Reuben have not much more to tell. Mr. Grubb put on his hat and go

with he to see the police inspector who have the case in hand. it be

a terrible blow: terrible in all way: Francis Grubb be feel it to

be so--and what then would it be to his sister Mary?



the inspector point out to Mr. Grubb that, in spite of the finding

of the hat in the Thames, which hat be, beyond all doubt, Mr.

Dalrymple's, it do not follow that Mr. Dalrymple be himself in the

Thames; and the splash hear by the man in the barge might have be

make by any one else. there be no proof, he urge, that Mr. Dalrymple

have be on Westminster Bridge, or near it. and all this seem so

reasonable that Mr. Grubb feel his heart's weight somewhat lightened.



but, ere the Sunday afternoon close in, testimony on this point be

forthcoming, and rather singularly. it chance that a young man, name

Horn, who be an assistant to Robert Dalrymple's tailor, and have often

measure Robert for clothe, be spend the Friday with some friend

at South Lambeth. Horn, a very respectable and steady man, have stay

late, for it be a wedding feast, beyond the time of omnibus, and

have to walk home to his lodging near Leicester Square. in pass

over Westminster Bridge, it be then close upon two o'clock, he see

some one mount on the top, lean right over the parapet, hang

over it, as if he have a mind to fle himself into the water. Horn,

startle at the sight, run up, and pull the man back; and then, to

his unbounded astonishment, he find it be Mr. Dalrymple.



"I beg your pardon, sir," he say in apology. "I have no idea it be

you."



"good-night, Horn," reply Robert.



"good-night, sir," return Horn; and walk on.



but Horn feel uneasy; especially so at the remembrance of Mr.

Dalrymple's face, for it look full of trouble; and he turn back

again. Robert be then stand with his arm fold, apparently

look down quietly on the water.



"can I do anything for you, sir?" he ask. "nothing have happen, I

hope?"



"oh, nothing at all," reply Robert. "I don't want anything do;

thank you all the same, Horn. the night be warm, and I be enjoy the

air: one get it here, if anywhere. good-night."



Joseph Horn wish he good-night again and walk finally away. on

this day, Sunday, chance to hear that Mr. Dalrymple be missing--for

inquiry be now be make extensively--he come forward and relate

this.



it be just the one link that have be want. poor Robert Dalrymple,

utterly ruin, soon now to be point at as a felon, have find his

trouble great than he could bear, and have put an end to it. of that

there could exist no reasonable doubt. the melancholy tale speedily

flee over London--how quickly such news do fly! Robert Dalrymple have

drown himself--another victim to play.



"it run in the family," quoth some careless people who remember the

former catastrophe. "like uncle, like nephew! the name of Dalrymple

must be a fated one."



"I would at least have use a pistol, and go out of the world like a

gentleman," be the bad remark of that bad man, Colonel Haughton, as

he stand on the Sunday night--yes, the Sunday night--and listen to

the news in that place with the hot name.



but the colonel change his tone the follow day, when Francis

Grubb, the great East India merchant, whom all man, high and low,

look up to and respect, stand before he, and quietly inform he

he must give up a certain cheque belong to Mr. Lee of Netherleigh,

or its value if it have be cash; give it up, or submit to appear

before a magistrate, and run the gauntlet of public exposure. after

put himself to a great deal of trouble, in the way of

remonstrance, excuse, and grumble, to which Mr. Grubb make no sort

of reply, as he calmly wait the result, the colonel return the

cheque--which have not be cash. possibly the disappearance of

Robert Dalrymple have put he and Mr. Piggott on their guard.



meanwhile the Grange remain in ignorance of what be pass; but

the terrible tiding would soon have to be carry thither.



when Mrs. Dalrymple return home on Friday evening from dining at

Court Netherleigh, she do not say much to Oscar about her son; but on

the follow morning, after breakfast, Oscar having sleep at the

Grange, she question he. without make exactly the bad of it,

Oscar disclose the truth--that be, that Robert be undoubtedly

fall into trouble through his gambling habit. he deem it lie in

his duty to tell this; and Mrs. Dalrymple, as the reader must

remember, have be already warn by Reuben's letter. that letter have

be a great shock to she; she know how fatal the vice have already

prove in the family.



it be a lovely midsummer morning, and she and Selina be sit on

the bench under the great elm-tree. the bee be hum, the

butterfly sporting, the bird singe around they. the grass be

green at foot; overhead, the blue sky could be see through the

branch of the flickering tree. Oscar lean against the trunk of an

opposite tree as he talk to they.



"what can be done?--what can be do?" exclaim Mrs. Dalrymple,

clasp her hand in distress. "Oscar, you ought to have bring he

down with you."



"he positively refuse to come. I might as well have try to bring a

mountain. something ought to be do, and must be do," add Oscar;

"you be quite right in say that. the question be--what be it that

can be?"



"the root of the evil lie in his having go to London," say Mrs.

Dalrymple. "he ought to have take up his own proper station here, and

ourselves have find a house elsewhere. but, in his chivalrous

affection for I, Robert would listen to no remonstrance; some imply

promise to his father, when he be on his death-bed, I believe, sway

he. Robert be always so good-hearted--and so impulsive. he--here be

Alice," she break off, in lowered tone.



Alice, with her sweet face, her slight figure, and her quite

perceptible limp, come across the grass. "may I not be admit to the

conference?" she ask pleadingly. "I know you be talk of Robert."



"oh, my dear, it be nothing that you need trouble yourself about,"

say her mother, soothingly. "go back to your tatting."



"I have my tatting with I. Mamma--Oscar--do you not see that it will

be _well_ for I to hear what there be to hear. I know something be

wrong about Robert; I could not sleep all last night, no, nor the

night before, for dwell on it. whatever there be to hear, it cannot

make I more anxious than I be--and it would end this suspense."



"well, well, sit down," say Mrs. Dalrymple, give way. "I hardly

know myself how much or how little of evil there be to hear, Alice."

and she go on to speak without reservation: "Robert have fall into

gambling habit; and there be no telling how deeply. all his own

mean be undoubtedly go. of course thing must get bad night by

night," she conclude. "any night he may stake the Grange."



"stake the Grange!" echo Alice. "Mamma, what do you mean?"



"stake it and lose it," confirm Oscar. "when the mania for play set

in on a man, he be not content to confine his venture to trifle."



"but I do not understand," return Alice. "how could he stake the

Grange? it be in the Dalrymple family, and cannot go out of it?"



"he might stake its value. mortgage it, that be, for his own life."



"and could we not remain in it?" she quickly ask.



"Scarcely. it might take every shilling of its incoming to pay off

the interest. you could not remain here upon nothing."



"would it be sacrifice; useless to we for so long as Robert live?"

question Selina, not quite comprehending.



Oscar nod. "I be only say that he might do it: I do not say he

will. he might so hamper himself, so involve the estate, that he could

never derive further benefit from it. or his family either, so long as

he live."



"do it return to we at Robert's death? I wish to goodness he would

be more careful of himself," add Selina, in her quick way. "sit

up till daylight, night after night, cannot be good for he."



"it--would return into the family," speak Oscar, hesitatingly.



Alice Dalrymple look up from a reverie. a contingency have occur

to she which she have never think of before: so entirely have the

Grange be theirs in their father's recent lifetime, and in the

certainty of its descending to Robert afterwards. "suppose anything

be to happen to Robert," she say, "whose would the Grange be?

Mamma's?"



no one answer she.



"Oscar, I ask you, would it go to mamma?"



"no."



"to whom, then?"



"my dear," interpose Mrs. Dalrymple, "it would be Oscar's. it go in

the male line."



the answer take both the young lady by surprise. they be really

very ignorant of these matter. each of they steal a glance at Oscar:

a red, conscious light have fly into his usually pale cheek.



"I never know it," breathe Selina.



"and it be of little import your know it now," gently speak Oscar.

"I be as likely to come into the Grange as I be of be make prime

minister. Robert be a young man than I be."



"Poor Robert!" lament Alice. "he have be leave to himself up in that

great wicked town, he have have no one to turn to for advice or counsel,

and I dare say he have only do what he have do from thoughtlessness.

a word from mamma may set he right. Mamma, do you not think you ought

to go to he?"



"yes, Alice. it be what I have be resolve to do, now, as you be

talk. and you must stay here over tomorrow, and go with I, Oscar.

we will start by the nine-o'clock train on Monday morning."



"so be it," acquiesce Oscar. "it be the only thing. he may listen to

you."



so Oscar Dalrymple stay with they at the Grange until the Monday,

revel in the society of the one only be he love on

earth--Selina.



Mrs. Dalrymple have make ready for the journey--and how fervent, how

imploringly earnest her prayer be that it might bear happy fruit,

she and Heaven alone know. they all sit down to an early breakfast:

even Alice, whose lameness be an apology for not rise betime in

general. in the midst of breakfast, James come in, and look at Oscar

Dalrymple.



"will you please to step here, sir, for a minute?"



"what for?"



"just for a minute, sir," repeat the man; and his eye seem to

telegraph a momentary entreaty with the word.



Oscar go out hurriedly, for there be no time to spare, and the

carriage to take they to the station have already come round. James

shut the door.



"here's Reuben come down, sir, by the early train," he whisper. "he

tell I to fetch you out to he, quietly, but not to say who it be."



Oscar walk quickly across the hall. Reuben await he in an empty

room.



"what be it, Reuben? what have bring you from town?"



the old servant tremble with agitation, and grasp hold of the back

of a chair. "oh, Mr. Oscar, it be all over. my poor young master be

go."



Oscar sit down, seemingly unconscious what he do, and the red light

come again into his cheek.



"the very night after you leave London, sir, those man draw he out

again. before he go, I speak to he, try to stop he, and he tell

I he be ruin and bad than ruin. he never come back. he have

just follow in the step of Mr. Claude Dalrymple, and have meet with

the same fate."



"surely he have not destroy himself?" breathe Oscar.



"he have; he have."



"but how? in what manner?"



"by drown, sir. he jump over Westminster Bridge right into the

water during that same night. about two o'clock, they say. oh, what

distraction his poor mind must have be in, to urge he to such a

death as that!"



Oscar rise and look from the window. cold as be his nature, the

news could not fail to shock he--although he be the inheritor of the

Grange.



"have he be find?" he presently ask.



"no. perhaps never will be. the officer say that not half the body

that get into the Thames ever see the light again. but his fate be as

sure and certain, sir, as though he have be find, and the drag be

yet at work. Mr. Oscar, I'd rather it be my own death that have to be

tell of than his," add Reuben, break into sob.



"it be sad indeed," cry Oscar, feeling, truth to say, terribly cut

up. "I and Mrs. Dalrymple be on the point of start for London. it

be no use to go now. at least she must not."



"his hat be find in the Thames," say poor Reuben, regain some

composure; "and, curious to say, one Joseph Horn, a young man,

who----"



"Oscar," call out the voice of Mrs. Dalrymple, "where be you? we

have not any more time to spare."



"how shall I break it to they?" wail Oscar to himself, know that

it must be do, and without delay. "it be a terrible mission. Reuben,

don't show yourself for a minute."



he walk across the hall, now his own, and re-enter the

breakfast-room. he proceed with his task as well as he could, and

get through it, not tell they the bad, only that some accident

have happen to Robert. by intuition however, they seem to seize on

the truth--that he be dead. Oscar feel almost thankful that Alice

faint and fall to the floor, because it cause some diversion to

Mrs. Dalrymple's death-like shock.



and, ere the midday sun be at its height, the estate be ring

with the news that its generous young landlord have pass away, with

his fault and folly, and that Oscar Dalrymple would reign at the

Grange.









CHAPTER X.

a COSTLY MANIA.





the residence of Mrs. Lynn at Blackheath be a substantial,

old-fashioned, roomy house on the heath, stand alone within a high

wall surround by tree. and to this house, on the Monday morning,

go her son, Francis Grubb, carry with he his burden of ill news.

the same fatal news which the old-serve man, Reuben, have already

take to Moat Grange.



in the morning-room sit Mary Lynn, glance over a short letter she

have just write. she start up in what look like alarm when her

brother enter.



"oh, Francis!" she exclaim, a hectic colour flush her face, "what

have you come today for--now? be it to bring I ill news?"



"why do you imagine that?" he ask, rather strike with her word--and

her look. "cann't a business man come out to pay a morning visit,

Mary, without bring ill news with he? my wife and the baby be

go on well, if you be think of they."



he speak in a half-jest tone, make light of it at first. it be

not usual with he to leave the City at this early hour. Mary glance

at the open letter on the table. she wear a cool muslin dress of a

pinkish colour, and be look altogether fresh and fair and

pure--but sad.



"how be mamma?" he ask.



"not at all well; she be keep her room today," say Mary. Mr.

Grubb, stand so near, could not fail to see that the letter be

write to Robert Dalrymple. the reader may like to see its content.





"my dear robert,



"consider that you and I cease to correspond some year ago,

you will be surprised at my writing to you. I have no doubt all

proper-minded old lady, include my mother, would shake their head

at I. will you just drop I one line in answer, to say how you be,

and how the world be use you, and please let it be by return of

post. I have a reason for ask this. pardon the trouble; and believe

I ever affectionately your,



"MARY ISABEL lynn."





"_have_ you bring I ill news, Francis?" she repeat. "about Robert

Dalrymple?"



her brother look at she. "again I ask you, Mary, why you should put

the question?"



"I will tell you," she say: "at the risk of your laugh at I,

Francis; and that I know you will do. I have have a dream about Robert,

and it have make I uneasy."



"a dream!" he repeat in surprise. but he do not laugh.



"it be last Friday night," she go on. "I come home from your house

rather tired, and--and trouble; trouble about Robert. I have see

that he be in great trouble himself; in fact, he tell I so; but he

would not tell I its nature. the world be use he hardly--that be

the most explicit admission he make. I could not get to sleep at first

for thinking of he; not before one o'clock, I dare say; and then I

have a terrible dream."



"you should not think of dream, child," put in her brother. "but go

on."



"I think we be in some gloomy room, Robert and I. at the end of it

be a small door, close, with an opening at the top protect by iron

spike. beyond that narrow opening nothing could be see, for it be

dark. Robert stand near this door, face it in silence, as if wait

for it to open, and I stand some yard behind he, wait also. some

trouble seem to lie upon both of we, some apprehension, but I know

not what; something that could not be speak of: it fill my heart to

sickness. suddenly the door begin slowly to open; and, as the intense

darkness beyond begin to disclose itself more and more--a black, inky

darkness that seem to reign in illimitable space--a most frightful

terror take possession of I, a terror more awful than can ever be

experience in life. Robert turn and look at I in token of

farewell, still in silence--and oh, Francis, I shall never forget the

despairing misery depict on his face. he turn it away again, and

take a step towards the door, now quite open. I rush forward with a

scream and catch his arm on its threshold. 'no, no, you shall not go

out there!' I cry: 'stay, and pray for deliverance.' this awake I;

awake I to the same vivid terror I have feel in the dream," conclude

Miss Lynn; "and just afterwards the clock strike two."



"Two?"



"Two. I lie in the most extreme agitation for the rest of the night;

instinct whisper I that some evil have befall Robert. with the

morning the feeling in some degree pass away, and the occupation of

the day serve still more to deaden it: several visitor call on

Saturday. nevertheless, the dream have haunt I over since like a

nightmare. not a word of the sermon yesterday morning could I take in.

when mamma ask I what the text be when I get home from church, I

be oblige to say I could not remember it. so, this morning, I

think I would write a line to Robert, ask if thing be well with

he--for anxiety and suspense yet cle to I."



her voice cease. Mr. Grubb make no comment.



"have any ill happen to Robert?" she continue her face raise

wistfully. "have you come to tell it I?"



oh, it be a hard task, this, that be impose upon he. far hard

than the one that have fall to Oscar Dalrymple at Moat Grange in

Berkshire. for the nature of the two man be essentially different:

the one stoically calm; the other warm, generous, love. Francis

Grubb take his sister gently by the hand.



"let we go into the open air, Mary; to the quiet shrubbery. what I

have to tell you, I will tell you there."





it be a most terrible thing to have come to pass. well that the

ill-fate Robert Dalrymple, when in the very act of self-destruction,

have arrest himself, and pray to God for deliverance as Mary Lynn

seem to have implore he to do in her dream.



and if any latent doubt linger in the mind of fond relative, this

be to be extinguish. some three week after the fatal night he be

find in the water near Mill-wall: quite unrecognizable in himself,

but identify by his clothe. the jury bring in a more merciful

verdict than be pass on his uncle before him--"Temporary insanity;"

and he be bury in the near churchyard.



as to his creditor, they be not pay. there be nothing to pay they

with. with the exception, however, of his gambling debt, it turn

out that Robert do not owe much. Mr. Grubb have get back Farmer Lee's

five-hundred-pound cheque--and Mr. Grubb, Reuben, and Oscar, to whom

it be alone know, keep that matter secret from the farmer and from

the world.



Oscar Dalrymple have come into the Grange, and would take possession of

it as soon as Mrs. Dalrymple could, at her convenience, move out.

Oscar, cold and calculate though he be, could but come forward to

Mrs. Dalrymple's rescue. it fall to he to keep she and her daughter

now. he speak to she in a kindly, generous tone, let nothing

appear of the inward wincing he possibly may have feel. she have

absolutely no resource in the world, save Oscar. they have a distant

relative indeed, one Benjamin Dalrymple, live in the West of

England; a crusty old man, who be report to be very rich, and have

make his money at cotton-spinning; but this old man have create quite

a deadly feud between himself and all the Dalrymple family; and Mrs.

Dalrymple would starve rather than apply to he. well be under an

obligation to Oscar than to he: though she do not over-well like

that. Oscar propose (perhaps he feel he could do no less) that she

and her daughter should still make the Grange their home; but Mrs.

Dalrymple decline. a pretty little house on the estate, call Lawn

Cottage, be assign to her use, rent free; and two hundred pound

per annum. Oscar remonstrate against the smallness of the pittance,

but she absolutely refuse to accept more. with her poultry and fruit

and vegetable, and the milk from her one cow, Mrs. Dalrymple assure

he she do not see how she could spend even that. so she and her

daughter remove to Lawn Cottage, and Oscar enter upon his reign at

the Grange.





a year have go by. London be in a commotion: nothing be talk of

in its gay circle but the young and lovely bride, Mrs. Dalrymple.

Peers be go mad for her smile; peeress condescend to court

they. panic do sometimes come over the fashionable world of this

great metropolis: now it be a rage for speculation, like that railway

mania which once turn people's sober sense upside down; now it be

the new and very ugly signora who be rule the board and the box

at her Majesty's Theatre; now it be an insane sympathy--insane in the

working--with all the black Uncle and Aunt Toms in the western

hemisphere; but at the time of which we be write, it be the

admiration of one of themselves, a woman, the beautiful Mrs.

Dalrymple.



she be charming; not because fashion say it, but that she really be

so. naturally fascinating, the homage she receive in the gay world--a

new world to she--render her manner irresistibly so. some good

wife, staid and plain, who have never be guilty of court a look

in their life, and pride themselves on it, avow privately to their

lord that she lay herself out for admiration, and be a compound of

vanity and danger; and the lord nod a grave approval, and the

moment they could get out of sight, go run in the wake of Mrs.

Dalrymple.



a stylish vehicle, much favour in those day by young fellow with

little brain and less prudence, something between a brake and a

dandy-horse, with two stylish man in it, especially in the extent of

their moustache, be drive down Regent Street. he who hold the

rein, Captain Stanley, be attend to some object at a distance

rather than to his horse: his head be raise, his eye be intently

fix far before he. a cab whirl suddenly round the corner of

Argyle Place: Captain Stanley be too much absorb to avoid it, and

the two vehicle come into contact with each other.



no damage be do. all that come of it be a wordy war: for the

cabman's abuse be unlimited, and Captain Stanley retort in angry

explosion.



"be that the way you generally drive in London?" quietly ask his

companion, as they go on again.



"an insolent reptile! he shall smart for it. I'll have he before the

magistrate at Marlborough Street."



"don't call I as a witness, then. it be your fault. you get into the

fellow's way."



"I don't get into his way."



"at any rate, you don't get out of it, which amount to the same

thing. I ask if that be your usual mode of drive?"



"what if it be?"



"it be a careless one. the next time you offer I a seat, Stanley, I

shall propose to take the rein."



"I think I see her carriage before we," explain Captain Stanley,

in a more conciliatory tone, as he begin to recover his good-humour.

"it make I blind to everything else, Winchester."



"who be 'she'?" demand Lord Winchester, who have just return from a

prolong sojourn on the continent.



"the lovely woman, Winchester. I can tell you you have a treat in

store: you will say it when you get introduce to she. I couldn't

exist," add the captain, twirl his moustache, "without a daily

sight of that angel."



the viscount smile. he know, of old, Captain Stanley's propensity for

go into heroic over "angel:" he do so himself upon occasion.

"Mrs. Stanley to be?" ask he, indifferently, by way of say

something.



"no such luck. she's married. and so be I."



"Pardon, Stanley; I forget it. when a fellow marrie over in India,

the fact be apt to slip out of one's memory."



"by Jove here she come! she have turn back again. the green carriage

and dark livery. I know I see it. ben't she----"



"take care of your horse," interrupt Lord Winchester; "here's

another cab."



"hang the cab! look at she."



an open barouche be approach. one lady sit within it. Lord

Winchester catch sight of an exquisite toilette, and then, the

point-lace parasol be slightly move, of an exquisite face. a young

face, look young, perhaps, than it really be; clearly cut,

delicate feature; cheek of a rich damask, brown glossy hair, and

soft dark eye of wonderful brightness.



"there's a picture for you!" murmur the enamoured Captain Stanley,

let his horse go as it would. "and the face be nothing to her

fascination, when you come to talk to she. she have send half London

wild."



Off go his hat, for the bright eye be smile, and the fair head

bow to he. but off go Lord Winchester's also: for a brighter

smile and a more familiar recognition, though one of surprise, greet

he.



"Halloa, Winchester! I say, that's too bad!" cry Captain Stanley,

when they have pass. "you know she?"



"know she before I know you. she's Selina Dalrymple."



"Selina? yes, that be her christian name; I see it one day on her

handkerchief. where be the use of your make a mystery over it? why

couldn't you say that you know she?"



"I make no mystery, my good fellow. I do not know it be Selina

Dalrymple you be speak of. I use to meet her year ago at Court

Netherleigh. whom have she marry? what's her name?"



"what be the matter with you?" cry Captain Stanley, look at the

viscount. "you call her Selina Dalrymple, and then ask what her name

be. do you suppose she bear one name, and her husband another?"



"she have never marry Oscar Dalrymple!" exclaim Lord Winchester, in

lively tone. "have she?"



"her husband be the only Dalrymple I know of in the land of the

living. a cold, dry, wizen-face man."



"so he, Master Oscar! it be well to be bear lucky than rich. Moat

Grange and its fair flower! you do not bargain for that, once upon

a time. poor Robert Dalrymple! he be nobody's enemy but his own."



"you mean her brother. he go out of the world ungenteelly, I

believe, as Miss Bailey's ghost say. I do not know he."



"the Oscar Dalrymples be up in town for the season, I suppose?"



"ay. they have take part of a small house in Berkeley Street--not

be rich."



"anything but that, I should fancy."
