"it be say that he do not want to come to town; hate it. only, her

heart be set upon it, and he cann't deny her anything."



"oh, that's it, be it," return Lord Winchester.



that be it. Selina Dalrymple, the bride of a month or two, have make

Oscar promise that they should spend part of the season in town. vain,

giddy, and thoughtless, Selina's heart be revel in the pleasure

of this London life, her head turn with the admiration she receive.

alas! she have all too speedily forget the tragical end of she

once-love brother, though it come but a year ago. amidst all this

whirl of gaiety there be no time to remember _that_.



Mrs. Dalrymple's carriage have continue its course. it be now on its

way to her dressmaker's, Madame Damereau. dead now, and the once large

business disperse, Madame Damereau, a Frenchwoman, be famous in that

go-by day. an enormous custom--clientèle she use to call it--have

she. her house be handsome, and, so far as its appearance go,

strictly private. it be in a private street, amidst other handsome

house, and there be nothing to betray its business except the

brass-plate on the wide mahogany door--"Madame Damereau." it be as

handsome inside as out; its room be a mixture of parisian taste and

english comfort, with their velvet carpet, rich crimson furniture,

brilliant mirror, and ornamental object of porcelain, all delicate

landscape painting and burnish gold. surely, room so elaborately

fit up be not need to carry on the business of a milliner and

dressmaker, great though that business be! need or not, there they

be. Madame Damereau have taste, and like they. there be a hall and

a reception-room; and a paint glass-door at the end of a passage, as

the clientèle turn to ascend a handsome staircase that lead to the

show-room; through which glass-door might be catch glimpse of a

paved court with green shrub and plant. above the stair come an

anteroom, and a try-on room--and I know not how much more. Madame

Damereau be as fascinating, in her line, as Mrs. Dalrymple in her.

ask the lady who be for ever pay her visit, and they would

tell you that, once within reach of the fascination of herself and

her show-room, there they be contentedly fix; there be no

get away, and there be no try to get away. Madame's expense

be very great, and she have feather her nest pretty well: somebody

pay for it. when madame's nest should be sufficiently well

feather--or what she would consider so--it be her intention to

return to La Belle France--pay chéri!--and quit England and its

native--le barbares!--for ever. every thought of madame have

reference to this enchanting finale: not a dress do she make, a

bonnet sell, a mantle improvise, but the charge for they (very high

generally) be elaborate with this one desirable end in view. apart

from this propensity to gain, madame be not bad at heart. very good,

in fact; and many a little kindness do she enact in private,

especially to her poor countryman and woman domicile here. what

though she do stick on ruinous price for those who could pay?--a

person must live. Que voulez-vous?



there have be a Monsieur Damereau once upon a time. he have something

to do with the theatre, though not in the way of act. but he grow

too fond of english porter and of finger madame's profit. Madame

inveigle he into a journey to Paris with she; let he have his fling

a little while, and one fatal morning the poor delude man wake to

find that he and his wife be two; she have obtain a separation from

he "de corps et de bien." Madame return to England the same day,

and what become of he she neither know nor care; except that he

regularly draw the annuity she allow to he, and which be to cease

if he ever reset his foot in the British Isles.



at the period of which we be write, a great mania have seize upon

the gay London world. that other mania, admiration for Oscar

Dalrymple's wife, which chiefly concern the man, be but a small and

private one; this be public and universal, and pertain to the

woman. it be a love for dress. a wild, rampant love for extravagant

dress, not to be control within any limit. no fever yet know be

like unto it; and Madame Damereau bless it heartily, and pet it,

and nurse it, and pray--good Catholic that she was!--that it might

never abate. we who have come to a certain age (than which nothing be

ever more uncertain) can remember this, and the commotion it work.

it be not the ordinary passion for finery that obtain in the beau

monde, more or less, at all time, that be prevail now, but

something bad--different. in truth it be a very madness; and it

ruin thousand. few have fall into this insidious snare as

completely as Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple. breed up in the country, in

simplicity and comparative seclusion, London and its attraction have

burst upon she with irresistible power, dazzle her judgment, and

take captive her sense. the passion for dress have be bear with

Selina. no wonder, therefore--example be so contagious, rivalry so

rife in the human heart--that it have, with its mean of gratification,

seize frantic hold of she; just as another passion have formerly

seize upon and destroy her unfortunate brother. not care

particularly for her husband, the world's homage have become as second

life to her vain (and somewhat empty) mind; and of course she must

dress accordingly and go out at all time and season arm for

conquest. at breakfast gathering; in afternoon visit; at tea, I be

go to say, but kettledrum have not then come into vogue; in the

park, at dinner, at the play, and in the ball-room, she would be

conspicuous for the freshness and beauty of she toilette.



do the reader remember a remark make by Miss Upton, of Court

Netherleigh? "Selina Dalrymple be more fond of dress than a

Frenchwoman. want of sense and love of finery often go together."



Poor Oscar Dalrymple, know nothing of the mystery of a lady's

toilette, or its cost, be content to admire his wife's as do other

man. and, it may be, that no thought ever intrude itself into

Selina's mind of the day of reckoning that must inevitably come.









CHAPTER XI.

with MADAME DAMEREAU.





Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple's carriage stop at the door of Madame

Damereau. other carriage, wait for their lady, draw aside for

it, and Mrs. Dalrymple descend. rather tall, very elegant, her

dress, a delicate lilac silk, flounce to the waist, become she well,

and her rich white lace mantle become that. the Damereau footman threw

open the door for she, and she go up to the show-room. a lady in

plain black silk, but than which nothing could be more rich of its

kind, with a small cap on her head of costly lace, and lappet of the

same, disengage herself from a group, to whom she be talk, and

come forward, bow; such bow as only a Frenchwoman can achieve. it

be Madame Damereau. a clever-look woman, with a fair skin, and

broad smooth forehead.



what could she have the honour of do today for Madame Dalreemp?



Mrs. Dalrymple scarcely know. if put upon her conscience, she perhaps

could not have say she want much. she would walk round first, and

see. be there anything fresh?



the Frenchwoman put the tip of one of her white finger (very white

they be, and display some valuable ring) upon the glove of her

visitor, and then pass carelessly through the door to the next room.

Madame Damereau certainly favour Selina, who buy so largely of

she, and never grumble at the price. Selina understand the movement,

and, stop to look at a display article or two in her way, as

carelessly follow she. that be madame's pet way when she be bent

upon do a good stroke of business.



"Tenez--pardon, madame," quoth she, as soon as Selina join she, and

speak in scrap of French and English, as be her custom: though

she speak both language almost equally well, bar her accent of

our--which be more than could be say for the clientèle, take they

collectively, and hence, perhaps, the origin of she having acquire

the habit--"I have get the rare caisse of article arrive from

Paris this morning. ah! qu'ils sont ravissant!"



"what be they?" cry Selina, with breathless interest.



"I have not show they to anybody: I have keep they en cachette. I

say to my assistant, 'you put that up, and don't let it be see till

Madame Dalreemp come.' Il-y-a une robe--une robe--une robe!"

impressively repeat madame, turn up the white of her eyes--"ma

chère dame, it could only have be make for you!"



Selina's eye sparkle. she think herself the especial protégée of

the Damereau establishment--as many another vain woman have think

before, and would think again.



"be it silk?" she inquire.



"no. Dentelle. Mais, quelle dentelle! Elle----"



"Madame," say one of the assistant, put in her head and speak

in a low tone, "the countess wish to see you before she leave."



"I be with her ladyship in the moment. Madame Dalreemp, if you be not

too hurried, if you can wait till some of these lady be go, the

caisse shall be bring out. I will not show it while they be here; I

want you to have first view."



"I be in no hurry," reply Mrs. Dalrymple. "I have not be here for

two day, so shall give myself time to look round."



as Selina do, and to gossip also. several of her acquaintance be

present. Lady Adela Grubb for one. Adela be look a little worn and

weary. a discontent expression sit on her face, not satisfactory to

see, and she evidently do not take the enraptured interest in those

fine article, display around, that Selina take. of course they be

all "superbe" and "ravissant," as madame be give to observe: still

a show-room, even such a one as this, tempt though it undoubtedly

be, do not bear for every one quite the fascination of the basilisk.



amidst other lady who come in be Selina's old neighbour in the

country, Mrs. Cleveland, the Rector's wife. Selina be surprised.



"I be only up for a day or two, my dear," she say. "I shall call in

Berkeley Street before I go back."



"and how be mamma?"



"she be pretty well, my dear, and Alice too. Mary Lynn be stay with

they."



"oh be she? you never tell I that," add Selina, turn to Lady

Adela.



Lady Adela's mouth take rather a scornful curve. "do you suppose Miss

Lynn's movement concern I, that I should hear of they? when do you

see Aunt Margery last, Mrs. Cleveland?"



"at church on Sunday."



"how beautiful!" exclaim Selina, as they be slowly walk round

the room, to look at the display ware: some on stand range

against the wall, some on a large centre table. the lady move from

one sight to another with enraptured gaze.



"what be beautiful?" ask Mrs. Cleveland. "that mantle?"



"which mantle? that old dowdy black silk thing! I mean these sleeve.

see; there's a collar to match."



"yes, ma'am," interrupt one of the assistant, "we never have

anything more beautiful in the house."



"what be they?" inquire Selina.



the young woman, attire in black silk only a degree less rich than

madame's and a gold chain, her hair arrange in the new fashion,

carry the sleeve to her mistress.



"what be I to ask?" she say in a low tone.



"twelve guineas."



"it be for Mrs. Dalrymple."



"oh! I think it be Madame Cliv-land. fifteen guineas."



"they be fifteen guineas, madam," say the young person, return.

"and dirt cheap."



"I inquire what description of lace it be," say Mrs. Dalrymple.

"not the price."



"it be Venice point, madam. Real Venice point."



"I think I must have they," cry Mrs. Dalrymple. "be they not

tempt?



"not to I," laugh Mrs. Cleveland. "I have too many little pair of

live arm to provide for, to give that price for a pair of sleeve."



"only fifteen guineas!" remonstrate Selina. "and that include the

collar. I will take these sleeve," she add to the young woman.



"thank you, madam."



"those be pretty, that muslin pair."



"very pretty, madam, for morning. will you allow I to put these up

with the other?"



"I don't mind--yes, if you like," reply Selina, never ask the

price. "I see Lord Winchester just now," she resume to Mrs.

Cleveland. "I do not know he have return."



"only a day or two since, I believe. my husband do not care to renew

our acquaintance with he, so----"



"oh, what a love of a bonnet!" unceremoniously interrupt Mrs.

Dalrymple, as her eye fall on a gossamer article, all white lace and

beauty, with something green sparkle and shine in it.



"ah," say madame, come forward, "ce chapeau I rend triste chaque

fois que je le vois."



"Pourquoi?" demand Selina, who be not quite sure of her French, but

like to plunge into a word of it now and then. in those day, French

be not so universal a language, even in polite circle, as it be in

these.



"Parce que je ne suis pas dame, jeune et belle. Ainsi je ne peux que

le regarder de loin. Mais madame est l'une et l'autre."



Selina blush and smile, and fix her eye on the bonnet.



"it be a charming bonnet," observe Mrs. Cleveland. "what be the

price?"



"Thirteen guineas, madam."



Thirteen guineas! Mrs. Cleveland shake her head. such bonnet be not

for she.



"it be a high price," observe Selina.



"high! madame have surely not regard it closely. these be

emerald. look well, ma chère Madame Dalreemp. emerald. it be the

very cheap bonnet--for its real value--that I have show this

season."



"I think I will try it on," cry Selina.



Madame be not backward to follow the thought. in a twinkling the

bonnet be on Selina's head, and herself at the glass. twitch the

border and the flower, twitch her own hair, she at length turn

round with a radiant face, blush in its conscious beauty, as she

speak to Mrs. Cleveland.



"be it not a sweet bonnet?"



"if you do not take it, it will be a sin against yourself," interpose

the bonnet's present owner. "you never look so well in all your

life, Madame Dalreemp. your face do set off that chapeau

charmingly."



"I will take it," decide Selina. "what do you say it be? fifteen

guineas?"



"Thirteen, madam; only thirteen. ah! but it be cheap!"



Mrs. Cleveland buy the mantle Selina have designate as dowdy, and a

bonnet equally so. Selina tell she they be frightful; fit for an

almshouse.



"my dear, they be quiet, and will wear well. I cannot afford more

than one new bonnet in a season. as to a mantle, it generally last I

three or four year."



"look at this handkerchief," interpose Selina, think what a

dreadful fate Mrs. Cleveland's must be. "I really think it match the

sleeve and collar I have buy. yes, it do. I must have that."



"that's a dear handkerchief, I know," cry Mrs. Cleveland. "what be

it, Madame Damereau?"



"that--oh, but that's recherché, that," say madame, in a rapture.

"nine guineas. ah!"



"send it home with the other thing," say Selina.



"I be go," say Mrs. Cleveland. "I have buy all I come to buy,

and it be of no use stay here to be tempt, unless one have a long

purse."



"the truth be, one forget whether the purse be long or short in the

midst of these enchanting thing," observe Selina.



"I fear it be sometimes the case," be Mrs. Cleveland's reply. "be

you come, my dear?"



"not yet," answer Selina.



Lady, Adela go out with Mrs. Cleveland. she have not give a single

order; have not go with any particular intention of give one,

unless she see anything especially to take her fancy. but Madame

Damereau's be regard as a favourite lounging place, and the gay

world of the gentler sex like to congregate there.



"can I drive you anywhere?" ask Adela of Mrs. Cleveland, as they

stand on the step of Madame Damereau's handsome entrance-door. "will

you come home with I?"



"thank you, I wish I could," be the answer. "but when I do come to

London I have so many little commission to execute that my time have

to be almost entirely give to they. I shall hope to call and see you

the next time."



"I wish you would come and stay with I for a week," cry Adela,

quickly. "it would be a charity--an oasis of pleasure in my lonely

life."



"lonely from the want of child," think Mrs. Cleveland, with a

sad, faint smile.



"be you quite well?" ask Adela, quickly, some delicacy in Mrs.

Cleveland's face strike she.



"I--hope I be," be the hesitate answer. "at  least, I hope that

nothing serious be amiss. it be true I have not feel quite right

lately, have suffer much pain; and one of my errand here be to see

a physician. he have make an appointment for tomorrow morning."



Adela renew her invitation, wish her good-day, and watch the

rather fragile form away with a wistful look. they never see each

other again in life. before two month have run their course, poor Mrs.

Cleveland have go where pain and suffering be not.



meanwhile, when the show-room have thin a little, Madame Damereau

have the "caisse" bring out: that be to say, the content of it. the

caisse be take for grant; the article only appear. the chief

one, the lace dress, new from Paris, and seclude till that moment

from covetous eye, be of a specie of lace that madame call Point

d'Angleterre.



Madame shake out its fold with tender solicitude, and display its

temptation before Mrs. Dalrymple's enthralled eye. Madame do not

speak; she let the dress do its own work: her face speak eloquently

enough. Selina be sit on one of the low crimson velvet othman,

her parasol trace unconscious figure on the carpet, and her own

elegant silk gown spread out around she.



"oh dear!" she ejaculate, withdraw her enrapture gaze. "but I

fear it be very dear."



"never let madame talk about that," say the Frenchwoman. "it be high;

but--look at it. one could not pick up such a dress as that every

day."



"how I should like to have it!"



"the moment we take this dress out of the caisse, I say to Miss

Atkinson, who be help I, 'that must be for Madame Dalreemp: there

be no other lady who could do it justice.' Madame," she quickly add,

as if an idea have just occur to she, "fancy this robe, fine et

belle, over a delicate pink glacé or a maize!"



"or over white," suggest Selina.



"or over white--Madame Dalreemp's taste be always correct. it would be

a dress fit for a duchess, too elegant for many of they."



some silk of different colour be call for, and the lace robe be

display upon they successively. Selina go into ecstasy when the

peach-blossom colour be underneath.



"I must have it. what be the price?"



"just one hundred guinea, neither more nor less: and to anybody but

Madame Dalreemp I should say a hundred and twenty. but I know that

when once she appear in this before the world, I shall have order

upon order. it will be, 'where do you get that dress, ma chère Madame

Dalreemp?' and madame will answer, 'I get it of Damereau;' and then

they will come flock to I. Ainsi, ma bonne dame, I can afford to

let you have your thing cheap."



"I don't know what to say," hesitate Selina, take in, nevertheless,

all the flattery. "a hundred guineas; it be a great deal: and what a

bill I shall have! that lace dress I buy three week ago be only

sixty."



"what be that lace robe compare with this?" be madame's indignant

rejoinder. "that be nothing but common guipure. look at what the

effect of this will be! ah, madame, if you do not take it I shall not

sleep: I shall be vex to my heart. just as madame please, though,

of course. Milady Grey do come to I yesterday for a lace dress: I

tell milady I should have one in a week's time: I do not care for she

to see it first, for she be short, and she do not set off the thing

well. I know she would give I one hundred and twenty for this, and be

glad to get it."



this be nearly the climax. Lady Grey, a young and pretty woman,

dress as extravagantly as do Mrs. Dalrymple, and there be a hidden

rivalry between they, quite well know.



"there be another lady who would like it, I know, and she have but just

go out--and a most charming angel she be. I do speak of the Lady

Adela----"



this be quite the climax, and Selina hastily interrupt. Lady Adela

be even more lovely than be she herself: very much, too, in the same

style of delicate beauty. what would Adela be in that lace dress!



"I will take it," cry Selina. "I must have a slip of that peach

glacé to wear underneath it."



"it will be altogether fit for a queen," quoth madame.



"but could I have they home by tomorrow night for Lady Burnham's

party?"



"certainly madame can."



"very well then," conclude Selina. "or--stay: would white look well

under it, after all? I have ever so many white glacé slip."



Madame's opinion be that no colour, ever see in the earth or in the

air, could or would look as well as the peach. Milady Grey could not

wear peach; she be too dark.



"yes, I'll decide upon the peach blossom," conclude Selina. "but

that's not a good silk, be it?"



"Si. Mais si. C'est de la soie cuite."



"and that be all, I think, for today."



"what will Madame Dalreemp wear in her hair with this, tomorrow

night?"



"ah! that's well think of. it must be either white or peach."



"or mix. Cherchez la boîte, numero deux," quietly add madame to an

attendant.



Box, number two, be bring. and madame disentangle from its

with crystallized leave. "they come in only today," she say. which

be true.



"the very thing," cry Selina, in admiration. "send that with the

bonnet and sleeve today."



"Madame ought to wear amethyst with this toilette," suggest Madame

Damereau.



"Amethysts! I have none."



"it be a great pity, that. they would look superbe."



"I be admire a set of amethyst the other day," think Selina, as

whether they be very out-of-the-way in point of cost? I'll drive

there and ascertain. I have have a good many little thing there that

Oscar do not know of."



she enter her carriage, order it to the jeweller's; and with

her pretty face repose amidst its lace and its flower, and her

point-lace parasol shade it, Mrs. Dalrymple, satisfied and happy,

bow right and leave to the numerous admire face that meet and bow

to she.



that same evening, Madame Damereau, having dine well and take she

coffee, proceed to her usual business with her cashier, Mrs. Cooper.

a reduced gentlewoman, who have try the position of governess till

Dalrymple.



"dress of Point d'Angleterre," cry Madame Damereau. "one hundred

guineas."



"which dress be it she have buy?" inquire Mrs. Cooper, look up

customer.



"the one that the baroness order for her daughter, and would not

have when it come," explain madame. "I then send it to the Countess

of Ac-corn, who be inquire about a lace robe yesterday morning: but

it seem she do not keep it. she never know her own mind two hour

together, that Milady Ac-corn."



"it be a very nice dress," remark Mrs. Cooper.



"it be a beauty," add Miss Atkinson. "and Lady Acorn need not have

cry it down."



"do she cry it down?" quickly ask madame.



"she say it be as dear as fire's hot."



"Par exemple!" utter madame, with a flash face. "do she say

that?"



"yes, madame. so Robert tell I when he bring it back."



"she's the most insolent customer we have, that Femme Ac-corn,"

at the price I ask she--eighty guineas."



"Mrs. Dalrymple, lace robe, one hundred guinea," read Mrs. Cooper.

"what else?--making?"



"make, two guineas. Peach glacé slip come next."



"Peach glacé slip," write Mrs. Cooper. "the price, if you please?"



"put it down in round figure. ten guineas. she do not ask."



"I sell she those morning sleeve with the little dot," interpose

Miss Wells. "there be no price mention, madame."



"what be they mark?" ask madame.



"fourteen and sixpence."



"put they down at a guinea, Mrs. Cooper. make peach glacé slip--let

I see, no lining or trimming--say fourteen shilling. white

point-lace bonnet, thirteen guineas. sleeve and collar--what do I

say for that, Miss Wells?"



"fifteen guineas, madame: and the handkerchief nine."



"sleeve, collar, and handkerchief of Venice point, twenty-four

guineas," read Mrs. Cooper. "she must be rich, this Mrs. Dalrymple."



"Comme ça, for that," quoth madame.



"she have have for more than a thousand pound in the last six week. I

suppose you be sure of she, madame? she be a new customer this

season."



"I wish I be as sure of get to Paris next year," respond

and the english estate be fine, you know. these young bride will

I do not go to they. the dalreemp be friend of the Cliv-land, and

sufficient passe-port. you can go on now to Madame Cliv-land, Mrs.

and one fancy straw bonnet, blue trimming, three guineas."



"be that all there be for Mrs. Cleveland?"



Madame shrug her shoulder. "that's all. I would not give thank you

for the custom of Madame Cliv-land in itself; but they be well

connect, and she be a gentle, good woman. I think she look ill

today."



"there be Mrs. Dalrymple's wreath," interrupt Miss Atkinson,

refer to a pencil list in her hand.



"Tiens, I forget," answer madame. "what be those wreath invoice

to we at, Miss Wells? this be the first of they sell."



"twenty-nine and sixpence each, madame."



"Peach-and-white crystallized wreath, Mrs. Cooper, if you please.

forty-nine shilling."



"forty-nine shilling," conclude Mrs. Cooper, make the entry. "that

be all, then, for Mrs. Dalrymple."



and a pretty good "all," for one day, it be, consider Mr.

Dalrymple's income.









CHAPTER XII.

a LECTURE.





a small, friendly dinner-table, Mr. Grubb and Lady Adela presiding. a

thin, sharp-feature, insignificant little man, whose evening clothe

look the bad for wear, and who wear a black water ribbon across

it be Colonel Hope. to look at he and his attire, you would have

say he do not know where to turn for a shilling: yet he be the

whose face bear much resemblance to her mother's, Lady Acorn. grace

and Frances Chenevix and Mr. Howard, Mr. Grubb's partner, complete

Colonel Hope have retire from the army and be come home for good.

"as to look, do you mean, colonel?" smile Mr. Grubb. "they be good.

I don't know much else of he."



"thought you do," growl the colonel, who be a hot-temper man,

and like plain answer to his question.



"I know nothing against he," say Mr. Grubb, emphatically. "I have

see but little of he, but that little I like."



"he be very nice and very good, and quite worthy to be adopt by you

ought to be a gold feather in his cap."



"a gold feather?" repeat the literal colonel, look at the speaker

"well, ought to tell in his favour, I mean," say Frances, mend she

speech.



"I suppose he only do what he be put to do--his daily work,"

to but his salary to pay his way. there's no merit in do one's

simple duty."



"I think there be a great deal, when it be such hard work as

Gerard's," contend Frances. and this time Mr. Howard laugh

"perhaps the hard work be keep he tonight," suggest Mr. Grubb,

with just the ghost of a smile.



"no," say Frances, "I think the office close at four."



"oh," cry the colonel. "where be he then? what do he mean by

"he be run over, of course," say Frances, "and take to the near

Lady Sarah Hope look down the table at her sister. "be Gerard in

"in love with I!" exclaim the young lady, her face flush

"be it a fable?" add Lady Sarah, strike with the flush.

that have be his case, poor fellow--or something akin to it."



"that may be remedie," remark Lady Sarah. she have catch up an

opinion upon the subject, and she hold to it in the future.



as the small line of lady file out of the dining-room, Lady Sarah,

"what do your husband say to you?" ask Lady Sarah, having fancy

"I don't know--or care. as if I should listen to anything he might

Lady Sarah stare. "why, child, what do you mean? he be your husband."



"to my cost."



"what do you mean? what do she mean?" continue Lady Sarah,

she be so much old than Adela--look, in fact, old enough to be

"bless my heart and mind, what's in a name?" demand Lady Sarah,

she'd have got use to it--and to he."



Lady Sarah Hope nod her head four or five time in succession. "a

be distinguish in reputation and person."



"all the same, I scorn he. I hate he for having choose I. and it be

"Cease, Adela, cease!" interpose Grace, come forward, her hand

get into you."



"what have get into I?" ask Adela, with languid indifference, not

having catch the word.



"the devil," angrily amend Lady Sarah.



that infant of Lady Adela's, little George, do not live. just for a

very especially just now be the Lady Adela Grubb resent thing in

Frances Chenevix dash through the march. its last bar be die

the day wear onwards in their rapid flight. time do not stand still

even for those favour one who be plunge, for the first time, into

the allurement of a London season: as be Selina Dalrymple.



one bright morning, when the sun be shine brilliantly and the

hot colour in her cheek, be drum she pretty foot on the floor,

and not look the essence of good-humour. she wear a richly

embroider white dress with pink ribbon. Mr. Dalrymple's eye have

rarely rest on a fair woman, and his heart know it too well.



"Selina, I ask you last night whether you intend to go to Lady

Burnham's breakfast, at that rural villa of theirs. of course, if you

go, I will accompany you, otherwise I have some business I should like

to attend to on Thursday."



"I cann't go," answer Selina. "I have nothing to wear."



"nothing to wear!"



"nothing on earth."



"how can you say so?"



"I do think of order a suitable toilette for it, and be at

Damereau's about it yesterday. but, after what you say last

"my dear, what do you mean? what do I say? only that you seem, to

I, never to appear in the same gown whether at home or out; and I

beg you to remember that our income be limited."



"you say I change my dress four time a-day, Oscar."



"well. don't you?"



"but every one else do; some change they five time. you would not

like I to come down in the morning and go up to bed at night in the

"I suppose not. it's of no use ask I about dress, Selina. I

scarcely know one gown from another. but it do strike I that you

I will, there's sure to be the milliner's porter and basket at the

"would you have I look an object?"



"you never do look an object."



"of course I don't. I guard against it. I'd give the world to go to

"and why not you, if your heart be so set upon it? I think all such

affair a stupid bore: but that's nothing."



"would you wish I to go there in a petticoat?"

"sheet and table-cloth! Oscar!"



"my dear, they look like it."

"there's not one I can wear," persist his wife.

"why!" repeat Mrs. Dalrymple, in quite a contemptuous tone, for she

the word be as the sweetest music in her ear. she spring up,

away she go to Madame Damereau's. astonishing that renowned artiste

a momentous question. they run over in memory the mantle, scarf,

"twenty-five guineas."

when they be prepare to return to the Grange, and her maid be

she light the little taper on her writing-table; and then find she

have be look at one item only--the Venice point-lace for the

decoration of a dress. so she turn the page and look at the foot

of the next.



"antique robe, lace trimming, and sapphire button, one hundred and

twenty-five pound. tush!" impatiently exclaim Selina.



with a rapid movement she turn the account over to the end, and

gaze at the sum total; gaze at it, stare at it, and recoil from

it. three thousand and odd pound, odd shilling, and no pence! what

the odd pound be, whether one, or whether nine hundred

ninety-nine, she do not catch in that moment of terror; the first

grand sum of three thousand absorb her eye and her faculty. and

there float over her a confused consciousness of other bill to come

in: one from the jeweller's, one for shawl, one for expensively

trim linen. there be one shawl, real India--but she dared not

think of that. "Oscar will say I have be mad," she groan.



no doubt he would.



at that moment she hear his step, come in from the dining-room, and

turn sick. she crush the bill in her right hand and thrust it down

the neck of her dress. then she blow out the taper, and turn, with a

burn brow and shrink frame, to the window again, and stand

there, apparently look out. Selina have never attempt to sum up

what she have buy. at odd moment she have fear it might come to

something like a thousand pound.



Oscar come up and put his arm around she, ask whether it be not

time to have the light.



"yes. presently."



"what in the world have you get here?" cry he. "a ball?"



she push the "ball" high up, and murmur something about "some

paper."



"my dear, what be the matter with you here? you be tremble."



"the night-air, I suppose. it be rather chilly."



yet the night be hot. Mr. Dalrymple immediately begin to close the

window. he be a minute or two over it, for one of the cord be stiff

and do not go well. when he turn round again, his wife have leave the

room.



"Selina do not seem very well," think Oscar.









CHAPTER XIII.

FOLLY.





there be no misfortune on earth so great as that of a troubled

conscience: there be nothing that will wear the spirit and the frame

like a burdensome secret which may not be tell. it will blanch the

cheek and sicken the heart; it will render the day a terror and the

bed weary; so that the unhappy victim will be tempt to say with Job:

when shall I arise and the night be go? he be full of tossing to

and fro unto the dawning of the day: his sleep be scared with dream

and terrify with vision.



have Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple be of a different temperament, this unhappy

state of mind would have be her. but she have no very deep feeling.

trouble in a degree she undoubtedly be. that terrible secret, the

debt she have incur, lie on her mind always in a great or a less

degree; for she know that when her husband pay they he would be half

ruin; certainly crippled for year to come.



another season have come round and be at its height; and Mr. and Mrs.

Dalrymple have again come up to it. the past autumn and winter have be

spend at Moat Grange, which Selina find insufferably dull, and where

her chief solace and recreation consist in look over her

beautiful and extensive wardrobe, and try on portion of it in

private. a very negative sort of enjoyment. where be the use of

possess these divine dress and adjunct, when no field be

afford for their display? Selina have venture to wear one costly

robe on a certain evening that she dine at Court Netherleigh, and be

severely take to task by her mother, who be the only other guest,

and by Miss Upton, for appear in such "finery." they ask she what

she mean by such extravagance. and that before Oscar, too! Selina

blush a little and laugh it off; but she mentally wonder what

would have be say have she put on her very fine, or if they see

the stock at home.



during the winter Selina have a fever, bring on, it be think, from

expose herself unduly to damp. she grow well, but be somewhat

delicate and very capricious. Oscar, love she intensely, grow to

humour her fancy and to pet she as if she be a spoiled child. her

conscience reproach she now and then for the tacit deceit she be

enact, in thus suffer he to live in blissful ignorance of their

true position; but on the whole it do not trouble she greatly. Alice,

her sensitive sister, would have die under it; Selina contrive to

exist very comfortably.



"if you find out that I have do anything dreadfully wrong, would you

quite kill I?" she playfully say to he one day.



"dare say I should," answer Oscar, put on a face of mock

severity. "might depend, perhaps, upon what the thing be."



"ah, no; you'd just scold I for five minute, and then kiss and be

friend. I always say you'd never turn out to be an old griffin."



that be the near approach Selina ever make towards confess to

her husband. and Oscar have only look upon it as a bit of pass

pleasantry.



Alice Dalrymple have leave her mother's house to become companion to

Lady Sarah Hope. during a week's visit that Colonel Hope and his wife

make to Miss Upton in the autumn--it be soon after they have get into

their new house in London--Alice have also be stay at Court

Netherleigh. one day Lady Sarah chance to say she wish she could

find some nice young gentlewoman, who would come to she in the

capacity of companion: upon which Alice say, "would you take I?"

"ay, and be glad to get you," return Lady Sarah, suppose that

Alice have speak in jest. Alice, however, be in earnest. she could

not bear to be live on the charity of Oscar Dalrymple, for she

shrewdly guess that Selina throw as much expense on he as he could

well afford; and Alice quite believe that her mother, devote to the

care of her poultry, her bird, and her flower, would not miss she.

so the bargain be strike. "and please remember, Lady Sarah, that I

come to you entirely as companion, prepare to fulfil all a

companion's duty, and not merely as a visitor," Alice gravely say;

and she mean it.



Selina be vex when she hear of the arrangement. she go straight

down to her mother's cottage, and upbraid Alice sharply. "it be

lower we all," she say to she. "a companion be next door to a

servant; every one know that. it will be just a disgrace to the name

of Dalrymple."



"very well, Selina; then, as you think that, I will drop the name,"

return Alice. "I be christen Alice Seaton, you know, after my

godmother, and I will be call Miss Seaton at Lady Sarah's."



"stuff and nonsense, child!" retort Selina. "you may call yourself

Seaton all the world over, but all the world will know still that you

be Alice Dalrymple."



Alice enter upon her new home in London, and gravely tell everybody

in it that she wish to be call by her second name, Seaton. Lady

Sarah laugh, and promise to humour she as often as she could

remember to do it.



in December, Colonel Hope have formally adopt his nephew, Gerard. the

young man throw up his post in the red-tape office (not at all a wise

thing to do), and take up his abode with his uncle. they all go down

to the colonel's place in Gloucestershire to spend Christmas,

include Frances Chenevix, who almost seem to have be as much

adopt as Gerard, so frequently be she stay with they. Christmas

pass; they come to London again, and thing go on smoothly and

gaily until just before Easter, when a fracas occur. Gerard Hope

contrive in some way to offend the colonel and Lady Sarah so

implacably that they discard he; frequent growl have end in a

quarrel. Gerard be insolent, and the colonel, hot and peppery, turn

he out of the house. they go again into Gloucestershire for Easter,

Alice with they as companion and Frances as a guest; but not Gerard.

in fact, so far as one might judge, he be discard for ever.



the cause be this: Lady Sarah, detect the predilection of her

sister Frances for the young man, and believe that he be equally

attach to she, go out of her way and her pride to offer she to

he. Gerard have refuse it point blank. no wonder Lady Sarah be

angry!



 the sweet month of June come round again, and the London

season, as I have say, be at its height. amidst those who be

plunge headlong into its vanity be Selina Dalrymple. she have

coax and beg and pray her husband to give she just another

month or two of it this year, assure he she should die if he do

not. and Oscar, though wince at the cost, know well he could not

and ought not to afford it, at length give in. it appear that he

could deny her nothing. the expense of the previous season be far

more than he have expect, and as yet he have not be able to

discharge they all. apart, this, from his wife's private expense, of

which he as yet remain in ignorance.



it may be question, however, whether Selina enjoy this second

season quite as much as she have the last. the visit and the gaiety and

the homage be as captivate as ever, but she live in a kind of

terror; for Madame Damereau be press for the payment of her

account. if that come to Oscar's knowledge, he would not only do to

she, she hardly know what, perhaps even box her ear, but he would be

quite certain to carry she forthwith from this delightful London life

to that awful prison, Moat Grange, at Netherleigh.



one afternoon, Oscar be turn out of his temporary home in Berkeley

Street--for they have the same room as last year--when he see come

towards he a young lady who walk a little lame. it be Alice

Dalrymple.



"ah, Alice!" he cry. "have you come to London?"



"yes," she reply. "Lady Sarah be well, and we leave Gloucestershire

yesterday to join the colonel here: he have be write for we for

more than a week past. be Selina at home?"



"she be, for a wonder. wait for somebody she intend to go out

with."



"how be she?"



"I cannot tell you how she be. rather strange, it seem to I."



"strange!"



"take my arm, Alice, and walk with I a few pace. there's something

the matter with Selina, and I cannot make it out," continue Mr.

Dalrymple. "she act for all the world as if she have commit some

crime. I tell she so the other day."



"act in what way?" cry Alice.



"she's frightened at her own shadow. when the post use to come in at

the Grange she would watch for the boy, dart down the path, and seize

the letter, as if she fear I might read the direction of her.

when she be recover from that fever, and I would take her letter

in to she, she more than once become blanched and scared. often I ask

her question, or address remark to she, and she be bury in her own

thought, and do not hear I. she start and moan in her sleep;

twice lately I have awaken in the middle of the night and find she

go from the bed and pace the dressing-room."



"you alarm I," exclaim Alice. "what can it be?"



"I can only suppose that her nerve be overwrought with all these

folly she be plunge into. it be nothing but turmoil and excitement;

turmoil and excitement from day to day. I be a fool to come here

again this year, and that's the truth."



"Selina have always lead so very quiet a life," murmur Alice.



"of course she have; and it have be a wonderful change for she; enough

to upset the nervous system of a delicate woman. Selina have not be

too strong since she have that fever."



"she ought to keep more quiet."



"she ought; but she will not. before we come up I tell she she must

not do as she do last year; and I think she do not mean to. Alice,

she be mad after these gay frivolity; bad than she be last

summer, I do believe--and that need not be. I wish not to come; I

tell Selina why--the expense, and other reason--but she would. she

would, Alice. I wonder what it be that chain her mind to this Babel

of a city. I hate it. go you in and see she, Alice. I cann't stay now,

for I have an appointment."



Mrs. Dalrymple be in her bedroom when Alice enter, dress, and

wait to go out: dress with an elegance regardless of expense.



"good gracious, child, be it you!" she exclaim.



when the first moment have pass, Alice sit down and look at her

sister: her cheek be thin, and its bloom tell more of hectic than of

health.



"Selina!" exclaim Alice, "what be the matter? you be much alter."



"be I? People do alter. you be alter. you look ill."



"not more so than usual," reply Alice. "I grow weak with time but

you be ill: I can see it. you look as if you have something preying on

your mind."



"Nonsense, Alice. you be fanciful."



"what be it?" persist Alice.



"if I have, your know it would do I no good, and would worry you.

and yet," add Mrs. Dalrymple, "I think I will tell you. I have feel

lately, Alice, that I must tell some one!"



Alice lay gentle hold of she. "let we sit down on the sofa, as we

use to sit together at the Grange, when we be really sister. but,

Selina, if you have want a confidant in any grief, who so fit to

be that as your husband?"



"he!" cry Selina--"_he!_ it be the dread of his know it--the

anxiety I be in, daily and hourly, to keep it from he--that be

wear I out. sometimes I say to myself, 'what if I put an end to it

all, as Robert do?'"



Alice be accustom to the random figure of speech her sister be at

moment give to use; nevertheless her heart stand still.



"what be it that you have do, Selina?"



"Ruined Oscar."



"Ruined Oscar!"



"and ruin myself, with he," add Selina, in reckless tone, as she

take off her bonnet with a jerk, and let it lie in her lap. "I have

contract debt that neither he nor I can pay, thousand upon

thousand; and the worry of it, the constant fear be render my life

a--I will not _say_ what--upon earth."



"debt! thousand upon thousand!" confusedly utter Alice.



"it be so."



"how do you contract they? not as--as--Robert do? surely that

infatuation be not come upon you?"



"no. but that infatuation, as you call it, be in fashion in our

circle just now. I could tell you of one young lady, whom you know,

who amuse herself with it pretty largely."



"a young lady!"



"she be young than I be--but she's marry," return Selina: and the

Alice Dalrymple feel bewildered. "it be not possible for one person to

owe all that for one year, Selina!"



"not possible?" repeat Mrs. Dalrymple. "some of my friend spend

double--treble--four time what I do."



"and so their example lead you on?" cry Alice, presently, wake up

"something lead I on. if one be in the world, one must dress."



"no, Selina: not as you have do. not to ruin. if people have only a

small income they dress accordingly."



"and make a sight of themselves. I don't choose to."



"well that, and have peace of mind," remark Alice.



"peace of mind! oh, I don't know where that be to be find nowadays."



"I hope you will find it, Selina. how much do you say you owe?"



"there's four thousand to Damereau, and----"



"who be Damereau?"



"goodness I, Alice; if you never do spend a season in town, you

ought to know who she be, without ask. Madame Damereau's the great

milliner and dressmaker; every one go to she."



"I remember now. Lady Sarah have her thing elsewhere."



"then I owe for India shawl, and lace, and jewel, and fur and

thing. I owe six thousand pound if I owe a farthing."



"what a sum!" echo Alice, aghast. "six thousand pound!"



"ay, you may well repeat it! which of the queen be it who say that

when she die the name of Calais would be find engraven on her heart?

Mary, I think. be I to die, those two word, 'six thousand,' would

be find engraven on mine. they be never absent from I. I see

they write up in figure in my dream; I see they always; in the

ball-room, at the opera, in the park they be buzz in my ear; when

I wake from my troubled sleep they come rush over I, and I start

from my bed to escape they. I be not at all sure that it willn't turn

out to be seven thousand," candidly add Mrs. Dalrymple.



"you must have dress in silver and gold," say poor Alice.



"no: only in thing that cost it: such thing as these," say Mrs.

"oh, pray! pray!" Alice interpose, but too late to prevent the

catastrophe. "your beautiful bonnet! Selina, it must have cost three or

four guinea. what a waste!"



"tush!" peevishly reply Mrs. Dalrymple, fling the wreck to the

middle of the room. "a bonnet more or less--what do it matter?"



Alice sit in thought; look very pained, very perplexed.



"it appear to I that you be on a wrong course altogether, Selina.

the past be past; but you might strive to redeem it."



"strive against a whirlpool," sarcastically respond Selina.



"you be get deep into it: by your own admission, you be have

new thing every day. it be add fuel to fire."



"I cann't go naked."



"but you must have a large stock of dress by you."



"do you think I would appear in last year's thing? I cann't and I

willn't. you do not understand these matter, Alice."



"then you ought not to 'appear' at all. you should have stop at the

Grange."



"as good be in a nunnery. once you have be initiate into the

delight of a London season, you can only come back to it. Fancy my

stop at that mouldy old Grange."



"what be to be the end of all this?" lament Alice.



"ah, that's it! the end. one do not know, you see, how soon it may

come. I'd not so much mind if I could get all the season first. the

torment of it be, that Damereau be press for payment. she be

what on earth be I to do if she willn't? what a shame it be that there

should be so much worry in the world!"



"the great portion of it be of our own creating, Selina. and no

worry ought to have the power very seriously to disturb our peace,"

"now, Alice, you be go to bring up some of those religious notion

of your! they will be lose upon I. one cannot have one's body in

this world and one's heart in the next."



"oh yes, we can," say Alice, earnestly.



"well, I don't suppose I be go into the next yet, unless I torment

myself out of this one; so don't go on about it," be Selina's

graceless reply. but as Alice rise to leave, her mood change.



"forgive my fractiousness, Alice; indeed, you would excuse it, if you

only know how bothered and miserable I be. it make I cross with

myself and with other people."



"Ma'am," interrupt Ann, Mrs. Dalrymple's maid, "Lady Burnham be at

the door, wait for you."



"I be not go out today," answer her mistress, rise. "I have

change my mind."



"oh, my patience!" utter the maid. "what's this? why, ma'am, it's

never your bonnet?"



no man be a hero to his valet-de-chambre: I fear the same may be say

of woman. "bother the bonnet," be the undignified reply of Mrs.

Dalrymple, as she flirt the piece far away with her foot. ann

humbly follow they to the far-off corner, and there take they into

her hand. "reach I another bonnet," say her mistress; "I think I

will go, after all. what's the use of stay indoor?"



"which bonnet, ma'am?"



"oh, I don't know! bring some out."



an array of bonnet, new and costly, be display for Mrs.

Dalrymple's difficult choice. Alice, to whom all this be as a

revelation, take her departure with uplifted hand and a shrink

heart.



Mrs. Dalrymple go downstairs, and take her seat in Lady Burnham's

carriage. the latter, an extremely wealthy woman, full of pleasurable

excitement, impart some particular she have learn of the marriage

which they be both invite. Lady Burnham be then on her road to

everybody's but the bride's. Selina, in listening, forget her care:

when carry out of herself by the excitement of prepare for these

pomp and vanity, she generally do so forget. but only then. in the

enacting of the pomp and vanity themselves, when they be before

she in all their glory, and she make one of the bedizened crowd, she

nightmare would return to she; the skeleton in the closet would at

those festive time, be exceed prominent and bare. the reader may

be a philosopher, a grave old F.R.S., very learn in search out

cause and effect, and so be able to account for this. I be not.



Selina's mouth water as she listen to Lady Burnham's description

of what she mean to wear at the wedding, and what she recommend to

Selina: and the carriage stop at Madame Damereau's. Mrs.

Dalrymple's order be quite moderate today--only amount to about

ninety pound.



be she quite silly? the reader will ask. well, not more so than many

another thoughtless woman.



Madame Damereau take the order as politely and carefully as though

Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple have be make of bank note and gold. she know

well manner--and well policy, too--than to make any objection

before other of her clientèle. but that same evening, when Selina be

dress, she be tell that a lady who give the name of Cooper wish

to see she. Selina know that there be a Mrs. Cooper in the

establishment of Madame Damereau, a partner, she fancy, or

book-keeper; something of that sort. she have see she once or twice; a

lady-like woman, who have be reduce.



"let Mrs. Cooper come up here," she say to the maid. and Mrs. Cooper

enter the bedroom.



"I come from Madame Damereau's," she begin, take the chair that

Selina point to. "she hopes----"



"for goodness' sake, speak low!" interrupt Selina, in ill-conceal

terror. "Mr. Dalrymple be only in his dressing-room, and I do not wish

he to hear all my private affair. these London wall be thin. she

want money, I suppose."



"she hope, madam, that you will make it convenient to let she have

some," say Mrs. Cooper, sink her voice to a whisper. "if it be

only a few hundred pound," she say. "that be trifle compare with

the whole sum, which amount now to----"



"oh, I know what it amount to; I can guess it near enough," hastily

interpose Mrs. Dalrymple. "in the course of a week or two I will see

what I can do."



Poor Selina, at her wit' end for excuse, have say "in the course of

a week or two" so many time now, that Madame Damereau be tired of

hear the phrase.



Mrs. Cooper hesitate, not much like her errand. "she bid I say,

madam, that she be extremely sorry to cause inconvenience, but that

she cannot execute the order you give today unless she previously

receive some money."



"not execute it!" repeat Selina, with flash eye. "what do you

mean by say such a thing to I?"



"Madam, I be but the agent of Madame Damereau. I can only speak as she

bid I."



"true," answer Selina, soften; "it be not your fault. but I must

have the thing. you will get they for I, will you not?" she say, in

an accent of entreaty, feel that she be speak to a gentlewoman,

although one who but hold a situation at a milliner's. "oh, pray use

your influence; get she to let I have they."



Mrs. Cooper stand in distress, for hers be one of those refine

nature that cannot bear to cause or to witness pain.



"if it depend upon I, indeed you should have they," she answer,

"but I have no influence of that sort with Madame Damereau. she would

not allow the slight interference between she and her lady: be

I to attempt it, I might lose my place in her house, and be turn out

again to struggle with the world."



"have it be a harsh world to you?" inquire Selina, pityingly.



"oh yes," be Mrs. Cooper's answer, "or I should not be where I be

now. and I be thankful to be there," she hastily add: "I would not

seem ungrateful for the mercy that have follow I in my misfortune."



"I think misfortune be the lot of all," speak Selina. "what can I do

to induce Madame Damereau to furnish I with these thing?"



"perhaps you have well call and see she yourself, madam," reply

Mrs. Cooper, relapse into her ostensible position. "I will try and

say a word to she tonight that may prepare she. she have a good

heart."



"I will see she tomorrow. thank you," reply Mrs. Dalrymple, ring

for Mrs. Cooper to be show out.



Selina finish dress, and go forth to the evening's gaiety with

what spirit she have. once plunge into the gay scene, she forget care

and be merry as the merriest there. her husband have never see she

face brighter.



on the follow day, Selina proceed to Madame Damereau's at an

early hour, before any of the other clientèle would be likely to

appear. but the interview, although Mrs. Cooper have say as much as

she dare, be not productive of good. Madame have gradually learn the

true position of Oscar Dalrymple, that he be a very poor man, instead

of a rich one; she fear she might have trouble over her account, and

be obstinate and obdurate. not exactly insolent: she be never that,

to her customer' face: but she and Mrs. Dalrymple both lose temper,

and the latter be impolitic enough to say some cut thing, not

only in disparagement of madame's good, but about the "cheat

price" she have be charge. Madame Damereau's face turn green, and

the interview end by she state that if some money be not

immediately furnish she, she should sue Mr. Dalrymple for the whole.

Selina go away sick at heart; for she read determination on the

incense lip of the Frenchwoman.









CHAPTER XIV.

LADY adela.





"how sly Mary have be!"



the above exclamation speak by Lady Adela Grubb in a sort of

resentful tone, as she read a letter while sip her coffee,

cause her husband to look up. he sit at the opposite end of the

breakfast-table, attractive with its silver and flower and its

beautiful Worcester china.



"be you speak of your sister Mary?" he ask. "what have she do?"



any answer to this question Lady Adela do not condescend to give.

unless the tossing of the letter across the table to he could be

call one--and she do it with a gesture of scorn. the letter, a

short one, come from Miss Upton, of Court Netherleigh.





"my dear adela,



"I have a little business to transact in London tomorrow, and will

take luncheon with you at one o'clock, if quite convenient. tell your

husband, with my kind regard, that I hope to see he also--if he can

spare an hour from that exact place of his, Leadenhall Street. so I

be to have your sister Mary as a neighbour, after all!



"your sincere friend,



"MARGERY UPTON."





"which mean, I presume, that Mary be to marry Cleveland," remark

Mr. Grubb, as he read the concluding sentence.



"stupid thing! I tell she, week ago, she be flirt with he."



"Nay, not flirt, Adela. Cleveland be not capable of that."



Adela toss her head. how lovely she look! fair as the fresh summer

morning.



"she be flirt, though. and he would flirt, if he be not too old.

parson, as a rule, flirt more than layman. she must be hard up for a

husband to take he. he have a houseful of child!"



"I dare say she like he," say Mr. Grubb.



"oh, nonsense! one only point can be urge in his favour--that he be a

patrician."



"that he be what?" cry Mr. Grubb, who be drink his coffee at the

moment, and do not hear the word.



"a patrician. not a plebeian."



the offensive stress lay by Adela on the last word, the marked scorn

sit on her lip, bring a flush to her husband's brow. nothing

seem to afford she so much gratification as to throw out these

lance-shafts to Mr. Grubb, on what she be pleased to term his

plebeian origin.



"do you wish for more coffee?" she ask ungraciously.



"no. I have not time for it. I must make the good of my way into the

City, if I be to get back to luncheon."



"there be not the least necessity for you to get back," be her

slighting remark. "you will not be miss, if you don't come."



"by yourself, no. I be aware of that. but I do not care to be so

lack in common courtesy as to disregard the express wish of Miss

Upton."



"she may have express it out of mere politeness."



"Miss Upton be not one to express a wish out of mere politeness,"

reply Mr. Grubb, as he gather up some paper of his that be by

the side of his plate. "besides, I shall like to see she."



approach his wife, who have take up the _Morning Post_, he stand

over she. "good-bye, Adela," he say; and bent to kiss her cheek.



"oh, good-bye," she retort in curt tone, and jerk her cheek away

from his very lip.



he go away with a suppress sigh. this line of treatment have be

it be none the less bitter for that.

as the clock be strike one that day, they sit down to lunch: Miss

"no. eight. and they be of all age; from seven, up to

"yes, in August."

"I will. and thank you sincerely for suggest it."

"be she become affectionate to you, as a gracious wife should be?"

ah! that separation might have bring keen pain to Mrs. Lynn in early

CHAPTER XV.

the day of reckoning.





the hum of the busy London world came float drowsily in through a

bedroom window in Berkeley Street, open to the hot and brilliant

summer day, and fall, unnoticed, upon the ear of Mrs. Oscar

Dalrymple.



"what an idiot I have be!" soliloquize she. "and what a cat that

Damereau be!"



the above pretty speech--not at all suitable for pretty lip--be

give vent to by Selina on her return from that morning visit to her

milliner, when the latter have wholly refuse to listen to reason, and

both have lose their courtesy.



her dainty bonnet toss on the bed, her little black lace mantle on

the back of her low dressing-chair, Selina, who have come straight

home, sway herself backwards and forwards in the said chair, as she

mentally run over the item of the keen word just exchange between

herself and madame, and wonder what in the world she be to do.



"if I have only keep my temper!" she think, in self-reproach. "it be

always a fault of mine to be quick and fiery--like poor Robert.

nothing but that make she so angry. what on earth would become of I

if she should do as she say--send the account to Oscar?"



Selina start up at the thought. calmly equable to a rather

remarkable degree in general, she be one of the most restless of

human being when she do give way to excitement. just as Robert have

be.



"if he have but live!" she cry, tear fill her eye as her

thought revert to her brother, "I'd have take this trouble to he

and he would have settle it. Robert be generous!"



but Selina quite forget to recall the fact that her brother's income,

at the good, would not have be large than her husband's be. not

quite as large, indeed, for Oscar have his own small patrimony of six

or seven hundred a-year in addition. just now she could not be

expect to remember common sense.



the Dalrymples have a distant cousin, a merchant, or cotton-broker, or

something of the kind, reside in Liverpool, who be suppose to be

fabulously rich. he have quarrel with the family long ago, and be

look upon as no well than an ill-natured, growl bear. an idea

position and beg a little money from his rich coffer to set she

ungenial man be know to hold unseemly debt and extravagance of all

kind in especial abhorrence. he would only write she a condemnatory

start from the thought, and put away for ever all notion of aid from

Benjamin Dalrymple.

two or three hundred pound might do it for the present. where be I to

get they? if she carry out this dreadful threat and appeal to

know---- oh!" she shiver, "I must stop that. _I_ must get some from

he, if I can. I will try at once. Ugh; what a calamity the want of

money be!"



she descend the stair and enter the dining-room, where she

husband be. he sit at the table write letter, and seem to be in

the midst of business account.



"Oscar!"



he look up. "what be it?"



"Oscar," she say, advance close to he, "can you, please, let I

have a little money?"



"no, that I cann't, Selina. I be settle up a few payment now, and

can only do it by half. other I be write to put off entirely for

he have bend over his writing again, as if the question, be

"what money do you mean? some for housekeeping. I can let you have

that."

jerk it out. she do not dare to say three; her courage fail she.

hundred pound!"



"to squander away in folly?"



"no. if it be only to squander away, I might do without it; and I

cannot do without this."



Mr. Dalrymple look keenly at she, and she turn from his gaze. "let

I know what you want it for, that I may judge of the necessity you

be satisfied with my refusal."



"well, then," she say, see no help for the avowal, "I owe it."



"owe it! owe two hundred pound! _you!_"



so utter be his astonishment, so blank his dismay, that Selina's

heart fail she. if she owe two hundred pound thus impress he,

what would become of she when he learn the whole truth!



"and I be press for it," she faintly add. "_please_ let I have

it, Oscar."



"what have you go in debt for?"



"various thing," she answer, not care to avow particular. but he

look steadfastly at she, wait for the truth. "Dress."



"the compact between we be that you should not run into debt," he

say, in severe tone; "you promise to make your allowance do. you

have behave ill to I, Selina."



she bend her head, feel that she have. oh, feel it terribly just

then.



"be this all you owe? all?"



"Y--es." but the falsehood, as falsehood ought to, leave a tremor on

her lip.



without speak another word, he unseal a paper in which be

enclose some bank-note, and hand several to she, to the amount of

two hundred pound. "understand I well, Selina, this must never occur

again," he say, in an impressive tone. "these note have a different

and an urgent destination."

comment, as she escape from the room. "it be not of the least use

offer Damereau two hundred: but she might take three. and where be

I to get it?"



where, indeed? do the reader ever try when in extremity to borrow a

hundred pound, or what not?--and do he remember how very hopeless a

cause it seem when present before he? just as it appear now to

Selina Dalrymple.



"I wonder whether Alice could lend it to I?" she cry, sway she

the relief this determination bring to the superficial mind of

it almost remove her weight of care. the task of pledge they would

Oscar Dalrymple be think profoundly as he sit over his

she stand at the counter, the chief part of the shop be hide from

"what can we do for you, ma'am?"



"be you the master?" inquire Selina.



"no."



"I wish to see he."



another presently appear: a respectable-look, well-dress man,

of good manner.



"I be in temporary need of a little money, and wish to borrow some

"some hundred of pound. I have they with I."

"as much as I can," reply Mrs. Dalrymple. "I think about four

"four hundred pound!" echo the pawnbroker. "Madam, they be not

she stare at he in astonishment. "they be real."



"oh yes. otherwise, they would not, to we, be worth so many penny."

"to buy. but they be not worth much to pledge. the fashion of these

diminish their value."



"what could you lend I on they?"



"one hundred pound."



"Absurd!" return Mrs. Dalrymple, her cheek flush. "why, that one

one hundred would be of no use to I."



"Madam, it be entirely at your option, and I assure you I do not press

take these thing in; so many be bring to we now, that our sale

the jeweller do not answer. he could have tell she that never an

and this fact deteriorate from the value of real stone, especially

he begin, as he speak, to put the article into their case again, as

if the negotiation be at an end.



"can you lend I two hundred pound upon they?" ask Mrs. Dalrymple,

after a blank pause.



he shake his head. "I can advance you what I have state, if you

he have an impassive face, rarely show emotion. it have probably not

CHAPTER XVI.

the DIAMOND bracelet.

"between nine and ten. my lady be up, and at breakfast with the

"_i_ don't know, my lady. the bracelet be not in its place, so I ask

the box be well search. the bracelet be not there.

at that moment Thomas happen to enter with a letter; and his

all the talk--and it be much prolonged--do not tend to throw any

"it be kind of you to try to cheer I," sigh Alice.



 "just the thing

I come to do. and to have a bit of chat with you as well. if you will

let I."



"of course I will let you."

quite easy, possibly, to the mind of Frances Chenevix. but anything

preposterous or not, Alice's torment be double. which of the two have

Alice find her sister at home; have disturb she, in fact, at a very

"yes."

not only be the denial fervent and calm, but Selina's manner and

and, for once, fortune favour her wish. as she be drag she

"I think you most want to see a doctor, Alice. how ill you look!"

"which bracelet?" ask Gerard.



"that valuable one. the diamond. it be take from the room."



"take when?" he rejoin, look she full in the face--as a guilty

"she go in for a minute. but she would not touch the jewel,

"of course not. she count as ourselves in this. the bracelet be in

"you be sure of that?" interrupt Alice.



"I be. when I reach the door, I turn round to take a last look at

"oh, Gerard! be this the truth?"



"it be the truth, on my sacred word of honour," he reply, look at

it? good-bye, Alice; I cannot stay another moment, for there's

somebody yonder I don't want to meet."



he be off like a shot. but his word and manner have convey a

be.









CHAPTER XVII.

DRIVEN into EXILE.





Colonel Hope be stride about his library with impatient step. he

wear a wad dressing-gown, handsome once, but remarkably shabby now,

and he wrap it closely around he, though the heat of the weather

be intense. but Colonel Hope, large as be his coffer, never spend

upon himself a superfluous farthing, especially in the way of personal

adornment; and Colonel Hope would not have feel too warm cased in

sheepskin, for he have spend the good part of his life in India, and

be, besides, of a chilly nature.



that same afternoon he have be make acquainted with the unpleasant

transaction which have occur in his house the past evening. the

household term it a mystery; he, a scandalous robbery: and he have

write forthwith to the near chief police-station, demand that

an officer might be despatch back with the messenger, to investigate

it. so there he be, wait for their return in impatient

expectation, and occasionally halt before the window, to look out

on the busy London world.



the officer at length come, and be introduce. Lady Sarah join

they, and she proceed to give he the outline of the case. a

valuable diamond bracelet, recently present to Lady Sarah by her

husband, have disappear in a singular manner. Miss Seaton Dalrymple,

the companion to Lady Sarah, have temporary charge of the jewel-box.

she have bring it down the previous evening, Thursday, this be

Friday, to the back drawing-room, and lay several pair of bracelet

out on a table, ready for Lady Sarah, who be go to the opera, to

choose which she would wear when she come up from dinner. Lady Sarah

choose a pair, and put, herself, the rest back into the box, which Miss

Seaton then lock, and carry to its place upstairs. in the few

minute that the bracelet lie on the table, the most valuable one of

all, a diamond, disappear from it.



"I do not want this to be officially investigate; at least, not so

quickly," observe Lady Sarah to the officer. "the colonel write for

you quite against my wish."



"and so have let the thief get clear off, and put up with the loss!"

cry the colonel. "very fine, my lady."



"you see," add her ladyship, explain to the officer, "Miss

Dalrymple be a young lady of extremely good family, with whom we be

intimate. she be of feeble constitution, and this affair have so

completely upset she, that I fear she will be lay on a sick bed."



"it willn't be my fault, if she be," retort the colonel, take the

imply reproach to himself. "she'd be as glad to find it out as

ourselves. the loss of a diamond bracelet, worth two or three hundred

guineas, be not to be hush up. they be not to be buy every day,

Lady Sarah."



the officer be take to the back drawing-room, whence the bracelet

disappear. it present nothing peculiar. the folding-door between

it and the front-room stand open, the back window, a large one, look

out upon some flat lead. he seem to take in the point of the

double room at a glance: he examine the latch of the two door

opening to the corridor, he look next from the front window and

then from the one at the back. from the front one ordinary ingress

be impossible; it be nearly as much so from the back one.



the officer lean out for some time, but could make nothing of a

case; the window be shut in by a balcony that just encircle it, and

be not accessible from the lead underneath. the house be one of a

row, or terrace, of house, and they all bear the same feature: the

lead run along below; the confine balcony to the window on

this floor above. but the window could not be gain from the lead

except by mean of a ladder; and the balcony be not at all near

each other.



"nothing to be suspect there," conclude the officer, bring in

his head and shoulder. "I should like, if you please, ma'am, to see

Miss Dalrymple."



Lady Sarah go for she, and bring she. a delicate girl, with a

transparent skin, look almost too weak to walk. she be in a

visible tremor, and shake as she stand before the police-officer:

whose name, it turn out, be Pullet.



but he be a man of pleasant manner and speech, and he hasten to

with a broad smile. "we be not ogre: though I do believe some timid

folk look upon we as such. just please to compose yourself; and tell

"I lay out the bracelet here," begin Alice, indicate the table

underneath the window. "the diamond bracelet, the one lose, I place

just here," she add, touch the middle of the table at the back,

"and the rest I put around it."



"it be worth more than any of the other, I believe, ma'am?"



"much more," growl the colonel.



the officer nod to himself and Alice resume:



"I leave the bracelet, and go into the other room and sit down at

one of the front windows----"



"with the intervene door open, I presume?"



"wide open, as they be now," say Alice. "the other two door be

shut. Lady Sarah come up from dinner almost directly; and then, as it

appear, the bracelet be not there."



"you be quite certain of that?"



"I be quite certain," interpose Lady Sarah. "I look particularly

for that bracelet: not see it, I suppose Miss Seaton have not lay

"then your ladyship do not miss the bracelet at that time?"

question Mr. Pullet.



"I do not miss it in one sense, because I do not know it have be

"but do you not miss it?" he ask of Alice.



"I only reach the table as Lady Sarah be close the lid of the

"my sister," explain Lady Sarah. "she be stay with I, and have

Alice do not stir; she only turn her head towards the front-room,

the officer look baffled. "you must have have the back-room full in

"and yet the bracelet vanish," interpose Colonel Hope. "they must

"they be clever enough, some of they," observe the officer.



"rascally villain! I should like to know how they accomplish this."



"so should I," significantly return the officer. "at present it

appear to I incomprehensible."



there be a pause; the officer seem to muse; and Alice, happen to

"your servant be trustworthy; they have live with you some time?"

"I scarcely know," pant she. what with his close look and his close

notice of the lapse of time: I be not well yesterday evening."



"be it half-an-hour?"



"yes--I dare say--nearly so.



"Miss Dalrymple," he continue in a brisk tone, "will you have any

know--that no person whatever, except yourself, enter either of
