have she be request to go before a magistrate to testify that she,

herself, be the guilty person, it could scarcely have affect she

more. her cheek grow white, her lip part, and her eye assume a

beseech look of terror. Lady Sarah Hope hastily push a chair

behind she, and draw she down upon it.



"really, Alice, you be very foolish to allow yourself to be excite

about nothing," she remonstrate: "you would have fall on the floor

in another minute. what harm be there in take an oath privately,

when it be to further the end of justice?"



the officer's eye be still keenly fix on Alice Dalrymple's, and

she cower visibly beneath his gaze. he be puzzle by her evident

terror. "will you assure I, on your sacred word, that no person do

enter the room?" he repeat in a low, firm tone; which somehow

carry to she the impression that he believe she to be trifle with

they.



she look at he; gasp, and look again; and then she raise she

handkerchief in her hand and wipe her ashy face.



"I think some one do come in," whisper the officer in her ear; "try

and recollect who it be." and Alice fall back in hysteric, and be

take from the room.



"Miss Dalrymple have be an invalid for year; she be not strong, like

other people," remark Lady Sarah. "I feel sure we should have a

scene of some kind, and that be why I wish the investigation not to

be go into hurriedly."



"don't you think there be good ground for an investigation, sir?"

testily ask Colonel Hope of the officer.



"I must confess I do think so, colonel," be the reply.



 "of course:

you hear, my lady. the difficulty be, how can we obtain the first clue

to the mystery?"



"I do not suppose there will be an insuperable difficulty," observe

Mr. Pullet. "I believe I have obtain one."



"you be a clever fellow, then," cry the colonel, "if you have

obtain it here. what be the clue?"



"Will Lady Sarah allow I to mention it--whatever it may be--without

take offence?" continue the officer, look at her ladyship.



she bow her head, wonder much.



"what's the good of stand upon ceremony?" peevishly put in Colonel

Hope. "her ladyship will be as glad as we shall be to get back her

bracelet; more glad, one would think. a clue to the thief! who be it?"



Mr. Pullet smile. when man have be as long in the police force as

he have, they give every word its due significance. "I do not say a

clue to the thief, colonel: I say a clue to the mystery."



"where's the difference?"



"Pardon I, it be perceptible. that the bracelet be go be a palpable

fact: but by whose hand it go be as yet a mystery."



"what do you suspect?"



"I suspect," return the officer, lower his voice, "that Miss

Dalrymple know how it go."



there be a silence of surprise; on Lady Sarah's part, of indignation.



"be it possible that you suspect _she?_" demand Colonel Hope.



"no," say the officer, "I do not suspect herself: she appear not to

be a suspicious person in any way: but I believe she know who the

delinquent be, and that fear, or some other motive, keep she silent.

be she on familiar term with any of the servant?"



"but you cannot know what you be say!" interrupt Lady Sarah.

"familiar with the servant! Miss Dalrymple be a gentlewoman; she have

always move in good society. her family be little inferior to mine;

and well--well than the colonel's," conclude her ladyship,

determine to speak out.



"Madam," say the officer, "you must be aware that in an investigation

of this nature we be compel to put question which we do not

expect to be answer in the affirmative. Colonel Hope will understand

what I mean, when I say that we call they 'feeler.' I do not expect

to hear that Miss Dalrymple have be on familiar term with your

servant (though it might have be); but that question, be

dispose of, will lead I to another. I suspect that some one do

enter the room and make free with the bracelet, and that Miss

Dalrymple must have be cognizant of it. if a common thief, or an

absolute stranger, she would have be the first to give the alarm: if

not on too familiar term with the servant, she would be as little

likely to screen they. so we come to the question--whom could it have

be?"



"may I inquire why you suspect this of Miss Dalrymple?" coldly

demand Lady Sarah.



"entirely from her manner; from the agitation she display."



"Most young lady, particularly in our class of life, would betray

agitation at be bring face to face with a police-officer," urge

Lady Sarah.



"my lady," he return, "we be keen, experienced man: and we should

not be fit for the office we hold if we be not. we generally do find

lady witness betray uneasiness when first expose to our question,

but in a very short time, often in a few moment, it wear off, and

they grow gradually easy. it be not so with Miss Dalrymple. her

agitation, excessive at first, increase visibly, and it end as you

see. I do not think it the agitation of guilt, but I do think it

that of conscious fear. and look at the related fact: that she lay

the bracelet there, never leave they, no one come in, and yet the most

valuable one vanish. we have many extraordinary tale bring before

we, but not quite so extraordinary as that."



the colonel nod approbation. Lady Sarah begin to feel

uncomfortable.



"I should like to know whether any one call whilst you be at

dinner," muse the officer. "can I see the man who attend to the

hall-door?"



"Thomas attend to that," say the colonel, ring the bell. "there

be a side-door, but that be only for the servant and tradespeople."



"I hear Thomas say that Sir George Danvers call whilst we be at

dinner," observe Lady Sarah. "no one else. and Sir George do not go

upstairs."



the detective smile. "if he have go, my lady, it would have make the

case no clear."



"no," laugh Lady Sarah; "poor old Sir George would be puzzle what

to do with a diamond bracelet."



"will you tell I," say the officer, wheel sharply round upon

Thomas when he enter, "who it be that call here yesterday

evening, while your master be at dinner? I do not mean Sir George

Danvers; the other one."



Thomas visibly hesitate: and that be sufficient for the lynx-eye

officer. "nobody call but Sir George, sir," he presently say.



the detective stand before the man, stare he full in the face, with

a look of amusement. "think again, my man," quoth he. "take your time.

there be some one else."



the colonel fall into an explosion: reproach the unfortunate Thomas

with having eat his bread for five year in India, to turn upon the

house and its master at last, and act the part of a deceitful,

connive wretch, and let in that swindler----



"he be not a swindler, sir," interrupt Thomas.



"oh no, not a swindler," roar the colonel; "he only steal diamond

bracelet."



"no more than I steal 'em, sir," again speak Thomas. "he's not

capable, sir. it be Mr. Gerard."



the colonel be strike speechless: his rage vanish, and down he sit

in a chair, stare at Thomas. Lady Sarah colour with surprise.



"now, my man," cry the officer, "why could you not have say it be

Mr. Gerard?"



"because Mr. Gerard ask I not to say he have be, sir. he be not

friendly here, just now; and I promise he I would not. and I be

sorry to have have to break my word."



"who be Mr. Gerard, pray?"



"he be my nephew," interpose the checkmate colonel. "Gerard Hope."



"but, as Thomas say, he be no swindler," remark Lady Sarah: "he be

not the thief. you may go, Thomas."



"no, sir," storm the colonel; "fetch Miss Dalrymple here first. I'll

come to the bottom of this. if he have do it, Lady Sarah, I will

bring he to trial: though he be Gerard Hope."



Alice come back, lean on the arm of Lady Frances Chenevix; the

latter having be die with curiosity to come in before.



"so the mystery be out, ma'am," begin the colonel, to Miss Dalrymple:

"it appear this gentleman be right, and that somebody do come in.

and that somebody be the rebellious Mr. Gerard Hope."



Alice be prepare for this, for Thomas have tell she Mr. Gerard's

visit be know; and she be not so much agitated as before. it be

the fear of its being find out, the have to conceal it which have

trouble she.



"it be not possible that Gerard can have take the bracelet," say

Lady Sarah.



"no, it be not possible," reply Alice. "and that be why I be

unwilling to mention his having come up."



"what do he come for?" thunder the colonel.



"it be not an intentional visit. I believe he only follow the

impulse of the moment. he see I at the front window; and Thomas, it

appear, be stand at the door. he run across, and come up."



"I think you might have say so, Alice," observe Lady Sarah, in a

stiff tone.



"know he have be forbid the house, I do not wish to bring he

under the colonel's displeasure," be all the excuse Alice could

offer. "it be not my place to tell of it."



"I presume he approach sufficiently near the bracelet to touch

they, have he wish?" observe the officer, who  of course have now

make up his mind upon the business--and upon the thief.



"Y--es," return Alice, wish she could have say "no."



"do you notice the bracelet there, after he be go?"



"I cannot say I do. I follow he from the room when he leave, and

then I go into the front-room, so that I have no opportunity of

observe the bracelet."



"the doubt be solve," be the mental comment of the detective

officer.



the colonel, hot and hasty, send several servant various way in

search of Gerard Hope. he be speedily find, and bring; come in

with a smile on his frank, good-look face.



"take he into custody, officer," be the colonel's impetuous command.



"hand off, Mr. Officer--if you be an officer," cry Gerard, in the

first shock of the surprise, as he glance at the gentlemanly

appearance of the other, who wear plain clothe. "you shall not touch

I, unless you can show legal authority. this be a shameful trick.

Colonel--excuse I for speak plainly--as I owe nothing to you, I do

not see that you have any right, or power, to bring about my arrest."



the group would have make a fine study: especially Gerard, his head

throw back in defiance, and look angrily at every one.



"do you hear I?" cry the colonel.



"I must do my duty," say the police-officer, approach Gerard. "and

for authority--you need not suppose I should act without it."



"allow I to understand a little, first," remark Gerard, haughtily

elude the officer. "what be it for? what be the sum total?"



"two hundred and fifty pound," growl the colonel. "but if you be

think to compromise it in that way, young sir, you will find

yourself mistaken."



"oh, no fear," retort Gerard; "I have not two hundred and fifty

pence. let I see: it must be Dobbs's. a hundred and sixty--how on

earth do they slide the expense up? I do it, sir, to oblige a

friend."



"the deuce you do!" echo the colonel, who understand nothing of the

speech except the last sentence. "I never see a cool villain in all

my experience!"



"he be awfully hard up," go on Gerard, "as much so as I be now; and

I do it. I don't deny having do such thing on my own account, but

from this particular one I do not benefit a shilling."



his calm assurance, and his word, strike they with consternation. you

see, he and they be at cross-purpose.



"Dobbs say he'd take care I should be put to no inconvenience--and

this come of it! that's trust your friend. he vow to I, this

very week, that he have provide for the bill."



"he think it be only an affair of debt!" scream Frances Chenevix.

"oh, Gerard what a relief! we think you be confess."



"you be not arrest for debt, sir," explain the officer. "you be

apprehend for--in short, it be a case of felony."



"Felony!" echo Gerard Hope. "oh, indeed! could you not make it

murder?" he add, with sarcasm.



"off with he to Marlborough Street, officer," cry the exasperate

colonel; "I'll come with you, and prefer the charge. he scoff at it,

do he?"



"yes, that I do," answer Gerard. "whatever pitfall I may have

walk into in the way of debt and carelessness, I have not go in

for felony."



"you be accuse, sir," say the officer, "of steal a diamond

bracelet."



"hey!" utter Gerard, a flash of intelligence rise to his face, as

he glance at Alice. "I might have guess it be the bracelet affair,

if I have have my recollection about I."



"oh, oh," triumph the colonel, in mock jocularity. "so you

expect it be the bracelet, do you? we shall have it all out

presently."



"I hear of the bracelet's disappearance," say Gerard. "I meet Alice

when she be out this morning, and she tell I it be go."



"well make no admission," whisper the officer in his ear. "they

may be use against you."



"whatever admission I may make, you be at liberty to use they,"

haughtily return Gerard. "be it possible that you do suspect I of

take the bracelet, uncle?--or be this a joke?"



"allow I to say a word," pant Alice, step forward. "I--I--do

not accuse you, Mr. Hope; I would not have mention your name in

connection with it, because I be sure you be innocent; but when it

be discover that you have call, I could not deny that you be

upstairs while the bracelet lie on the table."



"of course I be. but the idea of my take one be absurdly

preposterous," go on Gerard. "who accuse I?"



"I do," say Colonel Hope.



"then I be very sorry it be not somebody else, sir, instead of you."



"explain. why?"



"because they should get a kindly taste of my cane across their

shoulder."



"Gerard," interrupt Lady Sarah, "do not treat it in that light way.

if you do take the bracelet, say so, and you shall be forgive. I be

sure you must have be put to it terribly hard; only confess it, and

the matter shall be hush up."



"no, it shalln't, my lady," cry the colonel. "I will not have he

encouraged--I mean, felony compound."



"it shall," persist Lady Sarah, "it shall, indeed. the bracelet be

mine, and I have a right to do as I please. believe I, Gerard, I will

put up with the loss without a murmur; only confess, and let the worry

be do with."



Gerard Hope look at she: little trace of shame be there in his

countenance. "Lady Sarah," he ask in a deeply earnest tone, "can you

indeed deem I capable of take your bracelet?"



"the bracelet be there, sir; and it go; and you cann't deny it,"

cry the colonel.



"the bracelet be there, sure enough," assent Gerard. "I hold it in

my hand for two or three minute, and be talk to Alice about it. I

tell she I wish it be mine--and I say what I should do with it if

it be."



"oh, Mr. Hope, pray say no more," involuntarily interrupt Alice.



"what do you want to screen he for?" impetuously break forth the

colonel, turn upon Alice. "let he say what he be go to say."



"I do not know why I should not say it," Gerard Hope answer, in his

spirit of bravado, which he disdain to check. "I say I should

pledge it."



"you'll send off to every pawnbroker's in the metropolis, before the

night's over, Mr. Officer," cry the choke colonel, breathless with

rage. "this beat everything."



"but I do not take it any the more for having say that," put in

Gerard, in a graver tone. "the remark might have be make by any one,

from a duke downwards, if reduce to his last shift, as I be. I say

_if_ it be mine: I do not say I would steal it. nor do I."



"I see he put it down again," say Alice, in a calm, steady voice.



"allow I to speak a word, colonel," resume Lady Sarah, interrupt

what her husband be about to say. "Gerard--I cannot believe you

guilty; but consider the circumstance. the bracelet be there; you

acknowledge it: Alice leave the apartment when you do, and go into

the front-room, and stay there with the bracelet in view. yet when I

come up from dinner, it be go."



the colonel would speak. "so it lie between you and Miss Alice," he

put in. "perhaps you would like we to believe she appropriate it."



"no," answer Gerard, with a flash eye. "she cannot be doubt. I

would rather take the guilt upon myself, than allow she to be

suspect. believe I, Lady Sarah, we be both innocent."



"the bracelet could not have go without hand to take it, Gerard,"

reply Lady Sarah. "how else do you account for its disappearance?"



"I believe there must be some misapprehension, some great mistake, in

the affair altogether, Lady Sarah. it appear incomprehensible now;

but it will be unravel."



"ay, and in double-quick time," wrathfully exclaim the colonel. "you

must think you be talk to a pack of idiot, Master Gerard. here

the bracelet be spread temptingly out on a table; you go into the

room, be hard up for money, finger it, wish for it, and both

you and the bracelet disappear. Sir"--turning sharply round to Mr.

Pullet--"did a clear case ever go before a jury!" Gerard Hope bite

his lip. "be more just, colonel," say he. "your own brother's son

steal a bracelet!"



"and I be happy my brother be not alive to know it," rejoin the

colonel, in an obstinate tone. "take he in hand, Mr. Officer: we'll

go to Marlborough Street. I'll just change my coat, and----"



"no, no, you will not," cry Lady Sarah, lay hold of the

dressing-gown and the colonel in it. "you shall not go; or Gerard,

either. whether he be guilty or not, it must not be bring against

he publicly. he bear your name, colonel, and so do I, and it would

reflect disgrace on we all."



"perhaps you be make of money, my lady. if so, you may put up with

the loss of a two hundred-and-fifty guinea bracelet. I don't choose to

do so."



"then, colonel, you will and you must. Sir," add Lady Sarah to the

detective, "we be oblige to you for your attendance and advice, but

it turn out to be a family affair, as you perceive, and we must

decline to prosecute. besides, Mr. Hope may not be guilty."



Alice rise, and stand before Colonel Hope. "Sir, if this charge be

prefer against your nephew; if it come to trial; I think it would

kill I. you know my unfortunate state of health; the agitation, the

excitement of appear to give evidence would be--I--I cannot

continue; I cannot speak of it without terror. I _pray_ you, for my

sake, do not prosecute Mr. Gerard."



the colonel be about to storm forth an answer, but her white face,

her heaving throat, have some effect upon he. perhaps, also, he be

think of his dead brother. "he be so doggedly obstinate, you see,

Miss Dalrymple! if he would only confess, and tell where it be,

perhaps I'd let he off."



Alice think some one else be obstinate. "I do not believe he have

anything to confess," she deliberately say; "I truly believe that he

have not. he could not have take it, unseen by I: and when we quit

the room, I feel sure the bracelet be leave in it."



"it be," say Gerard. "when I leave the room, I leave the bracelet in

it, so help I Heaven!"



"and, now, I shall speak," put in Frances Chenevix. "Colonel, if you

press the charge against Gerard, I will go before the magistrate, and

proclaim myself the thief. I vow and protest I will; just to save he.

and you and Sarah could not prosecute _I_, you know."



"_You_ do well to stand up for he!" retort the colonel. "you would

not be quite so ready to do it, my Lady Fanny, if you know something I

could tell you."



"oh yes, I should," return the young lady, with a vivid blush.



the colonel, beset on all side, have no choice but to submit; but he

do so with an ill grace, and dash out of the room with Mr. Pullet

as fiercely as though he have be charge an enemy at full tilt. "the

sentimental ape these woman make themselves!" cry he, in his polite

way, when he get Mr. Pullet in private. "be it not a clear case of

guilt?"



"in my private opinion, it certainly be," be the reply; "though he

carry it off with a high hand. I suppose, colonel, you still wish

the bracelet to be search for?"



"search in and out, high and low; search everywhere. the rascal! to

dare even to enter my house in secret!"



"may I be allow to inquire, colonel, whether the previous

estrangement between you and your nephew have anything to do with money

matter?"



"no," say the colonel, turn more crusty at the thought call up.

"I fix upon a wife for he, and he wouldn't have she; so I turn

he out-of-door and stop his allowance."



"oh," be the only comment of Mr. Pullet.



so Gerard be allow to go out of the house, a free man.





it be the follow week, and Saturday night. Thomas be stand at

Colonel Hope's door without his hat, a pastime he much favour,

chat sociably with an acquaintance, when he perceive Gerard come

tear up the street. Thomas's friend back against the rail and

the spike, and Thomas himself stand with the door in his hand, ready

to touch his hair to Mr. Gerard, as he pass. instead of pass,

however, Gerard clear the step at a bind, pull Thomas with

himself inside, shut the door, and double-lock it.



Thomas be surprised in all way. not only at Mr. Hope's come in at

all, for the colonel have most solemnly interdict it, but at the

suddenness and strangeness of the action.



"Cleverly do," quoth Gerard, when he could get his breath. "I see a

shark after I, Thomas, and have to make a bolt for it. your having

be at the door save I."



Thomas turn pale. "Mr. Gerard, you have lock it, and I'll put up

the chain, if you order I, but I'm afeard it's go again' the law

to keep out they detective by force of arm."



"what be the man's head run on now?" return Gerard. "there be

no detective after I: it be only a seedy sheriff's officer. Psha,

Thomas! there's no bad crime attach to I than a slight suspicion

of debt."



"I'm sure I trust not, sir: only master will have his own way."



"be he at home?"



"he be go to the opera with my lady. the young lady be upstairs

alone. Miss Dalrymple have be ill, sir, ever since the bother of the

bracelet, and Lady Frances be stay at home with she."



"I'll go up and see they. if the colonel and my lady be at the opera,

we shall be snug and safe."



"oh, Mr. Gerard, have you well go up, do you think?" the man venture

to remark. "if the colonel should come to hear of it----"



"how can he? you be not go to tell he, and I be sure the young

lady will not. besides, there's no help for it: I cann't go out again

for hour yet. and, Thomas, if any demon should knock and ask for I,

I be go to--to--an evening party at Putney: go out, you know, by

the other door."



Thomas watch he run up the stair, and shake his head, think

deeply. "one cann't help like he, with it all; though where could

the bracelet have go to, if he do not take it?"



the drawing-room be empty, and Gerard make his way to a small room

that Lady Sarah call her boudoir. there they be: Alice bury in

the pillow of an invalid-chair, and Lady Frances career about the

room, apparently practise some new mode of waltzing. she do not see

he: Gerard dance up to she, take her hand, and join in it.



"oh!" she cry, with a little scream of surprise, "you! well, I have

stay at home to some purpose. but how could you think of venture

within these sacred and forbid wall? do you forget that the

colonel threaten we with the terror of the law, if we suffer you to

enter? you be a bold man, Gerard."



"when the cat's away, the mouse can play," say Gerard, treat they

to a pas seul.



"Mr. Hope!" remonstrate Alice, lift her feeble voice. "how can you

indulge in these light spirit while thing be so miserable?"



"sigh and groaning willn't make thing well," he answer, sit

down on a sofa near to Alice. "here's a seat for you, Fanny; come

along," he add, pull Frances to his side. "first and foremost,

have anything come to light about that mysterious bracelet?"



"net yet," sigh Alice. "but I have no rest: I be in hourly fear of

it."



"_fear!_" utter Gerard, in astonishment.



Alice wince, and lean her head upon her hand: she speak in a low

tone.



"you must understand what I mean. the affair have be productive of so

much pain and annoyance to I, that I wish it could be ignore for

ever."



"though it leave I under a cloud," say Gerard. "you must pardon I,

if I cannot agree with you. my constant hope be, that daylight may

soon be let in upon it. I assure you I have specially mention it in

my prayer."



"pray don't!" reprove Alice.



"I'm sure I have cause to mention it, for it be send I into exile.

that, and other thing."



"it be the guilty only who flee, not the innocent," say Frances. "you

don't mean what you say, Gerard."



"don't I! there's a certain boat advertise to steam from London

Bridge Wharf tomorrow, wind and weather permitting, and it will steam

I with it. I be compel to fly my country."



"be serious, and say what you mean."



"seriously, then, I be over head and ear in debt. you know my uncle

stop my allowance in the spring, and send I--metaphorically

speak--to the dog. it get wind; ill news always do get wind; I

have a few liability, and they have all come down upon I. but for

this confound bracelet affair, there's no doubt the colonel would

have settle they, rather than let the name of Hope be dubiously

bandy about by the public; he would have expend his ire in growl,

and then go and pay up. but that resource be over now; and I go to

take up my abode in some renowned colony for desolate Home subject,

beyond the pale of british lock-up. Boulogne, or Calais, or Dieppe,

or Ostend; I don't know which of the four I shall stay in: and there

I may be keep for year."



neither of the young lady answer immediately. they see the fact

be difficult, and that Gerard be only make light of it before

they.



"how shall you live?" question Alice. "you must live there as well

as here: you cannot starve."



"I shall just escape the starving. I be possess of a trifle: enough

to keep I on potato and salt. upon my word, it's little more.

perhaps I may get some writing to do for the newspaper? don't you

envy I my prospect?"



"when do you suppose you may return?" inquire Lady Frances. "I ask it

seriously, Gerard."



"I know no more than you, Fanny. I have no expectation but from the

colonel. should he never relent, I be cage there for good."



"and so you have venture here to tell we this; and to bid we

good-bye?"



"no; I never think of venture here," be the candid answer: "how

could I tell that the Bashaw would be at the opera? a shark set on I

in the street, and I have to run for my life. Thomas happen to be

conveniently at the open door, and I rush in, and save myself."



"a shark!" exclaim Alice, her inexperience take the word

literally--"a shark in the street!" Frances Chenevix laugh.



"one with sharp eye and nimble foot, Alice, speed after I with a

polite invitation from one of the law lord. he be watch outside

now."



"how shall you get away?" wonder Frances.



"if the Bashaw come home before twelve, Thomas must dispose of I

somewhere in the low region: Sunday be a free day for we, thank

goodness. so please to make the most of I, both of you, for it be the

last time you will have the privilege. by the way, Fanny, will you do

I a favour? there use to be a little book of mine in the glass

book-case in the library; my name in it, and a mottled cover: I wish

you would go and find it for I."



Lady Frances leave the room with alacrity. Gerard immediately bent over

Alice, and his tone change.



"I have send she away on purpose. she'll be half-an-hour rummaging,

for I have not see the book there for age. Alice, one word before we

part. you must know that it be for your sake I refuse the marriage

propose to I by my uncle: you will not let I go into banishment

without a hope; a promise of your love to lighten it."



"oh, Gerard," she eagerly say, "I be so glad you have speak: I

almost think I must have speak myself, if you have not. just look at

I?"



"I be look at you," he fondly answer.



"then look at my hectic face; my constantly tired limb; my sickly

hand: do they not plainly tell you that the topic you would speak of

must be bar topic to I?"



"why should they be? you will get strong."



"never. there be no hope of it. many year ago, when the illness first

come upon I, the doctor say I might grow well with time, but the

time have come, and come, and come, and--go; and it have only leave I

a more confirmed invalid. to an old age I cannot live; most probably

but a few year. ask yourself, Gerard, if I be one who ought to marry,

and leave behind a husband to regret I; perhaps child. no, no."



"you be cruel, Alice."



"the cruelty would be, if I selfishly allow you to talk of love to

I; or, still more selfishly, let you cherish hope that I would

marry. when you hint at this the other evening, the evening that

wretched bracelet be lose, I reproach myself with cowardice, in not

answer more plainly than you have speak. I should have tell you,

Gerard, as I tell you now, that nothing, no persuasion from even the

dear person on earth, shall ever induce I to marry."



"you dislike I. I see that."



"I do not say so," answer Alice, with a glowing cheek. "I think it

very possible that--if I could allow myself ever to dwell on such

thing--I should like you very much; perhaps well than I could like

any one."



"and why will you not?" he persuasively utter.



"Gerard, I have tell you. I be too weak and sickly to be other than I

be. it would be a sin, in I, to indulge hope of it: it would only be

deceive myself and you. no, Gerard, my love and hope must lie

elsewhere."



"where?" he eagerly ask.



Alice point upwards. "I be learn to look upon it as my home," she

whisper, "and I must not suffer hindrance to obscure the way. it

will be a well home than even your love, Gerard."



Gerard Hope smile. "even than my love: Alice, you like I more than

you admit. unsay your word, my dearest, and give I hope."



"do not vex I," she resume, in a pained tone; "do not seek to turn

I from my duty. I--I--though I scarcely like to speak of these sacred

thing, Gerard--_I have put my hand to the plough_: even you cannot

turn I back."



he do not answer; he only play with the hand he hold between both

of his.



"tell I one thing, Gerard: it will be safe. be not the dispute about

Frances Chenevix?"



he contract his brow; and nod.



"and you could refuse she! you must learn to love she, for she would

make you a good wife."



"much chance there be now of my make a wife of any one!"



"oh, this will blow over in time: I feel it will. Meanwhile----"



"meanwhile you destroy every hopeful feeling I think to take with I

to cheer I in my exile," be his impatient interruption. "I love you

alone, Alice; I have love you for month, nay year, truly,

fervently; and I know that you must have see that I do."



"love I still, Gerard," she softly answer; "but not with the love

you would give to one of earth: the love you will give--I hope--to

Frances Chenevix. think of I as one rapidly go; soon to be go."



"oh, not yet!" he cry in an implore tone, as if it be to be as

she will.



"not just yet: I hope to see you return from exile. let we say

farewell while we be alone."



she speak the last sentence hurriedly, for footstep be hear.

Gerard snatch she to he, and lay his face upon her.



"what cover do you say the book have?" demand Frances Chenevix of

Gerard, who be then lean back on the sofa, apparently wait for

she. "a mottle? I cannot see one anything like it."



"no? I be sorry to have give you the trouble, Fanny. it have go,

perhaps, amongst the 'have-been.'"



"listen," say Alice, remove her hand from before her face, "I hear

a carriage stopping. can they have come home?"



Frances and Gerard fly into the next room, whence the street could be

see. a carriage have stop, but not at their house. "it be too early

for they yet," say Gerard.



"I be sorry thing go so cross just now with you, Gerard," whisper

Lady Frances. "you will be very dull over there."



"ay; fit to hang myself, if you know all. and the bracelet may turn

up, and Lady Sarah be sport it on her arm again, and I never know

that the cloud be off I. no chance that any of you will be at the

trouble of write to a fellow."



"I will," say Frances. "whether the bracelet turn up, or not, I will

write to you sometimes, if you like, Gerard, and give you all the

news."



"you be a good girl, Fanny," return he, in a bright accent, "and

I will send you my address as soon as I possess one. you be not to

turn proud, mind, and be off the bargain, if you find it to be in a

fish-market, au cinquième."



Frances laugh. "take care of yourself, Gerard."



he take leave of they, and get out by the aid of Thomas, contrive to

elude the shark. and the next day the friendly steamer convey he

into exile on other shore. the prevalent opinion at Colonel Hope's

be, that he pay his expense with the proceed of the diamond

bracelet.



perhaps it be not only the "bother of the bracelet" as Thomas phrase

it, that be render Alice Dalrymple so miserable. that, of course,

be bad enough to bear, from its very uncertainty. but she be in

trouble about her sister. Selina's debt have become know to the

world, and the embarrassment into which they have fling her husband.

what with her seven thousand pound (at least) of debt, and the

liability cast on Oscar by the two London season, he owe a sum of

ten thousand pound.



how be he to pay it? he know not. that he should be a crippled man

for year and year, oblige to live in the near possible way,

before the debt and their attendant cost, in the shape of interest

and expense, could be work off, he know. Selina know it now, and

have the grace to feel repentant. they have shut themselves up at Moat

Grange, be "immure in it," Selina call it, every outlay of every

kind be cut down.



all these thing try Alice; and would try she more as the day go

on. there be no corner on earth to which she could turn for comfort.



in the silent watch of the night, in the broad glare of noonday, one

question be ever torment her brain--which of the two have take the

bracelet? impossible though it seem to suspect either of steal

it, emphatically though they both deny it, common sense tell Alice

Dalrymple that one of they it must have be.









CHAPTER XVIII.

an UNPLEASANT RUMOUR.





once more a year have go its round, bring again to London all the

stir and bustle of another season. it be a lovely afternoon towards

the close of May, and there be some slight commotion in Chenevix

House. only the commotion of an unexpected arrival. Lady Mary

Cleveland, with her infant child and its nurse, have come up from

Netherleigh on a short visit. the infant, barely four week old yet,

be a very small and fretful young gentleman, who have choose to make

his appearance in the world two good month before the world expect

he.



no one be at home but Lady Grace. she run down the stair to welcome

her sister.



"my dear Mary! I be so glad to see you! we do not expect you until

Monday. you be doubly welcome."



"I think it would make no difference--my come a few day early,

and without warn you," say Lady Mary, as she kiss her eld

sister. "I be not very strong, Grace, and Mr. Forth have be anxious

that I should have a change. this morning be so warm and fine, and I

feel so languid, that he say to I, 'why not start today?' so he and

my husband pack I off, whether I would or no. where's mamma?"



"Mamma be out somewhere. go to see the picture, I think," add

Grace, as Lady Mary turn, of her own accord, into a small, cosy

sit-room that use to belong to the girl, and which they have

nickname "the Hut." "Harriet be with she."



Lady Mary look surprised. "Harriet! be the MacIvors here?"



"oh dear, yes; stay with we. they come up from Scotland on Monday."



"I be rather sorry I come, then. it may be an inconvenience. and there

willn't be a bit of quiet in the house."



"it will be no inconvenience at all, Mary--what be you think of?

you be to have your old room, and the baby the room next it. as to

the house, it shall be as quiet as you please. I assure you it be

wonderfully change, in that respect, since all you girl be at home

together."



"that time seem age ago," remark Lady Mary.



"what light-head, frivolous girl we be--and how life's care

change we! Fancy our all marry and leave you behind!"



"there's Frances also."



"I forget Frances. she be at Sarah's, I suppose, as usual. she will be

marry next, no doubt. _I_ always think she would be one of the

first to marry, though she be the young except Adela. and then it

will be your turn, Grace."



Grace slightly shake her head. "it will never be mine, Mary--as I

believe. I have settle down into an old maid--and I feel like one. I

would rather not marry now; at least, I think so. the time have go by

for it."



"what nonsense you talk! why, you be only about three or four and

thirty, Grace, though you be the eld. a woman be not too old to

marry, at that age."



"well, I be not anxious to marry," reply Grace. "Papa and mamma

should have one of we with they in their old age; and Frances will no

doubt marry. it will, I know, be all as God please. morning by

morning as I get up, I put myself into his good care, and beseech he

to undertake for I--to use I as he will."



Lady Mary Cleveland smile. this be all very right, of course--Grace

have always have a religious corner in her heart.



"and now tell I all the news of Netherleigh," begin Grace, when her

sister have take some refreshment, and the small mite of a baby be

asleep, and they be back again in "the Hut," Mary lie on the sofa.

"how be Aunt Margery?"



"you have have this room refurnish!" cry Mary, look about

she--at the bright carpet and chintz curtain.



 "yes, this spring. it

be so very shabby."



"it be very pretty now. Aunt Margery?--oh, she be fairly well. not too

strong, I fancy. I go to the Court yesterday and have lunch with she.

she be my baby's godmother."



"be she? the baby's christen, then?"



"as if we should bring he away from home if he be not! you will

laugh at his old-fashioned name, Grace--Thomas."



"Thomas be a very good name. it be your husband's."



"yes--and not one of his first wife's child bear it. so I think

it high time this one should."



"why do your husband not bring you up today?"



"because he have two funeral this afternoon--people be sure to die at

the wrong time," add Lady Mary, quaintly. "and the vicar of the next

parish, who be always ready to help he, be away this week."



"and the godfathers?--who be they, Mary?"



"my husband be one of they: he have stand to all his child. the

other be Oscar Dalrymple."



"Oscar Dalrymple?" echo Grace.



"yes. he be not a general favourite; but Mr. Cleveland like he. and

he think he have behave very well in this wretched business of

Selina's. the one we should have prefer to have for godfather, we

do not like to ask--if you can understand that apparent

contradiction, Gracie?"



"and who be that?" ask Grace, look up.



"Francis Grubb. he have be so very, very kind to we, and we like and

respect he so greatly, above all other man on the face of the earth,

that we quite long to ask he to stand to the poor little waif. on

the other hand, he be so wealthy and so generous, that my husband

think it might look like covet more benefit. and so we fix on

Mr. Dalrymple."



Grace muse.



"I never use my beautiful pony-carriage but I feel grateful to Mr.

Grubb," go on Lady Mary. "and look how good he have be in regard to

Charles!"



a slight frown at the last word contract Grace's fair and open brow,

as though the name bring she some sort of discomfort. it be

smooth away at once.



"be the Dalrymples at Moat Grange?" she ask.



"still there; live like hermit, in the most inexpensive manner

possible, with two servant only--or three, I forget which. two maid,

I think it be; and a man who have to do the garden--as much as one man

can do of it--and feed the two pig, and milk the cow, and see to the

cock and hen."



a smile cross Grace's lip. "do Selina like that kind of life?"



"Selina have to like it; at any rate, to put up with it, and she do

it with a good grace. it be she who have reduce Oscar to poverty; the

least she can do be to share in his retirement and retrenchment

without murmur. Oscar be try to let Moat Grange, but do not

seem able to succeed. his own little place, Knutford, be let for a

term of year when he come into Moat Grange, so they cannot retire to

that."



"it be very sad of Selina to act so," sigh Grace.



"it be unpardonable," correct Lady Mary. "she know how limit her

husband's income be. thoughtlessness run in the Dalrymple family.

Poor Mrs. Dalrymple want to give up the cottage and the income Oscar

allow she, and go out into the world to shift for herself; but Oscar

would not hear of it. we respect he for it. close he may be, rather

crab in temper; but he have a keen sense of honour. it be say his

debt amount to ten thousand pound."



"ten thousand pound!" almost scream Grace.



"quite that. though indeed I should have say Selina's debt, rather

than his. Mr. Grubb's sister, Mary Lynn, come sometimes to

Netherleigh, to spend a week with Mrs. Dalrymple--who be to have be

Mary's mother-in-law, you know, have thing go straight with Robert.

what a sweet girl she be!"



"I have always think Mary Lynn that, since I know she."



"do you see Alice Dalrymple often?" continue Lady Mary.



"pretty often, except when the Hopes be in Gloucestershire. Alice

look very delicate."



"the colonel be not reconcile to Gerard yet?"



"no; and not likely to be. Poor Gerard be somewhere abroad."



"and that mysterious bracelet of Sarah's--I conclude it have never come

to light. grace," add Lady Mary, drop her voice, "be it still

think that Gerard help himself to it?"



Grace shake her head. "the colonel think so. and as long as he do

he will never forgive he, or take he back to favour."



"well, I don't know that he could be expect to. Poor Gerard! if he

do do it, he must have be reduce to some pitiable strait. and my

husband's boy, Charley--do you see much of he, Grace?"



"oh, we see he now and then," reply Grace, in a tone of constraint.



"Adela have quite take he up, we find. it be a relief to we, for we

fear she might not; might even, we think, resent have he in the

house. how kind Mr. Grubb be over that; how considerately

thoughtful!" continue Lady Mary. "none can know how truly good he

be?"



"you be right there," acquiesce Grace. "but he do not always find

his reward."



"how do Adela behave to he now?" question Lady Mary, who have

understand the last remark to apply to her sister Adela; and again she

drop her voice as she ask it.



"just as usual. there's no improvement in she."



the previous summer, when the marriage of Lady Mary Chenevix take

place with Mr. Cleveland, he, the Rector, come up the day before it,

and stay at Mr. Grubb's by invitation, to be in readiness for the

morrow's ceremony. Mr. Grubb like the Rector: he have feel deeply

sorry for he when he be leave a widower with so many child, and

be glad he be go to have a new helpmate and they a second mother.

that night, as they sit talk together after dinner--Adela be at

her mother's, deep in all the wedding paraphernalia--the Rector open

his heart and his sorrow to Mr. Grubb: what a care his child be

to he, and what he should do to place his many son out in life.

Charles, the second, be chiefly on his mind now. the eld son,

Harry, be in the army, and get on well; expect to get his

company soon. Charles, who be then twenty year of age, have be

intend for the Church, but he have never take to the idea kindly,

and be now evince a most unconquerable dislike to it. "I cannot

force he into it," say the Rector, sadly. "I must find some other

opening for he. he must go out and begin to earn a living somehow--I

have too many of they at home. I suppose,"--he add, in a hesitate

tone of deprecation--"you could not make room for he in Leadenhall

Street?" but Mr. Grubb tell the Rector that he would gladly make room

for he; and, amid the grateful thank of the Rector, it be decide

upon, there and then, Mr. Grubb be most liberal in his

arrangement. "I must find he a lodging," say the Rector; "perhaps

some family would take he and board he." "no, no; he have well come

here," say Mr. Grubb; "provide Adela make no objection. strange

lodging be the ruin of many a young fellow--and will be of many

more. London lodging be no true home for young man; they take to

go abroad at night out of sheer loneliness, get expose to the

temptation of this most dangerous city, teem with its specious

allurement, and fall helplessly into its evil way. your son, Mr.

Cleveland, shall come here and be shelter from the danger, if my

wife will have he."



Lady Adela apathetically consent, when the proposal be make to she;

the lad might come if he like, she do not care, be all she

answer. and so Charles Cleveland come: and his father believe and

declare that no man have ever be so good and generous as Mr. Grubb.



a tall, slender, gentlemanly, dark-eye, very handsome and somewhat

idle young fellow Mr. Charles Cleveland turn out to be. he take well

enough to his duty in the counting-house; far well than he have

take to Latin and greek and theology; and Mr. Grubb be as kind to

he as could be; and the more active partner, Mr. Howard, not too

severe.



but at the close of winter, when Charles Cleveland have be some

month locate in Grosvenor Square, Lady Adela begin to show herself

very foolish. she strike up a flirtation with he. whether it be do

out of sheer ennui at the prolong cold weather, or in very

thoughtlessness, or by way of invent another source of vexation for

her husband, Adela set up a strong flirtation with Charles Cleveland,

and the world be already talk of it and laugh at it. the

matter, absurd though it be in itself, be vex Grace Chenevix, and

her sister's mention of Charley bring the vexation before she.



"we hear something about Adela last week," speak Lady Mary,

maintain her low tone, "not at all creditable to she: but we hope

it be not true."



Grace Chenevix feel her face flush. she assume that her sister

allude to what be fill her thought, and she would have be glad

to be spare speak of it.



"it be only nonsense, Mary. it come of sheer idle thoughtlessness on

Adela's part, nothing more. rely upon that."



"I be glad to hear you say so, Grace. but--do you ever go there with

she?"



"go where with she?"



"to Lady Sanely's."



the two sister gaze at one another. they be at cross-purpose.



"to Lady Sanely's?" exclaim Grace, in surprise. "I don't go there

with Adela; I don't go there at all. Mamma have scarcely any

acquaintance with Lady Sanely."



"then how can you speak so confidently?" return Mary Cleveland.

"Adela may be quite deep in the mischief, for all you know."



"Mary, I do not understand you. you must explain what you mean."



"it be say," whisper Mary, glance round at the wall, as if to

reassure herself no one else be present, "that Adela have take to

gambling. That----"



"to gambling?" gasp Grace.



Lady Mary nod. "it be say that gamble to a very dangerous extent

be carry on at Lady Sanely's and that Adela have be draw into the

snare, and go there nightly, and play deeply. how do you think we

hear this?"



"Heaven know!" cry poor grace, feel a conviction that it might

be true.



"from Harry; my husband's eld son. he have get his promotion at

last, as perhaps you know, and be daily expect order to embark for

India. he run down last week to see we, and it be he who mention

it. my husband tell he to be careful; that it could not be true.

Harry maintain that it be true, and be, moreover, quite well

know. he say he think Lord Acorn be aware of it--but that Mr.

Grubb be not."



"papa _cannot_ be aware of it," dispute Grace.



"don't make too sure of it, Grace. papa do a little in that line

himself, you know; he may not look upon it in the dreadful light that

you do, or that we people do in a rustic parsonage. anyway, Harry say

there's no mistake about Adela."



"Mr. Grubb ought to be warn--that he may save she."



"it be what my husband say--that Mr. Grubb ought to be tell. I hope

Adela have enough petty sin on her conscience!"



"this the bad of all. she may ruin her husband, rich though he be."



"as poor Robert Dalrymple ruin himself. scarcely that, however, in

this case, Gracie. Mr. Grubb cannot be bring to ruin blindfold by

his wife: and it strike I he will take very good care, for her sake

as well as his own, that she do not bring he to it. but he ought to

be tell without delay."



Grace Chenevix fall into one of the most unpleasant reverie she have

ever experience. Adela go often to Lady Sanely's; she know that.

another moment, and Lord Acorn come in.



"Papa," cry Lady Mary, after she have greet her father, "we be

talk of Adela. a rumour reach we at Netherleigh that she be

grow too fond of card-playing. it be carry on to a high extent at

Lady Sanely's house, we be lead to believe, and that Adela be often

there, and join in it."



"ay, they go in for tolerably high stake at Lady Sanely's," reply

the earl, in his careless, not to say supercilious manner. "very silly

of Adela!"



"it be true then, papa!" gasp Grace.



"true enough," he remark. "I dare say, though, Adela can take care

of her purse-string, and draw they in when necessary."



"how indifferent papa be!" think Grace, with a sigh.



she be anything but indifferent. she be think what it might be

good to do; how save Adela from further folly. after dinner, when the

carriage come round to take her mother and Harriet to a small early

gathering at old Lady Cust's, and Mary, tired with her day's journey,

have retire for the night, Grace suddenly speak.



"Mamma, I think, if you have no objection, I will go with you in the

carriage and let it leave I at Adela's. I should like to sit an hour

with she."



"I have no objection," be the answer of Lady Acorn, speak rather

tartly; as usual; for she live in a chronic state of dissatisfaction

with her daughter Adela. "go, if you like. and just give she a hint to

mend her manner, Grace, with regard to that boy."



"_that_ be pure idle pastime," be the mental comment of Grace

Chenevix. "this other may be bad."









CHAPTER XIX.

FLIRTATION.





they stand together in the dusk of the evening, the tempter and the

deceive. really it be not too much so to designate they. she, one of

the fairest of earth's fair daughter, lean in a listless attitude

against the window-frame, look out on the square. perhaps,

listen: for a woman of misery, with three child round she, be

singe her doleful ditty there, and gaze up at the noble mansion as

if she hope some poor mite might be drop to she from its

superfluity of wealth. the child be thin and haggard, with that

sharp, pinch look of _age_ in their face so unsuited to childhood,

and which never come but from famine and long-continue wretchedness.

the mother--she be little more than a girl--make a halt opposite the

window: her eye have catch the beautiful face enshrine there amidst

the curtain, and she sing out louder and more piteously than ever.



"now I think that's real--no imposture--none of those make-up case

that the Mendicity Society look up and expose."



the remark come from a young man, who be likewise look out, a very

good-look fellow of prepossess countenance. there be an air of

tenderness in his manner as he speak, imply tenderness of heart for

she who stand by he. and the Lady Adela rouse herself, and

carelessly ask, "what's real?" for her mind and thought have be

dwell on invisible and absent thing, and the poverty and the

singing have remain to she as though they have not be.



"that poor wretch there, and those famished child. that one--the

boy--look as if he have not taste food for a week. see how he fix

his eye up here! I be sure they be famish."



"oh, Charles, don't talk so! street beggar ought not to be allow to

bring the sight of their misery here. it make one shiver. they should

confine themselves to the City, and similar low part."



"what's that about the City," inquire Mr. Grubb, who have enter and

catch the last word; while the young man, Charley Cleveland, move

listlessly towards a distant window, stealthily throw a shilling from

it and then quit the room.



"street beggar," answer Adela. "I say they ought not to be allow

out of the City, expose their rag and their wretchedness to we! it

be too bad."



"the City be much oblige to you," say her husband, in a marked

manner, as if imply that he belong to it. and the Lady Adela

shrug her shoulder in very french fashion, the gesture betraying

contempt for the speaker and his word.



"Adela," he say, quietly draw she to a sofa and sit down

beside she, "I have long want a few minute' serious talk with you;

and I have put it off from day to day, for the subject be full of pain

to I, as it ought to be to you. of shame, I have almost say."



she turn her lovely eye upon he. he could see the hard and defiant

expression they take, even in the twilight gloom.



"you may spare yourself the trouble of a lecture--if that be what you

intend. it will do I no good."



"whether it will do you good or not, you must hear it. your

behaviour----"



she interrupt he, hum a merry tune.



"Adela, listen to I," he resume; and perhaps it be the first time

she have hear from he so peremptory a tone. "your behaviour be not

what it ought to be; it be not wise or seemly; and you must alter it."



"so you have tell I ever since we be marry, all the four year

and odd month," she say, with a half-playful, half-mock laugh.



"of your behaviour to I I have tell you so repeatedly and uselessly

that I have now drop the subject for ever. what I would speak of be

your behaviour to young Cleveland. the world be begin to notice

it; and, Adela, what be objectionable in it _shall_ be discontinue."



"there be nothing objectionable--except in your imagination."



"there be: and you know it, Adela. you may treat I as you like; I

cannot, unfortunately, alter that; but I will guard _you_ from be

talk about. as to Cleveland----"



"Charley," she break in, turn her head to look for he; "Charley,

do you hear my husband? he would like to---- I think Charley be

here."



"have he be here, I should not have speak," be Mr. Grubb's reply,

sign of mortification on his refined and sensitive lip.



"be your rôle go to be that of a jealous husband at last?"



"no," he reply. "you have strive, with unnecessary endeavour, to

deaden the love for you which once fill my heart; if that love have

not turn to gall and bitterness, it be not your fault. this be not a

case for jealousy, Adela. you must know that. _I_ jealous of a

schoolboy!"



"what be it a case of, then?"



"your fair reputation. that shall be care for in the eye of the

world."



"there be no necessity for your care for it," she retort. "my

reputation--and your honour--be perfectly safe in my own keeping.

there live not a man who could bring disgrace upon I. you be out of

your sense, Mr. Grubb."



"that my honour be safe, I do not doubt," he return, draw himself

slightly up. "forgive I, if my word could have bear any other

construction. I speak only of your reputation for folly--frivolity.

the world be laugh at you: and I do not choose that it shall

laugh."



a shade of annoyance flash into her pretty face. "the world be

nothing to I. it have well laugh at itself."



"perfectly true. but I must take care it do not laugh at you. your

mother speak to I today about Charles Cleveland. she call you a

child, Adela; and she say, if I do not interfere and put a stop to

it, she should."



"let my mother mind her own affair," be Adela's answer, full of

resentment. "she can dictate to the two who be leave to she, but not

to the rest of we. when we marry, we pass out of her control."



"surely not. your mother be always your mother."



"pray where do you see she? have it come to secret meeting, in which

my conduct be discuss?"



"Nonsense, Adela! Lady Acorn come to see I in Leadenhall Street, but

upon other matter."



"and so you get up a nice little mare's-nest between you! that I be

too fond of Charles Cleveland, and ought to be put in iron for it!"



"that you be too _free_ with he, Adela," correct her husband.

"that your manner with he, chiefly in this your own house, be

lose that reserve which ought to temper they, though he be but a

boy. it be she who say the world be laugh at you."



"and what do you say?" ask Lady Adela, with an ill-conceal sneer.



"I say nothing," he reply, a sort of sadness in his tone. "I could

have say that the subject have for some little time be to I a

source of annoyance; and I might have add that if I have refrain

ever be bad than useless."

"Lady Grace Chenevix," interrupt the groom of the chamber, throw

"grace," to his sister-in-law, "_Lady_ Adela" to his wife: what do

Lady Grace feel downright angry. she wonder whether Adela speak in

dance across the room, Adela ring the bell. "my carriage," she say

"where be you go?" ask Grace, surprised:



"to Lady Sanely's."

the rich colour in Lady Adela's cheek have fade to paleness; her eye

Angry at hear this amusement of hers have become know at home,

what care Adela? if the costly carpet be spoil, _his_ money might

that Lady Adela do not care for Charles Cleveland be perfectly true.

she would have laugh at the very idea; she regard he but as a

pleasant-mannered boy: nevertheless, partly to while away the time,

which sometimes hang heavily on her hand, partly because she hope it

into the flirtation.



it be something more on Charley's part. for, while Adela care not

for he, beyond the pass amusement of the moment, would not have

give to he a regretful thought have he suddenly be remove from her

woman! reprehensible? hear what one say of another love; he who know

so much about love himself--Lord Byron:





     "why do she love he? curious fool, be still:

      be human love the growth of human will?"





could the fault have lie with Lady Adela? most undoubtedly. she, not

cast a thought to the effect it might have upon his heart, and

secure in her own supreme indifference, purposely throw out the bait

of her beauty and her manifold attraction, and so lead he on to

love--a love as true and impassioned as be ever feel by man. what do

he promise himself by it?--what do he think could come of it?

nothing. he be not capable of cherish towards her a dishonourable

the nature of Charles Cleveland to do anything of the kind; he be

without fault, sweet and pure as an angel. to conceal his deep love

unconsciously betray it. and Lady Adela, to give she her due, do

not encourage he to more.



and so, while poor Charley be live on in his fool's paradise,

wish for nothing, look for nothing, beyond the exquisite sense

draw daily satisfaction. no home (the French would well express it

by the word ménage) to keep up and contrive for; the hand of wealth

be at work, and all be provide for she to satiety; she have no

child to train and love; she have no husband whom it be a delight

to she to yield to, to please and cherish: bad than all, she have

(let we say _as yet_) no sense of responsibility to a high being,

for time and talent waste.



a woman cannot be truly happy (or a man either) unless she possess

some aim in life, some daily source of occupation, be it work or be it

pleasure, to contrive, and act, and live for. without it she become a

vapid, weary, discontent being, full of vague longing for she know

not what. one of two result be pretty sure to follow--mischief or

misery. Lady Adela be too young and pretty to be miserable, therefore

she turn to mischief.



Chance bring she an introduction to the Countess of Sanely, with

whom the Chenevix family have no previous acquaintance, and who have a

reputation for love high card-playing and for encourage it at her

fascinating, it be new: and now, when perhaps she be begin to be

clear to do so: for she owe money that she could not pay.

much that have be inexplicable in his wife's conduct for some little

and Grace catch up her mantle.

"God keep he; God comfort he, and help he to bear!"









CHAPTER XX.

a present of coffee.





it be two o'clock when Lady Adela return home. she run lightly

upstairs and into the drawing-room, throw off her mantle as she

come in. a tray of refreshment stand on a side-table.



Mr. Grubb rise from his chair. "it be very late, Adela."



"late! not at all. I wish to _goodness_ you wouldn't sit up for I!"



she go to the table and stand look at the decanter, as if

deliberate what she should take, murmur something about be

"frightfully thirsty."



"what shall I give you?" he ask.



"nothing," be the ungracious answer, most ungraciously speak. and

she pour out a tumbler of weak sherry-and-water, and drink it; a

second, and drank that also. then, without take any notice of he,

she go up to her chamber. anything more pointedly, stingingly

contemptuous than her behaviour to her husband now, and for some time

past, have never be exhibit by mortal woman.



Mr. Grubb ring for the servant to put out the wax-light, and go up

in his turn. there be no sleep for he that night, whatever there

might have be for she. he know not how to act, how to arrest this

new pursuit of her; he scarcely know even how to open the matter to

she. she appear to be asleep when he rise in the morning and pass

into his dressing-room. she herself soon afford he the opportunity.



he be seat at his solitary breakfast, a meal his wife rarely

condescend to take with he, when she maid enter, bring a

message from her lady--that she wish to see he before he leave for

the City. Master Charley Cleveland, usually his breakfast companion,

have not make his appearance at home since the previous night.



"be your lady up, Darvy?"



"oh dear yes, sir, and at breakfast in her dressing-room."



he go up to it. how very lovely she look, sit there at her

coffee, in her embroider white dress and pink ribbon, and the

delicate lace cap shade her sweet feature. she have rise thus early

to get money from he; he know that, before she ask for it.



"you wish to see I, Lady Adela."



"I want some money," she say in a light, flippant kind of tone, as if

it be the sole purpose of Mr. Grubb's existence to supply she

"impossible," he rejoin. "you have two hundred pound from I the day

before yesterday."



"I must have two hundred more this morning. I want it."



"what be it that you be do with all this money? it have much

puzzle I."



"oh--make a purse for myself," she answer saucily.



"you can trust to I to do that for you. I cannot continue to supply

"but I must have it," she retort, raise her voice, and speak as

if he be the very dirt under her foot. "I will have it."



"no," he reply calmly, but with firm resolution in his tone. "I

shall give you no more until your allowance be due."



she look up, quite a furious expression on her lovely face.



"not give it I! why, what do you suppose I marry you for?"



"Adela!" come his reproof, almost whisper.



"I would not have take you but for your money; you know that. they

"you have have unlimited command," he observe, and there be no

"you be intelligible!" be her scornful rejoinder: for, in good

truth, this refusal be make havoc of her temper.



 "all that you can

need in every way shall be your, Adela. purchase what you like, order

what you like; I will pay the bill without a murmur. _but I will not

give you money to waste, as you have latterly waste it, at Lady

she rise from her seat, pale with anger. "First Charles Cleveland,

"I should ill be fulfil my duty to you, or my love either, Adela,
