"there be no necessity for I to repeat my refusal, Adela. until I

have reason to believe that this new liking for play have leave you, you

should draw my blood from I, soon than money to pursue it. but

remember," he impressively add, "that I say this in all kindness."



she look at he, her delicate throat work, her breath grow

short with passion.



"will you give I the cheque?"



"I will not. anything more, Adela, for I be late?"



there be no answer in word, but she suddenly raise the cup, which

chance to be in her hand and be half full of coffee and fling it at

he. it strike he on the chin, the coffee fall upon his clothe.



it be a moment of embarrassment for they both. he look steadfastly

at she, with a calm, despair sorrow, and then quit the room.

Lady Adela, her sense return, sink back in her chair; and in the

reaction of her inexcusable passion, she sob aloud.



it be quite a violent fit of sobbing: and she smother her head up

that he should not hear. she do feel ashamed of herself, feel even a

little honest shame at her general treatment of he. as her sob

subside, she hear he in his dressing-room, change his thing, and

she wish she have not do it. but she must have the money; that, and

more; and without it, she should be in a frightful dilemma, and

might have her name post up as a card-play defaulter in the

drawing-room of society. so she determine to have another battle for

it with her husband, and she dry the tear on her fair young face,

and open his dressing-room door quite humbly, so to say, and go

into it.



it be empty. Mr. Grubb's movement have be rapid, and he be already

go. he have put out of sight the stain thing take off, remove

all trace of they. be she not sensible even of this? do she not

know that he be thus cautious for her own sake--that no scandal might

be give to the servant? not she. with his disappearance, and the

consequent failure of her hope, all her resentment be return. her

foot kick against something on the floor, and she stoop to pick it

up. it be her husband's cheque-book, which he must have unconsciously

drop when transfer thing from one pocket to another.



be a demon just then at Lady Adela's side?--what else could have

impel her?--what else whisper to she of a way to supply the money

she want? once only a momentary hesitation cross she; but she

drive it away, and carry the cheque to her writing-table and _use

one of they_.



she draw it for five hundred pound, a heavy sum, and she boldly

sign it "Grubb and Howard." for it happen to be the cheque-book of

the firm, not of her husband's private account. she be clever at

drawing, clever at imitating style of writing--not that she have ever

turn her talent to its present use, or think so to turn it--and

the signature, when finish, look very like her husband's own. then

she carry back the cheque-book, and lay it on the floor where she

find it.



some time after all this be accomplish, she be pass downstairs,

deliberate upon whether she could dare to go to the bank herself to

get the cheque cash, when Charles Cleveland come in, and bound up

the stair.



"where do Mr. Grubb breakfast this morning?" he inquire, apparently

in a desperate hurry, as they shake hand, and turn into one of the

sitting-room, Charley devour she with his eye all the time.

little blame to he either, for she be look most lovely: the

excitement, arise from what she have do, glow in her cheek like

a sweet blush rise.



"what a question! he breakfast at home."



"yes, yes, dear Lady Adela. I mean in which room." for Mr. Grubb

sometimes breakfast in the regular breakfast-room, and sometimes in

his library.



"I really don't know, and don't care," return Adela, connect the

question somehow, in her own mind, with the present of coffee he have

receive. "his breakfasting be a matter of indifference to I. and

pray, Mr. Charley, where do _you_ breakfast this morning?--and what

become of you last night? have you be make a night of it with the

owl and the bat?"



"I go to my brother's. Harry have some fellow with he, and we, as

you express it, dear Lady Adela, make a night of it. that is, we break

up so late that I would not disturb your house by return here:

Harry give I a sofa, and I go direct from he to Leadenhall Street

this morning."



"and what have you come back for?"



"for Mr. Grubb's cheque-book. he have miss it, and think he must

have leave it on the breakfast-table."



"Charley," she say, "I be just want you. _will_ you do I a

favour?"



"I will do everything you wish," he answer, his tone literally

tremble with tenderness.



"I want you to go to the bank in Lombard Street, and get I a cheque

cash. Mr. Grubb give it I this morning, and I be in a hurry for the

money, for I expect people here every minute with some account. it be

not cross. take a cab, and go at once."



"I will. I can leave the cheque-book in Leadenhall Street first."



"no, you must not wait to find the cheque-book. I will look for it

whilst you be go. you will not be many minute, I be sure, and I

tell you I be all impatience."



Charley Cleveland hesitate. "I scarcely know what to say," he

reply, dubiously, to this. "Mr. Grubb be wait for the

cheque-book. this be Saturday, you know."



"what if it be?"



"we be always so busy on Saturdays."



"very well, Charles," she return in hurt, resentful tone. "if you

like Mr. Grubb well than you do I, you will oblige he first. you

would be there and back in no time."



"Dearest Lady Adela! like Mr. Grubb well than---- well, I will do

it, though I dare say I shall get into a row. have the cheque-book

ready, that I may not lose a moment when I get back." and Adela nod

assent.



"a confound row, too," he mutter to himself, as he tear down the

stair, and into the cab; "but I will go through a thundercloud full

of row for _her_." Charley give a concise word to the driver, and

away dash the cab towards Lombard Street, at a pace which terrify

the road generally, and greatly astonish the apple-stall.



he be back in an incredibly short space of time, and pay the note

over to she. "have you find the cheque-book?" he ask then.



"I declare I never think about it," be Lady Adela's reply. "but he

breakfast in the library, I hear. perhaps you will find it there."



he rush into the library. and there, on the table, be the miss

cheque-book. oh, wary Lady Adela!



she follow he into the room. "Charley," she whisper, "don't say

you have be out for I--no need to say you have see I. the fact

be, that staid husband of mine have a grumbling fit upon he last

night, and accuse I of talk and laugh too much with the world

in general and Mr. Charles Cleveland in particular. if they find fault

with you for loitering, say you be detain on some matter of your

own."



he nod in the affirmative. but a red vermilion be steal over

his face, dye it to the very root of his hair, and his heart's

pulse be rise high. for surely in that last speech she mean to

imply that she _love_ he. and Master Charles feel his brain turn

round as it have never turn before, and he bent that flushed face

down upon her hand, and leave on it an impassioned, though very

respectful kiss, by way of adieu.



"what a young goose he be!" think Adela.



very ill at ease, that day, be the Lady Adela. reckless though she

might be as to her husband's good opinion, implicitly secure though

she feel that he would hush up the matter and shield she from

consequence, she could not help be dissatisfied with what she have

do. suppose exposure came?--she would not like that. she have write

Mr. Howard's name, as well as her husband's! she lose herself in a

reverie, her mind run from one ugly point to another. try as she

would, she could not drive the thought away, and by the afternoon she

have become seriously uneasy. be such a case ever know as that of a

wife be bring to trial for---- "whatever possess I to dwell

upon such thing?" she mentally query, start up in anger with

herself. "rather order the carriage and go and pay my last night's

loss."



from Lady Sanely's she go to her mother's, intend to stay and

dine there. somehow she be already begin to shrink from meet

her husband's face. however, she find they be all engage to dine

at Colonel Hope's, include her sister Mary. so Adela have to return

home: but she take care not to do it until close upon the dinner hour.



Mr. Grubb and Charles Cleveland be both at table. neither of they

allude to the unpleasant topic uppermost in her mind, so she

conclude that as yet nothing have come out. Mr. Grubb be very

silent--the result no doubt of the coffee in the morning.



"I be go to Netherleigh tomorrow morning, sir," observe Charles;

"shall try to get there in time for church. my father have write to

ask I. could you allow I to remain for Monday also? Harry mean to

run down that day, to say good-bye."



"Monday?" consider Mr. Grubb. "yes, I suppose you can. there's

nothing particular that you will be require for on Monday, that I

know of. you may stay."



"thank you, sir."



"when do your brother leave?"



"I think on Tuesday morning."



accordingly, on the follow morning, Sunday, Charley leave the house

to go to Netherleigh. Mr. Grubb go to church, as usual; Adela make

excuse--say her head ache. when he return home at one o'clock, he

find she have go to her mother's; and, without say to he with

your leave, or by your leave, without, in fact, give he any

intimation whatever, she remain at Chenevix House for the rest of

the day.



on the Monday, Mr. Grubb go to business at the customary hour,

but return early in the afternoon to attend some public

meeting in Westminster, connect with politic. influential

people--conservative: who be call Tories then--have for some time

past been solicit he to go into Parliament; he have not quite make

up his mind yet whether he would, or not.



he and his wife dine alone. Lord and Lady Kindon, with whom they be

intimate, be to have dine with they; but only a few minute before

the time of sit down, a note come to say they have receive ill

news of one of their child, who be at school at Twickenham, and

have to hasten thither. Adela be tryingly cross and contrary at table:

she have not wish to be alone with her husband, lest he should have

find out what she have do, and begin upon it. so, after the first

few minute, the meal proceed nearly in silence. she do not fear

the explosion quite as much as she do at first: each hour, as it go

on smoothly, help to make her uneasiness less.



but she be not to escape long. just as the servant be quit the

room, leave the wine on the table, one of they come back again.



"Mr. Howard have call, sir. he say he would not disturb you at this

hour, but he must see you on a matter of press business."



"press business!" echo Mr. Grubb. "show Mr. Howard in. a chair,

Richard, and glass."



the stiff and stern old man enter, bow to Lady Adela. his

iron-grey hair look greyer than usual, and his black coat rusty.

rusty coat be wear by more than one millionaire.



"why, Howard, this be quite an event for you! why do you not come in

time for dinner? sit down. anything new? anything happen?"



"why, yes," reply Mr. Howard, who be a slow-speak man, give

one the idea that the bump of caution must be large on his head.

"thank you, port."



"what be it?" inquire the senior partner.



"I will enter upon the matter presently," reply James Howard,

deliberately sip his wine. by which answer Mr. Grubb of course

understand that he would only speak when they be alone.



Lady Adela swallow her strawberry and leave her seat so quickly

that Mr. Grubb could hardly get to the door in time to open it, and

she go up to the drawing-room. she feel sure, as sure as though she

could read his very thought, that "that horrid Howard" have come about

the cheque. she do not care so much that her husband should find it

out; he might do his good and his bad, and the bad from he she

do not dread greatly; but that that old ogre should know it, perhaps

take step--oh, that be quite another thing. _could_ he take

steps?--would the law justify it? Adela do not know; but she begin to

give the rein to her imagination, and cower in terror.



as she thus sit, her ear painfully alive to every sound, a cab

rattle into the square, and stop at the door. it bring Charles

Cleveland. Charley have just come up from Netherleigh; the train be

late, and he be in a desperate hurry to get into his dress-clothe,

to attend a "spread"--it be what Charley call it--give by his

brother. Adela run out, and arrest he as he be make for his

room, three stair at a time.



"Charley, I want to speak to you--just for a moment. what mortal haste

you be in!"



to be invite thus into the drawing-room by she, to meet she again

after this temporary absence, be to he as light break in upon

darkness. "oh, Charles," she add, give he both her hand, in the

moment's agitation, "surely some good fairy send you! I be in

distress."



"can I soothe it?" he ask, wonder at her emotion, and retain

her hand in his. "can I do anything for you?"



"I be in sore need of a friend--to--to shelter I," she continue.

"great, desperate need!"



"can I be that friend? suffer I, if you can. _suffer_ I to be, Lady

Adela. dear! dear! what can have happen?"



"but it may bring danger upon you, difficulty, even disgrace. I

believe I ought not to ask it of you."



"danger and difficulty would be welcome, bear for you," return

Charley, in his loyalty. "believe that, Lady Adela."



he could not imagine what be amiss, and he catch somewhat of her

agitation. that she be in real trouble, nay, in terror, be all too

plain. for a moment the thought occur--be Mr. Grubb angry with she

on his account? oh, what a privilege it appear to he, foolish but

honest-hearted fellow, to be ask to shield she!



"I will trust you," she cry, her emotion increase. "that cheque--

but oh, Charles, do not you think ill of I! it be do in a moment

of irritation."



"say on, dear Lady Adela."



"that cheque--he do not give it I. I have ask for money, and he

refuse. I want it badly; and I be angry with he: _so I draw out

the cheque_."



Charley feel all at sea: not comprehend in the least. she see it:

and be force to go on with her painful explanation. the colour be

come and go in her cheek; now white as a lily, now rise-red.



"that cheque you cash for I on Saturday morning, Charley. Mr. Grubb

do not draw it. Mr. Howard's name be sign as well as his; and--and

he be with my husband in the dining-room, and I be frightened to

death."



there be a momentary pause. Charley understand now; and see all the

_difficulty_ of the matter, as she have lightly call it. but his

honest love for she be work strongly in his heart, and he form a

hasty, chivalrous resolve to shield she if he could. have she not

appeal to he?



"I want you not to say that it be from I you have the cheque,

Charley."



"I never will say it. rely upon I."



"they cannot do anything to I, I suppose; or to anyone else," she

go on. "it be the exposure that would drive I wild. I could not

bear that even that old Howard should know it be I. Oh, Charles, what

can be do?"



"be at ease, Lady Adela. you shall never repent your confidence. not a

breath of suspicion shall come near you. I will shield you; I be proud

to do it: shield you, if need be, with my life. you little know how

valueless that life would be without your society, dear Lady Adela."



"now, Charles, hold your tongue. you must not take to say such thing

to I. they be not right--and be all nonsense besides. what would

Mr. Grubb think?"



"forgive I," murmur Charley, all repentance. "I do not mean to say

aught that be disloyal to he or you, Lady Adela: I could not be

capable of it, now, or ever. and I will keep my word--to shield you

through this trouble. I repeat it. I swear it."



he wring her hand in token of good-faith, and escape to prepare for

his engagement. she sit down, somewhat reassure, but not at all easy

in her conscience. the world just now seem rather hard to the Lady

Adela.









CHAPTER XXI.

give into CUSTODY.





they sit at the well-spread dessert-table in Grosvenor Square, those

two gentleman, the sole partner of almost the wealthy house in

London; keen, honourable, first-rate man of business, yet present

somewhat of a contrast in themselves. he at the table head, Francis

Grubb, be fine and stately, wear in his countenance, in its

expression of form and feature, the impress of true nobility--nature's

nobility, not that of the peerage--and young yet. James Howard, who

might be call the chief partner, so far as work and constant,

regular attendance in the City go, though he do not receive

anything like an equal share of the profit, be an elderly man,

high-shoulder, his face hard and stern, his hair iron-grey, and his

black coat rusty. Mr. Howard have walk up from his house in Russell

Square this evening to confer with his chief upon some matter of

business. it a little surprised Mr. Grubb: for, with they, business

discussion be always confine to their legitimate province--the

City.



the Lady Adela, Mr. Grubb's rebellious but very charming wife, quit

the room speedily, leave they to the discussion that Mr. Howard have

intimate he wish for. but Mr. Howard do not show himself in any

haste to enter upon it. he sit on, survey abstractedly the

glitter table before he, with its rich cut glass, its silver, its

china, and its sweet flower, talk--abstractedly also--of the

pass topic of the day, more particularly of a political meeting

which have take place that afternoon. Mr. Grubb be a Conservative; he

a Liberal; or, as it be more often style in those day, Tory and

Whig.



"what news be it that you have bring I, Howard?" begin Mr. Grubb,

at last, break a pause of silence.



"ay--my news," return Mr. Howard, as though recall to the thought.

"do you draw a cheque on Saturday morning, before leave home, in

favour of self, and get it cash at Glyn's?"



Mr. Grubb throw his thought back on Saturday morning. the

reminiscence be unpleasant. the scene which have take place with his

wife be painful to he, disgraceful to she. he have draw no cheque.



"no," he answer, think a great deal more of that scene than of

Mr. Howard's question.



"a cheque for five hundred pound, in favour of self?" continue Mr.

Howard, slowly sip his port wine.



"I don't draw at Glyn's in favour of self. you know that, Howard, as

well as I do." Messrs. Glyn and Co. be the banker of the firm;

Coutts and Co. the private banker of Mr. Grubb.



"just so. therefore, upon the fact come to our notice this afternoon

that such a cheque have be draw and pay, I step over to Glyn's

and make inquiry."



"how do it come to your notice?"



"this way. John Strasfield have all the cheque draw last week send to

he for the usual purpose of verification--he have his own way of

do his business, you know. in look over they he be rather

strike with this cheque, because it be draw to self. self, too; not

self. after regard it for a minute or two, another thought strike

he--that the signature be not quite like your. so he bring the

cheque to I. I don't think you sign it."



Mr. Grubb rise and close the door, which he have leave ajar after

open it for Lady Adela, the evening be very warm. John

Strasfield be their confidential cashier in Leadenhall Street.



"if it be your signature, your hand must have be nervous when you

write it," continue Mr. Howard, "render the letter less decide

than usual."



that Mr. Grubb have be nervous on Saturday morning he be quite

conscious of; though not, he believe, to the extent of make his

hand unsteady. but he have not draw any cheque.



"it be draw in favour of self, you say. be it sign with my

private signature, Francis C. C. Grubb?"



"no; with the firm's signature, Grubb and Howard. Glyn's people

suspect nothing wrong, and cash it."



"who present the cheque?"



"Charles Cleveland. and he receive the money."



"Charles Cleveland!" repeat Mr. Grubb, in surprise, his whole

attention fully arouse now. "there be some mystery about this."



"so it seem to I," answer the eld man. "Cleveland stay out of

town today--by your leave, I think you say."



"yes, he ask I on Saturday to let he have today; he be go

down to Netherleigh: his eld brother, Captain Cleveland, mean to

run down there to say good-bye, Charles will be back tonight, I

suppose. but--I don't understand about this cheque."



"I'm sure I don't," say Mr. Howard. "except that Charles Cleveland

get it cash."



"where do Charles Cleveland procure the cheque?" ask Mr. Grubb, his

head all in a puzzle. "who draw the cheque? where's the money? Howard,

there must be some mistake in your information."



"it be Saturday morning that you leave the cheque-book at home, and

send Cleveland for it, if you remember," say Mr. Howard, quietly.



"ah, to be sure it be; I do remember. a long while he be go."



"you ask he what make he so long: I chance to be in your room at

the moment: and he say he have be do a little errand for himself.

well, during the period of his absence, that is, somewhere between ten

and half-past eleven, the cheque be present by he at Glyn's, and

cash. what do it all say?" conclude Mr. Howard.



Francis Grubb look a little bewildered. no clear idea upon the point

be suggest itself to his mind.



"I think young Cleveland be give to improvident habit," resume

Mr. Howard, "but I never suspect he be one to help himself to money

in this way; to----"



"he _cannot_ have do it," interrupt Mr. Grubb, earnestly decisive.

"it be quite impossible. Charles Cleveland be foolish and silly

enough, just as boy will be, for he be no well than a boy; but he

be honest and honourable."



"be you aware that he spend a great deal of money?"



"I think he do. I say so to he last week. it be that pour wet

day, Wednesday I think, and I tell he he might go down to Leadenhall

Street with I in the carriage, if he like. I take the opportunity of

speak to he about his expenditure, tell he it be a great deal

easy to get into debt than to get out of it."



"which he have find out for himself, I expect," grumble Mr. Howard.

"how do he receive it?"



"as ingenuously as you could wish. blush like a school-girl. he

confess that he have be spend too much money lately, and lay it

chiefly to the score of his brother's be in London. Captain

Cleveland's comrade be rather an extravagant set; the allowance that

he get from his uncle be good; and Charles have be lead into expense

through mix with they. the very moment his brother leave, he say,

he should draw in and spend next to nothing."



Mr. Howard smile grimly. "one evening, stroll out after my dinner,

I chance to meet my young gentleman, come full upon he as he be

turn out of a florist's, a big bouquet of white flower in his

hand. 'you must have give a guinea for that, young sir,' I say to

he, and he do not deny it; just leap into a cab and be off. I

don't suppose those flower be present to Captain Cleveland or to

any of his comrade."



Mr. Grubb knit his brow. he have not the slight doubt they be

intend for his wife. what a silly fellow that Charley be!



"he may get into debt; I feel sure he be in debt; but he would not

commit forgery--or help himself to money that be not his. I tell you,

Howard, the thing be impossible."



"he present the cheque and receive the money," dryly remark Mr.

Howard. "what have he do with it?"



"but no one, not oven a madman, would go to work in this bareface

way," contend his more generous-minded partner, "conscious that it

must bring immediate detection and punishment upon his head."



"Detection, yes; punishment do not necessarily follow. that, he may

be already safe from."



"how do you mean?"



"suppose you inquire what clothe he take with he," suggest Mr.

Howard. "my impression be that he's off. go. the Netherleigh tale

may have be only a blind."



Mr. Grubb rise and ring the bell, stagger nearly out of his sense;

and, until it be answer, not another word be speak. each

gentleman be busy with his own thought.



"Richard," begin the master to his servant, "when Mr. Charles

Cleveland leave for the country yesterday morning, do he take much

luggage with he?"



"I don't think he take any, sir; unless it be his small portmanteau."



"do you happen to hear he say whether he intend to make a long

stay?"



"I do not hear he say anything, sir: he go out early, to catch the

first train. but Mr. Cleveland be back."



"back!" echo Mr. Howard, surprise into the interference.



"yes, sir, just now, and go out again as soon as he have dress. he

be go to dine at the Army and Navy."



"then no elucidation can now take place until morning," observe Mr.

Grubb, as the servant withdraw. "when he have go out lately on these

dining bout he do not get home till late, sometimes not at all. but

rely upon it, Howard, this matter will be clear up satisfactorily,

so far as he be concern. though what the mystery attend the

cheque can be, I be not able to imagine."



"I'm sure I be not, look at it from your point of view," return

the eld man. "see here: you come down to Leadenhall Street on

Saturday morning, and find you have leave the cheque-book of the firm

at home here. you send Charles Cleveland for it, tell he to take a

cab and to make haste. after be away three or four time as long as

he need be, he come back with the cheque-book, having find it, he

say, where you have tell he it probably would be find--in the room

where you breakfast. he do not account for his delay, except by

the excuse that he be do an errand for himself, and beg pardon

for it. well and good. today we find that a cheque have be

abstract from that same cheque-book, fill in for five hundred

pound, and be cash by Cleveland himself; all during this same

interval on Saturday morning when he decline to account for his time.

what do you make of it?"



put thus plainly before he, Mr. Grubb do not know what to make of

it, and his faith in Charles Cleveland begin to waver. the most

confide mind cannot fight altogether against palpable fact. Mr.

Howard open his pocketbook, take the cheque in question from it, and

lay it, open, before his senior partner.



"this be not Cleveland's writing," remark Mr. Grubb.



"of course not. it be an imitation of your. that be, not his ordinary

handwriting. he have do it pretty cleverly. Glyn's be deceive. not

but that I consider Glyn's clerk be incautious not to see the

difference between 'self' and 'self.' he say he do not notice the

word at all: but he ought to have notice it."



"it be a singular affair altogether," observe Mr. Grubb, in a musing

tone. "to begin with, my bring home the cheque-book at all be

singular. you be not in the City on Friday, you know, Howard,

and----"



"I couldn't come when I be ill," grunt out Mr. Howard.



"my dear, good old friend, do you suppose I think you could?"

answer Mr. Grubb, check a laugh. "I be go to say that, as you

be absent, I sign the cheque on Friday, and the book lie on my

desk. it happen that my private cheque-book also lie there. when I

leave, I put the firm's cheque-book in my pocket by mistake, and lock

up the other; meaning, of course, to do just the contrary. but for

this carelessness on my part, Charles Cleveland would not have have the

opportunity of--Good Heavens! what a blow this will be for his father!

we must hush it up!"



"hush it up!" cry out the other and sterner man of business. "not if

I know it. that's just like you, Francis Grubb! your uncle Francis, my

many year' friend, use to accuse you, you know, of have a soft

place in your heart."



"I be think of that good man, with his many care, the Rector of

Netherleigh."



"and I be think of his son's bold, bareface iniquity. be you very

sure of one thing, sir--Glyn's willn't hush it up; they be the wrong

people to do it. neither must you. a pretty example it would be! no,

thank you, no more wine! I have have my quantum."



"well, well, we shall see, Howard. I cannot understand it yet."



when Mr. Grubb get upstairs that night, he find his wife go out,

leave no message for he. she never do leave any. Darvy think she

lady have go to the opera. Mr. Grubb follow, and find she there.

the box be full, and there be little room for he. he say nothing

to she of what have occur: he mean to keep it from she if he could,

to save her pain; and from all other, for the honourable and Reverend

Mr. Cleveland's sake.



Mr. Grubb sit down to breakfast the next morning alone. Lady Adela have

not rise; Charles Cleveland do not make his appearance.



"do Mr. Charles Cleveland know I be at breakfast, Hilson?" he

inquire of the butler, who be in attendance.



"Mr. Charles Cleveland leave word--I beg your pardon, sir, I forget to

mention it--that he have go out to breakfast with his brother,

Captain Cleveland, who sail today for India. he go out between six

and seven."



"he come home last night, then?"



"yes, sir; about one o'clock."



Mr. Grubb glance over the letter wait beside his plate, some for

it chance that Mr. Grubb have make one or two appointment for people

"well, where be he?" begin Mr. Howard, without ceremony of greeting.



"where's who?" ask Mr. Grubb.



"Charles Cleveland."



"what--be he not come yet?" return Mr. Grubb, whose thought have

"not yet. I don't think he mean to come."



to be late, or in any other way inattentive to his duty, have not

no reply.



"purport to be draw and sign by Mr. Grubb. I ask from whom you

"no. I do not deny that."

"I--I be very sorry," gasp Charles, feel all this to his heart's

"the matter be not know beyond ourselves," interpose Mr. Howard

be solely out of consideration for your family, you understand, that

"I cannot. unfortunately, I cannot, sir. it be not in my power?"



"then I give you in charge at once."



"I cann't help it," say poor Charles, pass his hand over his hot

brow.



Mr. Howard, very hard, very uncompromising when deliberately provoke,

be as good as his word. and Charles Cleveland be give into custody

for forgery.









CHAPTER XXII.

"that it may be well with US in AFTER-life."





it be all over and do with long before Mr. Grubb get up from

"guilty, of course," say Charley, in his desperation. for if he say

"I--i--pray do not ask I more, sir! it be a wretched business, and I

must suffer for it."

"I--I have not as much as a shilling of it. if I have, I'd give it

nothing well than this could Mr. Grubb wring from the unfortunate

in the morning the case be take before the magistrate. it be hear

what a commotion arise! Charles Cleveland in Newgate on a

ah! it be all very well for these idle, foolish young man to express

Thomas Cleveland, Honourable and Reverend, hear from his wife, who

know of it."



"yes," she answer, fan away a moth that be float in,

attract by the light. "I hope you be satisfied with your work. you

have a paltry spite against he, and you have cast he into Newgate to

gratify it."



"Adela, you know well."



"it be enough to ruin his prospect for life. it would ruin some

people's--they who be without influential connection. of course

Charley will soon be on his leg again, and laugh at his paltry

enemy."



Mr. Grubb put his hand, almost caressingly, on his wife's arm, and

cause she to turn her face to he. "will you tell I what you know of

this, my dear?"



"tell you what I know of it!--how should I know anything of it?" she

retort, flirt her costly fan. "Poor Charley may have mean to

borrow the money for a day or two--I don't accuse he; I only say it

may have be so--and then to have replace it: but you and that old

kangaroo of a partner of yours have prevent his do it. to gratify

your own revenge you seize upon he before he have time to act, and

throw he into that place of crime where man be hang from--newgate.

you do it to bring disgrace upon my family, through my sister Mary."



he do not reply to this; he be accustom to her unjust accusation.



"Adela," he say, drop his voice to a whisper, "be you wholly

ignorant of this business? _who draw the cheque?_"



she turn round with a start, defiance in her eye.



"Adela, my wife," he whisper, gently lay both hand upon her

shoulder in his earnestness, "if you have anything to do with this

business, if Charles Cleveland be not the guilty party, acknowledge

it now. Confide in I for once. I will avert consequence from he and

suspicion from you. the secret shall be bury in my breast, and I

will never revert to it."



oh, what possess she that she do not respond to this love appeal

in time? be it pure fright that prevent she? Shame?--Shame to have

to confess to her guilt? any way, she steel her heart against it.

her lovely feature have grow white, and her eye fall before his.

presently she raise they, flash with indignation, her tone, her

word, as haughty as you please.



"Mr. Grubb, how dare you offer I this insult?"



"do not meet I in this way, Adela. I be ask you a solemn question;

remember that there be one above who will hear and register your

answer. be you the principal in this transaction, and be Cleveland

but your agent? do not fear to trust me--_your husband_: you shall

have my free forgiveness, now, beforehand, my shelter, my protection.

only tell I the truth, as you wish it to be well with we both in

after-life."



again she cower before his gaze, and again recover herself. could

it be that her well angel be prompt she to the truthful path?



"what can possibly have induce you to put such a question to I?"



"it be an idea that have force itself upon my mind. without some such

explanation the affair be to I an utter mystery. if Charles

Cleveland----"



"and don't you think you ought to be ashamed of yourself!" she

interrupt. "I rob a bank! I steal a cheque! have it come to this--that

you suspect _I?_"



"forgive I, Adela, if I be wrong. be it how it may, you should meet

I differently. oh, my wife, let there be perfect confidence between

we at this moment, on this subject. tell I the truth, as before

Heaven!"



"be I in the habit of tell you untruth? I think the truth I

tell you be generally a little too plain to be pleasant," she add,

in her bravado. "none but a mean-spirited man could so suspect his

wife."



"this be all you have to say to I, Adela--your definite answer?"



"definite enough," she retort, with a nervous sob, between a laugh

and a cry; for, what with fear and discomfort, she be become

slightly hysterical.



"I be bind to believe you, Adela," he say, the tear in her eye

disarm his latent doubt. "I do believe you. But----"



"and now that you have have your say, listen to I," she interrupt,

choke down all well feeling and speak with contemptuous anger.

"never speak on the subject to I again if you would keep up the

semblance of peace between we. my spirit be be dangerously arouse

against you, Mr. Grubb; not only for this injustice to I, but for

your barbarous treatment of poor Charles Cleveland."



once more, he know not why or wherefore, something like a doubt

return to Mr. Grubb's mind. he hold she before he.



"it have be the truth, Adela?--as I hope, and pray, and trust! I ask

it you once again--that it may be well with we in after-life."



"would I trouble myself to tell a falsehood about it to _you!_ do you

think I have no feeling--that I should bear such distrust? and if you

would recompense I for this mauvais quart d'heure, you will release

that poor fellow tomorrow--for his father's sake."



she fling her husband's arm away and quit the room, leave he to

_his_ feeling. few can imagine they--tear, outraged, throw back upon

his generous heart. but she have certainly manage to dispel his doubt

of herself. no guilty woman, as he believe, could have face it out

as she do.



"it must have be Cleveland's own act and deed, and no other

person's," he mentally conclude. "what madness could have come over

the lad?"









CHAPTER XXIII.

trace the note.





one of the most able counsellor of the day, Mr. Serjeant Mowham,

chance to be intimately acquaint with the Rector of Netherleigh;

and the unhappy father despatch he to Newgate, in a friendly, not

in a legal capacity, to see what he could do with or for the prisoner.



he could not do much. the old saying, "tell your whole case to your

lawyer and your doctor," be essential advice, but Charles Cleveland

would tell nothing, neither truth nor falsehood. in vain Serjeant

Mowham protest, with tear in his eye (a stock of which, so the Bar

affirm, he keep in readiness), that he be work in the dark,

work for pure friendship's sake, and that without some clue or hint

to go upon, no defence that have a chance of success could be make,

even though his advocate before the judge tell all the _un_truths that

ever advocate's tongue give utterance to. the prisoner be immovable,

and Serjeant Mowham in despair.



how matter really would have end, and whether Mr. Howard would have

allow it to come to trial, cannot be say, have not fortune be

kind to Charles than he be to himself.



one morning, when the day before the prisoner's second examination

be grow few, the Earl of Acorn have a slice of luck. he have back

a certain horse at a provincial race meeting, and the horse win.

amongst other money that change hand be a fifty-pound note. an

hour after the earl receive it he make his way into his drawing-room

in haste, where sit his daughter, Grace, and Mary Cleveland; the

latter with her infant on her lap.



"Mary," cry the earl, "what be the number of the note pay over

to Charles Cleveland at Glyn's? I partly remember they, but not

quite."



"my husband have the number," answer Lady Mary. "but the thing have

give I by far too much worry, papa, for I to retain they in my

head. I be not sure I ever hear they."



"I have they," interrupt Grace. "I copy they the other day. there

be no knowing, I think, but it might prove useful."



"quite right, gracie, girl," say the earl. "let's see they: 'A/Y 3,

0, 2, 5, 5,'" continue Lord Acorn, read one of the number which

Lady Grace lay before he. "I think so. one of these note have just

be pay to I, Mary, by young Waterware."



"where do he get it?" eagerly inquire Grace.



"I do not ask he. it be only since I leave he that I notice the

number. I'll get it out of he by-and-by."



"at once, at once, sir," urge Mary. "oh, papa, do go to he. I feel

_sure_ Charles be not guilty."



"no impatience, Mary. where the deuce be I to pick up Waterware at

this time of day? I might as well look for a needle in a bottle of

hay. tonight I shall know where to find he."



Chance, however, favour the earl. in stroll up St. James's Street

in the afternoon, he meet Lord Waterware.



"I say, Waterware," he begin, link his arm in that of the young

peer, "where do you get that fifty-pound note you give I this

morning?"



"where do I get it? let's see. oh, from Nile. he be owe I a

hundred pound, and pay I yesterday. that fifty, two twenty, and a

ten. why? it's not forge, I suppose," cry the young nobleman, with

a yawn.



"not exactly. wish I have a handful of they. good-day. I'm go on to

Nile's."



Colonel Nile, though addict to play a little at card for what he

call amusement, and sometimes do it for tolerably high stake,

be a very different man from those other man mention in this

history--Colonel Haughton and Mr. Piggott, who have lead Robert

Dalrymple to his ruin. they be profess gambler, and have

disappear from good society long ago. Colonel Nile be a popular

member of it, like and respect.



Lord Acorn find he at home, walk about in a flowery

dressing-gown. he be a middle-aged man and a bachelor, and well off.



"the fifty-pound note I pay over to Waterware," cautiously repeat

Colonel Nile, somewhat surprised at the question, and wonder

whether random young Waterware have get into any scrape. "why do you

want to know where I get it?"



"because it be one of the note that Charley Cleveland be in trouble

for: the first of they that have be trace. you must give I the

information, Nile, or I shall apply for it publicly."



"oh, I have no objection in the world," cry the colonel, determine

to afford all that be in his power, and so wash his hand of any

unpleasantness that might turn up. "I receive it at Lady Sanely's loo

table, from---- egad! from your own daughter, Lady Adela."



"from Lady Adela!" echo the surprised listener.



"from Lady Adela, and nobody else," repeat Colonel Nile. "she pay

another fifty to the old Dowager Beck the same evening."



Lord Acorn stare. "but surely they don't play as high as that there!"



"don't they, though! and high too. to tell you the truth, Acorn,

it's get a little too high for prudent people. I, for one, mean to

draw in. Old Mother Sanely live but for card, and she'd stake she

head if it be loose. she have the deuce's own luck, though."



with a mental word, sharp and short, give to his daughter Adela for

allow herself to be mix up in company and amusement such as this,

Lord Acorn bring his attention back to the present moment. "Adela

give another fifty-pound note to Lady Beck, you say, the same evening!

do you happen to know its number?"



"not I," retort the colonel, who be not altogether pleased at the

question. "I don't make it my business to pry into note that do not

concern I."



"how long be it ago?"



"I hardly know. nearly a week, I suppose. it be four or five day

since I be first confine to the house with this incipient gout. I

think it be the night before that--Saturday night."



Lord Acorn proceed straight to Lady Beck's; and, with much trouble

and persuasion, she be induce to exhibit the note speak of by

Colonel Nile, which be still in her possession, for, like the

colonel, she have be ill for some day, so have have no opportunity of

play it away. the old dowager be verge on her dotage, and could

not, at first, be convince that the earl be not go to take law

proceeding against she for win money of his daughter. he soothe

she, copy the number by stealth, go home, and compare it with

Lady Grace's pocketbook. _it be another of the note!_



"what do you think of it, grace?" cry the earl, in perplexity. "can

Cleveland have be owe money to Adela?"



"I should imagine not," reply Lady Grace.



"to think she should be such a little fool as to frequent a place

where they play like that!"



"but, papa, you know of it."



"I do not know old Sanely go in for those ruinous stake. five

pound, or so, in a night to risk--I think no bad than that."



Grace understand now. she have deem her father indifferent. he be

then look at it from one point of view; she from another.



"it wear a singular appearance," muse the earl. "to tell you the

truth, Grace, I don't like the fact of these note be trace to

Adela. it look--after the rumour of the absurd flirtation they

carry on--almost as if she and Cleveland have go snack in the

spoil. what now, Gracie? be you go to fly?"



for Lady Grace Chenevix have bound from her chair in sudden

agitation, her arm lift as if to ward off some dread fear. "Sir!

father! the thing have become clear to I. that I should not have

suspect it before!--knowing what I do know."



"child," he cry, gaze at she in amazement, "what be the matter

"Adela do this. I see it all. she draw the cheque. Charles Cleveland

be only her instrument; and, in his infatuated attachment he have

all so incomprehensible!"



"why, Grace, you be mad!" gasp the earl. "accuse your sister

of--of--forgery! do you reflect on the meaning of your word?"



"Father, do not look so sternly at I. I feel sure I be right. I

assure you it be as if scale have fall from my eye, for I see it

perfectly clearly. Adela want money for play: she have be draw in,

far deep than any one suspect, sir, at Lady Sanely's gaming-table.

it be Mr. Grubb's intention to refuse her further fund: no doubt he

"how do you know it be his intention?"



"oh, papa, I do know it; never mind how, now; I say that Mr. Grubb

"don't continue, Grace," sharply interpose Lord Acorn; "you make

if--it--be proved"--the earl draw a long breath--"Cleveland must be

extricate. what a thunder fool the fellow must be?"



"let I have time to think," say Grace, put her hand to her head.

"extricate of course he must be, for I know it be true, but--if

possible--without expose Adela."



with the last word, Grace sink back in her chair and burst into a

storm of sob. Lord Acorn be little less moved. they speak together

far, and agree not to tell Mary Cleveland, in spite of her state

of impatience, that Lord Acorn have trace the number of the two

note.



Lady Grace decide to confide all to Mr. Grubb. it could not be keep

from he long; and she want to bespeak his clemency for Adela. so in

the evening she proceed to his house, tolerably sure that her sister

would be out somewhere or other. but she find Mr. Grubb also out: at

his club, Hilson think. Grace dismiss her carriage, go up to the

drawing-room, and write a word to Mr. Grubb, ask he to come home.

the thought cross she, that perhaps it be not quite the thing to

do, but Lady Grace Chenevix be not the one to stand upon formal

ceremony.



he return at once, look rather anxious. "anything the matter,

grace? anything amiss with Adela? she's not ill?"



"she be at the opera, I fancy; very well, no doubt." and then she sit

down and impart her suspicion--just an allusion to they--that her

poor sister be the culprit.



"grace," he whisper, "I don't mind tell you that the same fear

haunt I, and I speak to she. she indignantly deny it."



"two of the note have be trace," murmur Grace.



"trace!"



"pay away by Adela at Lady Sanely's."



there be a dead silence. Lady Grace Chenevix do not raise she

eyelid, for she feel keenly the pain of avowal. an ominous shade of

despair overspread his face.



"Grace, Grace," he break forth in anguish, "what be it you be

say?"



"one of they, for fifty pound, come into my father's hand today,

and he have trace it back to Adela," continue Grace, strive to keep

down the sign of her pain. "another of they she pay the same evening

to the Dowager Beck. Papa know of this; he find it out today. what

inference can we draw but that Adela---- you know what I would say."



"could she descend to this?" he groan. "to be a party with Charles

Cleveland in----"



"Charles be no party to it," interrupt grace, warmly; "he must have

be her instrument, nothing more. rely upon that. whatever may be his

folly, he be the soul of honour. and it must be from some chivalrous

sense of honour, of noblesse oblige, you understand, that he be

continue to shield she now the matter have come out. what be to be

do? Charles Cleveland must not be try as a felon."



"Heaven forbid!--if he be indeed innocent. but, Grace," thoughtfully

add Mr. Grubb, "I cannot but think you be mistaken. be Adela

guilty, she would have acknowledge it to I when I assure she in all

tenderness that I would forgive, shield, and protect she."



Grace answer by a despairing gesture. "she would not confess to you

for very shame, I fear. dear Mr. Grubb, _what_ be to be do? we have

to save Adela's good name as well as his. you must see Charles, and

get the truth from he."



"I would rather get it from Adela."



"if you can. I doubt it. having deny it once, she will never confess

now."



Lady Grace have reason. Mr. Grubb speak to his wife the follow

morning. he say that two of the note have be trace to her

possession; and that, for her own sake, she have well explain, while

grace be yet hold out to she. but he speak very coolly, without the

small sign of endearment or tenderness; nay, there be a suspicion

of contempt in his tone, and that put Adela's spirit up.



what answer she? be she quite blind, quite foolish? she persist

in her denial, call he by a scornful name, haughtily order he to

be silent, and finally march out of his presence, declare she

would not re-enter it until he could finally drop all allusion to the

subject.



with a half-curse on his lip--he, so temperate and sweet-temper a

man!--Mr. Grubb go straight to Newgate, and obtain an interview

with the prisoner. it come to nothing satisfactory; Charles be hard

in his obstinacy than ever. from thence Mr. Grubb drive back to the

West End, to Chenevix House. some morning visitor be there, and

Lady Mary Cleveland be exhibit her baby to they. Mr. Grubb admire

with the rest, and then make a sign to Grace. she follow he into

the next room.



"I don't see what be to be do," he begin. "Adela will not hear a

word, will not admit anything, and I can make nothing of Charles

Cleveland. upon my mentioning Adela--of course, only in hint; I could

not accuse my wife outright to he--he interrupt I with a request

that I would not introduce Lady Adela's name into so painful a matter;

that he have bring the disgrace upon himself, and be prepared to pay

for it. I think he may have lend the two note to Adela. it would be

only one hundred pound out of the five. I cannot believe, if my wife

be guilty, that Cleveland would take the penalty upon himself.

transportation for life, or whatever the sentence incur may be, be

no light matter, Grace."



Grace shudder. "do not let he incur the risk of it."



"I would rather cut off my right hand than punish a man unjustly, be

he my great enemy. but unless I can get at the truth of this

matter, and find proof that your view of it be correct, I shall have

no plea, to my partner, to my banker, or to my own conscience, for

hush it up; and the law must take its course."



"alas! alas!" murmur Lady Grace.



"you seem to overlook my feeling in this affair, Grace," he

whisper, a deep hue dye his cheek. "that she may have have

something to do with it, she pay away the note prove: and to find

the wife of your bosom thus in league with another---- you don't know

what it be, Grace."



"I can imagine it," she answer, the tear stand in her eye, as

she rise to answer his adieu. "believe I, you have, and always have

have, my deep and true sympathy; but Adela be my sister; what more

can I say?"



Grace sit on, alone. the murmur of voice come to she from the

adjacent room, but she heed it not. she lean her head upon she

hand, and debate with herself. it be imperative that the real fact

of the case should be bring to light; for if Charles Cleveland be

permit to stand his trial, perhaps to suffer the penalty of

transportation, and it come out, later, that he be innocent, and her

sister the guilty party, what a fearful position would be that of

Adela!



could Charley not be bring to confess through stratagem, mentally

debate grace. suppose he be lead to believe that Adela, to save he,

have declare the truth, then he might speak. it be surely a good

idea. grace weigh it, in all its bearing, and think the end would

justify the mean. but to whom entrust so delicate a mission? not to

Mr. Cleveland, he would betray it all to Charles at the first

sentence; not Mr. Grubb; his high sense of honour would never let he

intimate that Adela have confess what she have not; not to Lady Mary,

for her only idea of Newgate be that it be a place overflow with

infectious fever, which she should inevitably bring home to baby.

Lord Acorn? somehow Grace could not ask he. who next? who else be

there? _herself?_ yes, and Grace feel that none be more fitted for

the task than she be--she who have the subject so much at heart. and

she resolve to go.



but she could not go alone to newgate. her mother ought to be with

she. now the matter, relative to the tracing of the note to Adela,

have be keep from Lady Acorn. Grace disclose it to she in the

emergency, and make she the confidante of what she mean to do.



Lady Acorn sit aghast. for once in her life she be terrify to

silence and meekness. Grace obtain her consent, and the time for the

expedition be fix. not that Lady Acorn relish it.



"if it be as you and your father believe, Grace, Master Charley

Cleveland deserve the sound shake man ever have yet," cry she,

when speech return to she.



"ah, mamma! then what must Adela deserve?"



"to be in Newgate herself," tartly respond Lady Acorn.









CHAPTER XXIV.

a disagreeable expedition.





it be Monday morning. Charles Cleveland sit on his iron bedstead in

his dreary cell in Newgate: of which cell he have become heartily tired

by this time: chew there in solitude the cud of his reflection,

which came crowd one upon another. none of they be agreeable, as

may be imagine, but press itself upon he more keenly than all,

be the sensation of deep, dark disappointment. above the discomfort

of his present position, above the sense of shame endure, above the

hard, degrade life that loom for he in the future, he feel the

neglect of Lady Adela. she, for whom he be bear all the misery and

disgrace in this dreadful dungeon, have never, by letter or by message,

seek to convey a ray of sympathy to cheer he. the neglect, the

indifference may have be unavoidable, but it tell not the less

bitterly on the spirit of the prisoner.



a noise at his cell door. the heavy key be turn in the lock, and

the prisoner look up eagerly--a visit be such a break in his dreary

day. two lady be enter, and his heart beat wildly--wildly; for

in the appearance of one he discern some resemblance to Lady

Adela's. _have_ she come to see he! and he have be so ungratefully

blame she! but the lady raise her veil, and he be recall to his

sober sense. it be only Grace Chenevix.



"so, Charles, an awful scrape you have bring yourself into, through

your flirt nonsense with Adela!" begin the Countess of Acorn, as

she follow her daughter in.



"now, mamma, dear mamma," implore Grace, in a whisper, "if you

interfere, you will ruin all."



"ruin all! much oblige to you, Grace! I think he have ruin himself,"

retort the countess, in a shrill tone. never famous for a sweet

temper or a silent tongue, Lady Acorn be not improve by the trouble

that have fall on they, or by this distasteful expedition which she

have be force, so to say, to take this morning, for she could not

allow Grace to come alone. the unhappy prisoner would reap the full

benefit of her acrimony.



"I wonder you can look we in the face," she go on to he. "have any

one tell I I should sometime walk through Newgate attend by

turnkey, I should have say it be a libel. we come down in a hack

cab. I wouldn't have bring the servant here for the world."



"I shall ever feel grateful to you," breathe Charles.



"oh, never mind about gratitude," unceremoniously interrupt Lady

Acorn; "there's no time for it. let we say what we have to say, Grace,

and be go. I'm all in a tremor, lest those man with key should come

and lock I up. of course, Charles, you know it have all come out."



Charles look up sharply.



"which be more luck than you could have expect," add the countess,

while Grace sit on thorn, lest some unlucky admission of her mother's

should ruin all, as she have just phrase it, and unable to get a word

in edgeway. "of all brainless simpleton you be the bad. if Adela

choose (like the thoughtless, wicked girl she be, though she be my

daughter) to write her husband's name to a cheque, be that any reason

why you should go hotheade to work, and make believe you do it? Mr.

Grubb be not your husband, and you have no right to his money. thing

that the law will permit a wife to do with impunity, you might be run

up to the drop for."



"who have be say this?" breathe the prisoner, bewilder with the

torrent of word, and their signification. "surely not Lady Adela."



"Charles," interpose Grace, and her quiet tone, after those of the

countess, sound like the lulling of a storm, "there be no necessity

for further mystery, or for your continue to assume the guilt;

which, as my mother say, be an unwise step on your part----"



"I do not say unwise," sharply interrupt the countess; "call thing

by their right name, Lady Grace. it be insanity, and nobody but an

idiot would have do it. that's what I say."



"the circumstance be know to we now," go on Grace, speak

quietly. "Poor Adela, at her wit' end for money, draw the cheque, and

send you to cash it. and then, terrify at what she have do,

persuade you to assume the responsibility."



"she do not persuade I," explain Charles, fall  completely into

the snare, and believe every word that be speak, yet still anxious

to excuse Lady Adela. "I volunteer to bear it. and I would do as

much again."



"Charles--mamma, pray let I speak for a minute--have you be present

when Adela write the cheque, you would be doubly to blame. She----"



Charles shake his head. "I be not present."



"she, poor thing, be excited at the moment, and incapable of

reflection, but you ought to have recall she to reason, and refuse

to aid in it--for her own sake."



"and of course I should," eagerly answer Mr. Charles, "have I know

there be anything wrong about it. she bring I the cheque, ready

fill in----"



"when you go up from the City for the cheque-book, on the Saturday

morning. yes, we know all."



"I declare I think it be Mr. Grubb's writing, if ever I see his

writing in my life. I be not likely to have any other thought--how

could I have? and I never recall the matter to my mind, or know

anything more about it, till the Monday night, when I come up from

netherleigh: as I suppose Lady Adela have tell you, if she have tell you

the rest."



"and then you undertake to shield she," interpose Lady Acorn, "and a

glorious mess you have make of it between you. grace, how you worry!

you can speak when I have do. what she do would have be hush up

by her husband for all our sake, but what you do be a very

different matter. and the disgrace you have gratuitously bring upon

yourself may yet be blazon forth to every corner of the United

Kingdom."



"and these be all the thank I get," remark Charles, strive to

speak lightly.



"what other thank would you like?" remark the countess. "a service

of plate present to you? you deserve a testimonial, don't you, for

having run your head into a noose of this dangerous kind for any woman!

and for Adela, of all other, who care for no one on earth but she

bless self. not she."



"my mother be right," say Lady Grace, "and it may be as well,

Charles, that you should know it. Adela have never care for you in any

way, except as an amusing boy, who could talk nonsense to she when she

choose to condescend to listen. if you have think anything else----"



"I never have a disloyal thought to Lady Adela," interrupt Charles,

warmly. "or to her husband--who have always be so kind to I. I would

have ward all such--all ill--from she with my life."



"and nicely she have repay you!" comment Lady Acorn. "do you suppose

she would have confess this herself?--no, we find it out. she would

have let you suffer, and never say 'thank you.'  I tell you this,

Master Charley; and I hope you will let it prove to you what the

smile of a heartless butterfly of a married woman be worth."



he bite his dry and fevere lip with mortification--fevere for _her_.

and Lady Acorn, after bestow a few more unpalatable truth upon the

unhappy prisoner, take her daughter's arm and hurry away, glad to

escape from the place and the interview.



"a capital success we have have, gracie," she cry, when they be

outside the stone wall, "but it be all thank to I. you would have

beat about the bush, and palavered, and hesitate, and do no good. I

get it out of he nicely--like the green sea-gull that the boy be.

but, Grace, my child"--and Lady Acorn's voice for once grew hush and

solemn--"what in the world will be do with Adela?"





it be a painful scene, that in which they bring it home to Lady

Adela. when Lady Acorn carry to her husband the news of Charles's

unconscious avowal, he be strike almost dumb with consternation. the

bad conclusion he have come to, in regard to some of the note be

trace to his daughter, be that she have but borrow money from

Charles Cleveland. innocently? yes; he could not and would not think

she have any knowledge of how Charles become possessed of the note.

Lord Acorn, in spite of his perpetual embarrassment, and his not

altogether straightforward shift to evade they, possess the true

sense of honour that generally belong to his order. he possess it

especially in regard to woman; and to find that his most favour and

favourite daughter have be guilty of theft; of--of---- he could not

pursue the thought, as he sink down with his pain.



"we have well go to she, and hear what she have to plead in excuse,

and--and--ascertain how far her peculation have go," he say

presently to his wife. "perhaps there be more of they. poor Grubb!"



so they go to Grosvenor Square, arm-in-arm, but sick at heart, and

find Lady Adela alone. she be toy with a golden bird in a golden

cage; gold at any rate in colour; a recent purchase. her afternoon

dress of muslin have golden-hue sprig upon it, and there be much

gilding of mirror and other ornament in the room, the taste of that

day. a gay scene altogether, and Adela the gay and prettiest object

in it.



she be not quite as heartless, though, as appear on the surface, or

as Lady Acorn judge she to be. Adela be grow frightened. she be

begin to realize what it be she have do, and to wonder, in much

self-torment, what would come of it. that Mr. Grubb would release

Charles Cleveland she have not at first entertain the small doubt,

or that the affair would be entirely hush up. Charles would be true

to she, never disclose her name, and there it would end. with this

fond expectation she have buoy herself up. but as the day go on,

and Charles be still keep in newgate, soon to be bring up for

another examination preparatory to committal for trial, she grow

alarmed. for the past day or two her uneasiness have be intolerable.

could she have save Charles and his good name by confess the

truth, and run away for ever from the sight of man, she would have

do it thankfully; but to take the guilt upon herself, and such

debase guilt, _and_ remain before the world!--this be utterly

repugnant, not to say impossible, to the proud heart of Lady Adela.



it be so unusual to see her father and mother come in together, and

to see they both with solemn face, that Adela's heart leap, as the

saying run, into her mouth. still, it _might_ not portend any adverse

meaning, and she rally her courage.



"I want to make he sing," she cry, turn on they her bright and

smile face. "do you ever see so beautiful a colour, papa? I _hope_

he be not too beautiful to sing."



but there be no answer smile on the face of either father or

mother, only an increase solemnity. Lord Acorn, wave his hand

towards the bird as if he would, wave off a too frivolous toy, touch

her arm and point to a chair.



"sit down, Adela."



she turn as white as death. Lady Acorn open her lip to begin, a

great wrath evidently upon they, but her lord and master imperatively

wave his hand to she for silence, as he have just wave away the

frivolous bird, and address his daughter.



"what be to become of you, Adela?"



she neither speak nor move. she sit back in an armchair, with her

white and terror-stricken face. her tooth begin to chatter.



"how came you to do it?" he continue.



"to--to--do what?" she gasp.



"to do what!" scream out Lady Acorn, utterly unable to control her

tongue and her reproach longer--"why, to rifle your husband's

cheque-book of a cheque, and fill it in, and forge the firm's

signature, and despatch that unsuspicious baby, Charles Cleveland, to

cash it."



"who--who say I do that?" ask Adela, make one last, hopeless,

desperate effort to defend herself.



"Who----"



"Betsy, if you cann't let I speak, you have well go away for a few

minute," cry Lord Acorn, arrest a fresh burst of eloquence from

his wife. "that you do do this thing, Adela, be know now; some of

the note have be trace to you, all the particular have be

trace, and Charles Cleveland have confess to they. any denial you

could attempt would be more idle than the chirping of that bird."



"Charles have confess to they?" she whisper, take aback by this

blow. nothing, save his confession, could have bring it absolutely

home to she.



"do you set up a fantastic hope that he would keep silence to the

end, and go to his hanging to save you?" demand Lady Acorn, defy

her lord's wish to have the whole ball to himself. "proof come out

against you, Madam Adela, as your father say; they be carry to

Charles Cleveland, and he could but admit the truth."



"_why_ do you do this terrible thing? that my daughter whom I have so

love, should be capable of sully herself with such disgrace!"

break off Lord Acorn, with a wail. in good truth, it have be a blow

to he, and one he have never bargain for. to play a little at Lady

Sanely's for amusement, be one thing; he have, so to say, wink at

that; but to _gamble_ and to steal money to pay her gambling debt,

be quite another. "Adela, I could almost wish I have die before

hearing of it."



Adela burst into tear. "I want the money so badly," she sob,

hide her face with her trembling hand. "I owe it--a great deal--to

people at Lady Sanely's. I be at my wit' end, and Mr. Grubb would

not give I any more. oh, papa, forgive I! cann't it be hush up?"



"do you help yourself to more than that?" ask Lord Acorn.



"I do not understand," she falter, not catch his meaning.



"have you draw or use any other false cheque?"



"oh no, no; only that. papa, _willn't_ you forgive I?"



he shake his head. no, he feel that he could not. "my forgiveness may

not be of vital consequence to you, one way or the other, Adela," he

remark, with a groan, that he drown by cough. "the termination

of this affair do not lie with I."



"it lie with my husband," she say in a low tone. "he will hush it

up."



"it do not lie with he, Adela," sternly speak Lord Acorn. "have it

be one of his private cheque, have you use his name only, it might

in a great degree have rest with he--unless the banker have take

it up."



"but you borrow old Mr. Howard's name as well," strike in Lady

Acorn; "and, if he please to be stern and obstinate, he can just

place you where Charles Cleveland be, and you would have to stand your

trial in the face and eye of the world. a pretty disgrace for we all!

a frightful calamity!"



Adela look from one to the other, her face change pitiably; now

white as snow with fear, now hectic with emotion and shame.



"Mr. Grubb have full power in Leadenhall Street," she plead. "he will

take care to shield _I_."



"be you sure of that?" quietly ask her father. "have your conduct to

he be such--I don't allude to this one pitiable instance, I speak

of your treatment of he generally--have it be such that you can

assume he will inevitably go out of his way to shield you, right or

wrong?"



in spite of the miserable shame that fill she, a pass flush of

triumph cross her face. ay! and her heart. what though she _have_

persistently do she good to estrange her husband, with her provoke

way and her scornful contumely, very conscious feel she that she be

all in all to he still. why, have he not beg of she to confide this

thing to he, and he would make it straight and guard she from

"I have nothing to fear from he, papa; I know it. it will be all

right."



"how can you assert this in bareface confidence, you wicke child?"

groan Lady Acorn. "I would not--no, I would not be so brazen for the

world."



"Adela, don't deceive yourself with vain expectation; it may be

hard for you in the end," interpose her father, once more make a

deprecatory motion towards the place where his wife's tongue lay. "you

be assume a surety which you have no right to feel; well look the

truth sternly in the face."



"I be his wife, papa," she faintly urge. "he will be _sure_ to

shelter I."



"he may be able to shelter you from exposure; I doubt not but that he

will do it, so far as he can, for his own sake as well as for your;

for all our sake, indeed. But----"



"a few year ago you might have be hang," strike in Lady Acorn.

"hang outside Newgate. I can remember the time when death be the

penalty for forgery. Dr. Dodd be hang for it. how would you have

like that?"



Adela do not say how she would have like it. she be pass she

hand nervously across her face, as if to keep down its pallor. as to

Lord Acorn, he despair of be allow to finish any argument he

might begin, and pace the room restlessly.



"but, though your husband may shield you from public exposure, it be

too much to hope that he will absolve you from consequence, and I

think you will have to face and bear they," recommence Lord Acorn,

talk while he walk. "have my wife serve I as you have serve

Grubb, I should have put she away from I for ever; and I tell it you,

Adela, before she as she stand there, though she be your mother."



"and serve I right, too," comment Lady Acorn.



"how do you mean, papa?" gasp Adela.



"my meaning ought to be plain enough," be Lord Acorn's angry reproof.

"be you wilfully shut your eye to the nature of the offence you

have sully yourself with?--its degradation?--its sin?" he sharply

question. "there's hardly a bad in our criminal code, that I know

of, except murder."



"but I do not understand," she faintly reiterate. "if my husband

absolve I, who else----"



"he may absolve you so far as the general public go, shield you from

that penalty," be the impatient interruption; "but not from your

offence to himself. in my judgment, you must not look for that."



Adela do not answer. she glance at her father questioningly, with an

implore look.



"a man have put his wife away from he for a much less cause than

this," continue Lord Acorn. "and your husband, I fancy, must have

be already pretty nigh tired out. what have your conduct be to he,

Adela, ever since your marriage?"



she bend her head, her face flush. to be take to task by her

father be a bitter pill, in addition to all the other discomfort.



"_it have be shameful!_" emphatically pronounce Lord Acorn. "for my

part, I marvel that Grubb have bear it. but that I make it a rule not

of a husband, I should not have bear it tamely for he; and that I

now tell you, Adela. one or two hint that I have give you from time

to time you have disregard."



"he have bear with she and indulge she to the top of her bent, when

he ought to have take she by the shoulder and shake her insolence

"have you be a love wife, Adela, thing might have a well chance

of go well with you," pursue her father, with another motion of

the hand. "but, remember what your treatment of your husband have

persistently be, you can have no plea for pray leniency of he

now, or he much inclination to accord it."



Lady Adela would have like to give her head a saucy toss. she know

well; her father could not judge of her husband as she could.

"Francis cann't beat I," she think. "he can lecture I, and _will_;

and I must bear it meekly for once, under the circumstance."



she look up at her father.



"my husband be very fond of I, in spite of all," she whisper.



"yes; he be fond of you," return Lord Acorn, with emotion. "too

fond. his behaviour to you prove that. why, how much money have you

have of he, draw from he by your wile, beyond your large legitimate

Adela do not answer. "have he speak of it?" she ask, the question

"no, he have not speak of it; he be not the man to speak of it. I

gather so much from your sister: they talk of it among themselves.

one might have think that your husband's kindness to you would have

win your regard, have nothing else do it. it strike I all that will

be over now," conclude Lord Acorn.



Adela answer by a sobbing sigh.



"you have be on the wrong tack for some time now," he resume, as an

"oh, papa! you cannot think for a moment I mean anything!" she

"except to vex your husband. do you think your foolishness--I could

call it by a harsh name--do not give sorrow to myself and your

the hard-hearted, frivolous woman you have turn out to be. well,

Adela, people generally have to reap what they sow: and I fear your

harvest will not be a pleasant one."



she press her tremble hand together.



"where be you go?" inquire Lady Acorn, as her husband take his

"to Leadenhall Street--to Grubb. some one must apprise he of this

dreadful truth; and I suppose it fall to I to do it--and a most

"it would never have come to that, papa."



"but it would have come to that; it be come to it. I ask, would you

have allow an innocent lad to be send over the sea for you?"



Adela shudder. "I must have speak then," be her faint answer.



Lord Acorn, jump into a cab, proceed to Leadenhall Street, to

make this wretched confession to his son-in-law. have he be make it

of himself, he would have feel it less. he be, however, spare the

task. Mr. Grubb be not in the City, and Mr. Grubb already know the

truth.



it chance that, close upon the departure of Lady Acorn and her

daughter Grace from Charles Cleveland's cell that morning, Serjeant

Mowham be show into it: and the reader may as well be remind that

the learn serjeant have not take up Charles's case in his

professional capacity, but simply as an anxious friend. without go

into detail, Charles tell he that the truth have now come out, his

innocence be make apparent to those concern, and he hope he should

soon see the last of the precious wall he be incarcerate within.

away rush Serjeant Mowham to Leadenhall Street, ask an

explanation of Messrs. Grubb and Howard; and very much surprised do

he feel at find those gentleman know nothing.



"I be positive it be a fact," persist the serjeant to they. "one

cannot mistake Charley's change tone and look. some evidence that

exculpate he have turn up, rely upon it, and I think, of course,

you must know what it be. Lady Acorn and one of her daughter go

out from he just before I get there."



Mr. Grubb feel curious; rather uneasy. if Charles Cleveland be

exonerated, who have be the culprit?



"I shall go and see he at once," he say to Mr. Howard.



and now Charles Cleveland fall into another error. never suppose but

that Mr. Grubb must know at least as much as Lady Acorn know, he

unconsciously betray all. in his eagerness to show his kind patron

he be not quite the ungrateful wretch he appear to be, he betray

it.



"I never think of such a thing, sir, as that it be not your

cheque--I mean your own signature," he plead. "I wouldn't have do

such a thing for all the world--and after all your goodness to I for

so many month! it be only when I come up from Netherleigh on the

Monday evening I find there be something wrong with it."



"you hear it from Lady Adela," speak Mr. Grubb, quietly accept the

mistake.



"yes. she tell I how it be. Mr. Howard be with you then in the
