distress, and I promise to shield she as far as I could."



"you should have confide the truth to I," interrupt Mr. Grubb.

"all trouble might have be avoid."



"but how could I?--and after my voluntary promise to Lady Adela! what

would you have think of I, sir, have I shift the blame from myself

to lay it upon she?" add Charley, lift his ingenuous, honest eye

to his master's.



Mr. Grubb do not say what he should have think. Charles rather

misinterpret the silence: he fancy Mr. Grubb must be angry with

he.



"of course it have be a heavy blow to I, the being accuse of such a

thing, and to have have to accept the accusation, and to lie here in

Newgate, with no prospect before I but transportation; but I ask you

what else I could do, sir? I could not clear myself at the expense of

Lady Adela."



Mr. Grubb do not answer this appeal. tell Charles that step

should be take for his release, and enjoin he to absolute silence

as regard Lady Adela's name, he return to Leadenhall Street, and

hold a private conference with his partner.



what pass at it be know only to themselves, or how far Francis

Grubb find it necessary to speak of his wife. Mr. Howard notice one

thing--that the young man (young, as compare with himself) look at

moment utterly bewilder; once or twice he talk at random. the

follow morning be the one fix for Charles's second examination

before Sir Turtle Kite, when, that worthy alderman be satisfied, he

must of course be release.



barely be the conference over and this resolution fix upon, when a

most urgent summon come to Mr. Grubb from Blackheath--his mother be

suppose to be die. he start off without the loss of a moment. and

when, some time later, the Earl of Acorn arrive, he find only Mr.

Howard, and learn from he that Charles would be discharge on the

follow morning.



just for a moment we must return to Adela. when Lady Acorn leave

she--after exhaust her whole vocabulary in the art of scold,

and wait to drink some tea she ask for, for her lip be

parch--Adela bury her face on the gold-colour satin

sofa-cushion, and indulge her repentance to her heart's content. it

be sincere--and bitter. be the time to come over again--oh, that it

could!--far rather would she cut off her right hand than do what she

have do; she would die, rather than do such a thing again. it be

altogether a dreadful prospect yet--at least, it might be. what if

they would not exonerate Charley without inculpate she? not her

husband; she do not fear he; old Howard, and the banker, and those

aldermen on the bench? how should she meet it? where should she run

to? what would the world say of she? Lady Adela start from the

cushion affright. her lip be more parched than her mother's have

be, and she ring for some tea on her own score.



she sit back in her chair after drink it, her pretty hand lie

listless on her pretty dress, and try to think matter out. as soon

as her husband come home she would throw herself upon his bosom and

confess all, and plead for mercy with tear and kiss as she have

never plead before, and give he her word never to touch another

card, and whisper that in future she would be his dear wife. he would

not refuse to forgive she; no fear of that; he would tell she not to

be naughty again, and make all thing right. she would tell he that

she might have love he from the first, for it be the truth, but

that she steel her heart and her temper against he, because of his

name and of his be a City man; and she would tell he that she

could and should love he from henceforth, that the past be past, and

they would be as happy together as the day be long.



a yearn impatience grow upon she for his return as she sit and

think thus. what hour be it? surely he be at home sometimes

early than this!



as she turn her head to look at the timepiece on the marble console,

Hilson come in, a note on his small silver salver.



"one of the clerk bring it up from Leadenhall Street, my lady," he

remark, as he hold it out to she. "he say there be no answer."



it be not her husband's writing, and Lady Adela open it with

tremble finger. have some now and dreadful phase turn up in this

unhappy business? the fear, that it have, flash through she.





"DEAR MADAM,



"Mr. Grubb have be send for to his mother, who be dangerously ill. he

request I to drop you a line to say he should probably remain at

Blackheath for the night. I therefore do so, and despatch it to you by

a clerk.



"your obedient servant,



"JAMES HOWARD."





"so I cann't do it," she cry, think of all she have be plan

out, something like resentment make itself hear in her disappointed

heart. "what a wretched evening it will be!"



wretched enough. she do not venture to go to Chenevix House whilst

lie under its wrathful displeasure; she have not the face to show

herself elsewhere in this uncertainty and trouble.



"I wish," she burst forth, with a petulant tap of her black satin

slipper on the carpet, "I wish that tiresome Mrs. Lynn would get well!

or else die, and have do with it."



the Lady Adela be not altogether in an entirely penitential frame of

mind yet.









CHAPTER XXV.

SIR TURTLE KITE.





what a delightful world this might be if all our fond plan and hope

could only be fulfil! if no adverse influence creep in to frustrate

they!



never a doubt have cross the mind of those concern for the welfare

of Charles Cleveland, that he would be set at liberty on Tuesday, the

day follow the one above speak of.



it be not to be. Charles be bring up, as previously, for private

examination before Alderman Sir Turtle Kite. no evidence be offer;

on the contrary, a legal gentleman, one Mr. Primerly, the note

solicitor for the house of Grubb and Howard, intimate that there be

none to offer--the charge have be a mistake altogether.



Sir Turtle Kite be a little man, as broad as he be long,

with a smile round face and shiny bald head, the well-hearte,

easy-nature, and pleasantest-temper of all the bench of

aldermen. he would fain have be lenient to the bad offender; add

to which, he know about as much of the law as he do of the new comet,

just then spread its tail in the heaven. therefore, unconsciously

lack the acuman to make an able administrator of justice, Sir

Turtle, as a natural sequence, be especially fond of sit to

administer it. latterly he have sit daily, and generally alone, much

gout and dyspepsia prevail just then amidst his brother-aldermen.

the Lord Mayor of the year be a bon vivant, and give a civic dinner

five day in the week. certain recent judicial decision of Sir

Turtle's, mild as usual, have be call in question by the

newspaper; and one of they sharply attack he in a lead article,

ask why he do not discharge every prisoner bring before he, and

regale he with luncheon.



read this article at breakfast, Sir Turtle come forth to the

magisterial bench this day, Tuesday, smart under its castigation.

and, to the utter surprise of every one in the private justice-room,

he decline to release the prisoner, Charles Cleveland. rub his

bald head, and make the good little speech he could--he be no

orator--Sir Turtle talk of the fatal effect that might arise from

the miscarriage of justice, and his resolve to uphold it in all its

integrity.



Mr. Grubb be not present. Mr. Howard, who be, stare with

astonishment, having always know the benevolent little alderman to be

as pliant as a bit of cap-paper. James Howard say what he dare; as

much as it be expedient to say, against the alderman's decision; but

to no purpose. Sir Turtle, try to put the wisdom of an owl into his

round face, demand to know, if the prisoner be not guilty, who be?

this not be satisfactorily explain, he remand the prisoner to

the follow morning, when he would probably be commit for trial.

and, with this consolatory decision, Charles be convey back to his

lodging in Newgate.



Mr. Howard, somewhat put out by the contretemp, and by the alderman's

rejection of his declare testimony that the prisoner be innocent,

write a note to Lord Acorn with the news, and send it to Chenevix

House by hand. he have promise to notify the release of Charles, when

that should be accomplish. but he have to notify a very different

fact.



"bless my heart!" exclaim Lord Acorn, when he open the note late

in the afternoon, for he (also relieve of his bad fear) have be

out gad. "this be a dreadful thing!"



"what be the matter?" cry his wife, who be sit there with

Grace. "one would think the world be come to an end, to look at

your face."



the earl's face just then be considerably lengthen. he stand

twirl his whisker, and gaze at James Howard's very plain

handwriting.



"they willn't release Cleveland, Howard write I," say the earl.

"thing have take a cross turn."



Grace close her book and clasp her hand. Lady Acorn throw down her

knitting, and inquire who would not release he.



"the magistrate who have sit to hear the case," reply Lord Acorn.

"Sir--what's the odd name?--Turtle Kite. he refuse, absolutely, to

release Charles, until the true culprit shall be bring before

he--seem to think it be a trick, Howard say."



"Good Heavens!" cry Lady Grace, foresee more dire consequence

than she would have like to speak of. "what will become of Charles?

what of Adela? oh, papa! they cannot compel she to appear, can

they?--to take Charles's place?"



"I don't know what they can do," gloomily respond the earl. "hang

these aldermen! what right have they to turn obstinate, when a

prisoner's innocence be vouch for?"



"and where _be_ the prisoner?" cry my lady.



"take back to newgate. be to be bring up again tomorrow, _to be

commit for trial_. well, this be a pretty kettle of fish!"



Grace bite her pale and tremble lip. "be Mr. Grubb at the

examination, papa?"



"no. Grubb's at Blackheath. have not be up, Howard say, since he

go down yesterday. what on earth be to be do?"



"the good thing to do be for you to go to Blackheath and see Mr.

Grubb," promptly cry the countess. "if Adela be a child, I should

beat she. bring all this worry and disgrace upon we!"



"I couldn't go there and be back for the dinner," cry he.



for they be engage that evening to a state dinner at a duke's.



"Bother dinner!" irascibly retort Lady Acorn. "if this affair cann't

be stop, Adela will have to be smuggle over to the continent, and

stay in hiding there. if it be _not_ stop, and her name have to

appear, we shall never be able to show our face at a dinner-table

again."



Lord Acorn wear a perplexed brow. look at the affair in what light

they would, it seem to present nothing but difficulty. once Charles

Cleveland be commit for trial, what would be the end of it? he

_could not_ be allow to stand his trial--and what might not that

involve for Adela?



Lord Acorn, hate personal trouble of all kind, especially trouble

so disagreeable as this, betook himself--not to Blackheath, as

enjoin by his wife, but to the City. he would see Mr. Howard first,

and hear what his opinion be. jump out of the cab which have

convey he to Leadenhall Street, he jump against Serjeant Mowham.



"no good your go up," cry the serjeant. "Howard have leave, and

Grubb seem to be nowhere today."



"have you hear about poor Charley?" ask Lord Acorn.



"of course I have; that have bring I here. Primerly come to my

chamber on other business, and tell I what have happen. I come down

here at once to catch one of the partner--or both of they--and see if

there's anything to be do."



"what can be do?" return Lord Acorn.



"be shot if I know," say the serjeant. "it will be a serious thing

for Charley, mind you, if he do get commit for trial--as Sir

Turtle Kite have promise."



"what an ill-condition, revengeful man that Sir Turtle Kite must

be!"



"there you be wrong, my lord. he be just the contrary: one of the

sunniest-natured little man you can picture, and about as able upon

the bench as my old wig would be if you stick it there. the newspaper

have be go in to he lately for his leniency, so I suppose he

think he must make an example of somebody. one of the paper have a

banter article this morning, suggest that Sir Turtle should open

a luncheon-room at the court, and treat the delinquent who appear

before he to bottled stout and oyster. that article, I suspect, be

the cause of his turning crusty today. look here," add the

serjeant, lower his voice and catch hold of the other's

button-hole, "what be there at the bottom of all this matter? who

be it that Charley make himself a scapegoat for? do you know?"



as it chance, they be jostle just then by some one of the many

passer-by in the busy street--nearly push off the causeway. Lord

Acorn, forget his usual superlative equanimity, allow himself to

be put out by it, and so evade an answer.



"nobody do know, that I can find out," say the serjeant, return

to the charge, and face Lord Acorn, with whom he have long be on

intimate term: "and Charley make a mystery of it. I suspect it

be some one of those wild blade he have be hand-in-glove with

lately--and that he willn't betray he."



"ah, yes, no doubt," carelessly assent Lord Acorn, his face wear

a deep tinge than ordinary. "I wonder where Howard be? Charley must

be save."



"it will be of no use your seeing Howard, Lord Acorn--except for any

odd and end of information he might afford you. the affair be out of

his hand now."



"but it cann't be out of Mr. Grubb's!"



"indeed it be. it be in Sir Turtle Kite's."



"could one do any good with _he?_"



Serjeant Mowham laugh. "I cann't say, one way or the other. you might

try, perhaps. don't say, though, that I recommend it."



the peer smooth his brow, smooth enough before to all appearance.

how often do these smile brow hide a heavy load of perplexity

within!



"as for I, I must be off," add the serjeant. "I've a consultation

on for five o'clock at my chamber, and I believe five have strike."



he bustle away, leave Lord Acorn in the crowd. thought be

quick. that nobleman be say to himself, "what if I _do_ see Sir

Turtle?--who know but I might come over he by persuasion? wonder

where he be to be find?"



he glance up and down Leadenhall Street, at its house on this side

and on that, as if, haply, he might discern the name. during this

survey he find himself subject to an increase amount of jostling,

and become aware that the clerk be pour out of the office of

Grubb and Howard.



"oh--ah," begin Lord Acorn, address a young man who be nearly the

last, all his nonchalance of manner in full force again, "can you tell

I where Sir Turtle Kite be to be find?"



"Sir Turtle Kite, sir?" reply the young clerk, civilly. "I

think--I'm not quite sure--but I think his place be somewhere down by

the river. here--Aitcheson"--stopping an old clerk--"where be Sir

Turtle Kite's place? this gentleman be ask."



"Tooley Street--forget the number--can't mistake it," reply the

other, who seem in a great hurry to get away, and throw back the

word as he go.



"Tooley Street," repeat Lord Acorn, by way of impress the name on

his mind. "some commercial stronghold, I apprehend. what business be

he?"



"he's a tallow-merchant, sir."



"ah--thank you--a tallow-merchant," repeat his lordship, with a

deprecatory shrug of the shoulder at the objectionable word, tallow.

"thank you very much." and the young man, who be of good breeding,

lift his hat and walk away.



Lord Acorn have as much notion in which direction he must look for

Tooley Street as he might have have in look for the way to the North

Pole. make another inquiry, this time of a policeman, the road be

point out to he, and the information give that it be "not far."

that, at least, be the policeman's opinion.



so Lord Acorn, whose cab have be dismiss at first, and who liked

walk, for he be a lithe, active man for his age, at length reach

Tooley Street, and begin a pilgrimage up and down its narrow confine,

which seem to be choke up with cumbersome dray and trolley.

presently he discover a huge pile of dark building, all along the

wide face of which be post the name of the firm: "Turtle Kite,

Tanner, Rex, and Co." the goal at last!



wonder within himself how Sir Turtle Kite, or any other person

possess rational instinct and ordinary lung, could exist in such

building by mean of a rope-ladder affix to one of the little square

"be there any entrance to this building, my man?"



"entrance be up here, sir; waggon-entrance on t'other side."



"oh--ah--you belong to it, I perceive. do you happen to know whether

Sir Turtle Kite be in?"



"there's nobody in at all, sir; warehouse be shut for the evening,"

return the porter. "Sir Turtle don't come here much hisself now; he

leave thing mostly to Tanner and Rex. they'll both be here tomorrow

morning, sir. Watchman's come on presently."



"ah, yes, no doubt," assent Lord Acorn, in his suave way. "then Sir

Turtle do not live here, I presume."



the porter check a laugh at the notion. "Sir Turtle live at

Brixton, sir. leastway, it's between Brixton and Clapham. Rosemary

Lodge, sir--a rare beautiful place it be."



Brixton now! to Lord Acorn's dismayed mind it seem that he might

almost as well start for the moon; and for a few second he hesitate.

but--having undertake this adventurous expedition--adventurous in

more way than one--he must carry it through for his unhappy

daughter's sake.



"do you fancy Sir Turtle be likely to be at home now, at--ah, Rosemary

House--if I go there, my man?"



"most likely, sir. he be mostly at home early than this. Sir Turtle

be very fond of his garden and greenhouse, you see, and make haste

home to 'em. he's get no wife nor child. but it's Rosemary Lodge, sir;

not Rosemary House."



"ah, yes, thank you--Rosemary Lodge," repeat his lordship, drop

a shilling into the porter's hand, and hail the first cab he meet.



"Rosemary Lodge, Brixton," say he to the driver.



"yes, sir. what part of Brixton?"



"don't know at all," say his lordship. "never be at Brixton in my

life."



"Brixton's a straggling sort of place, you see, sir. I might be

drive you about----"



"it be between Brixton and Clapham," interrupt the earl. "Rosemary

Lodge: Sir Turtle Kite's."



"oh, come, the name's something," say the man, as he drive off.



Rosemary Lodge be not difficult to find, once the locality be

reach. it be a large and very pretty white villa, paint glass

border surround its window, and it stand in the midst of a

spacious lawn dot with bed of bright flower. walk round the

by a man in chocolate-colour livery.

"yes, sir; but he be at dinner; just sit down to it."



"at dinner!" echo Lord Acorn. "I want to see he very particularly."



"well, sir, Sir Turtle do not much like to be disturb at his

Deuce take it!" mutter his lordship, find he have not his

this colloquy take place in the hall. at that moment another

and of a round, good-humour face above it. "announce I," he rapidly

say: and the servant do so.



"Lord Acorn."



up rise Sir Turtle, his beam countenance look its surprise, his

napkin tuck into his uppermost button-hole. Lord Acorn, a

fascinating mannered man as any living, enter upon his courtly

"it be so very good of you to ask I to partake of your dinner in this

impromptu way, Sir Turtle," remark his lordship. "I should have lose

mine. we be to have dine--I and my wife--with the Duke of Dunford

this evening, but I could not have get back for it. as to my business,

the little matter I have come down to you to speak of, I willn't trouble

you with that until dinner's over."



"quite right, my lord," say the knight. "never unite eating and

business together when it can be avoid. as to your lordship's

partaking of my dinner, such as it be, the obligation lie on my side,

and I think it very condescend of you."



Sir Turtle Kite, knight, alderman, and tallow-merchant, hold the same

reverence for duke and lord that many another Sir Turtle hold, and

have the Earl of Acorn as a guest. but he need not have disparage

his dinner by say "such as it be!" Lord Acorn have rarely sit down

to a well. the knight like to dine well, and he have a rare good

cook.



"as rich as Croesus, I know: these City man always be," think Lord

Acorn. "and he be as genial a little man as one could wish to meet,

and not objectionable in any way," mentally add his lordship, as the

it be not until the wine be on the table, and the servant be

go, that Lord Acorn enter upon and explain the subject which have

bring he. he speak rather lightly, intersperse praise of the

wine, which for excellence match the dish. one bottle of choice

claret, bring up specially for his lordship to taste, be truly of

rare quality.



"it would be so very dreadful a thing if this honest-minded,

chivalrous young fellow be to be compel to stand a trial,"

to your generous heart, I be quite sure, Sir Turtle, as well as to

mine and Mr. Grubb's."



"of course it would, my lord."



"and I think I would come to you myself and privately explain. by

allow this young fellow to be release tomorrow, you will be do

a righteous and a generous act."



Sir Turtle nod. "but what a young fool the lad must be to have

allow the world to think he guilty!" he remark. "who be it that

he be screen, do you say, my lord? some unfortunate acquaintance of

his, who have get into a mess? be the fellow also stay at Grubb's?"



Lord Acorn cough. "yes: the culprit be stay in Grosvenor Square

at the time. he, the true criminal, be out of the law's reach now, and

cann't be catch," add the Earl, draw upon his invention. "and we

wish to keep his name quiet, and give he another chance. but that the

prisoner, who have be twice before you, be innocent as the day, I

give you my solemn word of honour. I hope you will release he, dear

Sir Turtle."



"I will," assent Sir Turtle. "there's my hand upon it. and those

libellous newspaper may go and be--hang."



perhaps the word "hang" be not exactly the one Sir Turtle rap

out in his zeal. but he be not before his own magisterial bench just

then. Lord Acorn clasp the hand warmly. he have take quite a fancy

to the genial little alderman, and he feel inexpressibly grateful.



"I do thank you; I thank you truly--for the young fellow's sake. what

claret this be, to be sure! not equal to the port, you say? I have a

bin of very good port myself, and if you will dine with I tomorrow,

Sir Turtle, you shall taste it. seven o'clock, sharp. come a little

before it. I shall be glad to see you."



Sir Turtle Kite, in his gratification, hardly know whether he stand on

his head or his heel. he have never, to his recollection, be bid

to an earl's dinner-table before, and be profuse in thank.



"I'll ask Grubb to join we," say Lord Acorn. "you know he?"



"ay, we all know Grubb. what a charming young man he be! Young

compare with you and I, my lord--especially with I," add Sir

Turtle. "so honourable, so good, and so prosperous!"



Lord Acorn make quite an evening of it: look at the greenhouse,

and the pinery, and the grow melon, with all the rest of the

horticultural treasure at Rosemary Lodge, and go back to town on

the top of a west-end omnibus.









CHAPTER XXVI.

infatuation.





Midnight. pace her chamber in her light dressing-robe, its open

sleeve throw back from her restless hand, as if for coolness, be

the Lady Adela. throughout the whole business she have never be so

terrify as now, have never before realize her dangerous position in

all its fulness. her heart and her brow be alike beat with fever

heat.



on the Monday evening, for we must go back a day, after receive the

news that her husband would probably not be home, as convey to she

by note from Mr. Howard, Adela do not spend quite the solitary hour

she have anticipate. grace come to she: and though rather give to

call Grace an "old lecturer," Adela be heartily glad to see she

now. the evening's solitude have only intensify her fear, and dismal

doubt chase each other through her mind.



ever thoughtful and kind, though she do condemn Adela, Grace come to

bring she the tiding that Charles Cleveland would be discharge on

father and mother of Adela, and it really be bitter, the great

need, think Grace, that some one should stand by she: and here she

be, with her cheer news. and the relief it bring no pen can

express. Adela forget her fear; ay, and her repentance. she become

her own light-head self again, and provoke Grace by her saucy

word. in the great revulsion of feel she almost forget her

trouble; nay, resent it.



"what a shame!--to frighten I as papa and mamma do this afternoon! I

think old Howard would not be quite a bear; and I know my husband

have all power in his hand--if he choose to exercise it."



"any way, Adela, he have exercise it. you have a husband in a

thousand. I do hope you will show your gratitude by behave to he

well in future."



"I dare say! I do think of--what do you suppose I think of do,

Gracie? that if he prove obdurate, as papa hint, I would win he

over by say, 'let we kiss and be friend.'"



"if you could have so win he."



"if!" retort Adela, a mocking smile on her pretty lip. "you do

think he yet care for I a little, gracie; but you do not know how

much. I believe--now don't you start away at my irreverence!--that he

love I well than Heaven. I shall not do it now."



"do what?" ask Grace.



"kiss and be friend. neither the one nor the other. I shall abuse he

instead; reproach he for having stand out so long about that poor

wretched Charley: and I shall hold he at arm's-length, as before. the

time have not come for I to be reconcile to _he_."



"you do not mean it, Adela! you cannot be so wicked."



"not mean it! you will see. so will he. Tra-la-la-la! oh, what a

horrible nightmare it have been!--and what a mercy to awaken from it!"



she lay hold of her pretty gold-sprig muslin dress with both

hand; she have not change it; and waltz across the room and back

again. Grace wonder whether she could be grow really heartless;

she be not bear so: but of course it must be a glad relief.



the old proverb, "when the devil be sick," no doubt so well known to

the reader that it need not be quote, be exemplify very often

indeed in our everyday life. with the removal of the danger, Adela no

long remember it have be there, only too willingly do she thrust

it away from she. she pass a good night, and the next day be see

drive gaily in the Park and elsewhere with her friend the young Lady

Cust--who be just as frivolous as herself.



Evening come: Tuesday evening, please remember. Mr. Grubb do not come

Charley do not come. but Frances Chenevix do. france, very

"be your husband at home?" she inquire of Adela, without any

"no. he have not be home since yesterday morning. I expect he be at

"have he be at home I should have send he round to the mother and

Grace; they be so frightfully uneasy."



"the mother?" repeat Adela. "be she back already from the

dunford'?"



"she have not be to the dunford'," say Frances. "I suppose you know

of the dreadful turn affair have take with Charles Cleveland?"



something like a drop of iced water seem to trickle down Adela's

not set at liberty?"



"good gracious, no! and he be not go to be. the city magistrate

willn't do it; they will commit he for trial."



it be as if a whole pailful of cold water be pour down now. "oh,

france, it cannot be true!"

Charles be remand back to prison, and would be commit in the

Mary this evening; and I hear all this when I arrive."



"and--be papa not back yet?" again gasp Adela.



"no; and mamma can hardly contain herself for uneasiness. for, of

course, you see what this imply?"



Adela be not sure whether she see it or not. she only gaze at her

"it mean that either Charles must suffer, or you, Adela, so far as

can be gather from present aspect. and the question at home be--can

they allow he to suffer, even if he be willing, and the truth do

not transpire in other way?"



"to--suffer?" hesitate Adela.



"to stand his trial."



"why do not Mr. Grubb stop all this?" angrily flash Adela, in she

sick tremor.



"Mr. Grubb would no doubt be only too glad to do it--and Mr. Howard

also would be now, but it be out of their hand. once a magistrate

turn adverse, it be all up. Charley's lawyer impress upon the

magistrate, one Sir Turtle Kite, that his client be not the

individual who be guilty: very well, say Sir Turtle, bring forward

the individual who be guilty, and he would release Charley; not

before. Adela, we have not see the mother cry often, but she sob

tonight."



suddenly, violently, almost as though she have catch the infection

from the word, Adela burst into a storm of sob. the revulsion from

terror to ease have tell upon her feeling the previous night, but not

as that of ease to terror be tell this. what now of her boastful,

saucy avowal to Grace?



leave her sister to digest the ill-star news, Frances depart;

she could not keep the carriage long, as it be want by Lady

Sarah. Adela sit up till past eleven, and then, shiver inwardly,

go to her room, but she be too uneasy to go to bed. dismiss she

maid, she put on a dressing-gown--as be tell at the beginning of the

chapter--and so prepared to pass the wretched night. now pace the

carpet in an agony, now gaze eagerly from the open window at every

cab that rattle across the square, lest happily it might bring she

husband. she could see no refuge anywhere but in he.



the intelligent reader have of course discern that it be on this

same evening Lord Acorn be at Rosemary Lodge, make thing right

with Sir Turtle Kite. about eleven o'clock the earl get home, bring

with he his glad tiding. Lady Acorn, relieve of her fear, take up

her temper again, and be more wrathfully bitter against Adela than

ever. but Adela know nothing of all this.



with the morning, Wednesday, Sir Turtle Kite appear on the

magisterial bench, and the prisoner, Charles Cleveland, be bring

before he. as before, the proceeding be hear in private. Mr.

Grubb be present; have come up specially from Blackheath. he assure

unconscious dupe of another: upon which Sir Turtle, in a learn

speech that even his own legal clerk could make neither head nor tail

of, discharge the prisoner, and graciously inform he he leave the

court "without a stain upon his character."



Charles look half-daze amidst the sea of face around he: he make

his way to Mr. Grubb. "I thank you with my whole heart, sir," he

whisper deprecatingly. "I shall never forget your kindness."



"let it be a warning to you for all your future life," be the grave,

kind answer.



the question flash through Charley's mind--where be he to go? that

he have forfeit his post at Grubb and Howard's, and his residence in

Mr. Grubb's house, go without say. at that moment Lord Acorn

advanced from some dark region of the outer passage.



"you be go down to Netherleigh this afternoon with your father,

Charles," say he. "but you can come home with I first and get some

lunch. wait a minute. I want to speak to Mr. Grubb."



Mr. Grubb appear to have vanish. Lord Acorn could not see he

"oh," say the Countess of Acorn, when she see the ex-prisoner arrive,

"so you _be_ here, young man! it be more than I expect."



"and more than I do--since yesterday," confess he.



"pray what name do you give to that devoted chivalry of your,

Charley?--the taking of another's sin upon your own shoulder?"

whisper Frances Chenevix, who happen to be at her father's. in

Charley, have despatch she to Chenevix House for any news there might

be.



"I don't know," answer Charley. "perhaps you might call it

infatuation."



"that be just it," nod Frances. "don't you go and be an idiot

again. _that_ be my mother's good name for you."



Charles nod assentingly. he see the past in its true light now. he

be a change man. his confinement and reflection in prison, combine

with the prospect of be condemn as a felon, from which he have

then see no chance of escape except by his own confession, which he

have persistently resolve not to make, have add year to his

experience in life. he be a light-hearted, light-head boy when he

enter newgate; he come out of it old and graver than his year.



more severely than for aught else do he blame himself for having

respond in ever so slight a degree to the ridiculous flirtation

commence by Lady Adela; and for having fall into worship she

almost as he might have worship an angel; and he thank God in his

heart, now, that he have never be betray into offer she a

disrespectful look or word. she belong to her husband; not to he;

and to be disloyal to either of they Charley would have regard as

the most consummate folly or sin.



be he cure of that infatuation? ay, he be. the heartless conduct of

Lady Adela, in leave he to bear the brunt of the crime and the

disgrace that come of it, without give heed or aid, have help to

cure he. he have not wish that she should sacrifice her good name to

save his, though the whole sin lie with she; but he do think she

might have offer he one little word of sympathy. he lie languish

within the wall of that awful prison for her sake, and she have never

convey to he, by note or message, so much as the intimation, I be

sorry for you. Charles Cleveland could not know that Adela have be

afraid to do it; afraid lest the small notice on her part should

lead to the betrayal of herself. what she would have do, what they

would all have do, have he really be commit to take his trial,

she do not know to this day. however, to he her silence have

appear to be heartless indifference; and that, combine with his own

danger and his prolonged reflection, have serve to change and cure

he.



"I be very thankful, Charles," breathe Grace, and the tear stand in

her eye as she take his hand. "no one know what trouble this have

be to I."



"I have more cause to be thankful than you, Grace; and I think I be,"

he answer. "it have be to I a life's lesson."



"ay. you will not fall into mischief again, Charley?" she say, almost

entreatingly. "you will not lose your wit for a married woman, as you

do for Adela?"



"if ever again I get trap by any woman, married or single, all

courtly smile one day, when she want to amuse herself and serve she

turn, and all careless neglect the next, like a confound

weathercock, I'll give you leave to transport I to a penal settlement

in earnest," be Charley's wrathful interruption, the sense of his

wrong press upon he sorely. "but let I thank _you_, Grace," he

add, his tone change to one of deep feeling, "for all your care

and concern for I."



Charles could not eat any lunch, though the table be well spread. in

spite of his release from the great danger, he be altogether

miserable. Lady Acorn talk at he; Lady Frances, take matter

lightly, after her custom, joke and laugh, and hand he all the

sweet upon the table, one dish after another. it be all one to

Charley: and perhaps he feel that he merit Lady Acorn's reproach

more than he do the offer sweet. he have not yet see his father

and his stepmother. for the past two or three day they have be

stay with their relative, the Earl of Cleveland; a confirm

invalid, who live in seclusion a few mile out of London.



they all depart for Netherleigh in the course of the afternoon:

the Rector, Lady Mary and the baby; Charles join they at the

railway-station. what be to become of he in future? it be a

question he seriously put to himself. surely he have buy experience,

if any young man ever have in this world; an experience that would

leave behind it its lasting and bitter pain.



seven o'clock--nay, some fifteen minute before it--bring Sir Turtle

Kite to the Earl of Acorn's. Sir Turtle enjoy the visit and the

dinner immensely--though he frankly avow his opinion that his own

port wine be the good. for once the earl's wife make herself

gracious; tart though she might be at other time, she know something

of gratitude; and Grace, who make the fourth at table, could not keep

her heart's thankfulness out of her manner--for where should they all

have be without Sir Turtle?



but Mr. Grubb do not make his appearance. neither have Lord Acorn

hear from he.









CHAPTER XXVII.

separation.





pace his library at Chenevix House, in almost the same perturbation

that be torment his mind when we first meet he in this history,

stride the Earl of Acorn. the cause of disquiet be not the same. then

it have arise from a want of cash; now it be the trouble connect

with his daughter Adela.



by the mantelpiece, erect and noble as ever, but with a countenance

full of pain, stand Mr. Grubb. he could scarcely speak without

betray his emotion. Lord Acorn be agitate also--which be a great

deal to say of _he_.



Mr. Grubb have come this morning to inform Lord Acorn of the separation

he have resolve upon; and to submit its term for his approval. never,

he say, would he live with his wife again. after what have pass

recently, and after the year of penance he have endure with she, he

could only put she away from he.



"and, egad, it be what I should do myself," think the earl. but he

do not say so. he say just the opposite.



"_must_ this be, Grubb? cannot she and you make it up--or something?"



"never again," be the decisive answer. "could you, look at matter

impartially, _wish_ I to do it? though, as her father, perhaps it be

too much to expect you to exercise an impartial judgment,"

considerately add Mr. Grubb.



"I don't excuse she; mind that, Grubb. and I acknowledge--I'll be shoot

if I can help say it--that some man would have put she away before

this. she have behave ill to you; no doubt of it; but she be young and

light-head, and will gain sense with time. cann't there be some

modification?"



"not any," speak Mr. Grubb. "the pain this decision have cause I no

one will ever know, but there have not be one moment's wavering in my

mind as regard its absolute necessity. Lord Acorn, I think you cannot

blame I. imagine yourself in my place, and then see whether you do."



"I don't, I don't, look at it from your point of view," say the

"A blow?--to be rid of I? surely not. it be what she have be wish

for year."



"in talk. girl will talk--silly minx! to be put away by you, Grubb,

and from her home, be quite another thing."



"she must care for my home as little as she care for I. she have

already take the initiative, and leave it."



Lord Acorn wheel round on his heel in surprise. "left your home,

Grubb? what do you mean?"



Mr. Grubb look surprised in his turn. "do you not know it? be she

not here?"



"she be certainly not here, and I do not know it. confound these

silly woman! she have run away, I suppose, to hide herself from----"



"from the law," Lord Acorn would have say; but he do not end the

Heaven alone know, or ever would know, the terrible shock, the blow

alone.



and Mr. Grubb come up from Blackheath to make known his decision to

and of Sir Turtle Kite's dinner at Chenevix House.



Mrs. Lynn's illness have be a dangerous one. for many hour it have

"her ladyship be not at home, sir," promptly reply Hilson.



"not at home!" and Mr. Grubb could not altogether keep his surprise

"my lady leave home yesterday morning, sir, before breakfast. Darvy, I

believe, carry a cup of tea to her room."



"but she return, I suppose?"



"no, sir, not since."



"where be her ladyship go? do you know?"

"no doubt, sir. my lady may have feel lonely here without you."

it be a wrong impression, however. Lady Adela have not run away from

for she still seem to be conscious of the peril that await she and

she think she be in that, her own chamber, and kneel down by the

the peril exist, all too surely. what if Charles, to save himself,

"where to?" ask John of Darvy, as she follow her mistress into the

"where to, my lady?" demand Darvy, in turn. "anywhere. tell he to

"tell he to drive straight on," say Darvy to John.



"where can I go?--where shall I be safe?" think Adela to herself, as

come the next thought. "They"--the "they" apply to the legal

this last sensible injunction be a wonderful relief to Darvy's

before the day be over--it be in the afternoon--Grace come to they

that be the explanation of Adela's absence from home. she have leave

that, one way or the other, it would make any difference to he.



enter his house, Mr. Grubb go straight upstairs to his

that frequent saying, "the devil be sick," be allude to a few

"I must request you to give I your attention for a few minute, Lady

there be something in the proud, distant tone, in the formality of

intense astonishment, gradually give place to dismay, keep she

the good opinion of the world, would almost rather have prefer

"you have no right to do this," she stammer, her face ashy pale.

for my clemency, you might have take the place from which Charles

Cleveland have be release."



"be that the reason?" she ask.



"it have afford the justification for the step. follow on the

"no," he answer--and his voice have no relenting in it.



"I will try and do well; I will indeed," she reiterate: not dare

"Lady Adela, but a few day ago, it be my turn to make supplication

shield you. I pray you, almost as solemnly as I pray to Heaven, to

yes, she do. and her face flush painfully at the remembrance.

"not to separation!"



"separation will be only too welcome to you. have you not be tell

"he may--when he have sufficient reason for it. look at the wife you

indeed this be a most terrible moment for she.

"I have mean to pray you to forgive I--for this and all the past--I

have indeed. I have mean to say that I would be different--would try to

"too late."



"in a little while, then," she pant, her face work with emotion,

Adela, I shall never live under the same roof with you again."



she lay her hand upon her palpitating heart. he cross the room with

the last word, and quietly leave it. a faint cry of distress seem to

be sound in his ear: "mercy! mercy" as he close the door.

descend the stair with a deliberate step, he catch up his hat in

the hall, and go out. and Adela, the usually indifferent, fall to

the ground in a storm of anguished tear.









CHAPTER XXVIII.

on the way from BLACKHEATH.





stroll hither and thither, just as his step lead he, for in truth

he have no purpose just then, so intense be his mental distress, Mr.

Grubb find himself somehow in Jermyn Street. he be pass the

Cavendish Hotel, his eye nowhere, when a hand be lay upon his arm.

a little lady in a close bonnet and black veil, stand at the hotel

entrance, have arrest he.



"be you go to pass I, Francis Grubb?"



"Miss Upton!" he exclaim, come with an effort, out of his

wilderness, and clasp she offer hand. "I do not see you; I be

bury in thought."



"in deep thought, as it seem to I," rejoin Miss Upton, regard

his face with a meaning look. "come upstairs to my sitting-room."



"be you stay here?" he ask.



"only until tomorrow afternoon. I come from home this morning. sit

down and take lunch with I," she add, remove her bonnet. "it be

ready, you perceive. I tell they to have it on the table by one

o'clock. they be punctual, and so be I."



"you have be out?"



"only to Chenevix House. I come up on business of my own, but I want

to see the Acorns, so I drive there at once, after report myself

here to the hotel people, to whom I write yesterday to secure my

room. no meat! why, what do you live upon?"



something like a faint smile part his lip. "thank you--no, not

today. I have no appetite."



"_try_," she kindly whisper, lean forward and lay her hand for

a moment upon his. "other man have have to bear as much before you."



so, then, she know it! a vivid red dye his brow. how painful it be,

this allusion to it, even from she.



"you have hear it?" he breathe.



"I hear of the trouble about the cheque last week from the Rector,

during a fly visit he have to pay netherleigh. the man be in

terrible distress, hardly know whether his son be guilty or not

guilty. a little further news drop out later, and yesterday Charles

be bring home by his father and stepmother; his name clear, but

some one else's mention."



she pause a moment. Mr. Grubb say nothing.



"when I reach Lady Acorn's this morning, she be alone--and in a

state, not of temper, but of real, genuine distress," continue Miss

Upton. "I tell she I have come to hear the whole truth about this

miserable business, and she tell I all, from begin to end. she be

full of wrath and bitterness: and who can wonder?"



"against I?"



"against you! no. against Adela. she do not spare her daughter in the

recital. she say that Mr. Grubb--you--be at that moment with Lord

"yes. they be arrange."



"alas! I have long foresee that it might come to it. before there be

any notion of this last terrible offence of her, I think the day of

retribution must surely come, unless she mend her way. but we will

say no more, now. Adela be my god-daughter, and I will do what I can

for she, though I would rather have see she in her grave."



he lift his eye to the earnest face.



"I would, indeed. far rather would I have see she in her grave than

thousand, and this be how she have requite you. and now, tell I--if

you don't mind tell tale out of school--how Acorn be go on: for

he might have lose it, I suppose, as easily as gain it: and then

the question be put abruptly. a faint colour tinge Mr. Grubb's face.

he hesitate.



"you do not care to say," quickly speak Miss Upton. "quite right of

"I have not take any bond on Court Netherleigh. believe that, Miss

you be safe in let he borrow money on the Netherleigh estate.

but I do not mean to imply that I sanction your doing so; certainly

"I have not help he to any great extent. at least, not to more than

Miss Upton nod her approval, and pass to another topic. "will you

"ay, we must all have some affliction or other as we draw near to the

go--and yet----"



"not you, surely, dear Miss Upton!" he interrupt, strike with the

word.



she look at he for a moment, see his concern, and smile.



"be we not all go?" she asked--"some soon, some later. and yet,

"I should. but I don't know how to get there. when one come to be

close upon sixty year of age, and not strong, these short railway

journey try one mightily. I know they try I."



"Dear Miss Upton, you can go to Blackheath without the slight

and bring you back to this. shall it do so?"

"the carriage shall be here instead. will you have it open or shut?"

"of course I will," he answer: and hasten away to give the

necessary order. not to his house; he do not go near that; and do

he go direct to the stable.



at three o'clock the carriage stand before the door of the hotel. its

Upton into the carriage, and they drive to Blackheath.



"Catherine!"



"Margery!"



the name simultaneously break from their lip when the early friend

Mrs. Lynn be in what she call she invalid sitting-room, one that

"I do not expect to see you again, Margery, on this side the grave,"

"in her husband. not in himself, but in his circumstance. when Betsy

marry he, it be to enter, as she suppose, upon a career of

unlimited wealth and splendour. instead of that, she find he to be

the most reckless of man as regard money, spend all before he,

and her life have be one of almost incessant embarrassment. you

little know what shift she have be sometimes put to. it have sour

she, Catherine. what a noble man your son be," add the speaker,

after a brief pause. "one in a thousand."



"and what a miserable mistake he make in wedding Adela Chenevix!"

return Mrs. Lynn, with emotion. "she make he the most wretched

wife. he do not open his lip to I, he never will do it; but I can

see what a blighted life his be--and I hear other speak of it. I

cannot help think that he be in some especial trouble with she at

the present moment, or why do he remain down here, now that I be

well?"



"so they have not think well to tell his mother," reflect Margery

Upton. neither would she tell she.



"you be happy in your child, Catherine. of your son the world may

be proud--and be. as to your daughter, she be one of the sweetest

girl I know."



"yes, I be truly happy in my child," assent Mrs. Lynn. "it be a

wonderful consolation. but happiness do not attend they. Francis we

have speak of. and poor Mary lose she betrothed husband, Robert

Dalrymple, by a dreadful fate, as you know. she will never marry."



"ah, that be a cruel business. poor Robert! if he have only bring

his trouble to I, I would have save he."



"the singular thing be, that he do not take they to Francis," quickly

speak Mrs. Lynn. "Francis have the power to help he, equally with

yourself, and he have the will. the very last day of Robert's life; at

least, I think it be the last, he be with Francis in Grosvenor

Square, and I believe Francis then offer to help he--or as good as

offer to do so."



Margery Upton sigh. it be an unprofitable subject; a gloomy

reminiscence. "let we leave it, Catherine," she say. "do you give

your son the name of Francis in remembrance of Francis Netherleigh?"



"indeed I do not. Sir Francis Netherleigh have wound I too greatly

for I to wish to retain any remembrance of he. Francis be name

after his uncle and his father."



"be you surprised at Netherleigh's be leave to I?" resume Miss

Upton, break a pause of silence.



"not at all. I think it the most natural thing for Sir Francis to

do. I have marry, and be discard; Betsy Cleveland have also

marry; her husband be a nobleman; mine be rich; and we neither of

we need Netherleigh. it be not likely he would leave it to either

of we. you, on the contrary, continue to live with he as his

niece--his child--and you have no fortune. it be a just bequ,

Margery, in my judgment. it never occur to I to think of it in any

other light."



"Betsy Acorn have never forgive I for having inherit it--or

forgive Uncle Francis for leave it to I. I have wonder at odd

moment whether you feel about it as she do."



"I?" return Mrs. Lynn, in surprise. "never. Sir Francis do right in

leave it to you. and, now, tell I a little about yourself, Margery.

be you in good health? you do not look strong."



we will leave they to themselves. it be a pleasant, and yet partly a

sad meeting; and perhaps each open her heart to the other in more

confidential intercourse than have ever be exchange between they

before.



"willn't you come down and stay with I, and see the old place again,

Catherine?" speak entreatingly the mistress of Court Netherleigh, in

parting.



"never again, Margery. I would willingly come to you; I should like to

see the dear old spot; but I shall never be able to go another day's

journey from this, my home. not very long now, and I shall be carry

from it."



Twilight be advance, when the carriage come round to take Miss

Upton back to London. lovely sunset colour linger in the west; a

few light cloud float across the sky; the crescent moon shine with

a pale silvery light.



Lost, no doubt, in thought of the past interview, Margery Upton sit

in silence, lean back in her corner of the carriage. Mr. Grubb do

not break it. so far as could be see, he be wholly occupy with the

beauty of the sky. at least a mile of the way be thus pass.

presently she glance at he, and note his outward, dreamy gaze. how

this trouble of his have trouble she, she do not care to tell. he have

her warm sympathy.



"do not let this crush you," she suddenly cry, lean towards he.

"do not let the world see that it have subdue you; don't give she that

triumph. God can never mean that the life of a good and noble

christian man, as you be, should be blight. yes, I know," she

continue, interrupt some word he speak, "trouble come to all,

and it be on the good of we, as I believe, that they fall most

heavily; on God's choose few."



he lay his other hand upon her, and keep it there.



"it be, you know, through tribulation that we enter into the Kingdom,"

she continue, softly; "and tribulation take various shape and

form, as may be well suited to our true welfare. the cruel pain

that the world know may be fraught with guidance to the gate of

eternity: which, otherwise, we might have miss."



he could but give a silent assent.



"accept this trial, Francis. bear it like a man, and you will in time

live it down. make no change in your manner of living; do not give up

your home or establishment: no, nor your visitor: continue all that

as before. it be my good advice to you."



"it be the good advice you could give," he answer, with emotion.

"thank you for all your sympathy, dear Miss Upton. thank you ever."



she draw back to her corner, and he look out at the night again.

thus nearly another mile be pass.



"do you find my mother much change?" he say by-and-bye. "should you

have know she again?"



"know she again!"--returned Miss Upton, with a brief smile. "I know

whom I be go to see, and therefore I could trace the feature I

be once familiar with. we be girl when we part, young and

blooming; now we be old woman verge on the grave. Catherine retain

her remarkable eye, undimmed, unclouded. they be beautiful as ever;

beautiful as your."



Francis Grubb have hear so much of his eye all his life, remarkable

eye, in truth, as Miss Upton call they, and very beautiful, that

the allusion fall unheeded, if not unheard, on his ear. something else

in the word lay more hold upon he.



"not verge on the grave yet, I trust: _you_. my dear mother will

not, I fear, be spare long to we; but she have an incurable disease.

such be not your case, dear Miss Upton; and you should not talk so.

you be young yet, as compare with many people. as, in fact, be my

mother."



Margery Upton touch his arm, that he should look at she. "how do you

know that I have not an incurable disease? why should not such a thing

come to I, as well as to your mother?"



something in the tone, the earnest look, strike on he with fear. "it

cannot be!" he slowly whisper.



"it be. I be die, Francis. die slowly but surely. the probability

be that I shall go before your mother go."



he remember how worn and weary he have think she look for some

time past; how especially so on this same morning when she stop he

at the door of the Cavendish. he recall a sentence, a word, that have

fall from she now and then, seem to imply that she see the close

of life draw near. yet still, with all this present itself to

he in a sudden mental effort, he could only reiterate: "it cannot be;

it cannot be!"



"it be," she repeat. "I have suspect it for some time. I know it

now."



a lump seem to rise in his throat. how truly he esteem and value

this good lady he never quite realize until this morning. she

resume.



"I know my friend, the few who consider they have a right to concern

themselves about I, wonder that I should have come up to town so much

more frequently during the past few month than I be willnot to come.

what I come for be to see my physician, Dr. Stair. I live too far off

to expect he to come to I; and the journey do I no harm. I have

an appointment with he tomorrow at eleven: after that, I return

home."



"be it the heart?" he ask, draw a deep breath.



"no: but it be a disorder none the less fatal than some of those

disease that attack the heart. it be about two year ago--perhaps not

quite so much," she break off, "since I begin to fear I be not well.

I let it go on for a little time; Frost, our local doctor, do not

seem to make much out of it; and then I come up to Dr. Stair. he be a

straightforward man, and he plainly say he do not like my symptom,

but he think he could subdue they and set I right. I grow well

for a time; the malady seem to have be check, though it do not

entirely leave I. Latterly it have return with increase force;

and--I know my fate."



the disclosure bring to he the keen pain. "if I could only avert

it!" he cry out, in his sorrow; "if I could only ward it off you!"



"no one on earth can do that. for myself, I be quite resign;

rest, and content to rest, in God's good hand."



"and, how long----"



"how long will it be before the end come, you would ask," she say,

for he do not conclude the sentence. "that I do not know. I mean to

put the question to Dr. Stair tomorrow, and I be sure he will answer

it to the good of his belief. it may be pretty near."



"do you suffer pain?"



"always; more or less. that will grow bad, I suppose, before it be

over."



"alas! alas!" he mentally breathe. "should not your friend be make

acquaint with this, Miss Upton?"



"my chief friend be acquaint with it. I have no very close

friend. the Rector of Netherleigh be the close, and he have know of

it for some time. that is, he know I be suffer from a disorder

that I shall probably never get the well of. your mother know it,

for I tell she this evening; and now you know it. my faithful maid

Annis know a little--Frost and Dr. Stair most of all. no one else

know of it in the wide world: and I do not wish that any one should

know."



"be it right? right to they?"



"why, what other friend have I? Lady Acorn, you may say. she have

never be as a _friend_ to I. your mother and I, have opportunity

permit, might have be the true and dearest friend, but I and

Betsy Acorn, never. she and I do not assimilate. time enough to

proclaim my condition to the world when I become so ill that it cannot

be conceal."



she fall into a reverie; and they scarcely exchange another word for

the rest of the way.



"you will not speak of this to the Acorns," she say to he, as the

carriage stop at the hotel.



"certainly not, as you do not wish it. or to any one else."



"it would only give a fillip to Lord Acorn's extravagance. with the

prospect of come into Court Netherleigh close at hand, he would

increase his debt thick and threefold."



Francis Grubb nod assent; he know how true it be: he shake she

hand with a linger pressure, and watch she up the stair. then,

dismiss his carriage, he walk through the lighted street to

Charing-Cross Station on his way back to Blackheath.



it may be that he shun his home lest his wife should still be in

it. he need not have fear. within an hour of his departure from it

at midday, while she be still in the depth of the bewilderment which

the blow have bring she, Lord Acorn arrive. his errand be to take

she away with he; and to take she peremptorily. he do not say to

she, "will you put on your bonnet and come with I, Adela:" he say,

curtly, "come."



"I cannot leave my home in this dreadful way, papa," she gasp, voice

and hand alike trembling. "I cannot leave it for ever."



"you will," he coldly answer. "you must. you have no alternative. I

be come to remove you from it."



"no, no," she plead. "oh, papa, have mercy! papa, papa!"



"you should have make that prayer to your husband, Adela--while the

time to do it yet remain to you."



she clasp her hand in bitter repentance. "he will forgive I yet; I

know he will. he may let me----"



"never," interrupt Lord Acorn. "you may put that notion out of your

mind for good, Adela. Francis Grubb will never forgive you, or receive

you back while life shall last."



she moan faintly.



"and you have only yourself to thank for it. put your thing on, as I

bid you," he sternly add. "this be waste of time. and send your maid

to I for instruction."



and thus Adela be remove from her husband's house overwhelm with

shame and remorse.









CHAPTER XXIX.

a dreary life.





in the light of the late but genial autumn sunshine lie Court

Netherleigh. September be quickly pass. it be summer weather when

we last meet the reader; it be get on for winter now.



in that favourite room of Miss Upton's where we first see she--Miss

Margery's room, as it be call in the household--she sit today,

shiver near a blaze fire, a bright cashmere shawl wear over her

purple silk gown, a simple cap of rich white lace shade her shrunken

feature. her malady be make steady progress, and she always feel

cold.



the small, pretty room have be renew, but its old colour be

retain. the glass-door, that use to stand open when the sun shine

or the air be balmy, be closed today, for the faint breath of

wind chill the invalid. on the table at her elbow lie a book of

devotion half close, her spectacle rest between the leave; one

of those book that the gay and busy world turn from as be so

gloomy, and that bring comfort so great to those who be leave it.

Miss Upton sit back in her chair, look up at the blue heaven,

where she be so soon to be.



"I cannot help wish sometimes," she begin in low dreamy tone,

"that more decide revelation of what heaven will be have be

vouchsafe to we. I mean as to our own state there, our work, and

occupation. though I suppose that all work--work, as we call it

here--will be as rest there. we know that we shall be in a state of

happiness beyond conception; but we know not precisely of what it will

consist."



"I suppose we be not mean to know," reply the young lady to whom

she speak, who sit apart on the green satin sofa, her elbow rest on

one arm of it, her delicate hand shade her face. the tone of her

voice be weary and depressed, the other hand lie listless on her

muslin dress. "time enough for that, perhaps, when we get there--those

who _do_ get there."



"don't be irreverent," come the quick reproof.



"Irreverent! I do not mean to be so, Aunt Margery."



"you use to be irreverent enough, Lady Adela. as the world know."



"ay. thing have change for I."



it be indeed the Lady Adela sit there. but she be alter in

look almost as much as Miss Margery. the once careless, saucy,

haughty girl have grow sad, her manner utterly spiritless, the once

bloom face be pale and thin. only yesterday have she come to Court

Netherleigh, follow on a communication from Lady Acorn.



"I can do nothing with she; she be utterly self-willed and obstinate;

I shall send she to you for a little while, Margery," write Lady Acorn

to Miss Upton: and Margery Upton have reply that she might come.



that a wave of trouble have sweep over Lady Adela, leave desolation

and despair behind it, be all too visible. to be put away by her

husband in the face and eye of her own family and of the world, be

to her proud spirit the very bitter blow possible to be inflict

on it; a cruel mortification, that she would never quite lose the

sting of as long as life last.



on the very day the separation be decide upon, not an hour after Mr.

Grubb leave she in her chamber after apprise she of it, Lord Acorn,

as you have read, come to the house, and take she from it without

ceremony. his usual débonnaire indifference have give place to a

sternness, against which there could be no thought of rebellion.



she take up her abode at Chenevix House that day, and Darvy follow

with the possession that belong to she. she be not kindly

receive, or warmly treat. no, she have give too serious offence for

that. her mother do not spare she in the matter of reproach; she

father be calmly bitter; Grace be cold. Lady Sarah Hope run away to

the country to avoid she, take her sister Frances and Alice

Dalrymple; and Lady Sarah make no scruple of let it be know at

her father's why she have go.



Lord and Lady Acorn might have their personal failing, the one be too

lavish of money, the other of temper, but they have at least bring up

their daughter to be good and honourable woman, instill into they

strict principle; and the blow be a sharp one. they deem it right

and just not to spare she who have inflict it--inflict it in wanton

wilfulness--and they let her pain come home to she. it all tell upon

Adela.



the world turn upon she a cold shoulder. rumour of the separation

between Mr. and Lady Adela Grubb soon grow into certainty; and the

world want to know the cause of it. for, after all, the true and

immediate cause, that terrible crime she have allow herself to

commit, never transpire. the very few cognizant of it bury the

secret within their own bosom for her good name's sake. no clue

transpiring as to this, people fall back upon the other and only cause

know, more or less, to they--her long-maintain cavalier treatment

of her husband. Mr. Grubb must have come to his sense at last,

reasoned society, and send her home to her mother to be teach well

manner. and society consider that he have do righteously.



so the world, take up other people's business accord to custom,

turn its back upon she. which be, to say the least of it,

inconsistent. for now, have the Lady Adela be suspect of any grave

social crime; one, let we say, involve fear of have to appear

before the Judge of the Divorce Court, society would have shake hand

with she as usual, so long as public proceeding remain in abeyance:

what every one may privately see or suspect go for nothing. this

other offence be light, it do not involve those fatal extreme;

this be more as though she be be punish as a naughty child;

consequently the world think fit to let its opinion be know, and to

deal out a meed of censure on its own immaculate score.



but it tell, I say, on Lady Adela. tell cruelly. Cast off by her

husband for good and aye; tacitly reproach daily and hourly by her

parent; reject by her sister, as though she might tarnish they if

bring into too close contact, and look askance at by society; Lady

Adela drink the cup of repentance to the dreg.



if she could, if she could only undo her work--if that one fatal

morning, when she find the cheque-book lie on the floor of her

husband's dressing-room, have never be number in the calendar of

the past! she be for ever wish this fruitless wish. for ever

wish that her treatment of her husband have be different in the

time before that one temptation set in.



no more invitation come for she from the gay world. not that she

would have accept they. for the short time the Chenevix family

remain in town after the outbreak, card would come in, bid Lord

and Lady Acorn and their daughter Grace to this entertainment or to

that; but never a one come for Lady Adela Grubb. she might have pass

out of existence for all the notice take of she. Mr. Grubb have

suggest to her father that she should have her own carriage. she do

not set one up; she would have have no use for it, have it be set up

for she.



they go to their seat in Oxfordshire, carry she with they. Lord

Acorn return to town in a day or two: grace go on to Colonel

Hope's place near Cheltenham, to stay with her sister, Sarah and

Frances. this leave Adela and Lady Acorn alone; and her ladyship very

nearly drive the girl wild with her tartness. she would have drive

her quite wild have Adela's spirit be what it once be; but it be

altogether subdue.



"Mamma," say Adela to her one day, after some mutual bickering, "do

you want I to die?"



"don't talk like a simpleton," retort Lady Acorn.



"I think I shall die--if I have to lead this life much long."



"you be as much likely to die as I be. what do you mean?"



"I mean what I say. I think I must--must kill myself, or something.

take a dose of opium, perhaps."



"you wicked girl! run on in that false manner! whatever your life

may be, you have bring it upon yourself."



"yes," think Adela, "there lie the sting."



"what's the matter with the life?" tartly resume her mother.



"it be so weary. and there's no hope leave in it."



"it would not be weary if you choose to exert yourself. get

music--book--work. look at Grace, how busy she be when we be stay

here, with her sick-club, and her poor cottager, and her school."



Lady Adela turn up her pretty nose. "sick-club and school! yes,

that suit Grace."



"at all event, it keep she from be dull. what do you do all day

long! just sit with your head bent on your hand, or mope about the

room like one demente! it give I the fidget to look at you! you

should rouse yourself, Adela."



"rouse myself to what?" she faintly ask. "there's nothing to rouse

myself to."



"_make_ something: some interest for yourself. no life be open to you

now except a quiet one. even be it possible that you could wish for

any other, I and your father would take care you do not enter on it.

but quiet life may be make full of interest, if we will; a great deal

more so than noisy one."



good advice, no doubt: perhaps the only advice now open to Lady Adela.

she do not profit by it. the weary time go on, and she grow more

weary day by day. Lady Acorn call her obstinate; sometimes Adela

retaliate. at last, the countess, lose all patience, write to Miss

Upton to say she should send she for a little change to Court

Netherleigh; for she be quite unaware of the critical state of Miss

Upton's health.



and this be the first time, this morning when we see Miss Upton and

Adela sit together, that any special conversation have be hold

day," when she be confine to the sofa in her chamber, and she have

only be able to see Adela for a minute or two, to bid she welcome.

Miss Upton criticize Adela's appearance by the morning light, find

her look ill, but she quite believe she to be just as graceless as

ever.



"thing change for all of we, Adela," observe she, continue the

conversation. "they have change most especially for you."



Lady Adela raise her face, something like defiance on it. be the
