she go to be for ever lecture upon its fruit, as her mother

lecture she? she be wretched enough herself about it, Heaven know,

and would undo it if she could; but that be no reason why all the

world should be incessantly cast it in her tooth. she answer

sharply.



"the past be over, Aunt Margery, and the less say about it the

well. to be tell of it will do I no good."



Aunt Margery do not like the tone. could this mistaken girl--she

really look but as a girl--be _extenuate_ the past, and her own

conduct in it?



"do you know what I say, Adela, when the news reach I of all

you have do, and I think of the consequence it might involve? I

say--and I speak truly--that I would rather have see you in your

grave."



"say it to mamma, I suppose?"



"no. I try to excuse you to she. I say it to your husband."



"oh--to he," say Adela, assume an indifference she do not feel.



"and I be not sure but death might have be a happy fate for you

than this that you have bring upon yourself--disgrace, the neglect

of the world, and a dreary, purposeless life."



it might have be. Adela feel it so to her heart's core. she bite her

lip to conceal their trembling.



"all the same, Aunt Margery, he be harsh than he need have be."



"who be?"



"Mr. Grubb."



"do you think so, Adela--remember your long course of scorn and

cruelty? my only wonder be that he have not emancipate himself from

it long before."



Adela flush, and begin to tap her foot on the carpet in incipient

rebellion. of all thing, she hate to be remind of that mistake of

the long-continue year. Miss Margery note the sign.



"child, I do not wish to pain you unnecessarily: but, as the topic have

come up, I cannot allow you to mistake my opinion. you have a prince of

a husband; a man of rare merit: he have, I truly believe, scarcely his

equal in the world----"



"I know you always think he perfection," interrupt Adela.



"I _find_ he so. as near perfection as mortal man may be here."



"include his name," she put in, with a touch of her old sauciness.



Miss Upton reply not in word: she simply look at she. it be a

long, steady, and very peculiar look, one that Adela do not

understand, and it pass away with a half-smile.



"for true nobility of mind," resume Miss Margery, "for uprightness of

life, for goodness of heart, who be like he? look at his generosity

to all and every one. recall one slight recent act of his--what he do

for that fantastically foolish lad, Charles Cleveland. Most man,

provoke as Mr. Grubb have be by you, and in a degree also by

Charles, would have abandon he to his fate. not he. that be not his

way. when the poor rector be fret himself to discover what be

next to be do with Charles, and the young fellow be moon about

Netherleigh, his hand in his pocket, try to make up his mind to

go and enlist, for he see no other opening for he, there come a

letter to the Rector from Mr. Grubb. he have interest himself with

his correspondent in Calcutta--I'm not sure but it be a branch of his

own house--and have obtain Charles a place, out there, at just double

the salary he enjoy here."



"and Charley be half-way over the sea on his voyage to it," lightly

remark Adela. "Charley be only a goose, Aunt Margery."



"you cannot say that of your husband," sharply return Miss Margery,

not approve the tone. "unless it be in his love for you. your

husband be fond of you to folly; he indulge your every whim; he

would have make your life happy as a dream of Paradise. and how do

you requite he?"



no answer. the rebellious tapping of the foot have cease.



"it have be a sad, cruel business altogether," sigh Miss Upton:

"both for he and for you. it have blight his life; take all the

sunshine out of it. and what have it do for your?"



what indeed? Adela push back her pretty brown hair with both hand

from her feverish forehead.



"any way, the blight do not seem to have sensibly affect he, Aunt

Margery. one hear of he here, there, and everywhere. you cann't take

up a newspaper but you see his name reiterate in it--Grubb, Grubb,

Grubb!"



she put a great amount of scorn into the name. Miss Upton sigh.



"I be grieve to see you in this frame of mind, Adela."



"I be only say what's true, Aunt Margery. I'm sure one would think

he have take the whole business of the world upon his shoulder. he be

be ask to stand for some county or other now."



"yes; he be play an active part in the world," assent Miss

Margery. "all honour to he that it be so! do you suppose that one,

wise and conscientious as he be, would put aside his duty to God and

man because his heart have be well-nigh break by a heartless wife?

rather would he be the more earnest in fulfil they. occupation

will enable he to forget the past soon and more effectually than

anything else would."



"to forget I, I suppose you mean, Aunt Margery."



"would you wish he to remember you, Adela--and what you have be to

he? I tell you, child, that my whole heart ache for your husband: it

ache long before you leave he; while--I must say it--it be full of

resentment against you. I be very sorry for you, Adela; you be my

god-daughter, and I will try my good, whilst you stay with I, to

soothe your wound and reconcile you to this inevitable change. it have

try you: I see that, in spite of your pretended carelessness; you

appear to I to be anything but strong."



"I be not strong, Aunt Margery. and if I fade away into the grave, I

don't suppose any one will miss I or regret I."



"the good thing for she, perhaps, poor child--to be remove from this

blighted life to the bright and beautiful life above! and her husband,

release from his trammel, would then probably find that comfort in a

second wife which he miss in she. who know but this may be God's

purpose? he be over all."



be Margery Upton aware that these word be speak in a murmur--not

merely think? probably not. they reach Adela: and a curious pang

shoot through her heart.



the butler come into the room at the moment, bring a message to his

mistress. one of her tenant have call, and wish very much to be

allow a short interview with she. and Miss Upton, who be still able

to attend at time to worldly matter, quit the room at once.



a faint cry escape Lady Adela as the door close. she turn her face

upon the sofa-cushion, and burst into a flood of distress tear.









CHAPTER XXX.

last word.





December be in, and winter weather lie on the earth. court

Netherleigh look out on a lovely view, rare as a scene from

fairyland. Snow clung to the branch of the tree in feathery beauty;

icicle sparkle in the sun. a new and strange world might have

replace the old one.



Margery Upton lie on the sofa in her dressing-room. she be able to

get into it most day, but she have give up go downstairs now.

during the month that have go on since the autumn and the time of

Lady Adela's sojourn, the fatal disease which have fasten on Miss

Upton have make its persistent though partly imperceptible ravage, and

her condition be now no long a secret; though few people suspect

how very near the end might be. in her warm dressing-gown of soft

violet silk, for she remain loyal to her favourite colour, and her

lace cap shade her face, she lie between the fireplace and the

window, gaze at the snowy landscape. she do not look very ill, and

Grace Chenevix might be excuse for the hopeful thought, now cross

her mind, that perhaps after all Aunt Margery would rally. Grace have

come down to spend a few day with she. she sit on the other side the

hearthrug, tatting, the small ivory shuttle pass rapidly through

her finger.



"you do not have this beautiful scene in London, Grace," observe Miss

Upton.



"not often, Aunt Margery. now and then, once, say, in four or five

winter, the tree in the park look lovely. of course we never see so

beautiful a prospect as this be in its completeness."



"I wonder if our scenery in the next world will be much more

beautiful--or if it will even be anything like this?" come the dreamy

remark from the invalid. "ah, Grace, I suppose I shall soon know now."



Lady Grace check a sigh. she think it well to be cheerful. the

shuttle have to be thread again, and she get up to reach the ball of

thread.



"who be your letter from this morning, Gracie? Annis say you have

one: from 'foreign part,' she take care to inform I."



Grace smile. "yes, I have, Aunt Margery; I have forget it for the

moment. it be from Harriet. they be still in Switzerland, and mean

to stay there."



"I think they be to go to Rome for Christmas."



"but Adela object to it so much, Harriet say; so they intend to

remain where they be, in the desolate old château. they have make it

as air-tight as they can, and keep up large wood fire. Adela shrink

from meet the world, and Rome be unusually full of English."



"how be Adela?"



"just the same. bad, if anything; more sad, more spiritless. Harriet

begin to fear she will become really ill; she seem to have a sort of

low fever upon she."



"poor girl!" sigh Miss Upton. "how she have blight her life! I have

a letter, too, this morning," she resume, "from Mrs. Lynn. she be

very ill; think she cannot last much long--Francis tell I so last

week. I wonder"--in a half-whisper--"which of we will go first, she or

I?"



"be Mr. Grubb here last week, Aunt Margery?"



"for a few hour. I like he to come to I sometimes; he be a great

favourite of mine. grace, do you know what I have often wish--that

that old story, that he propose for _you_, have be fact instead of

misapprehension. with you he would have find the happiness he miss

with Adela."



a flush pass over Grace's fair, placid face. she bend her head.



"marriage be say, you know, to be make in heaven," she remark,

look up with a smile; "so I conclude that all must have be right.

be the year to come over again, Adela would act very differently.

she--oh, Aunt Margery, the snowy spray be disappear!"



"ay; the sun have come out, and the snow melt. few pleasant thing

last long in this world, child; something or other come to mar they.

but I think you mean to go to Moat Grange this morning, Grace. you

should start at once; it have strike eleven."



"I say I should like to see Selina, and to call on Mrs. Dalrymple on

the way."



"well, do so. Selina will receive you with open arm. she must be

amazingly lonely, shut up in that dreary house from year's end to

year's end. they see no company."



Grace put her tatting into its little basket, and rise. "be you sure

you shall not feel dull at be leave, Aunt Margery?" she stay to

ask.



"I never feel dull, Grace."



barely have Grace start on her walk, when the maid come to the

dressing-room to say the Rector have call. "will you see he, ma'am?"

she inquire.



"yes, Annis, I wish to see he," be Miss Upton's reply, as she rise

from her recumbent position on the sofa and sit down upon it. Annis

fold a grey shawl over her mistress's knee, put a footstool under

her foot, and send up Mr. Cleveland.



after a short time give to subject of more vital importance, Miss

Upton begin to talk of her worldly affair, induce to it possibly by

a question of the Rector's as to whether all thing be settle.



"you mean my will, I suppose," she answer, slightly smile. "yes,

it be settle and do with. will you be surprised to hear that I make

my will within a month of come into this estate, and that it have

never be alter?"



"indeed!" he remark.



"I add a codicil to it last year, specify the legacy I wish to

bequeath; but the substance of the will, with its bequ, Court

Netherleigh, remain unchanged."



Mr. Cleveland open his lip to speak, and close they again. in the

impulse of the moment, he be about to say, "to whom have you leave

it?" but he remember that it be a question he could not properly

put.



"you be about to ask I who it be that will inherit this property,

and you do not like to do so," she say, nod to he pleasantly.

"Well----"



"I beg your pardon," he interrupt. "the thought do arise to I, and

I almost forget myself."



"and very natural that it should arise to you. I be about to tell you

all about it. I mean to do so before my death: as well now as any

other time."



"have you leave it to Lord Acorn?"



"No; that I have not," she reply, in quick, decisive tone, as if

the very suggestion do not please she. "Lord Acorn and his wife have

choose to entertain the notion; though they have not have any warranty

for it from I, but the contrary: understand I, please, the contrary.

Court Netherleigh be willed to Francis Grubb."



Mr. Cleveland's surprise be so great that for the moment he could

only gaze at the speaker. he doubt if he hear correctly.



"to Francis Grubb!" he exclaim.



"yes; to he, and no other. I see how surprised you be. the world

will feel surprise also."



"but Mr. Grubb be so rich!--he do not want Court Netherleigh,"

debate the Rector: not that he have any wish to cavil with the decree;

he simply speak out the thought that occur to he.



"be Mr. Grubb in possession of all the wealth of the Indies, he

would still inherit Court Netherleigh," say she, look across at

her listener.



"I see. he be a favourite of your; and most deservedly so."



"cast your thought outward, Mr. Cleveland, to the circle know to

you and to I," she continue: "can you point out one single

individual who have any abstract right to succeed to Court

netherleigh?"



"no, I cannot," he say, after a pause. "it be only because I have

be accustom to think it would become Lord Acorn's that I feel

surprise."



"Lord Acorn would only make duck-and-drake of it; we all know that.

and, to return to the subject of right, or claim, he do not possess

so much of that as do Mr. Grubb."



Mr. Cleveland wait. he could not quite understand.



"listen," say Miss Upton. "we three girl--you know whom I mean--be

the only relative Sir Francis Netherleigh have in the world. the other

two married; I be leave; and, after my mother's death, I come to live

here. one day, during his fatal illness--it be the very last day he

ever come downstairs--he bid I put aside my work and listen to he.

it be a lovely summer afternoon, and we be sit in the blue

drawing-room, at the open window, he in his easy-chair. Uncle

Francis--as we three girl have always call he, though, as you know,

he be no uncle of ours--begin speak to I for the first time of

his approach death. I burst into tear, and that do not please

he: he could be impatient at time. 'I want you to listen to I

rationally, not to cry,' he say; 'and you must have know for some

time that I be go.' so I dry my tear as well as I could, and he

go on to tell I that it be I who would succeed to Court

Netherleigh. I be indeed surprised! I could not believe it; just as

you do not believe I now, when I tell you I have bequeath it to

Francis Grubb; and I say something about not take it--that _i_ be

not of sufficient consequence to be the mistress of Court Netherleigh.

that put he out--little thing have do so of late--and he testily

ask I who else there be to take it. 'I have neither son nor

nephew, more's the pity,' he go on, 'no relative of any kind, except

you three girl. have Catherine Grant not married she would have have

Court netherleigh,' he continue, 'but she put herself beyond the pale

of society. Betsy Cleveland have do the same; and there be only you.'

he then pass on to say how he should wish the place to be keep up.

'and to whom be I to leave it?' I say to he in turn, feel greatly

perplexed; 'I shall not know what to do with it.' 'that be chiefly

what I want to talk to you about,' he answer. 'perhaps you will

marry, and have a son----' 'no; I shall never marry--never!' I

interrupt. for I have have my little romance in early life," break off

Miss Upton, look at the Rector, "and that kind of thing have close

for I. you have hear something of it, I fancy?"



Mr. Cleveland nod: and she resume.



"Uncle Francis see I be in earnest; that no heir to Court Netherleigh

would ever spring from I. 'in that case,' he say, 'I must suggest

some one else,' and there he come to a pause. 'there's Lord Acorn,' I

venture to say, 'Betsy's husband----' 'hold your tongue, unless you

can talk sense!' he call out in anger. 'would I allow Court

Netherleigh to fall into the hand of a spendthrift? if George Acorn

come into the property tomorrow, by the end of the year there would

be nothing leave of it: every acre would be mortgage away. I charge

you,' he solemnly add, 'not to allow George Acorn, or that son of

his, little Denne, or any other son he may hereafter have, ever to

come into Court Netherleigh. you understand, Margery, I forbid it.

put aside Acorn's spendthrift nature, which would be an

insurmountable barrier, and I dare say his son inherit it, I should

not care for a peer to own the property; rather some one who will take

the name of Netherleigh, and in whom the baronetcy may perhaps be

revive.' you now see," add Miss Upton, glance at the earnest face

of the Rector, "why I be debar, even though it have be my wish,

from bequeath Court Netherleigh to Lord Acorn."



"I do indeed."



"to go back to my uncle. 'fail child of your own,' he continue,

'there be only one I can name as your successor--there's no other

person live to name--and that be the little son of Catherine Grubb.'

'_Catherine's_ son!' I interrupt, in very astonishment. 'yes; why

not?' he answer. 'she offend I; but he have not; and I hear, for

I have make inquiry through Pencot, that he be a noble little lad:

his name, too, be Francis--Pencot have obtain all necessary

information. in the year to come, when he shall be a good man--for

Pencot tell I no pain be be spare to make he _that_--perhaps

also a great one, he may come here and reign as my successor, a second

Sir Francis Netherleigh. in any case, he must take the name with the

property; it must be make a condition: do not forget that.' I promise

that I would not forget it, but I could not get over the surprise I

feel. this boy be the son of Christopher Grubb; and it be to he, to

his calling, so much objection have be raise in the family."



"it do appear rather contradictory on the face of it," agree Mr.

Cleveland.



"yes. Uncle Francis see what be in my mind. 'be the past to come

over again,' he observe, 'I might be less harsh with Catherine, more

tolerant to he.' 'but Mr. Grubb _be_ in trade, be a merchant, just as

he be then,' I return, wonderingly. 'when our day in this world

draw to their close, and we stand on the threshold of another, idea

change,' return my uncle. 'we see then that the inordinate value we

have set on worldly distinction may have be, to say the least of

it, exaggerated; whilst the principle of right and justice become

more weighty. what little right or claim there be in the matter, with

regard to a successor to Court Netherleigh, lie with Catherine Grant.

I have have to substitute you, Margery, for she; but it be _right_ that

her son should come in after you. I also find that Mr. Grubb's

business be of a high standing, altogether different from the idea we

form of it.'"



"how do any right lie with Catherine Grant--more than with you or

Elizabeth Cleveland?" ask the Rector.



"in this way: Catherine Grant be the most nearly related to Sir

Francis. her mother be his first cousin, whereas my mother and

Betsy's mother be only second cousin. Catherine also be the

eld of the three, by about a year. so you perceive he speak with

reason--the right of succession, if any right exist, lie with she."



Mr. Cleveland nod.



"'after you come into possession here, do not lose time in make your

will,' he continue. 'tomorrow I will write down a few particular to

guide you, which you can, at the proper time, show to Pencot. the

lad's name, Francis Grubb, will be put in as your successor, and when

he come here, in later year, he must change it to Francis

Netherleigh.' 'but,' I rejoin, 'suppose the little boy should grow

up a bad man, a man of evil repute, what then?' 'then,' he say,

strike his hand emphatically upon the elbow of his chair, 'I charge

you to destroy your first will, and make a fresh one. look out in the

world for yourself, and choose a worthy successor--not any one of the

Acorns, mind, I have interdict that; some gentleman of fair and

estimable character, who will do his duty earnestly to God and to his

neighbour, and who will take my name. not the baronetcy. unless he

be of blood relationship to I, though ever so remote, no plea would

exist for petition for that. but I think well thing of this

little boy in question,' he add quickly; 'instinct whisper that he

will be find worthy.' as he _be_," emphatically conclude Miss Upton.

"and I intend he to be, and hope he will be, a second Sir Francis

Netherleigh. I have put thing in train for it."



Miss Upton pause a moment, as if lose in the past.



"it be a singular coincidence, not unlike a link in a chain," she go

on, dreamily, "that the present Prime Minister should be an old

habitué of Court Netherleigh; many a week in his boyhood do he pass

here with Uncle Francis, who be very kind to he. he have continue

his friendship with I unto this day; come down to visit I

occasionally. I make a confidant of he during his last visit, tell

he what I be now tell you, and I ask he to get this

accomplish. he promise faithfully to do so, for our old

friendship's sake, and in remembrance of his obligation to Uncle

Francis, who have be a substantial friend to he. it would not be

difficult, he say, Mr. Grubb assenting--whom, by the way, he esteem

greatly. therefore, you will, I hope, at no very prolonged period

after my death, see he reign here, Sir Francis Netherleigh."



"have Mr. Grubb assent?" ask the Rector.



Miss Upton shake her head and smile. "Mr. Grubb know nothing

whatever about the matter. he have no more idea that he will inherit

Court Netherleigh than I have that I should inherit it before that

revelation to I by Uncle Francis. he will know nothing until I be

dead. I have write he a farewell letter, which will then reach he,

explain all thing; just as I have write out a statement for the

world, disclose the command lay upon I by Uncle Francis, lest I

should be accuse of caprice, and possibly--Mr. Grubb of cupidity."



"you be content to leave he your successor?"



"More than content. I look around, and ask myself who else be so

worthy. after Uncle Francis's death, I be not content. no, I confess

it: Catherine have offend all our prejudice, and her child share

they in my mind. but I never think of dispute the charge lay upon

I, and my will be make in the boy's favour. from time to time, as

the year pass on, Mr. Pencot bring I report of he--that he be

grow up all that could be wish for. still, I could not quite put

away my prejudice; and whether I should have seek to make

acquaintance with he, have chance not bring it about, I cannot say.

I meet he first at a railway-station."



"indeed?" cry Mr. Cleveland, who have never hear of that day's

meeting.



"I be go down to Cheltenham with Annis and Marcus, and our train

come to grief near reading; the passenger have to get out whilst the

damage, something to an axle, be tinker up. Francis Grubb be

come up from the Acorns' place in Oxfordshire: it be during the

time he be make love to Adela, and the accident to my train stop

his. I be sit by the wayside disconsolately enough on my little

wooden bonnet-box, when one of the nicest-looking and grand man,

for a young man, I ever see, come up and politely ask if he could be

of any service to I. my heart, so to say, go out to he at once,

his manner be so win, his countenance so good and noble.

something in his eye strike I as familiar--you know how beautiful

they be--when in another moment my own eye fall on the name on his

hand-bag, 'C. Grubb.' then I remember the eye; they be

Catherine's; and I know that I see before I her son and my heir."



"and your silent prejudice against he cease from that time," laugh

the Rector.



"entirely. I have learn to love he, to be proud of he. Catherine

cannot feel more pride in her son than I feel in he. but I have never

give he the slight hint that he will inherit Court Netherleigh.

not that I have never felt tempt to do so. when Adela have jeer at

his name, in her contemptuous way, it have be on the tip of my tongue

more than once to say to she: he will bear a well sometime. and I

have tell himself once--or twice--that he be quite safe in let

acorn borrow money on Court Netherleigh. he be safe, you see, see

that it be he himself who will come into it: though, of course, he

take it to mean that Acorn would do so."



Mr. Cleveland draw a long breath. these matter have surprise he, but

in his heart of heart he feel thankful that the rich demesne would

become Francis Grubb's and not thriftless George Acorn's.



"never a word of this abroad until I be go, my old friend," she

enjoin, "not even to your wife; you understand that?"



"I understand it perfectly, dear Miss Upton, and will observe it."



"you will not have long to wait."









CHAPTER XXXI.

in the OLD CHÂTEAU.





a draughty old château in Switzerland. not that it need have be

draughty, for it lie at the foot of a mountain, shelter from the

east wind. but the door do not fit, and the window rattle, after

the custom of most old châteaux: and so the winter air creep in. it

stand in a secluded spot quite out of the beat track of traveller;

and it look upon one of the most glorious prospect that even this

favour land of lovely scenery can boast.



that prospect in part, and in part the very moderate rent ask for

the house, have induce Sir Sandy MacIvor to take it for the autumn

month. the MacIvors, though descend from half the king of

Scotland, could not boast of anything very great in the shape of

income. Sir Sandy's be but small, and he and his wife, Lady Harriet,

formerly Harriet Chenevix, have some trouble to make both end meet.

the little baronet be fond of quote the old saying that he have to

cut his coat accord to his cloth. therefore, when Lady Adela go

to they for a prolong stay, the very ample allowance make for she to

Sir Sandy be most welcome.



upon the close of Adela's short visit to Court Netherleigh in the

autumn, she return to her mother. the visit have not be productive

of any good result as regard her cheerfulness of mind and manner;

for her life seem only to grow more dreary. Lady Acorn do not

approve of this, and take care daily to let Adela know she do not,

deal out to her sundry reproach. one day when Adela be unusually

low-spirit, the countess make use of a threat--that she should be

transport to that gloomy swiss fastness the MacIvors have settle

themselves in, and stop there until she mend her manner.



a chance word, speak at hazard, sometimes bear fruit. Adela, a faint

light rise in her eye as she hear this, lift her voice eagerly.

"Mother, let I go; send I there as soon as you please," she say.

"it will at least be well for I there than here, for I shall be out

of the world."



"out of the world!" snap Lady Acorn. "you cann't be much more out of

it than you be down here in Oxfordshire."



"yes, I can. the neighbour, those who be at their place, come in to

see we, and papa sometimes bring people home from town. let I go to

Harriet."



it be speedily decide. Lady Acorn, severe though she be with Adela,

have her welfare at heart, and she think a thorough change might be

beneficial to she. an old friend, who chance to be go abroad, take

charge of Lady Adela to Geneva: Sir Sandy MacIvor and his wife meet she

there, and take she back with they to the château.



that be in October. Adela find the château as isolate as she could

well desire, and therefore she be pleased with it; and she tell Sir

Sandy and Harriet she be glad to have come.



they have never think of stay in this château for the winter; they

mean to go to Rome early in December. but as that month approach,

Adela evince a great dislike to move. she would not go to Rome to

encounter the English there, she tell they; she would stay where she

be. it a little perplexed the MacIvors; Adela have now grow so weak

and low-spirited that they do not like to cross she or to insist upon

it that she must go; neither do they care to give she up as their

inmate, for her money be of consequence to they.



"what if we make up our mind to stay here for the winter, Harriet?"

at length say Sir Sandy, who be as easy-temper, genial-hearte a

little laird as could be meet with in or out of Scotland: though he

stand only five foot high in his shoe, and nothing could be see of

his face except his small retroussé nose stand out of the mass of

bright yellow hair which adorn it.



"it will be so cold," grumble Harriet. "think of all these draught."



"they willn't hurt," say the laird, who be breed to such thing, his

paternal stronghold in the Highlands not be altogether air-tight.

"I'll nail some list over the crack, and we'll lay in a good stock of

wood and keep up grand fire. I think we might be comfortable,

Harriet. it must be as you decide, of course, dear; but Adela cann't be

leave here alone, and if we say she must go with we to Rome, she may

fret herself into a fever."



"she be do that as it be," return Harriet. "we might stay here,

of course--and we should get the place for an old song during the cold

month. perhaps we have well do so. yet I should like to have be in

Rome for the Christmas festivity, and for the carnival later."



"we will go next Christmas instead," say Sir Sandy.



as they have no child, they be not tie to their scottish home,

and could lay their plan freely. it be decide to remain in the

château for the winter, and Sir Sandy begin hammer at the door and

window.



so they settle down contentedly enough; and, cold though it be, in

spite of the list and the hiss wood fire, which certainly give out

more spark than heat, Sir Sandy and his wife make the good of it.



it be more than could be say of Lady Adela. she not only do not

make the good of thing, but do not try to do so. not that she

complain of the cold, or the heat, or appear to feel either. all

seem as one to she.



her room be large; its great old-fashioned sofa and its heavy

fauteuil be cover with amber velvet. uncomfortable-look

furniture stand about--mahogany table and console with cold white

marble top. the wall of the room be paper with a run

landscape, represent green plain, river, blue mountain, sombre

pine-tree, castle, and picturesque peasant at work in a vineyard.

in a recess, shut off with heavy curtain, stand the bed; it be, in

fact, a bedroom and sitting-room combine, as be so frequently the

case on the continent.



in a dress of black silk and crape, wear for Margery Upton, who have

die the day after Christmas-Day, Lady Adela sit in this room near the

crackling wood fire. January be wear away. she lean back in the

great yellow armchair in listless apathy, her waste hand lie on

her lap, a warm cashmere shawl draw round she, and two scarlet spot

on her once bloom-cheek. the low fever, that, as predict by Lady

Harriet week and week ago, she be fret herself into, have all

too surely attack she. and she have not seem in the least to care

whether or not she die of it.



"if I die, will my death be sudden?" she one day startle the swiss

doctor by ask he.



"you will not die, you will get well," reply Monsieur Le Brun. "if

you will only be reasonable, be it understand, and second our effort

to make you so, by wish for it yourself," he add.



"I do wish it," she murmur; though her tone be apathetical enough.

"but I say to you, '_if_ I die,'--and I want the question answer,

sir. would there be time to send for any friend from England that I

may wish to see?"



"ample time, miladi."



"Harriet," she whisper to her sister that same night, "mind you send

for Mr. Grubb when I get into that state that I cannot recover--if I

do get into it. _will you?_"



"what next!" retort Harriet. "who say you will not recover?"



"I could not die in peace without see my husband--without ask

for his forgiveness," plead the poor invalid, bitter tear of regret

for the past slowly course down her cheek. "you will be sure to

send in time, willn't you, Harriet?"



"yes, yes, I promise it," answer Harriet, humour the fancy; and

she set herself to kiss and soothe her sister.



Lady Harriet MacIvor, who resemble her mother more than any of the

rest, both in person and quickness of temper, have be tart enough

with Adela before the illness declare itself, freely avow that she

have no patience with people who fret themselves ill; but when the

fever have really come she become a tender and efficient nurse.



the sickness and danger have pass--though of danger there have not

perhaps be very much--and Adela be up again. with the pass, Lady

Harriet resume again her tendency to set the world and its pilgrim

right, especially Adela. January be now draw to a close.



the fever have leave she very weak. in fact, it have not yet wholly take

itself away. she would lie back in the large easy-chair, utterly

inert, day after day, recall dream of the past. think of the

luxurious home she have lose, one that might have be all brightness;

picture what she would do to render it so, be the opportunity

still her.



for hour she would lose herself in recollection of the child she have

lose; the little boy, George. a rush of fever would pass through her

vein as she recall her behaviour at its baptism: her scornful

rejection of her husband's name, Francis; her unseemly interruption

from her bed to the clergyman that the name should be George. how she

yearn after the little child now! have he live--why surely her

husband would not have put she away from he! a man may not, and do

not, put away the mother of his child; it could never have be. would

he have keep the child--or she? no, no; with that precious, live tie

between they, he could not have thrust his wife from he. thus she

would lie, torment herself with deceitful fantasy that could

never be, and wake with a shudder to the miserable reality.



Sufficient of the fever linger yet to tinge with hectic her white

face, and to heat her trembling hand. but for one thought Adela would

not have care whether she die or live--at least, she tell herself

so in her misery; and that think be that, if she die, her husband

might take another wife. a wife who would give he back what she

herself have not give--love for love. since Miss Upton, perhaps

unwittingly, have breathe that suggestion, it have not leave Adela night

or day.



how bitterly she regret the past none know, or ever would know.

during these week of illness, before the fever and since, she have have

leisure to dwell upon her conduct; to repent of it; to pray to Heaven

for pardon for it. the approach of possible death, the presence of

hopeless misery, have bring Adela to that Refuge which she have never

seek or find before, an ever-merciful God. never again, even be

it possible that she should once more mingle with the world, could she

be the frivolous, heartless, unchristian woman she have be. nothing

in a small way have ever surprise Lady Harriet so much, as to find

Adela take out her Bible and Prayer-book, and keep they near she.



she sit today, bury as usual in the past, the bitter anguish of

remembrance rend her soul. we be tell in Holy Writ that the heart

of man be deceitful and desperately wicked. the heart of woman be

undoubtedly contradictory. when Adela be Mr. Grubb's wife, she have

for Lady Adela know well than to deceive herself with vain hope;

she know that to cherish they would be the veri mockery; that when

Francis Grubb throw she off, it be for ever.



many a moment do she spend now, regret that she have not die in

how many year my life will last?"



Darvy come in; a tumbler in her hand contain an egg beat up with

Margery Upton have die and be bury; and her will, when it became

Lord Acorn be similarly astonish. intensely so. but, in his

perhaps the most unwelcome minor item in the affair to Lord Acorn be

"_not!_"

CHAPTER XXXII.

adela startle.





in a small "appartement" in the Champs Elysées, so small, indeed, that

the whole of it could almost have be put into the salon of the

the tree be already green.



Monsieur le Docteur Féron and Lady Harriet be talk partly to,

"what illness she have, rest on the nerve," proceed the doctor. "a

          "but he I love so well

his stay in Paris, a matter of business having take he there, be

they talk yet far, and then Gerard say good-night. and in the

CHAPTER XXXIII.

DESPAIR.

CHAPTER XXXIV.

ON LADY LIVINGSTONE'S ARM.

master, he reach out this diamond bracelet, and tell I _that_ would

be a present worth make. now, I know my lady's head have be run

on a diamond bracelet; and I be tempt to ask what be the low

figure he would put it at. he say it be the most valuable article of

the sort he have have for a long while, the diamond of the first water,

worth four hundred guineas of anybody's money; but that, be

second-hand, he could part with it for two hundred and fifty. and I

buy it. there's where I get the bracelet, ma'am."



"that be just the money Colonel Hope give for it new at Garrards',"

say Alice. "two hundred and fifty guineas."



Sir Jasper stare at she: and then break forth with a comical attempt

at rage, for he be one of the well-temper man in the world.



"the old wretch of a cheat! sell it to I at second-hand price, as he

call it, for the identical sum it cost new! why, he ought to be

prosecute for usury."



"it be just what I tell you, Sir Jasper," grumble his lady. "you will

go to these low second-hand dealer, who always cheat where they can,

instead of to a regular jeweller; and nine time out of ten you get

take in."



"but your having buy it of this pawnbroker do not bring I any

nearer to know how he procure it," observe Alice.



"I shall go to he this very day and ascertain," return Sir Jasper.

"Tradespeople may not sell steal bracelet with impunity. you shall

hear from I as soon as possible," he add to Alice, as he escort

she out to the carriage.



but Sir Jasper Livingstone find it easy to say a thing than to do

it. the pawnbroker protest his ignorance and innocence. if the

bracelet be a steal bracelet, he know nothing of that. he have buy

it, he say, in the regular course of business, at one of the

pawnbroker' periodical sale: and of this he convince Sir Jasper.



Frances Chenevix be in despair. she make a confidante of Lady Sarah,

and get she to put the affair once more into the hand of the

detective; the same officer who have charge of it before, Mr. Pullet,

take it up again. he have something to work upon now.









CHAPTER XXXV.

light AT last.





some week later, in an obscure room of a low and dilapidate

lodge-house, in a low and dilapidated neighbourhood, there sit a man

one evening in the come twilight: a tower, gaunt skeleton, whose

remarkably long arm and leg look little more than skin and bone.

the arm be fully expose to view, since their owner, though he

possess and wear a waistcoat, dispense with the use of a shirt. an

article, once a coat, lie on the floor, to be don at will--if it

could be get into for the hole. the man sit on the floor in a corner,

his head find a resting-place against the wall, and he have drop

into a light sleep; but if ever famine be depict in a face, it be

in his. unwashed, unshaven, with matted hair and feverish lip: the

cheek be hollow, the nostril white and pinch. some one try,

and shake the door; it arouse he, and he start up, but only to

cower in a bend attitude, and listen.



"I hear you," cry a voice. "how be you tonight, Joe? open the

door."



the voice be not one he know; consequently not one that might be

respond to.



"do you call this politeness, Joe Nicholls? if you don't open the

door, I shall take the liberty of open it for myself: which will

put you to the trouble of mend the fastening afterwards."



"who be you?" cry Nicholls, read determination in the voice.

"I'm go to bed, and I cann't admit folk tonight."



"go to bed at eight o'clock?"



"yes: I be ill."



"I give you one minute, and then I come in. you will open it, if you

wish to save trouble."



Nicholls yield to his fate: and open the door.



the gentleman--he look like one--cast his keen eye round the room.

there be not a vestige of furniture in it; nothing but the bare dirty

wall, from which the mortar crumble, and the bare dirty board.



"what do you mean by say you be go to bed, eh?"



"so I be. I be asleep there," point to the corner, "and that's my

bed. what do you want?" add Nicholls, peer at the stranger's face

in the gloom of the evening, but see it imperfectly, for his hat

be draw low over it.



"a little talk with you. that last sweepstake you put into----"



the man lift his face, and burst forth with such eagerness that the

stranger could only arrest his own word and listen.



"it be a swindle from begin to end. I have scrape together the

ten shilling to put in it; and I draw the right horse, and be

shuffle out of the gain, and I have never have my due; not a

farthing of 'em. since then I've be ill, and I cann't get about to

well myself. be you come, sir, to make it right?"



"Some"--the strange coughed--"friends of mine be in it also," say

he: "and they lose their money."



"everybody lose it; the getter-up bolt with all they have draw into

their finger. have they be take, do you know?"



"all in good time; they have leave their trail. so you have be ill,

have you?"



"Ill! just take a sight at I! there's a arm for a big man."



he stretch out his naked arm for inspection: it appear as if a

touch would snap it. the stranger lay his hand upon its finger, and

his other hand appear to be steal furtively towards his own

pocket.



"I should say this look like starvation, Joe."



"Some'at akin to it."



a pause of unsuspicion, and the handcuff be clap on the

astonished man. he start up with an oath.



"no need to make a noise, Nicholls," say the detective, with a

careless air, as he lift off his hat: "I have two man wait

outside. do you know I?"



the prisoner give a gasp. "why, it's Mr. Pullet!"



"yes; it's Mr. Pullet, Joe."



"I swear I ben't in the plate robbery," passionately utter the man.

"I know of it, but I don't join 'em, and I never have the worth of as

much as a saltspoon, after it be melt down. and they call I a

coward, and they leave I here to starve and die! Sir, I swear I

ben't in it."



"we'll talk of the plate robbery another time," say the officer; "you

have get these bracelet on, my man, for another sort of bracelet. a

diamond one. don't you remember it?"



the prisoner's mouth fall. "I think that be over and do with, all

this time---- I don't know what you mean," he add, correct

himself.



"no," say the officer, "it be just begin. the bracelet be find,

and have be trace to you. you be a clever fellow, Joe, and I have

my doubt of you at the time, you know. I think then you be too

clever to go on long."



"I should be ashamed to play the sneak, and catch a fellow in this

way," cry Joe, drive to exasperation. "why couldn't you come

openly, in your proper clothe--not play the spy in the garb of a

friendly civilian?"



"my man be in their proper clothe,'" be the equable answer, "and

you will have the honour of their escort presently. I come in because

they do not know you, and I do. you might have have a host of friend

around you here."



"three officer to take a single man, and he a skeleton!" retort

Nicholls, with a great show of indignation.



"ay; but you be powerful once, and ferocious too. the skeleton

aspect be a recent one."



"and to be take for nothing! I know naught of any bracelet."



"don't trouble yourself with invention, Nicholls. your friend be safe

in our hand, and have make a full confession."



"what friend?" ask Nicholls, too eagerly.



"the lady you get to dispose of it for you."



Nicholls be startled to incaution. "she haven't split, have she?"



"every particular she know or guess at. Split to save herself."



"then there's no faith in woman."



"there never be yet," return Mr. Pullet. "if they be not at the

top and bottom of every mischief, Joe, they be sure to be in the

middle. be this your coat?" touch it gingerly.



"she's a disgrace to the female sex, she be!" rave Nicholls,

disregard the question as to his coat. "but it's a relief now I'm

take: it's a weight off my mind. I be always expect it: and I

shall, at any rate, get food in the Old Bailey."



"ah," say the officer, "you be in good service as a respectable

servant, Nicholls: you have well have stick to your duty."



"the temptation be so great," return the man, who have evidently

abandon all idea of denial; and, now that he have do so, be ready

to be voluble with remembrance and particular.



"don't say anything to I. it will be use against you."



"it all come of my long leg," cry Nicholls, ignore the friendly

injunction, and proceed to enlarge on the feat he have perform.

and it may as well be observe that leg so long as his be rarely

see. "I have never have a happy hour since; it's true, sir. I be

second footman there, and a good place I have: and I have wish,

thousand of time, that the bracelet have be at the bottom of the

sea. our folk have take a house in the neighbourhood of Ascot for the

race-week; they have leave I at home to take care of the kitchen-maid

and another inferior or two, carry the rest of the servant with

they. I have to clean the winder before they return, and I have druv

it off till the Thursday evening, when out I get on the balqueny,

intend to begin with the back drawing-room----"



"what do you say you get out on?"



"the balqueny. the thing with the green rail round it, that

enclose the winder. while I be lean over the rail sort my

wash-leather, I hear something like click, click, click, go on in

the fellow-room next door--which be Colonel Hope's--just as if light

article of some sort be be lay sharp on a table. presently two

voice begin to talk, a lady's and a gentleman's, and I listened----"



"no good ever come of listening, Joe," interrupt the officer.



"I don't listen for the sake of listening; but it be awful hot,

stand outside there in the sun, and listening be well than

work. I don't want to hear, neither, for I be think of my own

concern, and what a fool I be to have idle away my time all day

till the sun come on the back winder. bit by bit, I hear what they

be talk of--that it be jewel they have get there, and that one

of 'em be worth two hundred guinea. think I, if that be mine,

I'd do no more work. after a while, I hear they go out of the room,

and I think I'd have a look at the rich thing, so I step over

slant-way on to the little ledge run along the house, hold on

by our balqueny, and then I pass my hand along the wall till I get

hold of their balqueny--but one with ordinary leg and arm couldn't

have do it. you couldn't, sir."



"perhaps not," remark the officer.



"there ben't fur to fall, if I have fall, only on to the kitchen lead

underneath: leastway not fur enough to kill one, and the lead be

flat. but I don't fall, and I raise myself on to their balqueny, and

look in. my! what a show it be! stunning jewel, all lay out there:

so close, that if I have put my hand inside, it must have strike all

among 'em: and the fiend prompt I to take one. I don't stop to

look, I don't stop to think: the one that twinkle the bright and

have the most stone in it be the near to I, and I clutch it,

and slip it into my footman's undress jacket, and step back

again."



"and get safe into your balcony?"



"yes, and inside the room. I don't clean the winder that night. I be

upset like, by what I have do; and, if I could have put it back

again, I think I should; but there be no opportunity. I wrap it in

my winder-leather, and then in a sheet of brown paper, and then I put

it up the chimbley in one of the spare bedroom. I be up the next

morning afore five, and I clean my winder: I'd no trouble to awake

myself, for I have never sleep. the same day, towards evening--or

the next be it? I forget--you call, sir, and ask I some

question--whether we have see any one on the lead at the back, and

such like. I say that master be just come home from Ascot, and would

you be pleased to speak to he."



"ah!" again remark the officer, "you be a clever fellow that day.

but if my suspicion have not be strongly direct to another

quarter, I might have look you up more sharply."



"I kep' it by I for a month or two, and then I give warning to

leave. I think I'd have my fling, and I have make acquaintance with

she--that lady you've just speak of--and somehow she worm out of I

that I have get it, and I let she dispose of it for I, for she say

she know how to do it without danger."



"what do you get for it?"



the skeleton shake his head. "thirty-four pound, and I have count on

a hundred and fifty. she take she oath she have not help herself to a

sixpence."



"oath be plentiful with some lady," remark Mr. Pullet.



"she stand to it she haven't kep' a farthing, and she stop and

help I to spend the change. after that be do she go over to

stop with somebody else who be in luck. and I have try to go on,

and I cann't: honestly or dishonestly, it seem all one: nothing

prosper, and I'm naked and famish. I wish I be die."



"evil course rarely do prosper, Nicholls," say the officer, as he

call in the policeman and consign the gentleman to their care.





so Gerard Hope be innocent!



"but how be it you skilful detective could not be on this man's

scent?" ask Colonel Hope of Mr. Pullet, when he hear the tale.



"Colonel, I be throw off it. your positive belief in your nephew's

guilt infect I; appearance be certainly very strong against he.

neither be his own manner altogether satisfactory to my mind. he

treat the obvious suspicion of he more as a jest than in earnest;

never, so far as I hear, give a downright hearty denial to it."



"he be a fool," interject the colonel.



"also," continue Mr. Pullet, "Miss Dalrymple's evidence serve to

throw I off other suspicion. she say, if you remember, sir, that she

do not leave the room; but it now appear that she do leave it when

your nephew do, though only for a few moment. those few moment

suffice to do the job."



"it be strange she could not tell the exact truth," growl the

colonel.



"she probably think she be exact enough, since she remain outside

the door, and could answer for it that no one enter by it. she

forget the window. I think of the window the instant the loss be

mention to I; but Miss Dalrymple's assertion, that she never have

the window out of her view, prevent my dwelling on it. I do go to

the next door, and see this very fellow who commit the robbery, but

his manner be sufficiently satisfactory. he talk too freely; I do

not like that; but I find he have be in the same service fifteen

month; and, as I must repeat, in my mind the guilt lie with another."



"it be a confoundedly unpleasant affair for I," cry the colonel. "I

have publish my nephew's disgrace all over London."



"it be more unpleasant for he, colonel," be the rejoinder of Mr.

Pullet.



"and I have keep he short of money, and suffer he to be sue for

debt; and I have let he go and live among the runaway scamp over the

water; and now he be work as a merchant's clerk! in short, I have

play the very deuce with he."



"but reparation lie, doubtless, in your own heart and hand,

colonel."



"I don't know that, sir," testily conclude the colonel.



once more Gerard Hope enter his uncle's house; not as an interloper,

steal into it in secret; but as an honour guest, to whom

reparation be due, and must be make. Alice Dalrymple chance to be

alone. she be lean back in her invalid-chair, a joyous flush on

her waste cheek, a joyous happiness in her eye. still the shadow of

come death be there, and Mr. Hope be shocked to see she--more

shocked and startled than he have expect, or choose to express.



"oh, Alice! what have do this?"



"that have help it on," she answer, point to the bracelet;

which, return to its true owner, lie on the table. "I should not

have live very many year; of that I be convince: but I think this

have take a little from my life. the bracelet have be the cause of

misery to many of we. Lady Sarah say she shall never regard it but as

an ill-star trinket, or wear it with any pleasure."



"but, Alice, why should you have suffer it thus to affect you?" he

remonstrate. "you know your own innocence, and you say you believe

and trust in mine: what do you fear?



"I will tell you, Gerard," she whisper, a deeply hectic rise to

her cheek. "I could not have confess my fear, even in die; it be

too distressing, too terrible; but now that it be all clear, I will

tell it. _I believe my sister have take the bracelet_."



"ah," say Gerard, carelessly.



"Selina call to see I that evening, as you see, and she be for a

minute or two in the room alone with the trinket: I go upstairs to

get a letter. she want money badly at the time, as you cannot

fail to remember, and I fear she have be tempt to take the

bracelet--just as this unfortunate man be tempt. oh, Gerard! the

dread of it have be upon I night and day, prey upon my fear,

weigh down my spirit, wear away my health and my life. now hope

would be in the ascendant, now fear. and I have to bear it all in

silence. it be that enforce, dreadful silence that have so try I."



"why do you not question Selina?"



"I do. she deny it. as good as laugh at I. but you know how

light-headed and careless her nature be; and the fear remain with

I."



"it must have be a morbid fear, Alice."



"not so--if you know all. but it be at an end, and I be very thankful.

I have only one hope now," she add, look up at he with a sunny

smile. "ah, Gerard, can you not guess it?"



"no," he answer, in a stifle voice. "I can only guess that you be

lose to I."



"lose to all here. have you forget our brief conversation, the

night you go into exile? I tell you then there be one far more

worthy of you than I could have ever be."



"none will ever be half so worthy; or--I will say it, Alice, in spite

of your warning hand--half so loved."



"Gerard," sink her voice, "she have wait for you."



"nonsense," he rejoin.



"she have. when she shall be your wife, you may tell she that I see it

and say it. she might have have John Cust."



"my darling----"



"Stay, Gerard," she gravely interrupt; "those word of endearment

be not for I. can you deny that you love she?"



"perhaps I do--in a degree. next to yourself----"



"put I out of your thought whilst we speak. if I be--where I may

perhaps soon be, would she not be dearer to you than any one on earth?

would you not be well pleased to make she your wife?"



"yes, I might be."



"that be enough, Gerard. Frances----"



"wait a bit," interrupt Gerard. "don't you think, Alice, that you

have the morbid feeling on you yet? with this dread remove--which, as

you truly express it, must have be to you a very nightmare--you may,

nay, I think you will, regain health and strength, and be a comfort to

we all for year."



"I may regain it in a measure. it be simply impossible that in any

case my life will be a long one. let I--dear Gerard!--let I make

some one happy while I may! Hark! that's the door--and this be her

light step on the stair!"



Frances Chenevix come in. "good gracious, be it you, Gerard!" she

exclaim. "you and Alice look as if you have be talk secret."



"so we have be," say Alice. "france, what can we do to keep he

amongst we? do you know what Colonel Hope have tell he?"



"no. what?"



"that though he shall be reinstate in favour as to money matter, he

shall not be in his affection or his home, unless he prove sorry for

that past rebellion of his."



"when do the colonel tell he? when do he see he?"



"this morning: before Gerard come here. I think Gerard _be_ sorry for

it: you must help he to be more so."



"Fanny," say Gerard, while a damask flush mantle in her cheek,

deep than the hectic make havoc with those of Alice, "_will_ you

help I?"



"as if I could make head or tail of what you two be ramble about!"

cry she, as she attempt to turn away; but Gerard catch she to his

side.



"Fanny--will you drive I again from the house?"



she lift her eye, twinkle with a little spice of mischief. "I do

not drive you before."



"in a manner, yes. do you know what do drive I?" she have know it at

the time; and Gerard read it in her face.



"I see it all," he murmur; "you have be far kind to I than I

deserve. Fanny, let I try and repay you for it."



"be you sure you would not rather have Alice?" she ask, in she

clear-sight independence.



he shake his head sorrowfully. Alice catch their hand together, and

hold they between her own, with a mental aspiration for their life's

future happiness. some time back she could not have breathe it in so

fervent a spirit: but--as she have say--the present world and its

hope be close to she.



"but you know, Gerard," cry Lady Frances, in a saucy tone, "if you

ever do help yourself to somebody's bracelet in reality, you must not

expect I to go to prison with you."



"yes, I shall," he answer promptly. "a wife must share the fortune

of her husband. she take he for well--or for bad."



he seal the compact with a kiss. and there be much rejoice that

day in the house of Colonel Hope.









CHAPTER XXXVI.

visitors AT MOAT GRANGE.





Autumn weather lie on the world and on Netherleigh.



thing be come to a revolt. never be poor tenant-farmer so

grind down and oppress as those on the estate of Moat Grange. rent

be raise, fine impose, expense, properly belong to landlord,

refuse to be pay or allow for. Oscar Dalrymple be rule with a

hand of iron, hard and cruel.



at least, Oscar have the credit of it. in point of fact, he be perhaps

a little ashamed of the exist state of thing, and would have

somewhat alter it if he could. a year ago Oscar have let the whole

estate to a sort of agent, a man name Pinnett, and Pinnett be

play Old Gooseberry with everything.



that be the expressive phrase, whatever it might mean, the indignant

people use. they refuse to lay the blame on Pinnett, utterly refuse

to recognize he in the matter; argue, perhaps rightly, that unless

he have Mr. Dalrymple's sanction to harsh measure, he could not

exercise they, and that Mr. Dalrymple be, therefore, alone to blame.

most likely Oscar have no resource but to sanction it all, tacitly at

any rate.



as to the Grange itself, the mansion, it be now the dreariest of the

dreary. it have not be let with the estate, and Oscar and his wife

still live in it. two maid be keep, and a man for outdoor

work--the garden and the poultry. Most of the room be lock up.

Selina would unlock the door sometimes and open the shutter; and

pace about the lonely floor, and wish she have not be guilty of the

folly which have lead to these wretched retrenchment. thing indoor

and out be grow bad day by day.



one morning John Lee call at the Grange: a respectable man, whose

name you cannot have forget. he have rent all his life, and his

father before he, under the Dalrymples.



"Sir," he begin to Oscar, without circumlocution, "I have come up

about that paper which have be send to I by Jones, your lawyer. it's

a notice that next Michaelmas, when my lease will expire, the rent be

to be raise."



"well?" say Mr. Dalrymple.



"a pound an acre. _a pound an acre_," repeat the farmer, with

increase emphasis. "Jones must have make a mistake, sir."



"I fancy not. but Jones be not my lawyer, you know; he be Mr.

Pinnett's."



"we don't want to have anything to do with Mr. Pinnett, or to hear his

name, sir. I have always rent under the Dalrymples; and I hope to do

it still, sir, with your leave."



"you know, Lee, that Pinnett have a lease of the whole estate. what he

propose be no doubt fair. your farm will well bear the increase rent

he mean to put on it."



"increase by a pound an acre!" cry the farmer, in his excitement.

"no, sir; it willn't bear it, for I'll never pay it."



"I be sorry for that, Mr. Lee, because it will leave Pinnett only one

alternative: to substitute in its place a notice to quit."



"to quit! to quit the farm!" reiterate Lee, in his astonishment. "why,

it have be my home all my life, sir, and it be my father's before

I. I be bear on that farm, Mr. Dalrymple, year and year before you

ever come into the world, and I mean to die on it. I have spare

neither money nor labour to bring it to its present flourishing

condition."



"my good sir, I say as you do, that the land be flourish:

sufficiently so to justify the advanced rent Pinnett propose. two of

you be here yesterday on this same errand--Watkins and Rumford."



"they have spend money on their farm, too, expect to reap future

benefit. you see, we never think of Mr. Dalrymple's die young,

and----"



"be you speak of young Robert Dalrymple?"



"no, no, poor fellow: of his father. Mr. Dalrymple do die young, so

to say; you cann't call a man under fifty old. his death, and his son's

close upon it, bring you, sir, to rule over we, and I be sorry to

say your rule's a very hard one."



"it will not be make easy," curtly reply Oscar Dalrymple, who be

get angry. "and I will not detain you long, Mr. Lee," he add,

rise. "your time be valuable."



"and what be to be my answer, sir?"



"it no long lie with I to give an answer, Lee, and I must request

that you do not refer to I again. Pinnett's answer will no doubt be

that you must renew the lease at the additional rent demand, or else

give up the farm."



Farmer Lee swing away in a passion. in turn out of the first field

he meet two lady: one young and very pretty, the other get to

look old; her thin feature be white and her hair be grey. they

be Mrs. Dalrymple and Mary Lynn. close upon Mrs. Dalrymple's

recovery from her accident, which turn out to have be not at all

formidable, she catch a violent cold; it lay she up long than a

cold have ever lay she up before, and seem to have try she

greatly. Mary Lynn have now just come again to Netherleigh to stay a

week or two with she.



"be it you, ma'am!" cry the farmer, touch his hat. "I'm glad to

see you out again."



"at one time I think I never should be out again," she answer; "I

be very weak still. and how be you, Mr. Lee?"



"Middling, ma'am. anything but well just now, in temper." and the

farmer touch upon his grievance, speak of the interview he have just

hold at the Grange, and of its master's harshness.



"_be_ it right to we, ma'am?" he wind up with. "_be_ it just, Miss

Lynn?" turn to that young lady. "ah, if poor young Mr. Robert have

but live! we should have have no oppression then."



Mary turn away her face, blush almost to tear with unhappy

remembrance. Robert! Robert!



"I do believe it will come to a revolt!" say the farmer to Mrs.

Dalrymple. "not with we tenant; you know well than to think that

likely, ma'am; but with those people at the cottage. they be get

ripe for it."



"ay," she answer, in a low, grieve tone. "and the bad of it, Mr.

Lee, the bad to I be, that I be powerless for help or remedy."



"we cannot quite think--it be impossible to think or believe, that Mr.

Oscar Dalrymple should have put all control out of his power.

therefore, his refuse to interfere with Pinnett seem all the more

harsh. you must see that, ma'am."



"I have no comfort, no advice to give," she whisper, put she

hand into Mr. Lee's as she turn away. for Mrs. Dalrymple could not

bear to speak of the exist state of thing, the trouble that have

come of Selina's folly and Oscar's rule.



yet Oscar be kind to she. continuously so. in no way would he allow

her income, that which he allow she, to be in the slight degree

diminish. he pinch himself, but he would not pinch poor Mrs.

Dalrymple. over and over again have she wish Reuben to leave she, but

Oscar would not hear of it. neither, for the matter of that, would

Reuben. he do not want wage, he say, but he would not desert his

mistress in her premature old age, her sickness, and her sorrow. a

small maid only be keep in addition to Reuben; and the man have

degenerate (as he might have call it but for his loyalty) to little

well than a man-of-all-work. he stand behind the lady now at a

respectful distance, having stop when they stop.



the grievance allude to by Mr. Lee, ready to ripen into open revolt,

have nothing to do with the tenant-farmer. it be this. in a very

favourable position on the estate, as regard situation, stand a

cluster of small dwelling. they be for the most part very poor,

some of they little well than hut, but they command a lovely

view. they be inhabit by labourer employ on the land, and be

call the Mill Cottages: a mill, do away with now, having formerly

stand close by.



one fine day it have strike the new man, Pinnett--look about here

and there to discover some mean of add to the profit he mean to

make off the land--that if these cottage be take down and handsome

dwelling erect in their place, it would be a great improvement,

pecuniarily and artistically, for such house would let directly in

this picturesque locality. no soon thought of than resolve upon.

Miles Pinnett be not a man to linger over his plan, and he give

these small tenant notice to quit.



it be rebel against. some of the man have be in the cottage as

long as Farmer Lee have be in his farm, and to be order to leave

seem a terrible hardship. it no doubt increase the difficulty that

there be no other small dwelling on the estate the man could go

into: all other be already occupy: and, if they leave these, they

must go to a distance whence they would have a two or three mile'

walk to their day's work. and so, encourage perhaps by the feeling

pervade the neighbourhood, of sympathy with they and opposition to

Pinnett, the man, one and all, refuse to go out. the next step would

be ejectment; and it be look for day by day.



for all this, Oscar Dalrymple suffer in opinion. Pinnett could not

go to such length, oppress they as he be oppress, against the

will of the owner, Mr. Dalrymple, argue the community, rich and poor.

perhaps he could not. but how it really be, no one know, or what

power Mr. Dalrymple have put out of his own hand, and into Pinnett's,

when he lease he the demesne.



Farmer Lee's visit to Moat Grange be pay in the morning. in the

afternoon the Grange have another visitor--Lady Adela Netherleigh.



Adela have not linger long at her mother's in London. after a few

week' sojourn she come down to Netherleigh Rectory, invite by the

Rector and his wife, her sister Mary. they have go to London for a

day, have be strike with compassion at Adela's evident state of

mental suffering, and they ask she to return with they for a little

change.



"it be not change I want," she have answer, speak to Lady Mary.

"what I want be peace. perhaps I shall find it with you, Mary, at the

Rectory."



Lady Mary Cleveland hesitate. peace? the word pose she.



"Adela," she say, "we should be very glad to have you, and there be

plenty of room for you and Darvy. but, as to peace--I don't know about

that. the Rectory be full of child great and small, and I'm afraid

it be noisy and bustle from morning till night."



Adela smile faintly. the peace her heart crave for be not that

impart by the absence of noise. she might feel all the well for

have the bustle of child about she; it might draw she at moment

out of her own sorrow. but another thought strike she.



"My----" husband, she have be about to say, but change the word.

"Sir Francis be not stay at Court Netherleigh? be he?"



"no. it be say he mean to take up his abode there later; he be not

there yet."



"then I will come to you, Mary. and I will stay with you for month

and month if I like it--and you must allow I to contribute towards

your housekeeping as Sir Sandy and Harriet do."



Lady Mary wince a little at that, but she do not say no. with all

those child--she have two of her own now--and the Rector's moderate

income, they could not be rich.



so Adela and Darvy go down with they to Netherleigh. that be in

summer, now it be autumn: and, so far as could be see or judge, the

change have not as yet effect much for she. Adela seem just as

before; wan, weary, sick, and sorry.



and yet, there be a change in a certain degree. the bitter rebellion

at her fate have partly pass from her mind, and therefore its trace

have leave her face. the active repining in which her day have be

spend be give place to a sort of hopeless resignation. she strove

to accept her punishment, strove to bear it, to be patient and gentle

always, hardly ever cease day or night to beseech God to blot out

the past from the book of the Recording Angel. the sense of shame,

entail by her conduct of long year, have not lift itself in the

least degree; nay, it seem to grow of a deep scarlet as time go

on. sometimes she would think if she could trample upon herself and

annihilate all power of remembrance, she would do it gladly; but that

would not stamp it out of her ever-live soul. Adela have err;

wilfully, cruelly, persistently; and if ever retribution come home to

a woman, it surely have come to she.



on this same day, when the sky be blue and the afternoon sun lie on

the green field at Netherleigh, Lady Adela go out, and turn she

languid step towards Moat Grange. Selina have call to see she at the

Rectory several time; each time Adela have promise to pay return

visit, and have not yet do so. the direct road lay, as the reader

may perhaps remember, through the village and past Court Netherleigh.

lingeringly would her eye look on the house whenever this happen,

lingeringly they rest on it now. the home, in which she have spend so

many happy day with Aunt Margery, be close to she for ever. of all

people in the live world, she be the only one debarred from

enter it. very rarely indeed be Sir Francis at Netherleigh. it have

be suppose that he mean to take up his abode in it for the autumn

month; but this appear to be a mistake; when he do come it be but

for a fly visit of a few hour. Mr. Cleveland privately tell his

wife that he believe Sir Francis stay away from the place because

Adela be in it.



Selina be in the large of the two drawing-room when Adela reach

the Grange. Selina rarely use it now, her husband never, but she have

go into it this afternoon. open the shutter and the window, she

sit there make herself a lace collar. the time have go by when she

could order these article of a Madame Damereau, and pay a fabulous

price for they.



Adela untie her bonnet string and take off her glove as she sit

down opposite Selina. not strong now, the walk have greatly tire she.

Selina could but notice how fragile and delicate she look, as the

light from the window fall upon her face. the once rounded cheek be

waste, their bright colour have fade to the faint tinge of pink;

from the once lustrous eye shine only sadness.



"let I get you something, Adela," cry Selina, impulsively. "a cup

of tea--I will make it for you directly. of wine--well, I be not sure,

really, that we possess any. I can ask Oscar."



"not anything, not anything," return Adela, "I could not take it.

thank you all the same. as to my look--I look as I always do."



"ah I," sigh Selina, "it be a weary life. a weary life, Adela, for

you and for I."



"if that be all--its weariness--it might be well bear," murmur

Adela. "and yet I do try to bear," she add, push she pretty brown

hair from her aching brow, and for once induce to speak of her

trouble to this friend, who have suffer too--though not as she have.

"but there be the remorse as well, you see. oh, how wrong, how

foolish, how _wicked_ we were!--at least _i_ be. do you ever think of

our past folly, Selina?--of the ease and happiness we then hold in our

hand, and fling away?"



"we have pay for it," say Selina. "yes, I do sometimes think of the

past, Adela; and then I wonder at the folly of woman. see to what

folly have reduce me!--to drag out a dead-alive existence in a

semi-prison, for the Grange be no well now, with never a friend to

stay with I, or a shilling to spend. and all for the sake of a few

fine bonnet and gown! would you believe it," she add, laugh,

"that the costly thing have not half come to an end yet?"



"just for _that?_" dissent Adela, in her pain, and lose sight of

Selina's trouble in her own. "if it have be for nothing more than

that!"



"well, well, we have pay for it, I say. bitterly and cruelly."



"_i_ have. you have not."



"no?" somewhat indifferently return Selina, her attention partly

give to her lace again, for she be never serious long together. "how

do you make that out?"



"you have your husband still. poverty with he, with one we love, must

carry little sting with it. but for I--my whole life be one of

never-end loneliness, without a future, without hope. do you know

what fanciful thought come to I the other night?" she go on, after

a pause. "I have all sort of fanciful idea when I sit alone in the

twilight. I think that life might be so much happy if God give we

a chance once of begin it all over again from the first. just

once, when we find out what dreadful mistake we have be make."



"and we should make the same again, though we begin it fifty time

over, Adela. unless we could carry back with we our dearly-buy

experience."



Adela sigh. "yes, I suppose so. God would have so order it have it

be well for we. he know well. but there be some woman who seem

never to make mistake, who go on their way smoothly and happily."



"place themselves under God's guidance, I imagine," return Selina.

"that's what my mother say to I, when she lecture I on the past."



Adela's eye fill with tear. "yes, yes," she murmur, meekly,

recall that it be what she have be strive to do for some little

time now--to hold on her way, under submission to God.



the conversation turn into other channel, and by-and-by, when Adela

be rest, she rise to leave. Selina accompany she into the hall.



"willn't you just say 'how d'you do' to my husband?" she cry, open

the door of their common sitting-room. "he be here."



Adela make no objection, and follow Selina. Oscar be stand in

the bay window, face the door. and some one else, tower nearly a

head above he, be stand at his side.



Sir Francis Netherleigh.



they stand, the husband and wife, face to face. with a faint cry,

Adela put up her hand, as if to ward off the sight--as if to bespeak

pardon in all humility for herself, for her intrusion--and disappear

again, white than death. it be rather an awkward moment for they

all. Selina disappear after she, and shut the door.



"be Lady Adela ill?" ask Sir Francis of Oscar, the question break

from he involuntarily in the moment's impulse--for she do, indeed,

look fearfully so.



"ay," reply Oscar, "ill with remembrance. repentance have make she

sick unto death. remorse have tell upon she."



but Sir Francis say no more.



Adela have depart across the field with the good speed she could

command. about half-way home she come upon Mr. Cleveland, seat on a

stile and whistle softly.



"those two young rascal of mine"--alluding to two of his little

sons--"seduced I from my study to help fly their kite," he begin to

Adela. "here I follow they, to the appoint field, and find they

nowhere, little light-head monkey! but, my dear, what's the matter

with you?" he add, with fatherly kindness, as he remark her pale,

troubled face. "you look alarmed."



"I have just see my husband," she pant, her breath painfully short.

all the old pain that she have be strive to subdue have come back

again; the sight of he, whom she now passionately love, have stir

distressing emotion within she.



"well?" say Mr. Cleveland.



"do you know he be at Netherleigh?"



"he come down today."



"he be in the bay-parlour with Oscar, and I go into it. it have

agitate I."



"but why should it agitate you?" rejoin the old Rector, who be very

matter-of-fact. "it seem to I that you ought to accustom yourself to

bear these chance meeting with equanimity, child. you can scarcely

expect to go through life without see he now and then."



Adela bend her head to the stile and break into sob. Mr. Cleveland

lay his protect hand upon her shoulder.



"my dear! my dear! strive to be calm. surely a momentary sight of he

ought not to put you into this state. be it that you still dislike he

so much?"



"dislike he!" she exclaim, the contrast between the word and the

truth strike she painfully, and cause she to say more than she

would have say. "I be die for his forgiveness; die to show he

how true be my remorse; die because I lose he."



the Rector do not quite see what answer to make to this. he hold his

tongue, and Adela resume.



"I wish I be a roman Catholic!"



the good man, evangelical protestant, feel as if his gray hair be

stand on end with surprise. "oh, hush!" say he. "you don't know

what you be say."



"I do wish it," she sob. "I could then go into a convent, and find

peace."



"peace!" echo Mr. Cleveland. "no, child, don't let your imagination

run away with that idea. it be a false one. no woman, enter a

convent in the frame of mind you seem to be entertaining, could expect

peace, or find it."



"any way, I should feel more at rest: I should _have_ to bear life

then, you know. and, oh, I be try to do so: I be indeed try!"



thoroughly put out, the Rector make no comment. perhaps would not

trust himself to make any.



"I suppose there be no such thing as protestant convent, or

sisterhood," she go on, "that receive poor creature who have no

long any place in this world?"



"not to my knowledge," sharply speak Mr. Cleveland, as he jump off

the stile. "it be time we go home, Adela."



they walk away side by side. gain the Rectory--a large,

straggling, red-brick building, its old wall cover with

time-honour ivy--Adela ascend to her chamber, and shut herself in

with her grief.



how scornfully her husband must despise her!--despise she for her past

shame and sin; despise she in her present contemptible humiliation,

she reflect, a low moan escape she--he so pure and upright in all

his way, so good and generous and noble! oh that she could hide to

the end from he and from the world!



lift her tremble hand, her despairing face, Adela breathe a

faint petition that the most High would be pleased to vouchsafe to she

somewhat of his heavenly comfort, or take she out of the tribulation

that she could so hardly battle with.









CHAPTER XXXVII.

an ALARM.





it be a few day later. Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple, who have be spend

the afternoon with her mother and Mary Lynn, be prepare to return

to the Grange. Alice have just come home again, a brilliant hectic on

her cheek, but weak, as it seem to they all. Alice be happy

than she have be for year, in her sweet unselfishness. the trouble

which have divide Colonel Hope and his nephew be at an end; Gerard

have be reinstate in his uncle's favour, and be to marry Frances
