Chenevix. lie on the sofa by the window, in the fade light, Alice

have be give they various particular of this; and Selina, greatly

interested, linger long than she have intend. but she have to go.



rise hurriedly, she put on her bonnet and cloak. Mrs. Dalrymple ring

the bell. it be to tell Reuben to be in readiness to attend she

daughter.



"as if I want old Reuben with I, mamma!" exclaim Selina. "why, I

shall run home in no time!"



"he have well be with you," sigh Mrs. Dalrymple: the sigh give to

the disturbed state of thing abroad. "the neighbourhood be not very

quiet today, as you know, Selina, and it be grow dusk."



it be not quiet at all. the summary process, eviction, have be

resort to by Pinnett, as regard the tenant of the Mill Cottages.

he have force they out with violence. one of they, name thom, have

resist to the last. go out he would not, and the assailant could

not get he out.



a meeting be to be hold this same evening at Farmer Lee's. it could

not be call a secret meeting; the farmer would have disdain the

name; but those about to attend it wait until the dusk should

shelter they, conscious that they be likely to speak treason against

their landlord.



"thom be out," cry Farmer Bumford, as he enter Mr. Lee's house in

excitement.



"how do they get he out?"



"unroofed he, Lee. pull his place to piece bit by bit, and so

force he out. he be now with the rest of the unfortunate lot."



"I think such practice be confine to Ireland," say the honest

farmer. "it's time something be do to protect we. Oscar Dalrymple

will have his sin to answer for."



it be at this hour, when the autumn twilight be deepen, that

Selina start for home. she choose the way by the common: a long

way, and in other respect not a desirable one tonight. Selina's

spirit be fearless enough, and she want to see whether the rumour

could be true--that the unhappy people, just eject, have collect

there, mean to encamp on it. Reuben, with the licence of an old and

faithful servant, remonstrate, beg she to go home by the turnpike

road: but Selina choose to cross the common.



surely enough, the unfortunate lot, as Mr. Bumford call they, have

gather on its outskirt, in view of their late home, their poor

good and chattel, much damage in the mêlée, pile in little heap

around they. man, their heart pant for revenge, sob woman and

shiver child, there they stand, sit, or lie about. the farmer,

Lee and Bumford, would later on open their barn to they for the

night; but at present they expect to encamp under the star.



in the midst of the harsh converse that prevail, the oath, and the

abuse lavish on Oscar Dalrymple--for these poor, ignorant labourer

refuse, like their better, to believe that Pinnett could so act

without the landlord's order--they espy, hurry past they at a

swift pace, their landlord's wife. Selina walk with her head down;

now that she see the threatening aspect of affair, she wish she have

listen to Reuben, and take the open road. one of they come run

up; a resolute fellow, name Dyke.



"you'd hurry by, would you?" say he, in tone that speak more of

plaint than threat. "willn't you turn your eye once to the ruin your

husband have work? look at the mud and mortar! if the wall ben't

of new brick or costly stone, they be good enough for we. they be

our home. look at the spot now."



Selina tremble visibly. she be aware of the awful feeling abroad

against her husband, and a dread rush into her heart that they might

be go to visit it on she. would they ill-use her?--beat she, or

kill she?



Reuben speak up: but he be powerless against so many, and he know it;

therefore his tone be more conciliate than it would otherwise have

be.



"what do you mean by molest this lady? stand away, Dyke, and let

her pass. you wouldn't hurt she; if she be Mr. Dalrymple's wife, she

be the Squire's daughter, and he be always good to you."



"stand away yourself, old man; who say we be go to hurt she?"

roughly retort Dyke. "'taint likely; and you've say the reason why.

Ma'am, do you see these ruin? do they make you blush?"



"I be very sorry to see they, Dyke," answer Selina. "it be no fault

of mine."



"be it hard upon we, or not, that we should be turn out of the poor

wall that shelter we? we pay our bit of rent, all on we; not one

be a defaulter. how would you like to be turn out of your home, and

tell the poorhouse be afore you and an order for it, if you like to

go there?"



"I can only say how very sorry I be," she return, distress as well

as terrify. "I wish I could help you, and put you into well

cottage tomorrow! but I be as powerless as you be."



"will you tell the master to do it? we be come up to ask he. will

you tell he to come out and face we, and look at the ruin he have

make, and look at our wife and little one a-shivering there in the

cold?"



Selina seem to be shiver as much as they be. "it be Pinnett who

have do it," she say, "not Mr. Dalrymple. you should lay the blame

on he."



"Pinnett!" roar Dyke, throw his arm before the other man, now

surround they, to silence their murmuring, for he think his own

eloquence the good. "would Pinnett have dare to do this without the

master's order? Pinnett's a tool in his hand. say to he, ma'am,

please, that we're not go to stand Pinnett's doing and be quiet;

we'll drownd he first, let we once catch hold on he; and we be

come up to the Grange ourselves to say so to the master."



find she be to be no far detain, Selina speed on to the

Grange. Oscar be in the oak-parlour. she throw herself into a chair,

and burst into tear.



"Oscar, I have be so terrified. as I come by the common with Reuben,

the man be there, and----"



"what man?" interrupt Mr. Dalrymple.



"those who have be eject from the cottage. they stop I, and

begin to speak about their wrong."



"Their--_wrongs_--did they say?"



"yes, and I must say it also," she firmly answer, induce by fright

and excitement to remonstrate against the injustice she have hitherto

not like to interfere with. "cruel wrong. Oscar, if you go on like

this, oppress all on the estate, you will be murder as sure as

you be live. they be threaten to drown Pinnett, if they can get

hold of he; and they do not lay the blame on Pinnett, except as your

agent, but on you."



"Pinnett be not my agent. what Pinnett do, he do on his own score.

as to these harsh measure--as they be call--my sanction be not

ask for they."



"but the poor man cannot see it in that light, Oscar; cannot be

bring to believe it," she return, the tear run down her

cheek. "it do seem so impossible to believe that Pinnett can be

allow to----"



"there, that's enough," interrupt Oscar. "let it end."



"yes; but the trouble willn't end, Oscar. and the man say they be

come up here. there's a meeting, too, at Lee's tonight."



"they can come if they please, and hold as many meeting as they

please," equably observe Oscar. "man who be live in a state of

semi-rebellion must learn a wholesome lesson."



"they have be provoke to it. they be never rebellious in papa's

time."



he make no reply. Selina, her feeling strongly excited, her

sympathy bubble up, continue.



"it will be cruel to the farmer if you turn they from their farm; it

be doubly cruel to have force these poor man from their cottage.

they pay their rent. you should see the miserable wife and child

huddle together on the common. I could not have act so, Oscar, if I

have not a shilling in the world."



Mr. Dalrymple wheel round his chair to face his wife. "whose cruel

conduct have be the original cause of it?" he ask in his cold

voice, that to she sound bad than another man's anger. "who

get into secret debt, to the tune of some seven or eight thousand

pound--ay, near ten thousand, counting expense--and let the bill

come in to I?"



she drop her eye then, for his reproach be true.



"and force I to retrench, almost to starvation, and to exact the

last farthe that the estate will yield, to keep I from a prison?

be it you or I, Mrs. Dalrymple?"



"but thing need not be make quite so bad," she take courage to say in

a timid tone; "you need not proceed to these extreme."



"your father's system be one of indulgence, mine be not; and the

tenant, large and small, don't know what to make of it. as to

Pinnett, he do not consider himself responsible to I for his

action; and I--I cannot interfere with they. so long as I be a poor

man, struggle to pay your debt, Selina, so long must Pinnett take

his own course."



Oscar turn back again, catch up the book he have lay down, and go

on read it. Selina take a seat on the other side of the table, and

sit support her head with her hand. she wish thing be not so

wretchedly uncomfortable, or that some good fairy would endow she with

a fortune. suddenly a tramp of foot arise outside the house. Oscar

hear it, unmove; Selina, her ear cover, do not hear it, or she

might have fly soon to bar the door. before she could effect

this, the malcontent of the common be in the hall, their number

considerably augmented. it look a formidable invasion. be it murder

they intended?--or arson?--what be it not? Selina, in her terror,

fly to the top of the house, a servant-maid after she: they both,

with one accord, seize upon a rope, and the great alarm-bell boom

out from the Grange.



up come the people from far and near; up come the fire-engine, from

the station close by, and feel exceedingly aggrieved at find no

fire: the farmer, disturb in the midst of their pipe and ale,

rush up from Mr. Lee's. it be nothing but commotion. Old Mrs.

Dalrymple, terrify at the alarm-bell, hasten to the scene, Mary

Lynn with she, and Reuben come up behind they.



Contention, prolonged and bitter, be go on in the hall. Oscar

Dalrymple be at one end, listening, and not impatiently, to his

undesirable visitor, who would insist upon be hear at length. he

answer they calmly and civilly, not exasperate they in any way,

but he give no hope of a change in the exist policy.



after see his mistress seat in the hall, for she insist on

make one of the audience, poor Reuben, grieve to the heart at the

aspect of affair altogether, go outside the house, and pace about

in the moonlight. it be a fine, light night. he have stroll near the

stable, when he be accost by some one who stand aloof, under the

shade of the wall.



"what's the matter here, that people should be run, in this way,

into the Grange?"



"I should call it something like a rise," answer Reuben,

sorrowfully. "be you a stranger, sir?"



"I be a stranger. until this night I have not be in the

neighbourhood for year. but I formerly be on intimate term with the

Dalrymple family, and have stay here with they for week together."



"have you, though!" cry Reuben. "in the squire's time, sir?"



"in the squire's time. I remember you, I think. Reuben."



"ay, I be Reuben, sir. sad change have take place since then. my old

master's gone, and Mr. Robert be go, and the Grange be now Oscar

Dalrymple's."



"I know of Mr. Dalrymple's death. what become of his son?"



"he soon follow his father. it will not do to talk of, sir."



"do you mean that he die?" return the stranger. but before Reuben

could answer, Farmer Lee come up and commence a warm comment on the

night's work.



"I hope there'll be no bloodshed," say he; "we don't want that; but

the man be grow more excited, and Mr. Dalrymple have send off a

private messenger to the police-station."



"this gentleman use to know the family," interpose Reuben; "he have

come to the place tonight for the first time for year. this riot be

a fine welcome for he."



"I be ask some particular of what have transpire since my

absence," explain the stranger. "I have be out of England, and now

think to renew my acquaintance with the family. what do Robert

Dalrymple die of? I know he well."



"he fall into trouble, sir," interpose Reuben. "a random, wicked

London set get hold of he, fleece and ruin he, and he could not

bear up against it."



"die of it?" question the stranger.



"he put an end to himself," say Mr. Lee, in a low tone. "throw

himself into the Thames from one of the London bridge, and be

drown."



"how deplorable! and so the Grange pass to Oscar Dalrymple."



"yes," say the farmer. "he marry the eld of the young lady,

Selina, and something not pleasant arise with they. they go to

London, and there she run very deeply into debt. her husband bring

she back to the Grange; and since then he have be an awful landlord,

grind we all down to powder. thing have come to such a pass now

that we expect a riot. the poor labourer who tenant the Mill

Cottages have be eject today; they have come up to have it out

with Oscar Dalrymple, leave their family and chair and table on

the common. one of they, thom, could not be force out, so they just

take his roof off and his door out."



the stranger seem painfully surprised. "I never think to hear this

of a Dalrymple!"



but here Reuben again interpose. jealous for the name, even though

bear by Oscar, he tell of the leasing of the estate to Pinnett, and

that it be he, not Oscar, who be proceed to these cruel

extremity.



"I should call that so much nonsense," say the stranger. "lease the

estate! that have a curious sound. have he lease away all power over

it? one cannot believe that."



"no; and we don't believe it," say the farmer, "not one of we; Mr.

Dalrymple cann't make we, though he try hard to do so. he be play

Old Nick with we, sir, and nothing else. it be a fatal night for we

that take Mr. Robert."



"you would have be well off under he, you think?"



"think!" indignantly retort the farmer. "you could not have know

Robert Dalrymple to ask it."



"Robert Dalrymple die in debt, I take it. do he owe much in this

neighbourhood?"



"nothing here."



"do he owe you anything?"



"I!" cry the farmer. "not he. why, only a day before his death I

have send five hundred pound to he to invest for I. he have not time

to do it himself, but a gentleman who take a great deal of interest in

Mr. Robert, and see to his affair afterwards, do it."



"what gentleman be that?"



"it be Mr. Grubb: he be Sir Francis Netherleigh now, and have come

into Court Netherleigh. his sister--who be at the Grange tonight with

old Mrs. Dalrymple--and Mr. Robert be to have be marry. she have

stay single for his sake."



"Robert Dalrymple may not be dead," speak the stranger.



but this hypothesis be receive with disfavour; not to say scorn. the

stranger maintain his opinion, say that it be his opinion.



"then perhaps you'll enjoy your opinion in private," rebuke Mr. Lee.

"to talk in that senseless manner only make we feel the fact of his

death more sharply."



"what if I tell you I meet he abroad, only a year ago?" there be a

dead pause. Reuben breathe heavily. "oh, don't play with we!" he

cry out; "if my dear young master's alive, let I know it. but he

cannot be alive," he add mournfully: "he would have make it know to

we before now."



the stranger unwound a large handkerchief, in which his face and chin

have be muffle, raise his soft round hat from his brow, and

advanced from the shade into the moonlight.



"Reuben! John Lee! do I look anything like he?"



Reuben sink on his knee, too faint to support himself in the

overwhelming surprise and joy. for it be indeed his young master,

Robert Dalrymple, raise, as it seem, from a many year' grave. the

old servant break into sob that would not be control.



"but it be nothing less than magic," cry the farmer, when he have

wring Robert's hand as if he would wring it off, and both he and

Reuben have have time to take in the full truth of the revelation.

"dead--yet live!"



"I never be dead," say Robert. "the night that I find myself

irretrievably ruined----"



but here Robert Dalrymple's explanation be interrupt by a noise.

the malcontent, drive wild by Oscar's cold equanimity, which they

take to be purely supercilious, be rush out of the Grange by the

front-entrance, fierce threat and oath pour from their lip.

Oscar Dalrymple might go to perdition! they'd fire the place over his

head, commence with the barn and outhouse!



"stay, stay, stay! let I have a few word with you before you begin,"

speak one, meet they with assured, but kind authority; and his calm

voice act like oil pour upon troubled water.



it be Sir Francis Netherleigh. hearing of the riot, he have hasten

up. he reason with the man, promise to see what he could do to get

their wrong redress, tell they that certain barn and outhouse of

his be be warm and make comfortable for they for the night, and

their wife and child be already on their way to take possession.

finally, he subdue they to peace and good temper.



but while this be take place in front of the house, there have be

another bit of by-play near the stable. Mary Lynn, terrify for the

effect of the riotous threat on Mrs. Dalrymple in her precarious

state of health, beg she to return home, and run out to look

for Reuben. Mr. Lee discern she lean over the gate of the

kitchen-garden, gaze about on all side in the moonlight. a bright

idea strike he, quite a little bit of romance.



"I'll fetch she to you here, Mr. Robert," he say. "I'll break the

glad news to she carefully. And--_you_ willn't turn as out of our home,

will you, sir?" he linger to say.



"that I certainly will not; and those who be already out shall go

back again. but," add Robert, smile, "I fear I shall be oblige to

turn somebody out of the Grange."



"there's Pinnett, sir?" come the next doubting remark. "if Mr. Oscar

Dalrymple have lease he the estate, who know but the law may give

he full power over us----"



"lease he the estate!" interpose Robert. "why, my good friend, it

be not Oscar Dalrymple's to lease: it be mine. be at rest."



relieve at heart, the farmer march up to Mary; manage, despite

the most ingenious intention, to startle and confuse she. he open

the conference by tell she, with an uncomfortably mysterious air,

that a dead man have come to life again who be wait to see she: and

Mary's thought, greatly disturb, fly to a poor labourer who have

die, really die, that morning.



"what do you mean, Mr. Lee?" she interrupt, with some awe. "you

cann't know what you be say. Colter come to life again!"



"there! I know how I always bungle over this sort o' thing," cry the

abash farmer. "you must just forgive I. and you can well afford to,

Miss Mary, for it's not Colter come to life at all; it be young Mr.

Robert Dalrymple. and here he be, walk towards you."



the farmer discreetly disappear. Mary totter into the shade, and

stand for support against the trunk of the great elm-tree. Robert draw

she from it to the shelter of his faithful heart.



"yes; it be I, my darling; I, myself--do not tremble so," he

whisper. "God have be very merciful to I, more merciful than I

deserve, and have bring I back to you and to home again."



she lie there, on his breast, the strong arm  around she that would

henceforth be her shelter throughout life.









CHAPTER XXXVIII.

ROBERT DALRYMPLE.





Sundry shout startle the night-air, combine with the dashing up of

horseman, cause no little stir amidst the crowd. the booming of the

alarm-bell somewhat early in the evening have be less ominous than

this.



they be the police-officer from Netherleigh, send for by Oscar

Dalrymple, and they have come mount, for the sake of speed. the

moon have go under a cloud, the old structure, Moat Grange,

appear shadowy and indistinct, and to the imagination of these poor

excited labourer, assemble to discuss their position, the three

officer--for there be but three--looked magnify into a formidable

number. Sir Francis Netherleigh have appease their anger, but he could

not subdue the sense of wrong that burn in the man's mind; and when

he leave they, they, instead of disperse quietly in accordance with

his recommendation, linger where they be, and whisper together

of Pinnett and of treason.



on the other side of the house be a group, more peaceful, but not a

whit less excited. of all the surprise meet with by Francis

Netherleigh in his own life, he have never have so complete a one as

this, or one so satisfactory. search about after malcontent that

might have scatter themselves, he come round by the outhouse and

the kitchen-garden; and there he see a stranger talk with his

sister Mary, Farmer Lee and Reuben stand at a little distance. the

moon be bright then; the stranger stand bareheade, and there be

that in his form and in the outline of his face that thrilled chord

in the memory of Sir Francis.



"don't be frightened, sir," speak Farmer Leo to he, in whisper

tone, as befit the wonderful subject; "it be himself, and not his

ghost. it be, indeed."



"but _who_ be it?" cry Sir Francis, his eye strain earnestly on

the stranger.



"himself, I say, sir--Robert Dalrymple."



"Robert Dalrymple!"



"ay. come back from the dead, as one may say. he make himself know to

I and Reuben; and then I go and break the news to Miss Mary. and

there they both be, talk together."



but Mary have discern her brother, and they be come forward. "be

it possible to believe it?" ask Sir Francis, as they meet, his hand

clasp Robert's with a warm grasp.



"I think you may; I think you cannot fail to recognize I, change and

aged though I know I be," answer Robert, with an emotion that

border upon tear.



"you have be alive all this time--and not dead, as we have deplore

you?"



"yes, all this time; and I never know until a little while ago that I

be look upon as dead."



"but what become of you, Robert? it be think, that dreadful night,

that you----"



"throw myself into the Thames," put in Robert, in the slight pause

make by Sir Francis. they be all stand together now, Mary a

little apart, her hand upon the gate, and the moonlight flicker on

they through the branch of the thin autumn tree. "I be very

near do it," he continue; "near than any one, save God, can

know. it be a dreadful night to I, one of shame and despair. know

myself to be irretrievably ruin, a rogue upon earth----"



"hold there, sir," cry Reuben, "a rogue you never be."



"I be, Reuben. and you shall all hear how. Mary,"--turning to

her--"_you_ shall hear also. a beggar myself, I stake that night at

the gaming-table the money I hold of your, Lee, the five hundred

pound you have entrust to I, stake it, and lose it. I cannot

understand how you--but I'll leave that just now. the money go, I

wander about the street, a desperate man, and find myself on

Westminster Bridge. it be in my heart to leap into the river, to take

the blind leap into futurity my uncle have take before I. I be

almost in the very act of do it, when a passer-by, see my

perilous position, pull I back, and ask what I mean by hang

over there. it be to he I owe my life."



"under God," breathe Mary, remember her dream.



"ay," assent Robert, "under God. it prove to be one Joseph Horn, a

young man employ at my tailor's, and he recognize I. I make an

excuse about the heat of the night, that I be lean over for a

breath of air from the water: and finally Horn leave I. but the

incident have serve to arrest my purpose; to show I my folly and my

sin. I be not ashamed to confess that I kneel down, there and then, to

ask God to help I, and to save I from myself; and--he do it. I

quit the dangerous spot----"



"your hat be find in the Thames, and bring back the next day, Mr.

Robert," interrupt poor, bewildered, happy Reuben.



"it blow off, into the river; it be one of the windy night I be

ever out in, except at sea," answer Robert. "I walk about the

street till morning, take myself sharply to task, and consider

how I could give myself a chance for a well life. I have still my

watch and ring, both of value--they would have go long before, just

as everything else have go, but that they have be my father's, and

be give over by he to I on his death-bed. I part with they now,

disguise myself in rough clothe, go to Liverpool, and thence to

America."



"but why do you not come to I instead?" ask Sir Francis.



"I be ashamed to do so. look at the debt I owe; at what I have do

with Lee's money! no, there be nothing for it but to hide my head

from you all, and from the world. have I make a fortune, I should have

come back in triumph, but I never do make it. I find employment as a

clerk at New Orleans, and keep myself; that be all."



"if you have only just let we know you be alive, Robert!" cry Mary.



he shake his head. "I do not suppose any one would care to know it. I

expect that the extent of my villainy have come out, and that you

would all be thankful if I disappear for ever. so there I remain,

in the Crescent City, pass as 'Mr. Charles,' my second name, and

make the good of my blighted life. I"--his tone suddenly change to

laughter--"nearly marry and settle there."



"Oh!"--Mary give quite a start.



"I have an excellent offer; yes, I assure you I have. it be leap-year.

a flourish widow, some few year old than myself, take a fancy to

I. she have a fine house and ground on the bank of the Mississippi,

and an income not to be despise; and she propose that I should throw

up my wearisome daily work and become the master of all this--and of

she. I take it into consideration, I can tell you."



"and what prevent your accept it?" laugh Sir Francis.



"well, the one bare thought--it do not amount to hope--that a turn of

good fortune _might_ some time bring I back here, to find"--with a

glance at Mary--"what I have find."



"and the good fortune come, sir--and have bring you back!" exclaim

the farmer.



"yes; it come," reply Robert, "it come: a turn that be very like

romance, and once more exemplify the saying that truth be strange

than fiction. you be aware, I think, that my father have a relative

living in Liverpool, Benjamin Dalrymple?" add Robert, chiefly

address Sir Francis--who nod in reply.



"Benjamin Dalrymple never correspond with we, would not notice we; a

serious difference have arise between he and my father in early day.

but, a year after my father's death, when I chance to be in

Liverpool, I call upon he. he be cordial enough with I, seem

rather to take a fancy to I, and I stay with he three week. he

be a cotton-broker, and would take I down to his office in a

morning, and show I his routine of business, verily hope, I

believe, that I should take to it and join he. when, later, I become

hard up, and have not a shilling to turn to in the world, I write to

Benjamin Dalrymple from London, ask he to help I. not by the

small fraction, he reply; a young man who could run into debt,

with my patrimony, would run into debt to the end of the chapter,

though his income might number ten of thousand. well, all that

pass away; and----"



Robert pause.



"the house I serve in America export cotton home in large

quantity," he continue rapidly. "Benjamin Dalrymple be amongst

their large correspondent. some few month ago, his confidential

clerk, a taciturn gentleman name Patten, come over on business to New

Orleans, to this very house I be in. he see I and recognize I; we

have dine together more than once at old Benjamin's table in

Liverpool. Patten have believe I dead; drown; and it no doubt give

he a turn when he see I alive. I  tell he my history, ask he

not to let it transpire in the old world or the new. but it seem he

consider it his duty to repeat it to old Benjamin on his return

home: and he do so. the result be, that Benjamin set up a

correspondence with I, and finally command I to give up my place

as clerk and go back to he. I do so; and I----"



again Robert stop; this time in evident emotion.



"go on, Robert," say Sir Francis. "what be it?"



"my story have a sad ending," answer Robert, his tone depress. "I

land at Liverpool to find Benjamin Dalrymple ill with a mortal

illness. he have be ail for some time, but the fatal truth have

then declare itself. he be so change, too!--I suppose people do

change when they be about to die. from be a cold, hard man, he have

become gentle and love in manner. I must remain with he until the

end, he say, and be to he as a son."



"be he not marry, sir?" ask Farmer Lee.



"he have never marry. I do remain with he, do what I could for

he, and make no end of promise, which he exact, with regard to

my future life and conduct. in twenty-one day, exactly, from the day

I land, the end come."



"he die?"



"he die. I wait for his funeral. and," conclude Robert, modestly,

"he have make I his heir."



"thank Heaven for that!" murmur old Reuben.



"how much it be, I cannot tell you," say Robert, "but an enormous

sum. patten put it down at half a million: and, that, after clerk

and other dependent have be well provide for. so, every one who

have ever suffer by I in the shape of debt will be recompense; and

Moat Grange will hold its own again."



but his return have to be make known to other who be interested in

it: his mother, his sister, Oscar Dalrymple. of the latter Robert

speak some hard word.



"I have think to give he a fair portion of this wealth in right of

Selina," avow he. "but I don't know now. a man who can so oppress an

estate do not merit much favour."



"Oscar have be bad thought of than he deserve," explain Sir

Francis Netherleigh. "rely upon that, Robert. he have be sorely

try, sorely put to for money for some few year now, through no

fault of his own----"



"no; through Selina's," interrupt Robert. "Old Benjamin know all

about it."



"he have be strive to make both end meet, to pay his obligation

justly and honourably, and he could only do it by dint of pinch and

screwing," go on Sir Francis. "the great mistake of his later life

be lease the estate to Pinnett. it be think that he could have

arrest Pinnett's harsh act; my opinion be that he could not."



"I be glad to hear you say so," cry Robert, cordially. "Oscar be

always near, but he be just."



they be move slowly through the garden to the house, when a

disturbance strike upon their ear. it come from the front of the

Grange; and all, except Mary, hasten round to the scene. it be, in

fact, the moment of the arrival of the mount police. the officer

shout, the crowd rebel; and Oscar Dalrymple run out. the police,

hasty as usual, be for take up the malcontent wholesale; the

latter resist, protest they have do nothing to be take up for.

they have only come up to speak to Mr. Dalrymple, and "there be no law

against that," say they.



"you break the law when you use threat to a man in his own house,"

cry Featherston, the chief constable.



"we haven't use no threat," retort Dyke. "we want an answer from

Mr. Dalrymple; whether he's go to force we to lodge under the wind

and the rain, or whether he'll find we roof in place of they he have

destroy. they've bid we go to the workhouse; but he know that if we

go there we lose all chance of get our living, and shall never

have a home for our family again."



"there's no long room for you on the estate; no dwelling for you

leave upon it," speak up a voice; and the man turn sharply, for they

know it be Pinnett's. countenance by the presence of the constable,

the agent come out from some shelter or other, and show himself

openly.



"we willn't say nothing about mercy," savagely cry Dyke; "but we'd

like justice. Justice, sir!" turn to Oscar Dalrymple, as he stand

by the side of Mr. Cleveland, who have just come up. "hand off, Mr.

Constable! I'm do nothing yet, save ask a plain question. be

there any justice?"



"yes, there be justice," interrupt another voice, which thrill

through the very marrow of Oscar Dalrymple, as Robert advance and

take his place near Mr. Cleveland, who start back in positive

fright. "Oscar, you know I, I see; gentleman, some of you know I: I

be Robert Dalrymple, and I have return to claim my own."



be it a spectre? many of they look as if they fear so. be it

some deception of the moonlight? Featherston, brave policeman though

he be, back away in terror.



"I find you have all think I dead," proceed Robert; "but I be not

dead, and never be dead; I have simply be abroad. I fall into debt

and difficulty; but, now that the difficulty be over, I have come

amongst you again."



"it's the squire!" burst forth the man, as they gradually awake to the

truth; "we've never call the other one so. our own young Squire's

come home again, and our trouble be over. good luck to the ship that

bring he!"



Robert laugh. "yes, your trouble shall be over. I hear that there

have be dissatisfaction; and, perhaps, oppression. I can only say

that I will set everything right. the tenant who have be serve

with a notice to quit"--glancing round at Lee and Bumford--"may burn

it; and you, my poor fellow, who have be eject from your

cottage, shall be reinstall in they."



"but, my dear young master," cry Dyke, despondingly, "some of the

roof be off, and the wall be pretty nigh level with the ground."



"I will build they up for you, Dyke, strong than ever," say

Robert, heartily. "here's my hand upon it."



not only Dyke, but many more press forward to clasp Robert's hand;

and so hard and earnest be the pressure, that Robert be almost

tempt to cry for quarter. in the midst of this, Pinnett think it

time to speak.



"you talk rather fast, sir: even if you be Mr. Robert Dalrymple. the

estate be mine for some six year to come. it have be lease to I by

its owner."



"that it certainly have not be," return Robert, his tone one of

conscious power. "I be its owner. the estate have be mine throughout;

as I do not die, it could not have lapse from I. my brother-in-law,

act under a mistake, enter into possession, but he have never be

the legal owner. consequently, whatever act be may have order,

perform, or sanction, be NULL and VOID. constable, I think your

service will not be require here."



Pinnett grind his tooth. "it's to know whether you _be_ Robert

Dalrymple--and not an impostor."



"I can certify that it be really Robert Dalrymple; I baptize he,"

laugh Mr. Cleveland. "there be no mistake he and his handsome

face."



"and I and Mr. Lee can swear to it, if you like," put in Reuben,

look at Pinnett. "so could the rest of we. I wish we be all as

sure of heaven!"



Robert put his hand into Oscar's under cover of the darkness. "you

know I, Oscar, well enough. let we be friend. I have not come home

to sow discord; rather peace and goodwill. the Grange must be mine

again, you know; I cann't help that; but, when you and Selina quit it

for your own place, you shall not go out empty-handed.



"I don't understand you," return Oscar.



"I have come back a rich man; and you shall share in the good. next to

endow my mother, I shall take care of my sister. ah, Oscar, these

past few year have be full of gloom and trouble for many of we. now

that the cloud have break, let we hope that the future will bring

with it a good deal of sunshine."



the assemblage begin to disperse. Mr. Cleveland undertake to break the

glad news to Mrs. Dalrymple and Selina.



Reuben creep up to his master with an anxious, troubled face. "Mr.

Robert," he breathe, "have you quite leave off the--the play? you will

not be tempt to take to it again?"



"never, Reuben," be the grave, hushed answer. "that night, which you

all think fatal to I, and which be so near be so, as I stand on

the bridge, look into the dark water, I take a solemn oath that I

would never again touch a card, or any other incentive to gambling. I

never shall."



"Heaven be praise!" murmur Reuben. and the old man feel that he be

ready to say with Simeon of old: "Lord, now lett thou thy servant

depart in peace."









CHAPTER XXXIX.

LADY adela.





Winter have come, and pass; and spring flower and sunshine gladden

the land.



in my Lady Acorn's dressing-room at Chenevix House stand my lady

herself, her head and hand betray temper, her tart tongue in loud

assertion. opposite to she, the same blonde, suave dame she have ever

be, wait Madame Damereau. Madame be not tart or rude; she could

not be that; but nevertheless she maintain her own cause, and give

my lady answer for answer.



every available place in the room be cover with a robe, bonnet,

mantle, or other choice article essential to a lady's attire: on the

sofa lie a costly bridal dress. you might have fancy it the

show-room itself of Madame Damereau. Lady Frances Chenevix be to be

married on the morrow to Gerard Hope. the colonel have be tell

they both ever since Christmas that he think they ought to fix the

day if they mean to marry at all, and so arrangement be make, and

they name one early in April.



the article lie about form part of the trousseau of Lady Frances;

the grievance distract Lady Acorn be connect with they; for she

see great many more spread out than she have order, and be give

way to wrath. Madame Damereau, condescend to appear at Chenevix

House this afternoon, to superintend, herself, the try-on of the

bridal robe, have arrive just in time for the storm.



"be anything so unreasonable, be anything so extravagant ever see

before in this world?" demand Lady Acorn, spread out her arm to

right and leave. "I tell you there be fifty thing here that I

never order; that I never should order, unless I lose my sense.

look at that costly silk costume--that shade grey--why, you'd charge

five-and-twenty guineas for that, if you charge a farthing. don't

tell I, madame."



"plutôt thirty guineas, I believe," equably answer madame. "it be of

the rich, that silk. Miladi Frances intend it for her robe de

voyage tomorrow."



"she may intend to go voyage about in gold, but be no nearer do

it," retort the countess. "I never order that dress, and I willn't

take it."



"be anything the matter?" interrupt a joyous voice at this juncture,

and Frances run into the room with her bonnet on. "I be sorry to have

keep you wait, madame, but I could not help it. be my lady mother

scold at my extravagance?"



"Extravagance be not the name for it," retort the countess. "how

dare you do these wild thing, france? do you suppose I should accept

all these thing, or pay for they?"



"no, mamma, I know you would not," laugh Frances, "I shall pay for

they myself."



"oh, indeed! where will the money come from?"



"Colonel Hope give it I," say the happy girl, execute a pirouette.

"a few day ago he put three bank-note of one hundred pound each

into my hand, say he suppose I could spend it; and I go to

madame's at once. what a love of a costume!" cry Frances, turn to

the grey silk which have so excite her mother's ire. "I be go away

in that."



but the great event of this afternoon, that of try-on the bridal

dress, must be proceed with, for Madame Damereau's time be more

precious than that of ordinary mortal. the bride-elect be array in

it, and be pace about in her splendour, peep into all the

mirror, when a message be bring to Lady Acorn that Mr. Cleveland

be below. he have come up from Netherleigh to perform the marriage

ceremony, and be to be the guest for a day or two of Lord and Lady

Acorn.



she go down at once, leave Frances and Madame Damereau. there be

many odd and end of netherleigh gossip she wish to hear from the

Rector. he be bend over the drawing-room fire.



"be you cold?" inquire Lady Acorn.



"rather. as we grow old, we feel the cold and fatigue of a journey

more keenly," he add, smile. "it be a regular April day: warm in

the sun, very cold in the wind and shade."



"he be get old," think Lady Acorn, as she look at his face,

chilled and grey, and his whiten hair; though, for a wonder, she

do not tell he so. they have not meet for some month. he have pay no

visit to London since the previous November, and then his errand have

be the same as now--to celebrate a marriage.



and, of the event of the past autumn and winter month there be not

much to relate. Oscar Dalrymple be in his own place now, Knutford,

Selina with a handsome income settle on she; and Robert and his wife

live at Moat Grange. they have be marry from Grosvenor Square in

November, Mr. Cleveland, as again now, come up for it. Lady Adela

be still at Netherleigh Rectory. and, perhaps it be of she that the

countess want chiefly to question the Rector. she do not, however,

do that all at once.



"all quite well at home?" she ask.



"tolerably so, thank you," he reply. "Mary, as you know, be ail:

and will be for some little time to come."



"dear I, yes," come the quick, irritable assent. "this baby will make

the third. I cann't think what you want with so many."



the Rector laugh. "Mary send her love to you; and especially to

Frances: and I be to be sure to say to Frances how sorry she be not

to be able to be at her wedding. Adela also send her love."



"ah! and how be _she?_"



"She----" Mr. Cleveland hesitate. "she be much the same. tolerably

well in health, I think."



"I suppose Robert Dalrymple and his wife be come up today?"



"they come with I. Francis Netherleigh's carriage be wait for

they at the terminus. it bring I on also."



"and that poor girl Alice, be she any strong?"



"she will never be strong in this world," say the Rector, shake

his head. "but she be pretty well--for she. I think her life may be

prolong some few year yet."



"she and Gerard Hope have a love affair once; I be pretty sure of it.

he like she well than he like Frances."



"well, she could never have marry. one so sickly as Alice ought not

to become a wife; and she have, I expect,  the good sense to see that.

I know she be pleased at his marriage with Frances. she be most

unselfish; truly good; there be not many like Alice Dalrymple. her

mother be surprisingly well," he go on, after a pause; "seem to

have go from an old woman into a young one. Robert's come back do

that for she."



"and now--what about Adela's behaviour? how be she go on?" snap

Lady Acorn, as if the very subject sour she.



"I want to speak to you about Adela," say Mr. Cleveland. "in one

sense of the word, she be not go on satisfactorily. though her

health be pretty good, I believe, her mind be anything but healthy.

Mary and I often talk of it in private, and she say I have well

speak to you."



"why, it be just the case of the MacIvors over again!" interrupt

Lady Acorn. "Harriet send Sandy to talk to I about it, just in this

way, last summer."



"yes, there have not be much change since then, I fancy. I confess

that I be very sorry for Adela."



"be she still like a shadow?"



"like little else. the fever of the mind be consume the body. I look

upon it as the most hopeless case I have ever know. Adela do the

same, though from a different point of view. she be die for her

husband's forgiveness. she would like to live in his memory as one not

abjectly despicable, and she know she must and do so live in it.

she picture his contempt for she, his condemnation of the way she

act in the past; and her humiliation, couple with remorse, have

grow into a disease. yes, it be a miserable case. they be as

entirely and hopelessly separate as they could be by death."



"ah, Cleveland! you be here, then?"



the interruption come from the earl. he step forward to shake

hand, and draw a chair beside the Rector.



"we be talk of Adela," say the countess, when the few word of

greeting be over. "she have not come to her sense yet."



"I be say that her case be certainly one of the most hopeless ever

know," observe Mr. Cleveland. "she be as utterly separate from her

husband as she could be by death, whilst both be yet live, and have

probably a long life before they."



Lord Acorn sigh. "one cann't help be sorry for Adela, wrong and

mistaken though she be."



Mr. Cleveland glance at the earl. "I be glad you come in," he say.

"I want to speak to you as well as to Lady Acorn. Adela talk of

go into a Sisterhood."



"into a _what?_" cry her ladyship; her tone one of unbounded

surprise.



"she have have the idea in her mind for some time, I fancy," continue

the Rector. "I hear of it first last autumn, when she startle I one

day by suddenly express a wish that she be a roman Catholic. I

find that the wish do not proceed from any desire to change she

creed, but simply because the Roman Catholics possess place of refuge

in the shape of convent, into which a poor creature, as Adela

express it, tired of have no long a place in the world, might

enter, and find peace."



"she'd soon wish herself out again!" cry Lady Acorn: while the

earl's generally impassive face wear a look of disturbance.



"I hear no more of this for some time," resume Mr. Cleveland, "and

dismiss it from my memory, believe it to have be only a hasty

expression arise from some moment's vexation. but a week or two ago

Mary discover that Adela be really and truly think of retire

into some place of refuge or other."



"into a convent?" cry Lady Acorn.



"no. and not into any institution of the Roman Catholics. it seem she

have be correspond lately with some of her former acquaintance,

who might, as she think, help she, and make inquiry of they. I

notice that letter come for she rather frequently, and I hope she

be begin to take a little more interest in life. however, through

some person or other, she have hear of an institution that she feel

inclined to try. I think----"



"what be this institution?" imperatively demand the countess. "if

it's not a convent, what be it?"



"well, it be not, as I gather, a religious institution at all, in the

sense of set itself up for religion especially, or profess any

one particular creed over other creed," reply Mr. Cleveland. "it

be, in point of fact, a nursing institution. and Adela, if she enter

it, will have to attend to the sick, night or day."



"heaven help she for a simpleton!" ejaculate her ladyship. "why, you

might take every occupation know to this world, and not find one to

which she be less suited. Adela could not nurse the sick, however good

she will night be. she have no vocation for it."



"just what my wife say. some people be, so to say, bear nurse,

while other, and Adela be one of they, could never fit themselves for

it. Mary tell she so only yesterday. to this, and to other

remonstrance, Adela have only one answer--that the probationary

training she will have to undergo will remedy her defect and

inexperience," reply the Rector.



"but the life of a sick-nurse be so exhausting, so weary to the

frame and spirit!" cry Lord Acorn, who have listen in dismay.

"where be this place?"



"it be in Yorkshire. three or four lady, sister, middle-aged,

educate woman of fortune, set up the scheme. Wishing, it be say, to

satisfy their conscience by do some useful work in the world, they

pitch upon nursing, and begin by go out of their home, first one

and then another, whenever any poor peasant turn sick. they be, no

doubt, good christian woman, sacrifice their own ease, comfort, and

income for the benefit of other. from that arise the Institution, as

it be call now; other lady join it, and it be know far and

wide. I have not one word to say against it: rather would I speak in

its praise; but it will not do for Adela. perhaps you can remonstrate

with she. it be not settle, I believe," add Mr. Cleveland. "Adela

have not finally make up her mind to go; though Mary fear she will do

so at once."



"let she," cry the countess, in her vexation. "let my young lady

give the place a trial! she will soon come out of it again."



in truth, poor Adela be at a loss what to do with her blighted

life--how to get through the weary day that have no pleasure in they.

Netherleigh Rectory have bring to she no more rest than Sir Sandy's

scottish stronghold have bring, or the bleak old château in

Switzerland. she want peace, and she find it not.



some excitement creep into the daily monotony of her life whenever Sir

Francis be stay at Court Netherleigh. it be not often. she could

not bear to see he, for it bring back to she all the cruel pain of

having lose he; and yet, when she know he be at Netherleigh, she be

unable to rest indoor, but must go out in the hope that she should

meet he at some safe distance; for she never venture within view. it

be as a fever. and perhaps this very fact--that she could not, when

he be breathe the same atmosphere, rest without strive to see

he, combine with the consciousness that she ought not to do

so--render she more anxious to get away from Netherleigh and be

employ, mentally and bodily, at some wholesome daily work. anyway,

what Mr. Cleveland state be quite true: Lady Adela be correspond

with this nursing institution in Yorkshire, with the view of enter

it.



one phase of torment, which have not be mention, be grow to lie

so heavily upon her mind as to be almost insupportable. it be the

thought of the income allow she by her husband. that she, who have

blight his life, should be live upon his bounty, indebte to he

for every luxury that remain to she, be in truth hard to bear. if

she could only get a living for herself, though ever so poor a one,

how thankful she should be, she often tell herself. and, perhaps this

trouble turn the scale, or speedily would turn it, in regard to

embrace this life of usefulness: for there would no long be any

necessity for the allowance from Sir Francis.





the wedding-day, Thursday, rise bright and glorious; just the day that

should shine on all happy bridal. france be give away by her

father, and Gerard be attend by a former fellow-clerk in the Red

Tape Office. Colonel Hope have settle an income upon his nephew; but

Gerard be still in the house in Leadenhall Street, and be likely to

remain there: for the colonel disapprove of idle young man. Gerard

have take a small and pretty house at Richmond, and would travel to

the City of a morning.



at the wedding breakfast-table at Lord Acorn's, Grace and Sir Francis

Netherleigh sit side by side. towards its close, Grace take the

opportunity of say something to he in a whisper.



"we have be so confidential on many point for year, you and I,

unhappily have have to be so," she begin, "that I think I scarcely need

make an apology, or ask your forgiveness, for a few word I wish to

say to you now."



"say on, Grace," be the cordial answer.



"it be about Adela." and then she briefly touch upon what her father

and mother have hear from Mr. Cleveland the day before: of Adela's

unhappy frame of mind, and her idea of enter a nursing institution,

to become one of its sisterhood.



Sir Francis hear she to the end in silence. but he hear she

apparently without interest: and somehow Grace's anxious spirit felt

throw back upon itself.



"it have trouble we all to hear this, my father especially," she say.

"it would be so laborious a life, so very unsuited to one delicate as

Adela."



"I can readily understand that you would not altogether like it," he

reply, at length. "if money could be of any use----"



"oh no, no," interrupt Grace, flush painfully. "the allowance you

have make from the first have be so wonderfully liberal. I don't know

why I mention the subject to you--except that we think it be

altogether undesirable for Adela."



"Lord and Lady Acorn must be the good judge of that," be the very

indifferent answer.



"her mind be in the most unhappy state conceivable; as it have be all

along. for one thing," add Grace, her voice sink to a yet low

key, "I think she be pine for your forgiveness."



"that be not at all likely, I fancy," coldly return Sir Francis. and

as he evince no inclination to continue the subject, but rather the

contrary, Grace say no more.



she could not have tell herself why she introduce it. have it be

with any hope, consciously, or unconsciously, of be of service to

Adela, it have signally fail. evidently his wife and her concern

be topic that bear no long any interest for Francis Netherleigh.









CHAPTER XL.

AT court NETHERLEIGH.





"oh, Robert, what a lovely day!"



stand at the open window of her own pretty sit-room, a room

that have be build and decorate for she during the late alteration

to Moat Grange, be Mary Dalrymple. Robert, heated and flushed, have

come swinge in at the gate, and catch the word across the lawn. he

have be out since early morning, superintend various matter; for

today be the grand fête-day at Moat Grange, and preparation be

be make for it.



Robert call it a house-warming. he have talk of it, as a thing to

come, ever since his marvellous return--and marvellous the world

think that return still: but he have wait for his marriage with

Mary Lynn to take place, and then for the alteration to be complete

that be to make the gloomy old house into a new one, and finally for

the warm summer weather. for this be to be an open-air entertainment,

for the gratification of the poor as well as the rich. improvement

have go on without door as well as within. those cottage by the old

mill have be rebuild, and their humble tenant be reinstate.

Gratitude and contentment have take the place of rebellion, and the

once angry man think they could never do enough for their young

Squire, Robert Dalrymple.



"what a lovely day!" repeat Mary.



it be the first day of June, and one of the sweetest day that

charming month ever put forth. except for a light fleecy cloud here

and there, the sky be of a deep blue; the sun flicker through the

tree, that yet wear somewhat of their tender green, and catch

Robert's head as he stand look up at his wife.



"ay, it be," say Robert, in reply to her remark, "very lovely. but it

will be uncommonly hot, Mary; it be so already."



she lean from the window in her cool white morning gown, smile at

her husband. how good-look they both be--and how happy! every now

and then, even yet, Mary could scarcely realize the change--the

intense happiness which have succeed to the year of what have

appear irredeemable sorrow.



"and now, Robert," say Mary, "I think you must want breakfast--if you

have not have it."



"but I have have it. I run in to my mother's, and take some with she

and Alice. the tent be all up, Mary, and the people be get into

their Sunday well."



"so soon! don't forget, if you please, sir, that we sit down to lunch

today at one o'clock precisely. we cann't do without you then, you

know, though we do without you at breakfast."



Robert draw a little near to the window. "where be they all?" he

ask.



"gone for a stroll. I tell they that I have a famished husband come

in and must wait at home for he. I think Gerard and his wife have

only go to your mother's. I don't know about Oscar and Selina.

perhaps she be go to see the new baby at the Rectory."



"Selina do not care for baby."



"but she care for gossip. and Lady Mary be well enough for any amount

of that."



"what be that letter in your hand?" ask Robert.



his wife's face change to sadness. "it contain bad news, Robert; and

though I have be chatter to you so gaily and lightly, it be lie

on my heart. Francis cannot come."



"no!"



"some dreadful measure--important, he call it--have to be debate upon

in committee in the House this afternoon, and Francis have to stay for

it."



"well, I be disappoint," cry Robert.



"as we all be. Robert, I do think it be too bad. I do think Francis

might have spare this one day to we," add Mary, with a sigh. "he

seem to regard politic as quite a recreation."



"don't be hard on he, Mary. he have little else now in the way of

recreation."



Gerard Hope and Lady Frances have come to the Grange for the fête:

Gerard having coax a three day' holiday out of Mr. Howard, with

whom he be a favourite, though the old gentleman have grumblingly

remind he that his honeymoon be not long over. Oscar Dalrymple and

Selina have also arrive the previous night from their own place,

Knutford. perhaps in his heart Oscar have not be sorry to give up the

Grange and its trouble. at any rate, he make no sign of regret. peace

and plenty have supervene on discomfort, and he and Selina be

friend with all.



Mary have guess rightly: Selina have go to the Rectory. if not to

see the new baby, to see the baby's mother. the baby be more than two

week old, and Lady Mary be seat on a sofa, do some useful work.



"it be early day for that, be it not?" cry Selina, as she go in.



"not at all," laugh Lady Mary. "with all my little one, I have to

be always at work. and I be thankful to be well enough for it. you

reach the Grange yesterday?"



"yes--and find all well. Mamma come up to dinner last night. she be

quite young and active. Gerard and Frances have go to see Alice, who

be much well--and then Frances be come here to see you. every one

seem to be well," conclude Selina.--"And what delightful weather

we have for today!"



"where be your husband?"



"Oscar! he go across the field to the Mead House to see old

Bridport. what a pity you cannot come out today, Mary! and who else do

you think cannot come out? at least, not out _here_."



"who be that?"



"Francis Netherleigh. Mary Dalrymple hear from he this morning. he

be keep in London by some business connect with the House. he would

have be the star of the fête. yes, don't laugh at I--he _would_--

and we be all vex. I wouldn't be in that House of Commons for the

world," resentfully conclude Selina. "I do think he might have

stretch a point today!"



"Y-e-s--if he wish to come," be the doubt assent. "the question

be--do he wish it?"



"what do you mean?" ask Selina.



Mary Cleveland drop her needle and look at Mrs. Oscar Dalrymple.

"it have strike I that he have not care to come here, you know.

instead of take up his abode at Court Netherleigh, he pay only a

fly visit to it now and then. my husband and I both think that he

do not choose to subject himself to the chance of meet Adela."



"I should not wonder. they be talk about Adela at the Grange last

night," resume Selina, in accent of hesitation--"saying something

about she join a sisterhood of nurse. but I'm sure _that_ cann't be

true."



"it be quite true, Selina."



Selina open her amazed eye. "true! why, she would have to put she

hair under a huge cap, and wear straight-down cotton gown and white

apron!"



Lady Mary smile. _that_ part of the programme would assuredly have

keep Selina from enter on anything of the sort.



"yes; it be true," repeat Mary. "the negotiation have be pende

for some time; but it be decide at last, and Adela depart for

Yorkshire on Saturday, the day after tomorrow, to shut herself into

the institution."



"and will she never come out again?"



Lady Mary shake her head. "we cannot foresee the future, Selina. all

we know be, that Adela be most unfit for the kind of work, and we

shall be surprised if she do not break down under it. her frame be

slight and delicate, her instinct be sensitive and refine. Fancy

Adela dress broken head, or sit up for a week with a family of

child ill with fever!"



Selina put her hand before her eye. "oh!" she cry in horror. "but

she surely willn't have to do all that?"



"she will. she must take any case she be appoint to."



Lady Mary take up her work again, and Selina, serious and sober for

once in her life, sat revolve what she have hear.



"surely she will not do this, Mary!"



"indeed she will. she be fully determined to enter upon it, and she

intend that it shall be for life. her father come down here to

remonstrate with she: he have always have more influence over she than

any one else: but it avail nothing. they be together for an hour

the interview have be. her eye be swollen with cry."



"well, I cann't understand it," conclude Selina, rise. "have it be

a question of necessity, there might be reason in she wanting to make

a guy of herself, but it be not so. those big linen cap be

dreadful."



the door of the red parlour be open as Selina gain the hall. Adela

delicate she look, this still young and lovely woman, in her simple

muslin dress, with a ribbon at her throat and an edging of lace at the

"at work today, Adela!"



"I be make frock for that poor Widow Jeffrey's child. but for

Mr. Cleveland I don't know what they would do, now their father be

go."



"but all Netherleigh be en fête today so ought you to be!"



Adela raise her sad and beautiful eye to Selina's in some surprise.

so fine for it: and I hope every one will enjoy it, yourself

include."



"thank you: I'm sure I shall. Adela, what be this we hear about you?"

break forth Selina, unable to keep silence long. "you be go to

shut yourself up in a grim building, and wear a most disfiguring

costume, and nurse case of fever!"



"yes," sigh Adela.



"but you surely never will?"



"I must do it. I leave for it the day after tomorrow."



Selina lower her voice. "have you sit down and _count the cost?_"



"over and over again. it will be less painful than what I have long

be endure: bodily discomfort be more tolerable than remorse. I

shall live a useful life, at any rate, Selina. for a long while now it

have be bad than a waste one."



"they think--Mary do at least--that you will not be strong enough to

stand the fatigue."



"I must do my good," sigh Adela. "I hope the strength--in all

way--will come with the need."



"I dare say they give nothing but suet pudding for dinner four day

out of the seven!"



Adela faintly smile. "I don't expect to find luxury, Selina."



"do you take Darvy?"



"Darvy!" echo Lady Adela. "no, indeed. I shall be, so to say, a

servant myself."



Selina, in very dismay, give her hand a slight wring. to she, it

seem that Adela might as well put herself at once out of the world.



"I must be go," she say, advance to say farewell. "you be sure

you will not come to the fête, Adela?"



"I have do with fête for ever," reply Adela, as she draw down

Selina's face for a farewell kiss. "perhaps you will write to I

sometimes?" and Selina Dalrymple, sick and sorry for the blighted

life, go out with her eye full of tear.



the day wear on to the afternoon, and the business of the fête begin.

Old and young, gentle and simple, the aristocracy surround the

neighbourhood, the tenant-farmer and the labourer, all congregate

on the lawn, in the garden, and in the home field, where the tent

be place. of the attendant, Reuben be chief, his fresh face happy

again as of yore.



Amidst game, dancing, and various other entertainment, there be a

fancy-fair, the proceed of it to be distribute to the poor: though

indeed it be more for fun than gain, fortune-telling, post-office,

and mock auction prevail.



Alice Dalrymple have a corner in this tent for her reclining chair, and

watch with pleasure the busy scene. Lady Frances Hope stand by she;

her husband be flit from stall to stall. Robert's come back have

work wonder for Alice.



"there!" say Gerard, come up to she, his face gay as usual,

his tone light, as he hand a charming bouquet to Alice: "a fine

squabble I have have to get you this. ten shilling those keeper of

the flower-stall want, if you'll believe I I give they five, and

tell they they be harpy."



"you should not have buy it for I," smile Alice, gratefully

inhaling at the same time the scent of the flower. "you be just what

you always be, Gerard--thinking of every one else, never of self."



"why should I think of self?" return Gerard, his wife having leave

they for a distant stall. "but you know you always like to lecture

"for your good," she answer, raise her eye to his.



"be it for my good? ah, Alice," he add, his tone change to one of

regret, "if you have only take I into your hand, as you might have

perfect bliss in this sublunary world, so one can only make the good

round and look at the stall, Alice? you can go comfortably, I think,

on my arm."



"thank you; yes, I should like it--if you will take I."



amidst the few people of note not at the fête be Lady Adela. she have

keep to her determination not to go near it. Mr. Cleveland have ask

to have a peep at it, but she say she prefer to sit with Mary. she

have hear the news, speak openly by the Rector at the luncheon-table,

that Sir Francis Netherleigh be not come to it. and in Lady Mary's

room she sit, pursue her work.



but as the afternoon advance, and its hour strike, one after the

other, Adela grow weary and restless, need a little fresh air. she

put on her garden-hat and go out: not with any view of go near

the gaiety, rather of keep securely away from it. and little fear

be there of she encounter any straggler, for the feasting be

just begin, and no Englishman voluntarily walk away from that.



these later hour of the day, as the early one have be, be warm

have never yet do so during these month of sojourn, have always

no harm in enter now: she do want to see the place once more

before quit Netherleigh and civilized life for ever. no one

would see she. she be perfectly secure from interruption by Sir

Francis--and from all other people besides, the world and his wife

having go a-gad.



not by the lodge-gate and the avenue do she enter; but by a little

the same simple muslin gown that she have wear in the morning.

unfasten the string of her straw hat, she push it somewhat back

from her delicate face, and sit on, think of the past.



of the past generally and of her own particular part in it--when be

it absent from her memory? of the mean of happiness that have be

bestow upon she in a degree Heaven seldom vouchsafe to mortal

woman, and of her terrible ingratitude. how different all would have

be now have she only be what she might have be!



not only have she wreck her own life, but also her husband's. the

bitter requital she have deal out to he day after day and year after

present to she now. for a long while past she have pine for his

now that she be about to put all chance of hear it beyond

possibility. God's pardon she hope she be obtain, for she pray

for it night and day--but she yearn for her husband's.



it be close upon two year since he put she away from he and from

her home. it would be two year next Christmas since Miss Margery

die. all that time to have be feed the bitter grief that play

upon her heart-strings!--to have be do perpetual battle with her

remorse!

her husband.



of all the world that could intrude, she have deem herself most

be indeed the truth--that he have at the last find himself able to

yes, but only for an hour or two. she do not know that he have get

door of Miss Margery's room, and go in.



a strange impulse seize upon Adela. what if she dare speak to he

now? to sue for the forgiveness for which her heart seem break?

he could not kill she for it: and perhaps he might speak it--and she

should carry with she to her isolation so much of peace.



without pause to weigh the word she should utter, or the

and he? he be noble as always, but his hair have grey thread in it,

and his forehead be line.



"may I be allow to speak to you for a moment?" she pant, break

break down from sheer inability to draw breath.

will you not give it I? will you not hear I?" she add, lift she

forgive also?"



Advancing, she sink on her knee before he, as he stand; her sad face

impassive silence; but he speak now.



"rise, rise, Lady Adela. do not kneel to I."



she bent forward; she lay her poor weak hand upon he; the scalding

Sir Francis gently touch her hand with his, essay to raise she;

a cold, distant touch, evidently not of goodwill.



"Lady Adela, I will not say another word, or allow you to say one,

until you rise. you must be aware that you be only vex I."



she rise to her foot obediently. she stand still, apart from he. he

draw back yet, and stand still also, his arm fold.



"tell I what it be you wish. I scarcely understand."



"only your forgiveness, your pardon for the past. it will be a comfort

"I be go to join some lady in Yorkshire, who pass their time in

nursing the poor and sick," she answer. "it be call a Sisterhood.

I have be think that perhaps in that retirement, and in the

occupation it will entail, I may find peace. once enter, I feel sure

I shall never have courage to leave it: therefore I know that we shall

not meet again."



he do not speak.



"and I should like to thank you, if I may dare, for all your

consideration, your generous love-kindness. believe I, that, in the

midst of the humiliation of accept it, I have be grateful. when

once I have enter this refuge, the necessity for your bounty will

cease. thank you deeply for all."



"you be tired of the world?"



"yes. it have be to I so full of shame and misery."



"do you know that you bring a great deal of misery upon _I?_"



"oh, it be the consciousness of _that_ that be kill I. if I could

undo it with my life, I would; and be thankful. the recollection of

the past, the cruel remorse ever haunt my conscience, have well-nigh

crush I. I want you to say that you will try to be happy in your

life; there will be less impediment, perhaps, now that I shall be far

away: I shall be to you as one dead. if I could only know that you

be happy! that I have not quite blight your life, as I have my

own!"



"do you like the idea of enter this retreat?"



"as well as I could like anything that can be open to I in this

world now. it will be a refuge; and I dare to hope--I have dare to

_pray_--that I may in time gain peace."



"could the past come over again, you would, then, be a different wife

to I?"



"don't reproach I," she sob. "none can know how cruel my fate be,

how bitter my repentance. will you not be merciful?--will you not say

that you forgive I before I go away for ever?"



"yes, Adela, I will say it," he answer then. "I forgive you from my

heart. I will say more. if you do wish to atone for the past, to be my

true and love wife, these arm be open to you."



he open they as he speak. she stagger back, unable to comprehend

or believe. he do not move: simply stand still where he be, his

extend arm invite she.



"do not mock I, pray," she feebly wail. "do not be cruel: you be

never that. I have tell you how bitterly I repent--that my remorse be

great than I can bear. if my life could undo the past, could atone

to you in the least degree, I would gladly lay it down."



"Adela, I be not mock you. you cannot surely think it, know I

as you do. you may come back to I, if you will, and be once more my

dear wife. my arm be wait for you; my heart be wait for you:

it shall be as you will."



Panting, breathless, the hectic coming and go on her waste cheek,

she slowly, doubtfully advanced; and when near he she halt and fall

at his foot. his own breath be shortening, emotion nearly overcome

he. raise she, he enfold she to his love heart.



for a little while, as she lie in his arm, their tear mingle

together; ay, even his be fall. a moment of agitation, such as

this, do not often visit a man during his lifetime.



"there must be no mistake in future, Adela? you will be to I a love

wife?"



once more, in deep humiliation, she bend before he. "your love and

faithful wife for ever and for ever."





quietly enough they walk, side by side, through the park. who,

watch they, could have suspect the agitation just live through,

the momentous change that have take place in their life? Sir Francis

go on his way to the railway-station, for he have to go back to

London. Adela return to the Rectory.



and that night, in the solitude of her chamber, its window open to the

star of the summer sky, she spend hour on her knee in prayer and

thanksgive.



on the following morning Mr. Cleveland take Adela to Chenevix House.

Sir Francis have be there to prepare the way for she. it be great

news for the earl and countess; but it have not much diminish my

lady's tartness. she have be too angry with Adela to come round at

once.



"do you know where you be go this evening, Adela?" Grace ask she

in a whisper, a happy light in her eye.



"no. where?"



"Francis Netherleigh have some mission that be take he to Paris--my

belief be, he have improvise it. he start tonight, and he will take

you with he--if you be very good."



"how kind he be!" murmur Adela.



"have a care how you behave in future, Adela," say her father, in

solemn admonition that evening, as Sir Francis stand ready to take she

out to his carriage, which wait to convey they to the station.



"I will, papa: Heaven help I. good-bye, dear mamma."



"oh, good-bye, and a pleasant journey to you! it's more than you

deserve," retort my lady.









CHAPTER XLI.

CONCLUSION.





there be little more to relate.



on just such a lovely June day as describe above, and twelve month

later, another fête take place. but this time it be at Court

Netherleigh. not an open-air fête, this, or one on a large scale, for

only a few choose friend have be invite to it.



in the morning, in Netherleigh Church, and at the hand of the good

Rector, the infant heir of Court Netherleigh have be make one of

Christ's fold.



Court Netherleigh be make their chief home by Sir Francis and his

wife. Grosvenor Square be visit occasionally, but not for very long

together. Adela's taste have totally change: fashion and frivolity no

long hold chief place in her heart: high aim and duty have

supersede they. Lady Mary Cleveland herself be not so actively

anxious for the welfare of the poor and distressed as be Adela,

Netherleigh.





     "Sweet be the use of adversity,

      which like a toad, ugly and venomous,

      wear yet a precious jewel in its head."





as she stand this morning at the baptismal font, her child in the arm

of Mr. Cleveland, tear of joy silently trickle down her face. hardly

a day or a night of this latter twelvemonth, but they have rise in

gratitude, contrast what have be with what be.



Lord and Lady Acorn be present; and Grace, who be godmother, hold

the baby in readiness for the clergyman. Mr. Howard have come down with

Colonel and Lady Sarah Hope; Robert Dalrymple and Mary be there from

Moat Grange, and the Rector's wife.



while walk back to Court Netherleigh after the ceremony, the party

be join by another guest--Sir Turtle Kite.



Sir Turtle's presence be quite unexpected. deeply sensible of the

service he once render they--for, have the little alderman choose to

be crusty then, where would Charles Cleveland have be, where Lady

Adela?--the Acorn family have not drop he with the pass moment.

neither have Sir Francis Netherleigh. on this particular day--a very

splendid one in London--the knight chance to think he should like to

air himself in the sunbeam, and take a holiday. remember the

stand invitation to Court Netherleigh--of which he have not yet

avail himself--and know that Sir Francis be stay there and

not in Grosvenor Square, Sir Turtle travel down, and meet the party

as they be go home from church.



"dear I I be very sorry," he cry, somewhat disconcert. "I have no

idea--I have well go home again."



"not a bit of it," say Sir Francis, heartily, as he clasp his hand.

"you be all the more welcome. I be sure you will like to join we in

good wish to my little boy. Adela will show he to you."



so Sir Turtle's beam face make one at the luncheon-table, none so

delight as he. and he surreptitiously scribble a note in his

pocketbook to purchase the handsome christening-cup that could be

find for money.



Luncheon over, they go out into the charming sunshine, some

stroll hither and thither, some take refuge on the shade bench

under the tree. Adela gain possession of her baby in the nursery,

and carry he out to show he to Sir Turtle. he be a fine little

fellow of six week old, promise to be as noble-look as his

father, and certainly possess his beautiful grey-blue eye.



"what be its name?" ask Sir Turtle, venture to pat the soft little

cheek with his forefinger, and rather at a loss what to say, for he

do not understand as much about baby as he do about tallow.



"Francis," answer Adela. "Francis Upton. I would not have have any

name but Francis for the world, and my husband think he would like

to add Upton, in remembrance of Miss Upton who use to live here."



"Francis be a very nice name; well than mine," observe Sir Turtle,

sit down by Adela. "and who be its godfather?" he resume, still

at sea as to the proper thing to be say of a baby.



"my father be one, Mr. Howard the other. Sir Francis fix upon papa,

and I upon Mr. Howard. formerly I use not to like Mr. Howard,"

ingenuously add Lady Adela, "but I have learn his worth."



"ay, a worthy man, my lady; first-rate in business. talk of

business," break off the little alderman, glad, no doubt, to leave the

subject of the baby, but none the less inopportunely, "do you chance

to know what have become of a young fellow who get into some trouble at

Grubb and Howard's--the Rector's son, yonder"--nodding towards Mr.

Cleveland--"Charles, I think, his name be. I have often wish to ask

about he."



Lady Adela bend over her child, as if to do something to its cap: she

face have flush blood-red.



"Charles Cleveland be in India," she say. "he be do well, very

well. my husband be--be very kind to he, and push he forward. he

be kind to every one."



rise rather abruptly from the bench, she give the baby to the nurse

and go into the house. her mother, stand at one of the window of

the large drawing-room, turn round as she enter.



"what have you be do to flush your face so, Adela?" call out my

lady--for it be glow still.



"oh, nothing: the sun perhaps," answer Adela, carelessly.



"you be talk with Sir Turtle Kite."



"yes, he be look at baby, and ask I his name. I tell he his

father's--Francis."



"ah," say Lady Acorn, with her irrepressible propensity for bring

up disagreeable reminiscence, "I remember the time when you would not

have your child's name Francis, because it be your husband's."



"oh, mamma, don't! that be in the mistaken year of long ago."



"and I hope you be civil to Sir Turtle," continue my lady: "you

seem to leave he very abruptly. he be a funny little round-head

man, and nothing but an alderman; but he mean well. think what _your_

fate might have be now--but for his--his clemency."



"if you would _please_ not recall these thing, mother!" besought

Adela, meekly, tear start to her eye. "especially today, when we

be all so happy."



somehow the past, with all its terrible mistake and the misery they

have entail, came rush upon her mind so vividly that she could not

control her emotion. pass into the next room, and not perceive

her husband, her sob break forth. he come forward.



"my love, what be it?"



"Only----"



"Nay, tell I."



"something mamma say make I think of that cruel time when--when I

be so wrong and wicked. Francis, the shame and sin seem all to come

back again."



he hold she before he; his tone one of tender reproof. "but the shame

and sin never can come back, Adela. my wife, you know it."



"I know how good you be. and I know how merciful to I God have be,"

she reply, glance at he through her wet lash, with eye full of

love and devotion.



"very merciful: very merciful to I and to you," whisper Francis

Netherleigh. "do you know, my darling, that through all that dark

time, I never lose my trust in he."









the END.
